*Editor’s Preface*

*The* Fanghu Waishi *(Outer History of the Square Flask)* is the title of works by Lu Qianxu of the Eastern Lineage. *The* Yuanqiao Neipian *(Inner Chapters of the Round Peaks)*, then, is the title of works by Li Hanxu of the Western Lineage. Thus was established the opposing stance of East and West, and thus too was the Dao of their shared inheritance determined. The Western Star (Lu Qianxu’s alternate name, Lu Xixing, also called Chang Geng) and the Western Moon (Li Hanxu’s courtesy name, Tuanyang, also called Li Xiyue) illuminated one another, their radiance resplendent—how could this be mere chance?

Yet within the Daoist lineages, all know only of the *Fanghu Waishi* in circulation, while none are aware of the publication of the *Yuanqiao Neipian*. Why is this? For no other reason than that disciples and compilers of the Western Lineage failed to grasp the original intent behind the Patriarch Li’s writings. Lacking systematic organization, each piece bore its own title, and so the overarching name *Yuanqiao Neipian* was entirely submerged, its traces all but lost.

Master Chen Yingning, the late president of the Daoist Association, wrote prolifically with profound insight, yet even he was not immune to the irony of "seeking the ox while riding it," lamenting in his works *Daoqiao Tan* (*Discourses on the Dao’s Apertures*), *Sanche Mizhi* (*Secret Essentials of the Three Vehicles*), and *Yuanqiao Neipian* that they had never been published and that he had not even seen a manuscript. The eminent scholar Li Daoshan, in his *Brief Biography of the Perfected Li Hanxu*, likewise noted: "(The Patriarch) received the mandate of the Three Perfected Ones and composed *Annotations on the Thirteen Scriptures of the Most High*, *Elucidations on the Scripture of the Great Cavern Elder Immortal*, and two commentaries on *The Song of the Rootless Tree*... all of which were published. However, *The Inner Chapters of the Round Peaks*, *The Secret Essentials of the Three Vehicles*, and *Discourses on the Dao’s Apertures* remain unpublished." Alas!

Little did they realize the principle of correspondence between the two works—external alignment and internal alignment as inevitable complements. I racked my brains, exhaustively studying the classics, yet could not reconcile the discrepancy. Given the full title’s symmetry of inner and outer, how could it be that a monumental work of such grandeur had vanished without a trace, never to be published? Reluctantly, I turned again to *Hanxu Mizhi* (*Secret Essentials of Hanxu*, a volume in the *Eastern Daoist Cultivation Library*). By chance, while poring over the preface to *Annotations on the Thirteen Scriptures of the Most High*, I grasped the key. The preface was written by the Three Recluses of Mount Shu, who concealed their true names. It reads:

"One day, while wandering among Daoist temples, I came upon several volumes of the *Yuanqiao Waishi* (initially intended to parallel the Eastern Patriarch’s *Fanghu Waishi* and thus also called *Outer History*; later revised to *Inner Chapters* for symmetry, though it is the same work). Among them was *Annotations on the Thirteen Scriptures*, with the commentary on the *Daode Jing* being the most exhaustive. Appended was *Xuntu Jiu Ceng* (*Nine Layers of the Path*). Though brief, these few words suffice to reveal its essence and resolve the enigma."

I then examined the preface by Master Pure Yang, which states:

"Hanxuzi was a transcendent talent... Upon hearing the teachings, he immediately gained insight and, roused by his own abilities, composed the *Yuanqiao Waishi* as a counterpart to Lu’s work. Thus, where Lu has *Xuanfu Lun* (*On the Subtleties of the Mysterious*), this work has *Daoqiao Tan*; where Lu has *Jiuzheng Pian* (*Seeking Rectification*), this has *Xuntu Shuo* (*Discourse on the Path*). Most wondrous of all is the commentary on the *Daode Jing*, which serves as an eternal beacon, titled *Donglai Zhengyi* (*Eastern Advent of Right Meaning*), on par with Lu’s *Nanhua Fumo* (*Supplementary Ink to the Southern Florescence*)."

Reading this, I could not help but clap in admiration, for at last the truth became clear. Thus, I have compiled the Patriarch Li’s various works into a single volume.

Herein it is rightfully titled *Inner Chapters of Yuanqiao*, fulfilling the long-cherished wish of Patriarch Li. Some say that the Western Patriarch Li Xiyue was the reincarnation of the Eastern Patriarch Lu Xixing. In the *Records of Spiritual Resonance* shown to me by Li, he himself did not deny this. Thus, history may cycle, human nature may reappear, and images may repeat. Reading the "Three True Poems" in the *Eastern Advent of Right Meaning*, how can one not marvel at the wonder!

Among them, Lu Xixing’s poem reads:
*"A single commentary can unlock the profound mysteries of the Dao,
Once more the purple qi arrives from the East.
Strumming the zither, playing the flute—a true scholar,
Expounding the Dharma, discoursing on sutras—a master of eloquence.
I dwell in Square Pot, gazing upon the vast sea,
You ascend Yuanqiao, overshadowing Penglai.
Today, seated together by the riverside pavilion,
Reciting the *Nanhua*, we laugh at our own folly."*

Is this praise for Li? Or self-aggrandizement? For two men—or one? The ancient Buddhas manifest in countless forms; Zhuangzi’s coffin was split once, twice, thrice. This startled the courage of the Tian clan of old—where now is the Prince of Chu?

My aspiration is to emulate the Three Recluses: I shall carry only this commentary on the *Daode Jing* and the *Ninefold Layer* chapter, singing long songs among cliffs and valleys, their echoes drowning the clouds over the streams. I shall forget my name and age—this is my preface.

Written by Tianlezi Chen Yuzhao beneath the wondrous winds of Shanshui Mountain

**Volume One: Discourse on the Dao’s Apertures**
By Changyi Mountain Recluse Li Hanxu
Collated by Chen Yingning of Wanjian

**Reader’s Guide to *Discourse on the Dao’s Apertures***

1. The author of this work is surnamed Li, named Xiyue, styled Hanxu, and also known as Tuanyang. A native of Leshan, Sichuan, he attained the Dao in the bingchen year of the Xianfeng reign of the Qing dynasty.

2. In China’s tradition of immortals’ Dao, there were originally two schools: the Southern and Northern. The Southern School began with Zhang Ziyang of Tiantai, Zhejiang, and the Northern School with Wang Chongyang of Zhongnan, Shaanxi. By the Jiajing era of the Ming, Lu Qianxu wrote the *Outer History of Square Pot*, greatly expounding the subtle Daoist winds, and was known as the Eastern School. The author of this work, Li Hanxu, was collectively regarded as the Western School. The Western School’s lineage transmission bears nine characters: *The Western Dao is universal; the great river flows east; the sea and sky are boundless.*

3. Zhang Ziyang received the elixir method from an extraordinary figure in Chengdu but did not disclose his identity. At the same time, Wang Chongxi encountered Liu Haichan, who transmitted the oral formula of the Golden Elixir. Chongxi once remarked that no Daoist in the world could comprehend it except Zhang Pingshu. Thus, Lu Yanfu concluded that Ziyang must have also received transmission from Haichan. Haichan was a disciple of Zhongli Quan, the True Man of Zhengyang, and a fellow student of Lü Zu. Wang Chongyang’s collected works also refer to *"Zhengyang as ancestor, Pure Yang as master, Haichan as uncle."* From this, it is evident that the Southern and Northern Schools share a common origin. Furthermore, Lu Qianxu of the Ming claimed to have met Lü Zu at the Grass Hall by the Northern Sea and personally heard the subtleties of the Dao. Li Hanxu of the Qing also encountered Lü Zu at a Chan monastery on Mount Emei, who secretly imparted the original sound. Thus, the four schools—Southern, Northern, Eastern, and Western—may all be considered transmissions from Lü Zu.

4. The Northern School’s practice emphasizes tranquility. Yet among the Seven Perfected, Liu Zu was renowned for cultivating in brothels, unlike the desolate sitting of the True Man of Everlasting Spring. The Southern School’s oral formulas emphasize yin and yang. Yet among the Five Ancestors, Bai Yuchan was ordained from childhood, wandering as a cloud and water for life, unlike the Chan master Daoguang, who returned to lay life. In expounding Daoist texts, Lu’s works are refined and pure, while Li’s are complex. In explaining the *Human-Primordial Elixir Method*, Lu’s account is simple, while Li’s is intricate. From this, one may see that while the Dao is the same, methods may differ in skill. Though methods may align, oral instructions may vary in simplicity or complexity. It is for the scholar to harmonize and comprehend them, not to impose self-limitation.

5. Zhang Ziyang attained the Dao during the Xining era of Emperor Shenzong of the Song, approximately 830 years before the founding of the Republic. Wang Chongyang attained the Dao during the Dading era of Emperor Shizong of the Jin, approximately 740 years before the Republic. Lu Qianxu attained the Dao during the Longqing era of Emperor Muzong of the Ming, approximately 340 years before the Republic. Li Hanxu attained the Dao during the Xianfeng era of the Qing, approximately 60 years before the Republic.

6. Li Hanxu’s works include *Annotations on the Thirteen Classics of the Supreme*, *Annotations on the Daoist Ballad of the Rootless Tree*, and the compiled and edited *Complete Works of Zhang Sanfeng*, all of which have long circulated widely. Only *Discourse on the Dao’s Apertures*, *Secret Essentials of the Three Vehicles*, and *Inner Chapters of Yuanqiao* remain unpublished, and even the manuscript of *Inner Chapters of Yuanqiao* has not been seen. Now, *Discourse on the Dao’s Apertures* and *Secret Essentials of the Three Vehicles* are published first to satisfy the longing of those devoted to the Dao.

7. This book is a hand-copied manuscript from the collection of Mao Fuchu of Fujian, personally brought to Shanghai by Deng Yucang of Liancheng, Fujian, who entrusted its collation and publication to me. The costs of typesetting, printing, binding, and paper were jointly advanced by Deng Yucang and Zhang Zhuming through arrangements with the Alchemical Scripture Engraving Society. Only now can this work meet its readers—Mao, Deng, and Zhang have rendered no small service.

8. This book has been corrected by me in approximately several dozen places, all of which were errors from successive transcriptions.

The errors herein lie merely in whether the phrasing is coherent or not, not in whether the theories are sound or flawed. The implications are relatively minor, so there is no need to compose a separate collation record, lest it occupy excessive space.

9. At the time, Master Deng urged me to extract the essential points of this book for the benefit of readers. In my humble opinion, terms long customary in alchemical scriptures—such as *li* and *kan*, mercury and lead—are all figurative expressions. They may refer to the heart and kidneys, to spirit and *qi*, or even to male and female; it rests with the reader to delve deeply and grasp their meaning. Were one to insist on equating a given term with a specific entity, opinions would diverge along lines of wisdom and folly, and disputes over right and wrong would never cease. I fear this might inadvertently narrow the book’s scope of application, contravening the original intent of dissemination—better to leave it be.

10. The most illuminating phrase in this book is “the other household.” For instance, Chapter Three states: *“To nourish my own mercury, one must employ the true lead of the other household.”* Chapter Five says: *“In inner refinement of the self, one uses the lead of the other household to nourish the mercury of my household. In inner cultivation of the self, one likewise uses the lead of the other household to nourish the mercury of my household.”* Chapter Eight declares: *“This lead is not the lead of returning elixir; it is the true fire of the other household.”* Chapter Ten observes: *“When the original root leaks away, essence, *qi*, and spirit all fall into the postnatal. If one cannot seek it within oneself, one must seek it in the other.”* Chapter Seventeen notes: *“Original essence resides in my household; true essence resides in the other household.”* Chapter Eighteen states: *“The embodiment of supreme virtue, amply endowed by Heaven, need not seek it in the other household—hence it is called the Heavenly Origin.”* Chapter Twenty-Five says: *“I direct a speck of yin-fire essence to plant within the other household.”* And Chapter Twenty-Nine explains: *“Gathering and refining means gathering the yang lead of the other household to refine the *qi* of the child-pearl in my household.”* From the examples above, the references to “the other household” are indeed thorough. Yet precisely how to interpret these two words remains open to inquiry. If “the other household” refers to the *qi* within the kidneys, then those who refine only the spirit in the heart are mistaken. If it refers to the Great Void beyond the body, then those who refine only the spirit in the heart are likewise mistaken. If it refers to a partner of the opposite sex, then solitary practitioners devoted solely to meditation are mistaken. Readers must focus on these profoundly subtle points to truly perceive the Way in all its manifestations.

Written on the birthday of the Hundred Flowers in the mid-spring of the *dingchou* year of the Republic of China (1937), by Chen Yingning of Wanjiang, at the Abode of Myriad Wonders in Shanghai’s western outskirts.

**Preface by Zhang**
My late great-uncle, Master Jingchuan, was fond of cinnabar arts, originally intending only to cultivate *qi* and refine his nature. As his understanding of the Dao deepened, he grew ever more inspired and diligent. He cherished every book on this path, preserving them all. Some were printed editions, others hand-copied—countless volumes, all sources of daily delight. In the late spring of *xinhai* (1931), as his final hour approached, he summoned my elder brother, Bochun, and said: *“What I have always loved are these few books on the Dao. When I die, take care to preserve them. In ten years, someone will come to claim them—do not begrudge them.”*

Before the appointed time arrives, do not lightly neglect cultivation. This is my final testament—pray do not forget it!”

Though still a child, I too once heard these words. After my elder brother passed away, my younger brother Zhongkuan aired the books, and some, already gnawed by insects, had gone unnoticed. By chance, I came upon them and intended to make a fresh copy to serve as a future master text. Just as I began, my friend Liu Xuanfu paid a visit. Seeing the books, he asked, “Are you also tending to the Dao?”

I explained my purpose, and he exclaimed, “Excellent! This endeavor of yours. I, too, have long cherished these books, though none have guided me in correcting their errors. Now, seeing your late uncle’s original copies—all distilled to essentials, refined from the obscure to the luminous—I am so delighted I cannot contain myself. Might I borrow a copy to transcribe? And if I make an extra one to share your labor, how would that be?”

I had originally wished to keep them private, but recalling my uncle’s words—that when someone came to claim them, they should be handed over without hesitation—how could I begrudge lending a single copy, especially when he offered to transcribe an additional one as a gift? This volume is his transcription. From now on, the preservation of these books—is this not the aid of Xuanfu? And if Xuanfu indeed perfects his learning—is this not the fulfillment of the “ten years hence”? My uncle began it, my friend completed it, and I sit to enjoy the fruits—could anything be easier?

Thus, I set down these beginnings and ends to record them.

Respectfully inscribed by Zhang Richang of Meigao.

Should any virtuous man or faithful woman, moved by good intention, undertake to publish and disseminate these two works—*Talks on the Dao’s Apertures* and *The Secret Essence of the Three Vehicles*—though they themselves may not cultivate in this life, may the Patriarchal Sovereign of Blessed Instruction and the Perfected Lord of Benevolent Teachings, in the life to come, guide them through manifold means, patiently enlighten them, and lead them to renounce the mundane and enter the Dao, escaping the sea of suffering and ascending the ranks of immortality. Should anyone study these two books in depth, pondering their subtleties and putting them into practice, the two Patriarchs will surely uplift them in secret, even in this very life. Though they may not attain the rank of Great Golden Immortal, they shall certainly be spared the torments of rebirth.

Those who print and distribute these two books to fellow seekers, illuminating the great path and sparing them from heterodox and demonic ways, shall see their nine generations of ancestors ascend to heaven, and their descendants shall never bear offspring of evil or lust.

Respectfully added by Zhang Richang of Meigao.

---

**Chapter One: Admonition to Fellow Practitioners**

Deep in the night, seated in meditation—tranquil and natural.
On a rope bed or bamboo couch, cross-legged at the fifth watch.
Having sat, one rises again, at ease and content.
Thus, what has been attained is recorded by lamplight, and I say to my friends:

When the night’s qi is clear and the breath is regulated, the spirit abides.
If one regulates as it is regulated, one avoids the pitfall of dull emptiness.
If one abides as it abides, one avoids the trap of clinging.
At such a time, neither forgetting nor forcing—seeming to forget, seeming to retain.
Silent yet alert, perfectly round and luminous.
Water naturally clears, fire naturally arises, spirit naturally merges, qi naturally converges, wind naturally aligns, the cart naturally moves, withdrawal naturally withdraws, advance naturally advances, addition naturally adds, retreat naturally retreats.
Only because this wondrous attainment is uniquely grasped does one wield control as if by heart—drowsiness naturally departs, distraction naturally gathers.
Could anything be more joyous?

Yet to shun the lamp and feign sleep—I cannot bear to see it; it rests with your own discipline.
To lean on the desk and pretend wakefulness—I dare not partake in it; it rests with your own faith.
To close the eyes and indulge in thoughts—I cannot know it; it rests with your own elimination.
Those who succumb to drowsiness in the clear night are not diligent in this practice.
Those who fall into distraction in the clear night are not devoted to this practice.
Lacking diligence and devotion, one betrays the profound hopes of the Patriarchs.
Our Dao is most wondrous and mysterious; those afflicted by such faults must amend them.

---

**Chapter Two: Dialogue on Opening the Passes**

Several friends asked Tuanyangzi:
“Your discourse on nothingness may be called pure, authentic, and lucid—opening the apertures of the gateway. Yet I do not understand why the Sun Tao school holds that ‘opening the passes and expanding the apertures’ should occur *before* foundation-laying, while Master Qianxu claims the ancient immortals never spoke of it, and now it has arisen. They further say, ‘Adding a snake’s leg is superfluous; a steed’s bones are priceless. The peril of the Great Dao lies in such men.’ Thus, they deem ‘opening the passes and expanding the apertures’ contemptible. Since you share the same teacher, I beg you to explain.”

Tuanyangzi replied:
“Alas! What Master Qianxu spoke of was not contempt, but lament for those unfortunate ones who have lost their primordial purity, compelling them to resort to such minor techniques. The virtue of the lower grade is effortless, not sought through scrutiny. A child whose primordial qi remains unbroken may cultivate purity and attain the immortal embryo without need of return or reversal—why open the passes? Thus, this is deemed a peril to the Great Dao. It is akin to the lament of the Most High: ‘When the Great Dao declines, benevolence and righteousness arise.’

Yet our generation takes saving others as its merit. Of those who transmit these teachings, how many retain the purity of childhood? Thus, expanding the apertures and opening the passes serve to unlock the mystery and clear the path, while returning to the origin and reverting to the root aid our kind and restore the true body. For those who begin the Dao in middle age, as long as they concentrate the spirit with method, regulate the breath with measure, and when the yin heel qi stirs, draw it into the crucible—neither forgetting nor forcing—then when the postnatal qi arises, they refine it again. The true mechanism will move of itself. Seizing its movement to guide it, one need not force the opening, for the pass opens naturally; one need not force the expansion, for the aperture expands naturally. Once the true qi ascends to the Mud Pill Palace, the path of the River Cart may be traversed. All occurs of itself, naturally.

Seizing the moment between movement and stillness, one gently stokes the fire, driving it past the Tailbone Pass and reversing its flow to the Heavenly Valley. Naturally, essence is refined into qi, irrigating the Three Palaces. Thereafter, having obtained the wondrous medicine from without, one captures and restrains the true qi of the body, causing it to congeal without scattering. Then, in mutual affection and longing, like a dragon nurturing its pearl or a hen brooding her egg, the warmth unbroken, they settle together in the Yellow Court, coalescing into a cinnabar orange—this is called the ‘inner elixir.’ Thus, the work of the first stage is complete, the marvel of longevity attained, and the Way of preserving the form perfected.”

Upon hearing this, the friend rejoiced and said, “Without your words, I would have nearly lost myself in longing. Though we are not quick of wit, we shall withdraw and cultivate accordingly.”

**Chapter Three: An Exegesis on the Postnatal Methods**

Of old, the postnatal methods were divided into only two stages: laying the foundation and refining the self. Yet Master Tao Cuncun further observed, “If a cultivator of the Dao does not first open the passes before hastily speaking of laying the foundation and refining the self, it is like scratching an itch from outside the boot—of no benefit whatsoever.” Thus, he divided the postnatal sequence into four stages: opening the passes, laying the foundation, obtaining the medicine, and refining the self.

Fearing that others might harbor doubts, I have composed this to clarify: the ancient division into two stages represents the broad outlines of the postnatal path, while the present division into four stages delineates its finer progression. Opening the passes clears the way for laying the foundation; obtaining the medicine supplies what the foundation requires; and refining the self completes the work of laying the foundation. Though four stages are now distinguished, they remain two in essence, and those two are ultimately one.

As the ancients said, “Subtle details and fine points cannot be transmitted except by a true teacher, nor spoken of except by a worthy disciple.” Though laying the foundation and refining the self do not belong to the highest alchemy, their finer stages exceed even what Master Tao described. The *Cantong Qi* states, “The inferior virtue practices it without ceasing.” Precisely because it does not cease, one perceives its many stages.

Divergent teachings abound, and differing views arise, but all depend on the practitioner’s capacity for synthesis. The Perfected Sun Jiaoluan said, “Those who cultivate the body must first employ the crucible to open the passes.” He also said, “The crucible is the numinous father and numinous mother.” Why are the numinous father and numinous mother called the crucible? Because the parents who give birth to immortals and buddhas differ from ordinary parents, and so they are named the numinous father and numinous mother of the postnatal crucible.

The numinous father and numinous mother unite in reverse flow, whereas ordinary parents join in forward motion to sustain life. The method of reverse flow is constant from beginning to end. Once the crucible is established, spirit and qi intermingle, and accumulation deepens. As accumulation deepens, vigor surges. When vigor surges, the passes expand. When the passes expand, the path of reverse circulation opens, and the route of the River Chariot is traversed.

Yet moving the River Chariot is not the same as opening the passes. Opening the passes relies on postnatal true qi, while the River Chariot relies on postnatal metal and water. When the work reaches the stage of the River Chariot, the daily tasks of laying the foundation and refining the self become indistinguishable.

Qianxu said, “With ceaseless circulation and irrigation, over time it becomes perfectly refined. Qi fills the Three Fields, and above and below harmonize. This is what is meant by ‘constantly allowing qi to surge through and penetrate the joints, naturally filling the valley spirit with essence.’”

I have often called the study of the postnatal path “nurturing the self.” If one carefully examines my words and fathoms its comprehensive stages, the Way of prolonging life and preserving the body will be attained.

What is nurturing the self? The *Cantong Qi* states, “Within, nurture the self in tranquil emptiness.” This is the essential teaching of the postnatal path. The self is one’s inherent nature, the primordial spirit. Within refers to the inner realm, the state of emptiness. In emptiness, there is constant tranquility and peace; only when at peace can one deliberate, and only when tranquil can one respond.

Yet to still the spirit, one must regulate the breath with measure. One exhalation and one inhalation are called a single breath. It must follow its natural course, yet not be left entirely to its own devices. Zhuangzi said, “The true person breathes from the heels.” The heels signify unbroken continuity, an unceasing, subtle presence. Qi penetrates the Bubbling Spring, flowing without interruption.

My teacher said, “Let the inner breath follow the outer breath, and the outer breath follow the inner breath, breath after breath following breath after breath.” This is the subtlety of following the heels. When the inner breath exhales, the outer breath inhales; when the inner breath inhales, the outer breath exhales. Inner and outer breaths, reversing and aligning, generate one another. With measured opening and closing, a wondrous spontaneity arises, requiring no forced effort.

The ancients said, “If one asks how to begin laying the foundation, first understand the mysterious pass of the bellows.” To know the inner breath is to know the bellows. The alchemists say, “Exhale without passing the throat; inhale to return to the root.” This refers to the inner breath. How could mouth and nose breathing prevent it from passing?

Once the breath is regulated, one must also concentrate the spirit. Concentrating the spirit means remaining utterly still, inwardly illuminating the body. Yet this concentration of spirit and regulation of breath are not two separate practices. Regulating the breath gathers the spirit; gathering the spirit awakens awareness; awakened awareness generates qi; and qi, thus generated, is inwardly gathered and stilled. The golden crucible daily fills, primordial yellow and true lead unite, true qi surges into the heart, and is drawn to the Tailbone Pass. With a single thrust, the Three Passes open, the path of the Cowherd and Weaver Girl is revealed, and the Silver River may be drawn.

Yet the Way of nurturing the self is vast indeed. It encompasses regulating the breath, concentrating the spirit, gathering qi, and piercing the passes, while also including the work of laying the foundation and refining the self.

Shangyang said, “Treasure essence and enrich qi—this is nurturing the self. Face circumstances without attachment—this is refining the self.” Nurturing the self aids refining the self from within, while refining the self eliminates external distractions to nurturing the self. To refine the self without nurturing the self makes the elixir foundation difficult to complete. To nurture the self without refining the self makes the mercury nature difficult to stabilize. Together, nurturing the self and refining the self are one and the same Way.

Yet to nurture one’s own mercury, one must employ the other family’s true lead—the prior-heaven within the postnatal, the metal and water within the postnatal, the state of having qi without form. When true lead first arises, it steams between the two kidneys, and one immediately mobilizes the River Chariot to refine it, ascending from the Tailbone Pass to the Heavenly Valley, pouring sweet dew to return to the Yellow Court, sprinkling and irrigating the Three Palaces, using lead to restrain mercury. When qi transforms into fluid and retreats the yin tally, the flowing pearl no longer escapes.

When fluid transforms into qi and advances the yang fire, the River Chariot rises once more.

Moreover, refining one’s nature and cultivating the mind, externally eliminating worldly distractions, dwelling concealed amidst the marketplace, harmonizing one’s light with the vulgar—thus both body and mind become settled, and the inner mercury solidifies. Only then may one seek the eight ounces of prior-heaven to pair with the half-catty of postnatal, and the Reverted Elixir may be attained.

---

**Chapter Four: Laying the Foundation and Refining the Self**

Laying the foundation and refining the self—these two are one. Yet there are minor and major foundations, external and internal refinement of the self. One must not be ignorant of this.

What is the minor foundation? Drawing primordial yang into the inner crucible, sustaining embryonic breathing, then generating the postnatal medicine and performing the jade refinement. This is what the Sun Tao school calls "once the foundation is laid, one dares to obtain the medicine; once the inner medicine congeals, one dares to refine the self."

What is the major foundation? Nurturing the spirit pearl to produce external lead, with metal and water flowing abundantly, diligently practicing the marvels of the Celestial Cycle to complete the work of perfecting one’s nature. This is what the *Collected Explanations* refers to as "opening the passes to pave the foundation’s path, obtaining the medicine to supply the foundation’s needs, and refining the self to conclude the foundation’s work."

Internal refinement of the self concerns the work of the River Chariot and the jade fluid’s efficacy—this is the *Can Tong Qi*’s discourse on "internally nurturing the self." External refinement of the self means all phenomena are empty, not a speck of dust clings—this is the ancient teaching of "forgetting emotions when facing circumstances."

In essence, internal refinement is the major foundation, and the major foundation is nurturing the self. Nurturing the self still aids internal refinement, and internal refinement still requires external refinement. All elixir scriptures interweave the three and five, their words differing but their substance the same. Thus I say: laying the foundation and refining the self are one and two. May scholars skillfully comprehend this.

---

**Chapter Five: Nurturing the Self and Refining the Self**

I previously stated that nurturing the self is the internal aid to refining the self, while refining the self removes the external disturbances to nurturing the self. This was provisionally distinguished for clarity, not to imply they are unrelated.

Yet there are indeed differences. External refinement of the self begins with refining in the face of circumstances, which differs from internal refinement and even from external nurturing of the self. Why? External refinement of the self refines the heart-mind to stabilize it. When the heart-mind is settled, the body is settled; when the body is settled, sensual desires cannot sway it, nor can wealth and profit dazzle it. Only then can true mercury be preserved, and the elixir’s foundation secured.

As for internal refinement of the self, it shares similarities with internal nurturing of the self. In elixir scriptures, refining the self means smelting mercury into cinnabar. Master Tao says: "Refining the self is not mere empty refinement." Shangyang says: "Cultivating the elixir is easy; refining the self is most difficult." The self here refers to the true fire of one’s mercury. One must first refine this true fire, subdue this true dragon, and command it at will, preventing it from bolting—only then can one subjugate the white tiger and obtain the true gold of the supreme treasure. Thus refining the self originally involves effort.

This effortful refinement of the self is internal refinement of the self, which is also internal nurturing of the self. Internal refinement of the self uses the other family’s lead to refine one’s own mercury, causing them to restrain and generate each other. Internal nurturing of the self also employs the other family’s lead to nurture one’s own mercury, enabling them to support and guard each other. Hence their shared meaning.

---

**Chapter Six: Nurturing the Self and Refining the Self**
*(This chapter transmits the oral teachings of the Western School, renaming "laying the foundation" as "nurturing the self"—though the name differs, the principle remains the same.)*

Nurturing the self and refining the self form a continuous practice. Nurturing the self means treasuring essence and enriching qi—this is laying the foundation. Refining the self means forgetting emotions when facing circumstances—this is completing one’s nature.

Must refining the self precede nurturing the self, or does nurturing the self first supply refinement? When essence and qi are cultivated, the earth of the self grows ever more solid and thick—hence the character *foundation* (基) is derived from earth (土). Thus laying the foundation is nurturing the self. When emotions and circumstances are tempered, the heart-mind of the self grows ever clearer and undying—hence the character *nature* (性) is derived from heart (心). Thus completing one’s nature must begin with refining the self.

Though nurturing the self requires essence and qi, essence is guarded internally while qi comes from without. To strengthen one’s guard, one must employ the self; to await its arrival, one must employ the self. The Way of nurturing the self thus demands tranquility as its work.

I distinguish two paths for nurturing the self: self-nurturing and mutual nurturing. Mutual nurturing pertains to essence and qi; self-nurturing pertains to tranquility.

Though refining the self occurs amidst emotions and circumstances, emotions are inwardly attenuated while circumstances are outwardly emptied. When one is naturally content, the self must have something to delight in; when one is unburdened, the self must have something to uphold. The Way of refining the self thus demands both movement and stillness.

I distinguish two aspects of refining the self: internal refinement and external refinement. External refinement is harmonizing one’s light with the vulgar; internal refinement is smelting mercury into cinnabar.

A gatha says:
*To know the true path of cultivation,*
*First practice these two endeavors.*
*Clarifying nurturing and refining the self,*
*Helps one better read the immortal scriptures.*

The *Scripture* states: "The inner heart should be alive; the outer heart should be dead." To enliven the inner heart, one must internally nurture the self. To extinguish the outer heart, one must externally refine the self. This is how I elucidate internal nurturing and external refinement of the self.

---

**Chapter Seven: The Postnatal Sequence**

After the initial foundation, the postnatal serves as the marvelous function. Yet there is a postnatal that can be used, and one that cannot. The unusable postnatal does not even deserve the name—being utterly yin and turbid, it is unworthy of mention. Here, I discuss only the usable postnatal in sequence:

First, the postnatal;
Second, the prior-heaven within the postnatal;
Third, the prior-heaven;
Fourth, the prior-heaven within the prior-heaven.

The postnatal is the qi of the Yin Heel Vessel, the root of human life, which, when first stirred, becomes primordial essence. The practitioner strikes the bamboo to summon it, drawing it into the inner crucible, where it naturally refines essence into qi and opens the passes. This qi surges through the five viscera, suffuses the hundred bones, coils through the meridians, and returns to the Cinnabar Field. With concentrated spirit and regulated breath, one awaits the movement of the mechanism. When the mechanism stirs and the flute sounds, a single thread ascends—this is the prior-heaven within the postnatal. It is gathered with the sword, tuned with the zither, circulated with the River Chariot, and sealed in the Yellow Court—this is the work of refining the self with jade liquid. With long and thorough practice, body and mind become steadfast; only then does one enter the chamber and approach the furnace to seek the prior-heaven. This prior-heaven is the primordial ancestral qi. First, one establishes the furnace and crucible by setting the true yin and true yang—kindred, sentient substances—each weighing eight ounces. Using the true qi of this furnace and crucible as a dharmic image, one revolves it through the celestial cycles to lure out the prior-heaven and immediately seize it. In less than half a quarter-hour, it coalesces into a single grain, adhering to the crucible—this is the lead mother, called the external elixir. The prior-heaven within the prior-heaven is so named because yang is born within lead, and the curtain’s light shines through. One gathers this most true yang qi to capture and subdue the essence-qi of one’s own body—this is what is meant by “When gold returns to the original nature, it is then called the Returned Elixir.” Thereafter, one nurtures and stabilizes it, daily revolving the yin tally and yang fire. By nurturing and fostering it, it transforms into the substance of golden liquid. Swallowing it into the five viscera is called the Golden Liquid Returned Elixir. After ingestion, it coalesces into the sacred embryo. With ten months of work complete, the yang spirit manifests. Beyond the reach of the five phases, one is titled a true immortal. To further ascend, one faces the wall for nine years—this is called refining the spirit to return to emptiness. Facing the wall means that within the moment, a ten-thousand-fathom cliff stands before one, untouched by worldly dust—it is not merely sitting facing a wall. The nine years refer to the nine revolutions. With the work of nine revolutions deepened, one attains a hundred million transformation bodies.

---

**Chapter Eight: The Inner and Outer Medicines**

The inner medicine serves to complete one’s nature; the outer medicine is needed to complete one’s destiny. For the practitioner to fulfill nature and destiny, one must first cultivate the inner medicine and then the outer medicine. This inner medicine is half a catty of mercury; this outer medicine is eight ounces of lead. One must first gather the outer medicine to capture the inner medicine. This outer medicine is the qi of the kidneys; this inner medicine is the essence of the heart. The postnatal work concludes here. As for establishing nature and destiny, one must first rely on the inner medicine to plant the outer medicine. This inner medicine is mercury within cinnabar; this outer medicine is lead within water. One must first refine the outer medicine and then the inner medicine. This outer medicine is the qi of the elixir mother; this inner medicine is the sacred embryo. The prior-heaven work concludes here. Yet one must understand the dual use of inner and outer: which is the medicine, and which is the elixir? The inner elixir is true mercury, the earth of the self, which returns to the gate of *li*; with time, it is refined into wondrous cinnabar. The outer elixir is true lead, the earth of *wu*, concealed in the door of *kan*; with time, it manifests as beautiful golden blossoms. To congeal the inner elixir, one must first use lead to restrain mercury—this lead is not the lead of the Returned Elixir but the true fire of the other household. To refine the outer elixir, one must first use mercury to welcome lead—this lead is not the lead of the congealed elixir but the one qi of the prior-heaven. Thus, congealing the elixir differs from returning the elixir. Congealing means coagulation: taking the qi of the other household to coagulate the qi of one’s own household. The transformation occurs within the postnatal crucible, inseparable from the celestial fire-tending, and only then can one succeed. Returning means restoration: gathering the gold of *dui* to restore the gold of *qian*. The transformation occurs within the prior-heaven crucible, requiring the union of kindred yin and yang to achieve completion. Congealing the elixir completes the inner elixir; returning the elixir employs the outer elixir. The inner elixir is the yin elixir—mercury is originally yin within yang. The outer elixir is the yang elixir—lead is yang within yin. The earthly origin is the outer elixir, whose salvific and bestowing functions are all directed toward others. The human origin is the inner elixir, whose principles of nature and destiny are all directed toward the self. There is more one must know: the inner elixir is the inner medicine, yet the Golden Liquid Returned Elixir is also called the inner medicine because its transformation occurs within. The outer elixir is the outer medicine, yet the Golden Elixir is also called the outer medicine because its transformation occurs without. This is the great elixir that encompasses both inner and outer. There is also what one must know: the outer elixir is the outer medicine, yet there is an outer medicine that has not yet become an elixir. When the great *kan* and *li* intersect, the River Chariot revolves, transforming qi into liquid, which descends to the Yellow Chamber—this is also called the outer medicine, though it has not yet become an elixir. The inner elixir is the inner medicine, yet there is an inner medicine that has not yet become an elixir. When one establishes the prior-heaven foundation, the Crimson Palace transforms liquid, which flows back to the primordial sea, and the liquid reverts to qi—this is also called the inner medicine, though it has not yet become an elixir. This is the work of purity and quiescence that encompasses both inner and outer. The distinctions between elixirs and medicines are fully recorded here for the learner’s benefit.

---

**Chapter Nine: The Resemblance of Medicinal Substances**

In the teachings of the prior-heaven and postnatal, the medicinal substances have layers that closely resemble one another, differing only in scale. Now, I will discuss those that resemble each other. The postnatal *kan* and *li* serve to lay the foundation of the elixir; the prior-heaven *kan* and *li* serve to establish the foundation of the elixir. The postnatal lead and mercury serve to congeal the minor elixir; the prior-heaven lead and mercury serve to return the great elixir. Three years of refining the self serve to complete one’s nature; nine years of facing the wall serve to complete one’s destiny. The postnatal *kan* and *li* refer to the union of original spirit and original qi to lay the foundation of the elixir, generating the minor medicine. The prior-heaven *kan* and *li* refer to the union of true yin and true yang to establish the foundation of the elixir (that is, the elixir mother), generating the great medicine.

Postnatal lead and mercury refer to the golden crucible refining the generated medicine—this medicine is external lead. The river chariot transports and restrains the flowing pearl—the flowing pearl is internal mercury. When lead and mercury constrain each other, the minor elixir is congealed. Prior-heaven lead and mercury refer to the union of like yin and yang to form *wu* earth—*wu* earth is external lead. Harmonizing the fire timing with *ji* earth—*ji* earth is internal mercury. When lead and mercury meet, the great elixir returns. During the refinement of the minor elixir, the abdomen feels warm and intoxicated. Then, borrowing the *zi-wu* cycle, one gradually gathers and congeals, causing lead to subdue mercury, forming yin cinnabar. After the great elixir congeals, the furnace glows with enduring redness. Then, borrowing the external furnace’s talismanic fire, diligently increasing and decreasing, one causes lead to dry and mercury to manifest, forming yang cinnabar.

Refining the self to complete one’s nature means nurturing yin cinnabar. Internally, one treasures essence and enriches qi; externally, one faces the mirror and forgets emotion—thus both body and mind are firmly established. "Three years" signifies the meaning of "three months without deviation." Facing the wall to complete one’s destiny means nurturing yang cinnabar. One must ensure both form and spirit are wondrous, truly shattering the void—thus transformations become boundless. "Nine years" signifies the meaning of the "ninefold great return."

**Chapter Ten: Mutual Nourishment of the Three Treasures**
The *Xinyin Jing* states: "The three superior medicines are spirit, qi, and essence." These are the supreme treasures of cultivation. Their ceaseless generation and transformation mutually nourish one another—this is elucidated for the scholar. Practicing the Dao of reverse cultivation transforms essence into qi, and qi into spirit. Practicing the Dao of sequential cultivation generates spirit from qi, and qi from essence.

One asks: Why reverse refinement? Since the original root leaks, essence, qi, and spirit all fall into the postnatal. If one cannot seek them within, one must seek them without. Seeking them without is reverse refinement. Essence transforming into qi means this essence, residing in the yin heel vessel, reversely enters the purple palace to be refined—thus it transforms into qi. Qi transforming into spirit means this qi, residing in the yang furnace, reversely enters the yellow court to be refined—thus it transforms into spirit.

Though this reverse refinement begins with essence, the sequential refinement begins with spirit. The two have a mutual necessity and do not conflict. To reverse-refine original essence, one must first concentrate the spirit. When spirit is concentrated, qi gathers; when qi gathers, essence is generated. For when spirit and qi unite, they naturally produce celestial essence. This essence is the water of the celestial one, *ren* in *kan*, also called mother qi or external essence. The practitioner uses mother qi to cultivate child qi, and external essence to replenish internal essence—this is the work of like kind. Child qi is heart qi. Internal essence is heart essence. Postnatal cultivation proceeds from external to internal, so one must first refine the external medicine to return to the internal medicine.

There is also the principle of spirit transforming into essence, and essence into qi. This refers to the crimson palace transforming fluid, which flows back to the original sea, the fluid then transforming into qi, later transported by the river chariot. There is further the principle of qi generating essence, and essence generating spirit. This refers to white clouds ascending to court, sweet dew descending, drawing out the yang of *kan* to replenish the yin of *li*.

Excellent indeed! The words of Immortal Tao: "Knowing the internal but not the external, one cannot open the passes. Gathering the external but not the internal, one cannot secure the root." Those who embody this understand the wondrous mutual nourishment of essence, qi, and spirit.

**Chapter Eleven: The Five Passes of Refining Practice**
Elixir methods divide into three passes: refining essence, refining qi, and refining spirit. Yet the depths of cultivation truly exceed these three. If the stages are incomplete, one risks skipping levels. Let us now discuss them.

The first pass refines essence, requiring the crucible. One unites original yellow to copulate, transforming the golden crow to ascend—thus essence transforms into qi. The second pass refines qi, requiring clarity on *zi-wu*. One draws out the yang within *kan* to replenish the yin within *li*—thus qi transforms into spirit. Both foundation-laying and elixir-returning employ such methods, differing only in the scale of medicine, crucible, and furnace.

As for the Dao of refining spirit, there are three passes: first, refining spirit to complete one’s nature; second, refining spirit to complete one’s destiny; third, refining spirit to return to emptiness. Refining spirit to complete one’s nature is the Dao of refining the self with jade fluid. Lead subdues mercury, congealing the elixir foundation. Internally, true fire is unceasing; externally, the *zi-wu* cycle gathers and congeals. Thus mercury is refined into yin cinnabar. Refining spirit to complete one’s destiny is the Dao of refining form with golden fluid. Lead returns to restrain mercury, congealing the fetal infant. Internally, true fire glows enduringly; externally, yin and yang are applied, daily increasing and decreasing. Thus mercury is dried into yang cinnabar.

Refining spirit to return to emptiness ascends further, uniting with the Dao in truth. One moves the spirit to the upper courtyard, sits upright with a tranquil mind, directly merging with the great void to universally illuminate the great thousand worlds. Thus the Dharma body is perfected, the śarīra radiates light, and emanations respond boundlessly. As the saying goes: "Children and grandchildren, a hundred thousand myriad transformations." At this stage, responding to the moment to establish merit, the body returns to the Three Purities, and the Dao transcends the nine ancestors.

**Chapter Twelve: The Stages of Medicine Production**
Medicines have three stages: first, emerging from non-being into being; next, entering from being into non-being; finally, producing being from non-being.

Emerging from non-being into being refers to postnatal lead fire. Though generated externally, it is formless and without substance—this is the initial arising of golden qi. Entering from being into non-being refers to sending it to the western region.

Though cultivated from within, it is utterly empty and void—this is the cavernous yang realm of Kun’s domain.

Producing being from non-being corresponds to the union of Kan and Dui. The primordial qi of the precelestial arises from the formless. From formlessness, wondrous form is born; from insubstantiality, numinous substance is generated. Seek it in the second watch, unite it in the fourth watch, and the golden elixir is accomplished, the sacred embryo congealed, the nurturing complete, and the yang spirit manifests.

Disciples often fail to distinguish between "emerging from non-being into being" and "producing being from non-being," which differ by ten thousandfold. Thus, I have set forth this explanation to clarify.

**Chapter Thirteen: The Stages of Medicinal Refinement**

Medicines have three stages: first, taking the external medicine to refine the internal medicine; next, using the internal medicine to cultivate the external medicine; finally, consuming the external medicine to unite with the internal medicine.

Taking the external medicine to refine the internal medicine refers to the work of laying the foundation and refining the self. This external medicine pertains to the refinement of the lesser medicine, the stage of refining essence into qi.

Using the internal medicine to cultivate the external medicine pertains to the work of the Qian-Kun crucible and vessel. This internal medicine is the true mercury at the moment of dispersing essence and sowing the seed.

Consuming the external medicine to unite with the internal medicine refers to welcoming the lead to refine the mercury, the work of the mother recognizing the child. This external medicine is the great medicine. Seized suddenly, it induces profound intoxication; possessed eternally, it bestows longevity. Harmonized and firmly sealed, it becomes the sacred embryo. When the work of nurturing is complete, it becomes the sage—this is the moment when the great man’s merit is accomplished and his name established.

Someone asked Tuanyang: “Master Shangyang said, ‘The internal medicine completes nature to congeal the elixir; the external medicine completes life to return the elixir.’ Thus, disciples should first refine the internal medicine before proceeding to the external. Why then does Yingchanzi claim that ‘one must begin with the external medicine before turning to the internal’?”

Tuanyang replied: “You conflate the postcelestial external medicine with the precelestial external medicine, regarding both simply as ‘external medicine.’ Yet the postcelestial external medicine precedes *gui*, while the precelestial external medicine follows *gui*. Though their names are the same, their realities differ. Clinging to the letter and fixating on appearances—this is the common flaw of those who lack the master’s oral transmission.”

Thus, I set forth this three-tiered exposition to elucidate the matter.

**Chapter Fourteen: The Two Kinds of Cinnabar**

In the study of the postcelestial, one governs mercury with lead to form cinnabar. This cinnabar is the treasure of the sevenfold return, utterly pure and flawless—the Lesser Returned Elixir.

In the study of the precelestial, one extracts lead to augment mercury, forming cinnabar. This cinnabar is the supreme treasure of the ninefold transmutation, its golden radiance enfolding the body—the Greater Returned Elixir.

**Chapter Fifteen: Spirit and Qi, Nature and Life**

The postcelestial Dao pertains to spirit and qi. The precelestial Dao pertains to nature and life. Though nature and life resemble spirit and qi, they are in truth far removed. Thus, the *Mirror for Ingredient Entry* states: “It is nature and life, not spirit and qi. The lead of the watery realm is but a single flavor.” These words merit deep reflection.

Disciples who grasp this distinction should complete the postcelestial with spirit and qi, and complete the precelestial with nature and life. Why is this? That which nature commands is called life—these two are originally one. This is the heart-method of establishing life.

The *Awakening to Reality* says: “Different names arise from the same source, yet few know this; both are profoundly profound, the essential mechanism.” Here, life is the differing name, while nature is the shared source. Lead is the differing name, while mercury is the shared source. Thus, the lead of the watery realm is but a single flavor. This single flavor—lead—originates from the single flavor of mercury.

The *Daodejing* states: “Through non-desire, observe its mystery; through desire, observe its aperture.” Observing mystery and observing aperture—this is the profoundly profound mechanism. One should attain utmost stillness and desirelessness to observe its mystery, which is the work of stabilizing nature. Yet when the time arrives and the mechanism stirs, the primordial True Unity qi emerges from emptiness—indeed, there is an aperture.

Though these two bear different names, both originate from the Supreme Ultimate, which is their shared source. By observing the non-being, one attains the mystery, gaining one profundity. By observing the being, one attains the aperture, gaining another profundity. Profound beyond profundity—nature resides herein, and life resides herein.

Nature originates from Heaven’s mandate, while life originates from the fulfillment of nature. Heaven forms things through qi, yet principle is also bestowed. Thus, mandate is conferred upon human nature, hence it is said: “What Heaven confers is called nature.” Humans create things through principle, and qi begins to arise. Thus, life is established through nature, hence it is said: “Fulfill nature to attain life.”

In Heaven, principle emerges from qi; in the Dao, qi emerges from principle. When principle emerges from qi, qi is prioritized. This qi is the harmonious, transforming qi of primordial chaos. Humans receive this qi to form their bodies, after which principle has a dwelling—thus, this qi is to be prized.

When qi emerges from principle, qi is again prioritized. This qi is the primordial True Unity qi. Yin receives the yang radiance, and within the lead seed, another yang arises—thus, this qi is even more prized. The Dao of the Golden Liquor Returned Elixir cannot be accomplished without this single lead qi; those who recognize this treasure it.

**Chapter Sixteen: Direct Pointers to the Precelestial**

The precelestial transcends the postcelestial, being the most primordial and original, the root and the source—it is the venerable designation of the singular qi. Yet this precelestial qi has three aspects, and the term “precelestial” has two meanings.

What are these two meanings? The first precedes Heaven. The second originates from Heaven.

That which precedes Heaven is earlier than Heaven, the precelestial that generates Heaven and Earth. This qi encompasses the form of primordial chaos, initially called Supreme Non-Being. Before Heaven and Earth divided, this qi already existed. This is its precedence over Heaven—hence, it is called precelestial. This is the first aspect.

That which originates from Heaven arises from Heaven, the precelestial that generates humans and all things. This qi contains the image of harmonious fusion, latent within the Great Void. Before humans and things were produced, this qi already existed. This is its origination from Heaven—it is also called precelestial. This is the second aspect.

As for the precelestial that generates immortals and buddhas, it encompasses both preceding meanings. This qi emerges from emptiness, called the Supreme Unity. The Golden Elixir relies upon it to be completed—termed the Ancestor, the Origin, the Embracer of Reality. It may be likened to the precelestial that precedes Heaven and the precelestial that originates from Heaven, forming three corresponding aspects. Thus, it is also designated as precelestial.

The elixir scriptures of past sages, whether speaking of the precelestial that generates Heaven and Earth or the precelestial that generates humans and things, are without exception metaphors for this precelestial that generates immortals and buddhas.

Should one inquire about the precelestial that generates Heaven and Earth, Tuanyang replies: This precelestial is born of the Supreme Ultimate. As the *Classic* states: “There is a thing, chaotically formed, born before Heaven and Earth. Nameless, it is forcibly called the Dao.” It is the ancestor of all phenomena, the origin of the two great powers—without form, without shape, without sound, without scent. Initially, it is profoundly obscure, beyond the reach of the Five Agents. Yet again, it is faintly discernible, the qi arising naturally. When clarity and turbidity separate, and the Dark and Yellow distinguish themselves, then Qian and Kun settle into position, and Heaven and Earth become manifest.

Should one inquire about the precelestial that generates humans and things, Tuanyang replies: This precelestial is governed by Heaven and Earth. One becomes three, and three become one. The one is qi; the three are essence, qi, and spirit. All living beings are forged through these three-in-one.

In speaking of essence, it is the essence of the Two and Five. In speaking of qi, it is the qi of yin and yang. In speaking of spirit, it is the spirit of emptiness. The spirit of emptiness interacts with the qi of yin and yang. The essence of the Two and Five is co-generated with the qi of yin and yang. Its qi is numinous—hence, it is spirit. Its qi is wondrous—hence, it is essence.

Above coiling and below bounding—this is qi, and within it lies the spirit and essence of Heaven and Earth. Humans receive this qi to be born, which is then called the primordial qi of Heaven. Yet this qi may be clear or turbid, firm or yielding. Those who receive the firm become male; those who receive the yielding become female. Those who receive the clear become wise; those who receive the turbid become dull.

Before father and mother unite, this qi abides in profound stillness. When father and mother engage in union, this qi descends to its inception. As essence and blood intermingle and the embryo is fully formed, this qi already pervades the womb in undifferentiated unity. At this time, there is no spirit—qi serves as spirit. At this time, there is no essence—qi serves as essence. When qi is concentrated and securely contained, it is essence.

This is the primordial qi, primordial essence, and primordial spirit—the precelestial endowment for human birth. If a youth encounters a master and receives the method, preserving this purity through cultivation, they may aspire to become a Daoist immortal of non-action.

There is also true qi, true spirit, and true essence—the precelestial foundation for internal alchemy. If scholars fail to recognize the true mechanism, they will have no starting point and cannot attain this supreme treasure.

Now, the precelestial: it cannot be used when seen, and cannot be seen when used. It is what alchemists seek through emptiness and stillness, refining the real by means of the provisional, generating being from non-being. At this stage, the Way of three, two, and one naturally divides and unites.

Spirit becomes the spirit beyond spirit; essence becomes the essence of utmost essence; qi becomes the true unity of qi—these are the three. The spirit beyond spirit is spirit in its most spiritual form—this is the nature of the Dragon. The essence of utmost essence is essence refined to its purest—this is the essence of the Tiger. These are the two. As for the true unity of qi, it is the true lead for completing life, uniting the Dragon and Tiger’s nature and temperament into one, called the Mother of the Elixir—this is the one.

Ingesting this true unity causes the Three Corpses and Five Thieves to flee, filling the six palaces of the body with eternal spring. How could this compare to the qi, essence, and spirit of birth? How could it be approached by the postcelestial qi, essence, and spirit?

The postcelestial consists of the qi of breathing, the spirit of thought, and the essence of intercourse—three things that can be heard, seen, measured, and inferred. These serve the body after birth, hence they are called postcelestial.

When a person is in the womb, there is only a single point of primordial qi, with no qi of breathing. Only after ten months, when the fetus is complete and leaves the mother’s body, does it borrow the orifices of mouth and nose to externally absorb the harmony of Heaven and Earth—thus the origin of breathing qi. From this, the spirit of thought also arises, relying on this qi to enter. It lodges in the body as a temporary abode, seizing the dwelling to reside within. This spirit is the seed of karmic rebirth, arriving first at birth and departing first at death, ceaselessly abandoning the old and seeking the new.

When an infant first emerges, its initial cry subtly reveals the suffering of rebirth. As it is nurtured and grows, the acquired spirit takes control, emotions and desires entangle, and the primordial qi gradually dissipates. Even the qi of breathing never ceases for a moment—how tragic!

As for postcelestial essence, it is not brought at birth nor taken at death. Only as the body’s primordial qi gradually fills and overflows, reaching fifteen years of age, does yang reach its peak and yin begin to grow, yin lengthening as yang wanes. This causes the undifferentiated qi to transform into the passions of intercourse. Intercourse involves both union and sensation—with union and sensation, there is essence; without them, there is none. This essence is compelled by desire and transformed from qi and blood.

There are also those who lose essence through dream-induced sensations or dream intercourse, which occurs when qi and blood are unstable and the kidney’s gate cannot retain it. This is the essence of intercourse.

I urge those who study the Dao to seek only the precelestial and not the postcelestial, so that the supreme medicine may be obtained. Even if one does not dare to immediately pursue the highest grade but instead builds the foundation and refines the self, one must refine primordial essence into primordial qi, and primordial qi into true lead, making the postcelestial precelestial to complete the half-pound of postcelestial substance. Then one may seek this supreme precelestial.

**Chapter Seventeen: On Spirit, Qi, and Essence**

The *Heart Seal Scripture* states: "The three superior medicines are spirit, qi, and essence." These are the wondrous substances of internal alchemy. The most exalted among them are primordial spirit, primordial qi, and primordial essence—true essence, true qi, and true spirit.

What is primordial? The precelestial. What is true? Also the precelestial. The primordial precelestial is born of the imperial descent—this is the celestial primordial of youth. The true precelestial is accomplished through the great Dao—this is the human primordial of our kind.

If one cannot cultivate the celestial primordial, must they not rely on the human primordial?

Some may ask: "The celestial primordial is how Heaven and Earth, through yin-yang and the Five Phases, transform and generate beings, forming shapes with qi and endowing them with principle. The qi that gives life to humans is primordial qi. Before father and mother unite, this qi abides in profound stillness. When father and mother engage in union, this qi descends to its inception. The Confucians speak of 'Heaven giving birth to the multitudes, with things and patterns'—this refers to it. This qi is most numinous; being numinous, it has spirit, which is primordial spirit. This qi is most clear; being clear, it is utmost essence, which is primordial essence.

Before the embryo is formed, there is only primordial qi within, with no postcelestial breathing qi. Only after ten months, when the form is complete and the mouth and nose exist, does it follow the mother’s breathing to externally absorb the great harmony of Heaven and Earth. The karmic yin spirit also relies on this breathing to enter. Thus, the postcelestial spirit and qi are both complete, and the infant immediately cries upon birth. Fortunately, the mouth cannot speak, the eyes cannot smile, and there is no knowledge or perception. The primordial qi remains undifferentiated, without thought or deliberation, rendering the yin spirit useless."

The primordial qi relies on the primordial spirit for mutual nourishment. By the age of sixteen, when spirit is complete and qi is full, yang reaches its peak and yin arises, giving birth to postcelestial generative essence. Desire-fire blazes, and the yin spirit indulges its will. Thus, those of youthful purity and supreme virtue, if they encounter a teacher, may preserve their celestial primordial body through pure cultivation, becoming effortless celestial immortals. Once postcelestial forces take hold, the primordial recedes.

One might ask: If the celestial primordial is so easily cultivated, why not take children into the mountains to realize fruition, making them immortals at once? The answer: A fine question! Heaven and Earth generate humanity to establish the Dao of Heaven and enact the virtue of Heaven. Therefore, those who inwardly preserve perfection need not refrain from outwardly fulfilling their human destiny, thereby expanding their creative transformation. Otherwise, though the immortal Dao may flourish, the human Dao will surely wane. Thus, after following the natural course of human life, the method of reversing birth to become an immortal is emphasized. This is the great Dao of the human primordial—how it nurtures all things without omission, encompassing Heaven and Earth without overstepping.

The key lies in recognizing this essence, qi, and spirit.

Someone asks: "What is the difference between primordial spirit and true spirit?"
Answer: The primordial spirit is turbid and indistinct. The true spirit is bright and luminous. One lies hidden in chaos, without light; the other, refined through tempering, becomes useful. Confucians speak of "tranquility, stability, and the ability to contemplate"; Buddhists, of "entering deeply into the great prajñā"; Daoists, of "supreme stillness generating wisdom." This true spirit is perfect knowing and perfect awareness. Thus, even children may cultivate purity, while ordinary people must engage in quiet refinement to attain the true spirit of the perfected.

Another asks: "What is the difference between primordial essence and true essence?"
Answer: Primordial essence resides in my domain; true essence resides in the other’s domain. That in my domain is the undifferentiated qi of the Crimson Palace, accumulated over time to generate numinous fluid. That in the other’s domain is the vigorous qi of the Flowery Pond, what the *Awakening to Reality* calls the "primary essence." On the fifteenth day of the eighth month, when metal qi is full and water’s tide arises, it precisely aligns with the true signal of the two equinoxes. If cultivators recognize this essence, drawing it in with a single breath, they may instantly attain celestial immortality—it is no ordinary thing.

Another asks: "What is the difference between primordial qi and true qi?"
Answer: Primordial qi is what children receive from Heaven—what is called the qi that forms the body, increasing with the years. True qi, however, is not like this. The primordial, original ancestor arises from within emptiness and nonbeing. Only with the oral instructions of a true teacher, first establishing the cauldron and vessel of qian and kun, harmonizing the true dragon and true tiger, and uniting true yin and true yang, does it coalesce into the mother of lead in half a double-hour. Yang born within lead becomes true qi. Thus, Heaven uses primordial qi to generate humans and things, while the Dao uses true qi to generate immortals and buddhas. The human primordial method of refining qi seizes the creative transformation of Heaven and Earth—no easy feat.

Upon hearing Master Tuanyang’s discourse on the human primordial’s refinement of qi to seize Heaven and Earth’s creative transformation, someone asks about the difference between the celestial primordial and the human primordial. Tuanyang replies: Listen, and I will tell you. "What Heaven commands is called nature"—principle emerges from qi: this is the celestial primordial. "Fully realizing nature to reach destiny"—qi emerges from principle: this is the human primordial. Supreme virtue is effortless, not seeking through observation, a practice of purity and stillness—this is the celestial primordial. Inferior virtue acts, its function unceasing, the path of return—this is the human primordial. Those of supreme virtue receive Heaven’s bounty in abundance, yet still engage in pure cultivation. They must refine primordial qi and primordial spirit into utmost clarity and emptiness, transforming them into perfect equanimity and perfect awakening, only then fully realizing the principle of the celestial primordial. Thus, the celestial primordial is not a ready-made matter for those of supreme virtue. Those who take supreme virtue as the celestial primordial do so because their original self remains unharmed, naturally close to the celestial primordial.

Those of inferior virtue are the most numerous among people, so the wondrous truth of return is taught first. They must forge yin elixir and yang elixir into a single mass, refining them into supreme nonbeing and supreme emptiness, only then fully realizing the Dao of the human primordial. Thus, the human primordial is not the ready-made body of inferior virtue. Those who take inferior virtue as the human primordial do so because their own and others’ brilliance is manifest, hence it is called the art of the human primordial.

The original body of supreme virtue is endowed with both nature and destiny. The wondrous function of inferior virtue fully realizes both nature and destiny. Yet both rely on a single qi for transformation. Thus, humans attain the primordial true unity of qi to become immortals, just as Heaven bestows yin-yang and five-phase qi to become human. The elixir path seizes the creative transformation of Heaven and Earth because it is one with the Dao of Heaven.

**Chapter Eighteen: Further Discussion on Essence, Qi, and Spirit**

In the original body of supreme virtue, essence, qi, and spirit are all called "primordial," for what is received from Heaven is abundant, and there is no need to seek from the other’s domain—hence it is called the celestial primordial. In the practice of inferior virtue, essence, qi, and spirit are all named "true," for what is taken from humanity is plentiful, and it cannot be sought from one’s own domain—hence it is called the human primordial. Yet in this human primordial practice, there are wondrous subtleties of gathering the primordial and secret mechanisms of seeking the primordial. Thus, those who use humanity to return to Heaven gather primordial essence to supplement primordial qi, refine primordial qi to nourish primordial spirit, and refine primordial spirit to become true spirit—then the postcelestial work is complete. Using true spirit to generate true qi, using true qi to seek true essence, seizing true essence to form true lead—then the primordial work is complete. When one returns to the origin and embraces the primordial to guard the One, it becomes entirely identical to the practice of supreme virtue.

Those who cultivate inferior virtue but do not reach the realm of nonaction, or embrace supreme virtue but do not investigate the mystery of nonaction, cannot ingest the celestial primordial or attain the rank of celestial immortal.

**Chapter Nineteen: The Order and Reversal of Nature and Life**
The principles of nature and life possess both order and reversal. The ordered completion of nature and life is bestowed by Heaven, wherein one encompasses two. The reversed completion of nature and life is cultivated by man, wherein one unites two. That which encompasses two—qi is endowed with principle, and when qi and principle merge, nature and life become wholly integrated. That which unites two—water is raised to extinguish fire, and when water and fire interact, nature and life endure forever.

What Heaven commands is called nature; within life resides nature—this is ordered completion. Mencius spoke of form and appearance as Heaven’s nature, for what is commanded possesses this form, and thus possesses this nature. Innate knowledge and innate ability are both lodged within the commanded form. To fully realize nature is to reach life; within nature, life is cultivated—this is reversed completion. The perfected person plants the life-treasure with the fire of spirit, for what is realized in nature possesses this spirit, and thus possesses this life. In response to stimuli and summons, all arises from the radiance of the spirit within nature.

Now we know that supreme virtue is pure and still, preserving the way of ordered completion to congeal the immortal embryo—this is Heaven perfecting man. We also know that inferior virtue returns and refines the way of reversed completion to congeal the sage embryo—this is exhausting man to unite with Heaven. Let those who study the Dao understand this.

---

**Chapter Twenty: The Mysterious Pass of the One Aperture**
The mysterious pass of the one aperture is born from emptiness and nothingness. It does not reside among the five viscera or six bowels, nor anywhere in the limbs. Speaking of its name: this pass is a wondrous mechanism, hence it is called the *mysterious pass*. This aperture is the place where all methods return to the One, solitary and without counterpart, hence it is called the *one aperture*. In a single phrase to encompass it: it is the *center*.

The center lies between above and below, yet it does not lie between above and below—for it is both dead and alive. What is dead? To take the Yellow Court, the qi-cavity, or the Cinnabar Field as this center is dead. What is alive? To concentrate spirit and gather qi, revealing this center, is alive. In dead terms, it is called the Yellow Court, the qi-cavity, or the Cinnabar Field. In living terms, it is reckoned as the mysterious pass of the one aperture. Thus it is said: born from emptiness and nothingness. The true mechanism is directly revealed. Those who attain it must keep it secret.

---

**Chapter Twenty-One: Further Discussion of the Mysterious Pass**
The mysterious pass is the numinous radiance where spirit and qi unite. When first glimpsed, the mysterious pass flickers without constancy. When first entered, it is indistinct and without support, for spirit and qi have only just merged and cannot yet solidify. Only when their embrace becomes pure and familiar, and the heart dies without departing, does one begin to recognize that within the mysterious pass, self and other are forgotten, and even spirits and gods cannot fathom it. (Beyond this, one cannot evade impermanence.) Here, it is utterly pure and undifferentiated, serene and soaring. Within this, the mysteries transform endlessly, beyond description. No wonder its names are many—each person’s perception differs. Each names it according to their vision, and titles it according to their use. Ancient immortals kept it secret and unspoken; only after receiving the transmission with the touch of a hand to the crown could one be taught. Even then, their instructions amounted to no more than: it is neither heart nor kidney. I say it is also not the Yellow Court, the qi-cavity, or the Cinnabar Field. Now I reveal it further—let those who recognize it keep it secret.

---

**Chapter Twenty-Two: The Method of the Two Apertures**
In the alchemical tradition, there is one cavity, and this cavity has two apertures. Its center is hollow, while its two ends are perforated, hence it is called the *cavity of two apertures*. The master’s transmission—“mouth to mouth, aperture to aperture”—refers precisely to this realm. It is the place where the Conception and Governor Vessels merge, where yin and yang converge, the land where the Crow and Hare come and go.

Within the one cavity and two apertures lies the method of practice—this method is most profoundly mysterious. The *Triple Concordance* states: “When the upper is closed, it is called *having*; when the lower is closed, it is called *nothing*. *Nothing* serves the upper, where the power of spirit resides. This method of the two apertures also requires the qi of metal.” These few lines exhaust the method of practice.

Upper and lower are Heaven and Earth. *Closed* means dark union. *Having* and *nothing* are the wondrous apertures. *Called* refers to naming and describing. One above, one below—all are concealed within this cavity. Whether *having* or *nothing*, all reside within this cavity. When one attains emptiness and preserves stillness, at the time when Heaven and Earth darkly unite, one may observe its wonder.
