The wondrous *having*—though indescribable, it may be named, hence it is called *having*. This is what is meant by: “In the profound and obscure, there is essence; within it, there is sincerity.” To observe the aperture through *nothing*—the aperture of emptiness and nothingness—though describable, it cannot be described, hence it is called *nothing*. This is what is meant by: “Within it, things return to no-thing.”

*Nothing* serves the upper—not as an empty return, but as the true lead of the primordial. This is what Laozi spoke of: “The form of the formless, the image of the imageless. One meets it but does not see its head; one follows it but does not see its rear.” In the elixir method, *nothing* serving the upper is to return *nothing* to *having*. What is meant by *serving*? Who offers reverence? Who presents the offering? When the power of spirit humbly resides above, its qi naturally returns—*returning* is *serving*. The only fear is that above, there is no power of spirit. *Above* refers to the region above the Yellow Court; *power* refers to the power of humility and softness. This is what the *Token* meant by: “Reversal is the verification of the Dao; weakness is the handle of virtue.”

To attain emptiness, one employs the Dao; to seek lead, one employs virtue. Virtue acts, while the Dao does not—one must not fail to understand this method. The two apertures are the gate of the Mysterious Female, the place where the golden elixir is transformed and born. If one knows and practices the method of pursuit and absorption within the one cavity and two apertures, then both gates open. Only then does metal return to nature, and one may be called *returned elixir*. Thus it is said: “the qi of metal also requires it,” and so on. *Requires* means it requires this method of absorption.

**Chapter Twenty-Three: The Root-Foundation of the Mysterious Female**

Those who cultivate the Mysterious must, whether refining the Great Elixir or the Lesser Elixir, quietly nurture the Valley Spirit to establish their root-foundation. The Valley Spirit is the name for the primordial, empty, and numinous—it is our original nature. Where is it nurtured? In the Mysterious Female.

Master Shangyang said: “The Mysterious Female consists of two things. Without these two, how could the myriad things come to be?” For *xuan* (玄) is Heaven, and *pin* (牝) is Earth—this is already evident in the first hexagram of the *Book of Changes*. Thus, the one aperture of the Mysterious Female is truly the source of all generation and transformation. How could one who enters the Dao fail to seek this source of generation and transformation? This source of generation and transformation is precisely the aperture of the Mysterious Female.

Some cite the words of Lord Lao: “The Valley Spirit does not die; this is called the Mysterious Female.” But why do they also take the Valley Spirit to be the Mysterious Transformation? The answer is: by borrowing the Mysterious Female of emptiness and nothingness to nurture the Valley Spirit of emptiness and nothingness, they thus name the Mysterious Female after the Valley Spirit. This is the meaning of naming according to function.

Moreover, there is further explanation: *The Four Hundred Words on the Golden Elixir* states: “This aperture is no ordinary aperture; it is formed by the union of Qian and Kun. It is called the Cavity of Spirit and Qi, within which lies the essence of Kan and Li.” Thus, the Mysterious Female not only nurtures spirit but also nurtures qi. Now, when spirit and qi merge, the Mysterious Female appears. Therefore, one must concentrate spirit and gather qi, allowing the two to intermingle, only then can the circle of Qian and Kun be formed. Within this lies the function of inversion. Why? That which congeals from above downward is spirit. As the noble body of Mysterious Heaven performs the humble bow of Earthly Female, above and below intermingle harmoniously, and qi and spirit unite.

The *Dao De Jing* says: “The union of all under Heaven is the Female of all under Heaven.” For this aperture lies at the center, hence it is called the union of all under Heaven; within it lies the Way of softness, hence it is called the Female of all under Heaven. When speaking of the Female Way, the Mysterious Way is also implied, thus it is called the Mysterious Female. The Mysterious Female is one Qian and one Kun, one firm and one yielding. Without this, spirit would be strong and qi would be strong, opposing and separating from each other. Therefore, it is said: the male descends to the female, spirit descends to qi, inverting and uniting, yin and yang mating. Only then do spirit and qi meet, and the root-foundation is established. Otherwise, spirit remains spirit and qi remains qi, qi remains qi and spirit remains spirit. If spirit does not obtain qi, there is nothing to supplement spirit. If qi does not return to spirit, there is no function to nurture spirit. How could one hope for the original spirit to abide long?

Moreover, if spirit dwells in the Crimson Palace, the Crimson Palace becomes the bright hall of governance, and all knowledge, perceptions, and disturbances will disturb it. Only by congealing it in the Yellow Court can sounds and thoughts cease—this, too, is not without its quiet nurturing. Thus, *Awakening to Reality* says: “To keep the Valley Spirit ever undying, one must rely on the Mysterious Female to establish the root-foundation. When true essence returns to the Golden Chamber, a single luminous pearl will never depart.”

The Valley Spirit is the most empty and numinous mercury-nature. True essence is the purest and most tender lead-emotion. The root-foundation is the place where mercury welcomes lead to forge the Golden Elixir. The Golden Chamber is the Yellow Room. As it is the place where gold returns, it is called the Golden Chamber. When metal-lead and wood-mercury merge, only then is a luminous pearl formed. The luminous pearl is a grain of Golden Elixir, as large as a millet seed. When metal returns to nature at the outset, it may be called the Returned Elixir. Once obtained, it is eternally possessed, hence it is said to “never depart.”

I have often said that this poem by Ziyang directly reveals the true formula of the ages, applicable to both the precelestial and postcelestial. What I have explained pertains to the precelestial. Even from the postcelestial perspective, one must first seek the Mysterious Female to lay the foundation of the Elixir. For the Valley Spirit relies on it to stand, and true essence also relies on it to return. Nurture the Valley Spirit with the Mysterious Female, and nurture true essence with the Valley Spirit. Spirit is cultivated by essence to strengthen the original, and essence is transformed by spirit into qi.

The *Triple Concordance* says: “Inwardly nurture the self, abiding in stillness and emptiness.” It also says: “The nature-master resides within, establishing the barriers.” From this, we know that the essential task of cultivating the body is to first nurture the Valley Spirit in stillness as the root-foundation.

---

**Chapter Twenty-Four: The Direct Indication of the "Center"**

The *Dao De Jing* says: “Much speech hastens exhaustion; it is better to guard the center.” To recognize this center is to possess the seed of sages, worthies, immortals, and buddhas. Otherwise, there is no ground for cultivating the Dao, and with every step, one falls into the demonic pit.

What is this center? It is the Mysterious Pass. The *Triple Concordance* says: “In movement, do not lose the center,” and “Float and wander, guarding the compass of the center.” Both refer to this.

Immortal Tao said: “The center is not the middle among the four directions or above and below. The Confucians speak of the center as the state before joy, anger, sorrow, and pleasure arise; the Daoists speak of the unmoving thought as the Mysterious Female; the Buddhists speak of neither thinking of good nor thinking of evil, and in that very moment, ask: ‘What is the original face?’—this is the true center.”

The wondrous state of the center naturally nurtures the self, congeals spirit, enters the chamber to return the Elixir, and even transcends the womb to achieve spirit-transformation—all depend on this. Therefore, those who first enter the Dao must recognize this center to have a path of ascent.

In ancient times, Celestial Master Wen Shi asked the Most High: “What chapter contains the essentials of cultivating the body?” The Most High replied: “It lies in deeply rooting and firmly establishing, guarding the center and embracing the One.”

Let me now attempt to expound on these words. At the outset of practice, the learner must firmly hold the sinews and bones, fiercely battle the sleep demon, seal the senses and lower the curtains, detach from all delusive thoughts, and turn the light inward to illuminate the three cavities. These three cavities are the Yellow Court, the Sea of Qi, and the Cinnabar Field.

Yet though one turns the light inward to illuminate the three cavities, one must neither fix the mind upon them nor let it wander among them. Only then does spirit settle within, breath follow its natural course, and all distinctions dissolve into utter oblivion, leaving no trace or sign. This is precisely what the *Classic* means by “desireless, observe its mystery.”

At the moment of perfect emptiness and quiescent guarding, when spirit congeals and qi unites, an unexpected realm may suddenly emerge from the compass of awareness—boundless without, infinitesimal within—whereupon the Mysterious Pass appears. This is what the *Classic* means by “desiring, observe its aperture.”

Further examining the *Contract*, it states: “Closing above is called *having*; closing below is called *not-having*.” I submit that these “above” and “below” both lie concealed within the character *center*. Yin and yang course within it; Kan and Li ascend and descend between them, merging above and below into its midst. Thus, when the upper is closed and the lower sealed, the mechanism remains subtly secure. Within the Great Void, the primordial qi moves alone—hence *not-having*, which is also the purport of observing mystery. When the lower is closed and the upper sealed, the hidden remains unseen. Yet within the profound obscurity, essence abides, and within it, a sign exists—hence *having* and *desiring*, all residing in the subtleties of the Mysterious. Therefore, the single aperture of the Mysterious Pass is variously called the aperture of having and not-having, the upper and lower crucibles, the cauldron of yin and yang, or the cavity of spirit and qi. All derive from this, and all are subsumed within the One Center.

The Master commented: “To study the Dao, study the Dao—first one must grasp its mystery. Without grasping its mystery, one cannot glimpse its aperture. To glimpse its aperture, one must hear the Dao.” I and Tuanyang shared a knowing smile.

**Chapter Twenty-Five: A Direct Explanation of Medicinal Substances**
What are medicinal substances? Shangyang said: “This medicine arises from within things.” Medicines are of two kinds: lesser medicine and greater medicine, corresponding to the Dao’s division into prior-heaven and posterior-heaven. Posterior-heaven medicine is lesser medicine, used for forming the elixir. Prior-heaven medicine is greater medicine, used for returning the elixir. Posterior-heaven medicine is formless and without substance yet truly exists; prior-heaven medicine has form and function yet is truly non-existent.

Posterior-heaven true lead discards *gui* and selects *ren*, concealing yang within yin to generate being from non-being. The *Wuzhen Pian* states: “How could the three primals and eight trigrams depart from *ren*?” The three primals are the three treasures: essence, qi, and spirit. *Ren* is born of Heaven’s One, residing before *zi*, the origin of the first yang. Both *ren* and *gui* dwell in the northern Kan, where water pertains to yin. *Ren*-water is yang within yin; *gui*-water is yin within yin. Moreover, *ren* and *gui* are stems, while *hai* and *zi* are branches. To seek true lead, one must take the heavenly stems as primary and the earthly branches as secondary, for heaven precedes earth.

In essence, *ren* signifies only the primal meaning—the beginning without thought or deliberation, the moment before movement returns to stillness. *Zi*, by contrast, is the time of knowing and awareness, the threshold between stillness and movement. *Gui*-yin is not used, and *hai*, not yet free of yin, is also not used. This is the method for seeking lesser medicine.

Prior-heaven medicine is otherwise: it is produced in Kun and sown in Qian, generating non-being from being, seeking the other from the self. When Qian-metal enters Kun, it is called “metal within Kun.” Kun truly becomes Kan, called “metal within water.” Kan resides in the north, while Dui in the west is its neighbor, so it may be said to lodge in Dui. It is not sought in Qian, for Qian is the place of sowing. It is not sought in Kun, for Kun is the place of containing the primordial. It is not sought in Kan, for though Kan contains yang, it remains hidden within water, its qi-mechanism not yet revealed. It is sought directly in Dui, where lead produces yang and the metal manifests—like the moon’s third day, emerging from the *geng* direction.

Thus, Kan-water is the river’s source, and Dui-metal is the medicinal substance. It is not that Qian, Kun, Kan, and Dui are arrayed within the body; rather, all is a matter of generating non-being from being, seeking the other from the self. I move a single drop of yin-fire’s essence, sowing it within the other’s household, thereby generating yang within lead. When yang-qi stirs, it is gathered and returned, then sown within my own womb-palace to form the true person. The *Wuzhen Pian* states: “Relying on Kun’s position, the body is formed; sown in Qian’s household, the palace of mutual resonance.” The sowing in Qian is the initial planting; the sowing in Qian’s household is nurturing. If Cui and Zhang were to rise again, they would surely affirm this humble interpretation.

The mechanism of sowing lead to obtain lead is thus. Yet the method of sowing lead must lie between the sequence of *ding* and *ren*, then the effects of Zhen and Dui may take their turn. My teacher’s oral instructions are all contained herein. The true mechanism of inner and outer medicines is now laid bare. Those who grasp these words are thrice blessed—do not treat them lightly.

**Chapter Twenty-Six: Discerning Lead and Mercury**
The spirit within the heart is called mercury-nature; the essence within the heart is called mercury-fluid. Gathering mercury-nature in the Yellow Court and congealing mercury-fluid in the Purple Palace produces dragon-mercury, which is true mercury, the inner elixir, the yin elixir, and the posterior-heaven half-catty.

The qi within the body is called lead-essence; the essence within the body is called lead-flower. Observing lead-essence in the Kan Palace and gathering lead-flower at the Dui Gate produces tiger-lead, which is true lead, the outer elixir, the yang elixir, and the prior-heaven eight-ounce mother.

**Chapter Twenty-Seven: A Direct Explanation of the Crucible**
In the elixir method, Qian and Kun serve as the crucible, and Kan and Li as the medicinal substances. Taking from Kan to fill Li, metal first returns. Kun’s form has six segments, its body fundamentally empty.

At the extreme of earth’s yin configuration, one yang returns to restore. Qian’s form has three continuous lines, its body entirely solid. Within heaven’s flourishing yang configuration, one yin suddenly arises. Between heaven and earth, the solid cannot contain things, while the empty can receive. Thus, one borrows Kun’s emptiness to conceal its solidity and first casts Qian’s solid into its emptiness. The method’s workings are thus, yet one must also understand the transference of metal to distinguish the maturity of the medicine. From the prior-heaven Qian-metal concealed within Kun’s position, at this moment yin contains yang. Though it resembles the one within Kan, the form lies hidden beneath water, secret and unrevealed. Only when metal appears within water—like the moon rising in the west at Dui—does it become usable metal. Harvested in the span of one talisman, this is precisely the time of having qi without substance. Taking from Dui is like taking from Kan; producing from Dui is like producing from Kun. Yet without the radiance of Qian-father’s essence, this great medicine cannot be produced. Though it serves as host, I become the guest. While it seeks to ascend, I descend instead. Guest and host rise and sink, all operating within the crucible. Only then does one realize the crucible’s design lies in its emptiness.

Master Tao says: “Within the crucible, originally there is nothing. After the two-sevens’ period, moved by Qian-father’s radiant essence, yang qi first stirs. Within the Qian crucible, originally there is also nothing. At the time of harvesting, receiving Kun-mother’s yang-lead, the golden elixir begins to congeal. All is created ex nihilo. The term ‘crucible’ merely borrows this as a vessel to hold things.” His words are most profound, hence recorded here.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight: Qian, Kun, Li, and Kan**
Prior-heaven is Qian and Kun; posterior-heaven is Kan and Li. Yet prior-heaven has Qian and Kun while also containing Kan and Li. Posterior-heaven has Kan and Li while also containing Qian and Kun. What is the reason? Prior-heaven as Qian and Kun means primordial yang remains unbroken in youthful purity, internally possessing Qian’s image with yang firmly established, externally possessing Kun’s image with yin firmly established—hence called prior-heaven Qian and Kun. Posterior-heaven as Kan and Li means that in middle age, primordial yang has departed, Kun envelops Qian’s yang to form Kan, and Qian envelops Kun’s yin to form Li—hence called posterior-heaven Kan and Li. Prior-heaven having Qian and Kun while also containing Kan and Li means using Qian and Kun as the crucible and Kan and Li as the medicinal substances. Posterior-heaven having Kan and Li while also containing Qian and Kun means employing Kan and Li in subtle work to restore Qian and Kun’s original body.

There is more to know: the substance refined in posterior-heaven’s self-cultivation is called prior-heaven Kan and Li, referring to taking from Kan to fill Li to form the Jade-Fluid Returned Elixir. The substance refined in prior-heaven’s return to the origin is also called prior-heaven Kan and Li, referring to transforming Kan into Li to produce the Golden-Fluid Returned Elixir. Both prior-heaven and posterior-heaven’s taking from Kan are called prior-heaven, differing only in whether the medicine comes from another or is planted in one’s own field.

In my humble opinion: prior-heaven Qian and Kun are the celestial medicine, just as posterior-heaven Kan and Li are the human medicine. A youth who receives the method and meets a teacher sits guarding Qian and Kun to attain the Dao. Once fallen into posterior-heaven, Qian and Kun are renamed the crucible.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: The Subtle Work of Harvesting and Refining**
Harvesting and refining means gathering the other’s yang-lead and refining the qi of one’s own child-pearl. Yang-lead is earth’s po, for it is concealed within the extreme yin outside, hence called earth’s po. When obtained during self-refinement, it can control one’s mercury-nature and transform it into cinnabar. As the *Awakening to Reality* says: “Simply use earth’s po to capture mercury-sand”—one need only know how to harvest and refine.

What is harvesting? Harvesting with non-harvesting. What is refining? Refining with non-refining.

What is non-harvesting harvesting? Dragon at ease, tiger at rest, guarding the feminine without masculinity. In the month of the winter solstice, its qi begins to rise. The spirit immediately follows its ascent, reversing into the crucible, drawing it in—this is non-harvesting harvesting.

What is non-refining refining? When it is in my home, the medicine itself is fire. After prolonged fusion, its yin naturally transforms. Yang then grows accordingly, accumulating in the furnace and naturally circulating—hence called non-refining refining.

Yet one must concentrate the spirit to await it, then harvest it. Regulate the breath to guard it, then refine it. When essence is entirely transformed into qi, the abdomen becomes full, and the inner elixir can be formed.

**Chapter Thirty: The River Chariot’s Subtle Essentials**
The River Chariot is the essential method of moving the medicine after obtaining it, not the method of imagined transference. It is the fire-timing of advancing and retreating at midnight and noon, the closing of yin and yang, the ascending and descending of internal and external, the natural fire of heaven and earth. From the foundation-building stage onward, after the golden crucible is full, regulate the inner breath and concentrate the inner spirit. When spirit and breath depend on each other, wind and fire merge. Suddenly, the spiritual sprout sprouts, and the qi-mechanism stirs. I then activate the River Chariot to refine it, causing it to move from below to the rear, entering through the Governing Vessel, flowing backward to the Celestial Valley, and returning to the Central Palace—this is the proper procedure after obtaining the medicine.

Alas, later generations lacking true transmission often fall into imagined transference, moving emptily back and forth—what benefit is there? Only because few attain subtle realization do immortals’ teachers find it difficult to explain. Now I shall attempt to clarify: its subtlety lies in intent guarding within and spirit driving without. Yet since this explanation exists, skeptics abound.

The reason is that the true spirit is the true intent—how can they be divided in two places? Presiding within while also presiding without, how can one fulfill two roles alone? This doubt is inevitable. Yet one must know that when the spirit guards the inner court, it values concentration, not movement. Movement necessarily employs intent.

The marvel of the celestial cycle lies in its external movement being effortless while its internal governance is laborious—thus the internal governance must be borne by intent. It is like a person sitting before a lamp: the shadow appears on the wall, and as the body moves, the shadow moves with it. Speech arises within the room, and sound flows beyond the walls—words uttered, sound follows. Intent is like the body and speech of the spirit; the spirit is like the shadow and echo of intent. There has never been a case where they do not see or hear each other. Therefore, when intent calculates within, the spirit naturally moves without—though two, they remain one. Moving within is moving without; one need not attend to it, for they naturally know each other.

Why is this so? True intent resides at the center, directing breath to respond within and without—this is inherently knowing the internal. Yet as true intent flows, piercing barriers and crossing the crown, there is a subtle mutual awareness. Is this the spirit? Is this intent? Does this spirit return to this intent? Master Wu said: "There is a subtle intent of mutual knowing"—this is precisely it. I do not know how spirit and intent transform their forms or divide their presence, nor how they coordinate within and without. To claim ignorance is true knowledge.

I simply submit to my intent and regulate my inner self: here, qi stirs; there, qi ascends. Here, wind moves; there, wind carries. Here is intent; there is spirit. Spirit and intent—divided yet undivided. One feels that the guardian within manages household affairs, while the driver without ascends to the heavenly gate, yet knows not how this subtle intent of mutual awareness arises.

Profound, oh profound! Riding the immortal raft, drifting on the Silver River, paying court to the azure vault, returning to the Yellow Court—mechanism smooth, spirit flowing, bliss unbounded. Day after day in circulation, morning after morning in passage, qi surges through the hundred joints, infusing the three palaces. Only then does the obtained medicine avoid idleness and waste, and true qi grow ever more abundant.

Alas! The world’s people, oblivious to the river chariot’s essence, toil in vain with laborious transference, exhausting their minds.

Should the true transmission not be expounded in detail,
No matter where you seek, your search shall be in vain.

**Chapter Thirty-One: On the True Heart**

The Dao of the Golden Elixir values the attainment of true spirit, true qi, and true essence, for only then can the work of creation be accomplished. Yet without employing the true heart, one cannot obtain this true essence, true qi, or true spirit.

The true heart resides in the moment before discriminating thoughts arise, before human desires intermingle, before the sprouts of yin qi are tainted. The wondrous Dao of cultivating the body begins with settling into stillness. For the student whose refinement of the self remains impure, only this practice can yield results. It is what strategists call striking when the enemy least expects, attacking when they are unprepared.

When the mind-ground momentarily clears, I seal the Three Treasures, concentrate the spirit, and regulate the breath. Inner thoughts do not stray outward; outer thoughts do not intrude. At this time, before desires arise, effort yields immediate effect. Seizing the moment before the spirit of consciousness is employed, one may perceive the true spirit. Seizing the moment before turbid qi stirs, one may nurture the true qi. Seizing the moment before lustful essence scatters, one may await the true essence.

With single-minded devotion, one attains utmost emptiness and abides in profound stillness, thereby recognizing the mysterious pass. This is the foremost principle of dual cultivation of nature and life. How wondrous is the function of the true heart!

If, however, one waits until affairs, interactions, laughter, speech, or eating stir the mind’s mechanism before attempting to restrain it, the inner heart will already be stirred, and杂念 will have arisen. If I suppress them and they cease, it is fortunate. But should they cease only to rise again, or be driven away only to return—each attempt to expel them only multiplying their number—then one faces a formidable foe. Closing the eyes, one wages war between heaven and humanity; touching the heart, one finds a field of ruin. The heart is defeated. What then can be done?

Generally, beginners cannot compare to those long detached from worldly ties, who forget emotions when facing circumstances. Those who suddenly awaken to emptiness are indeed of superior wisdom, while those who gradually awaken to their nature are not of inferior rank. The student need only seek the true heart as the starting point. Though their refinement of the self may be incomplete, they may still enter the gate of stillness, provided they grasp the true heart whenever it appears.

This true heart may arise at dawn, at dusk, or at any hour within the twelve. One must recognize and seize it for oneself. Though this teaching may seem novel, it is not truly so. Moreover, it benefits beginners and harmonizes with the ancient alchemical classics without contradiction.

If one gradually cultivates thus—guarding the true at all times, embracing the true each day, never failing to perceive it—then through gradual progress one attains constancy. Over time, purity and stillness become complete, and the work of refining the self is naturally encompassed. Is this not grand?

Thus, when the mind-ground is momentarily clear, it is a precious opportunity for practice, a golden moment, a wondrous state. May one treasure it as if a single instant were worth a thousand gold.

**Chapter Thirty-Two: Direct Exposition on the Heart and Spirit**
*(Including the Method of Breath Regulation)*

The heart is the master of the entire body; the spirit is the supreme medicine of the three grades. One must discern whether heart and spirit are two or one.

Laozi said: "The human spirit delights in clarity, but the heart disturbs it. The human heart delights in stillness, but desires drag it away. Thus, constantly dispelling desires, the heart becomes still of itself. Clarifying the heart, the spirit becomes clear of itself."

Lu Qianxu said: "The method of regulating the breath begins with regulating the heart. The method of concentrating the spirit begins with regulating the breath." This is the bridge of sages, immortals, and buddhas, the path by which we enter virtue.

Those who begin to study the Dao must gather their thoughts and return to stillness. In movement and rest, sitting and lying down, all must reside within the cavity of the chest, for only then can the guarding of stillness be steadfast.

For the mind with thoughts is the deluded heart; the mind without thoughts is the true heart. If one can collect their thoughts in daily life and return them to their proper place, they can focus their mind in the moment and solidify the foundation of entering samadhi. As the sages said: "Knowing where to stop, one attains stability; with stability, one attains stillness."

When the heart is still, the breath is easily regulated. The subtler the heart, the more refined the breath. When the breath is regulated, the spirit returns. Thereupon, one further stabilizes the spirit, concentrating it within the qi cavity.

The heart, then, is also called spirit. Why? The heart resides in the Crimson Palace. When stirred, it becomes thoughts; when stilled, it returns to being the heart. Returning the still heart to the chamber of spirit, it becomes spirit. Spirit is without thought, without worry, without action—yet within non-action, all actions are possible; within non-use, great use is concealed. This is the supreme medicine of the three grades.

When concentrating the spirit, one must ensure it remains dependent on the breath. Do not let the spirit chase the breath, nor let it manipulate the breath. Chasing the breath scatters the spirit; manipulating the breath agitates it. Simply allow the breath to move naturally, following its own rhythm. Thus, the spirit becomes ever more concentrated.

When stillness reaches its extreme and movement arises, the spirit has grasped the mechanism of qi. The qi first breaks through primordial chaos—motionless, yet responsive and interconnected. Is this not the active practice of the Daoist cultivator?

**Chapter Thirty-Three: The Wondrous Function of Spirit and Breath**

Spirit is fire; breath is wind. To comprehend the mysterious mechanism of wind and fire, one must properly settle spirit and breath.

The spirit should be inwardly luminous yet silent; the breath should rely on true qi, flowing continuously. Simply stabilize the spirit without chasing the breath. It is like gathering fire in a furnace while drawing wind through bellows. The wind fans the flames, making them burn brighter, and the metal naturally melts. Thus, the wind comes to assist the fire—it is not the fire pursuing the wind.

Yet within this lies a subtle mechanism: to ensure the bellows’ qi blows with focused intensity, the wind pipe must press close to the furnace, allowing it to pass through the regulator. Only then can it fan the furnace’s flames, deepening their azure hue.

When the practitioner gathers spirit and collects qi, it is like fire melting metal. When the breath blows into the Kan (Water) trigram, it is like guiding the pipe close to the furnace, creating the momentum of wind aiding fire. As the furnace’s fire blazes, yang radiance surges. At this moment, spirit is qi, and qi is the elixir—just as fire forges iron red-hot, and red-hot iron is itself fire. This is what Qiongguan Weng meant by “fire is the elixir, the elixir is fire.” When fire and elixir merge, the Golden Elixir is instantly formed. If the breath does not blow inward but merely plays at the throat and nose, it is like the pipe not pressing close to the furnace. Is this not laughable?

**Chapter Thirty-Four: Further Discussion on Spirit and Breath**

When the breath is still, the spirit returns. The method of gathering spirit relies on regulating the breath. When the spirit is settled, the breath rests. The method of regulating the breath also relies on gathering the spirit. For when spirit is preserved in emptiness, the internal breath truly exists. Thus, the breath lingers because of the spirit, and the spirit remains because of the breath. Spirit and breath are balanced, neither fully present nor absent—one does not know whether spirit is breath or breath is spirit. Wind fans the fire, and fire burns with the wind. They sustain and intertwine, so that one does not know whether wind is fire or fire is wind. When the practice is fully refined, there is truly something beyond description in words.

**Chapter Thirty-Five: The Wondrous Use of Qi and Breath**

Cao Yuanjun said: “I tell you all the truth: the root of life has always resided in the true breath.” Why is the true breath the root of life? Because the postnatal breath harmonizes with the prenatal breath, and the prenatal breath is the true qi within the body, stirred by the breath into gentle ebb and flow. At this time, is it the breath moving? Or is it the qi moving? When the breath moves, the qi moves as well—neither is distinct. The breath contains qi, hence it is called the “misty true breath.” The qi contains breath, hence it is called the “bellows of true breath.” When the true breath moves, true qi is born; when true qi arrives, the root of life is born. The root of returning to life, the source of nurturing life, the treasure of protecting life—all truly lie in the true breath.

**Chapter Thirty-Six: The Wondrous Use of Spirit and Intention**

Spirit values concentration, as the *Cantong Qi* says: “Abide in tranquility and emptiness, inwardly illuminating the body.” Spirit is not intention, as it is said: “To inwardly illuminate the Mysterious Pass, one must employ true intention.” I have heard Chongxu say: “True intention is the right awakening within emptiness.” Qianxu said: “To infuse above and below, the primordial spirit must operate within.” The primordial spirit’s operation is the primordial spirit’s right awakening. One cannot say that the primordial spirit is true intention—they differ in substance and function. For in profound stillness and non-action, the spirit governs and moves in quietude. In composed elegance, intention manages affairs without confusion. Thus, spirit is the lord of the elixir, and intention is its emissary. Spirit and intention truly differ in substance and function. Since they differ in substance and function, they are two. Yet because function arises from substance, they may also be regarded as one. However, there is a further point: to cultivate true intention, one must nurture primordial qi. True intention arises from extreme stillness, thereby perfecting one’s wondrous use. This is what the Dao calls “constantly responding, constantly still,” what the Confucians call “being at ease before contemplation,” and what the Buddhists call “wisdom arising from samadhi.”

**Chapter Thirty-Seven: Further Discussion on Spirit and Intention**

At the beginning of sitting with lowered eyelids, spirit and intention need not be distinguished. But when movement and stillness alternate, then spirit and intention part ways. Sometimes one should guard the Central Palace with spirit, and it will not do to substitute intention. This applies when attaining emptiness, preserving stillness, and observing return—such as at the moment of tending the furnace. Sometimes one should guard the Central Palace with intention, and it will not do to lead with spirit. This applies when capturing lead, restraining mercury, and wielding the Celestial Stem—such as during the alchemical union. Why? Spirit is non-action, while intention is action. Spirit is non-action yet all-accomplishing; intention is action with purpose. Spirit is the spirit of intention, and intention takes spirit as its true origin—spirit governs stillness. Intention is the intention of spirit, and spirit takes intention as its right awakening—intention enables movement. To nurture primordial spirit, one must take “no thought” as the foundation, thereby achieving non-action and all-accomplishment. Shangyang’s commentary on the *Cantong Qi* says: “The true person lurks in the deep abyss, responding with no thought. Floating and wandering within the compass, employing with no thought. Breathing in harmony, achieving with no thought. The three natures unite, entering with no thought.” The use of no thought is not limited to this. To activate true intention, one must first refine the self, thereby achieving action and purposeful action. Qianxu’s *Jiuzheng Pian* says: “Refine the self to seek lead, welcoming it with the self. Draw fire into the crucible, sending it with the self. Smelt and bathe, guarding it with the self. Warm and nurture, completing it with the self.” The wondrousness of the self’s earth is also not limited to this. Ah, spirit! Ah, intention! They run through the entire process of Golden Elixir cultivation, never to be parted for even a moment. There are other wondrous uses, but they all depend on the practitioner’s own inquiry. I have merely compiled these teachings to open the way.

**Chapter Thirty-Eight: The Debate on Attaining Immortality**

The ancients said: “The highest establish virtue, the next establish merit, and the next establish teachings—all three are immortal.” To preserve the immortal spirit is the Dao. The three are also immortal because they assist the Dao. Benevolent virtue is the substance of the Dao, and humble virtue is its function. Universal salvation is the substance of the Dao, and self-cultivation is its function. As for teachings, they are the record of merit and virtue, the writings that convey the Dao.

Thus do they contend together for immortality.

Now, what decays is common, and what does not decay is sagely. That by which one may contend for immortality lies in having nothing to contend for, yet also in having something to contend for. Having nothing to contend for, one places oneself last and paradoxically becomes first; one softens one’s resolve and yet overcomes the rigid. Having something to contend for, one displays the beard and brows of a man, the backbone of a true hero, the boldness of a champion, and the ferocity of a great soul. Not contending with others for a fleeting moment, one contends with them for all eternity.

Confucius said: “When it comes to benevolence, one does not yield even to one’s teacher.” The teacher fears precisely that the disciple may fail to contend—merely collapsing into cowardice. A former Confucian master said: “The qi of dawn’s clarity and the spirit of the still night differ not at all from those of the sages. If one can expand upon this, even Confucius and Yan Hui would not surpass them.” The Śākya tradition states: “In the stillness of able benevolence, how does one differ from Śākyamuni? In the profound practice of prajñā, how is one distinct from freedom itself?” Daoist texts say: “Did Gautama arise from the earth? Did Zhong and Lü descend from heaven?” All these contend for the Dao and do not necessarily yield to those who came before. Let the aspirant take heed!

The myriad truths and ten thousand sages fundamentally do not begrudge others contending for the Dao.

**Chapter Thirty-Nine: On Retiring After Success and Fame**

The *Daodejing* states: “To retire after success and fame is the Way of Heaven.” I hold that the Way of Heaven is also the Way of humanity, and likewise the Way of self-cultivation. Heaven’s success and fame lie in the flourishing of growth and fulfillment; as the seasons turn and beings are nurtured, the heavenly Dao retreats and withdraws, concealing itself in winter’s reign. Such is the Way of Heaven. For humans, success and fame lie in utmost loyalty; having achieved merit, one retires with courage, and having attained fame, one conceals oneself—thus do heroes follow the example of Master Red Pine. Or, success and fame may lie in perfect filial piety: the mutual nourishment of will and body is the merit, and the universal praise of the clan is the fame. When parents reach a hundred years, the filial child’s duty is complete; one retires to preserve life—such is the Way of humanity. For the alchemist, attaining emptiness and guarding stillness is merit without merit; the profound and indistinct is fame without fame. When the return is accomplished, the sacred embryo is formed, and fame is attained, one retires to the ancestral aperture, embracing the One and returning to emptiness—such is the Way of elixir cultivation. Those who comprehend this should reflect on its resonance with virtue and human nature.

**Chapter Forty: On the Unity of Immortal and Buddha Cultivation**

The dual cultivation of nature and life is the essential principle for becoming an immortal, attaining buddhahood, or achieving sagehood. Some claim that Buddhists cultivate nature, Daoists cultivate life, and Confucians govern the world, each with distinct paths—yet this stems from a shallow understanding of their fundamental principles.

In my view: Buddhism emphasizes nature, yet within it lies a special transmission beyond the teachings. It is not that life is absent, but rather that it is spoken of in secret. The emphasis on nature is intended to enable one to establish life through nature, expanding the capacity of nature to illuminate the ten directions without boundary. Daoism emphasizes life, yet within it lies a true transmission within the teachings. It is not that nature is unmentioned, but rather that it is discussed sparingly. The emphasis on life is intended to enable one to comprehend nature through life, securing the root of life to endure through ten thousand kalpas without end.

If the cultivation of nature is perfected yet the external cultivation of life is neglected, true nature will be difficult to preserve, ultimately remaining in empty stillness—how then can one encompass the realms of sand and dust? If the cultivation of life is thorough yet the internal cultivation of nature is incomplete, true life may be preserved, but only the physical body is maintained—how then can one attain spiritual penetration of the three realms?

Only Buddhism possesses a special transmission beyond the teachings, enabling one to establish life through nature, so that the Land of Ultimate Bliss becomes ever more luminous and empty. Only Daoism possesses a true transmission within the teachings, enabling one to comprehend nature through life, so that the Heaven of Great Purity becomes ever more transcendent. Thus, immortals, buddhas, and sages—all engage in dual cultivation, not single cultivation.

When Śākyamuni entered deep meditation, his crown was pierced and his knees were transfixed. When Mahākāśyapa expounded the true meaning, he caused the flagpole of the monastery to topple. What do you make of this?

Master Lü said: “To cultivate nature alone without cultivating life is the foremost illness in spiritual practice.” Master Ziyang said: “Even if you fully awaken to the true suchness of nature, you will still have to discard one body only to enter another. How much better to cultivate the great medicine as well, and swiftly transcend the leaky to become a true person.” These examples may be taken as one corner from which the rest may be inferred.

When prajñā is practiced profoundly, the five aggregates are all empty. When the elixir matures and the great return is achieved, one faces the wall for ten years. At sixty, the ears are attuned; at seventy, the heart follows its desires. Only then are nature and life both perfected, ascending together to the realm of emptiness and transcendence—thus do immortals, buddhas, and sages all attain fulfillment.

The *Song of Enlightenment* by the First Patriarch Bodhidharma states:
*“The three teachings share one method,
Do not regard them as two.
Nature and life must be cultivated together,
So that heaven and earth do not decay.”*

Why, then, must one engage in disputes?

Moreover, there is this further point: the transmission of the three teachings remains unknown to their followers. If they understood this, they would not contradict one another. Each should simply fulfill its own Way and return to the Dao.

Now, suppose there were a master of the great Dao who addressed the three teachings, saying: “The three teachings are the three pillars of my Way. Though divided into three, they are united as one. The Dao cannot be divided, for then it would lack transformation; the Dao cannot be united, for then it would lack a governing principle. Thus, the three pillars establish its ultimate expression.

The Buddhist and Daoist teachings speak of nature while silently transmitting life; the Daoist and immortal teachings transmit life while silently transmitting nature. The Confucian teaching takes the bearing of worldly responsibilities as its urgent task. Nature is rarely spoken of, life is even more seldom mentioned, and both nature and life are cultivated in silence. As a result, the descendants of the three traditions, each adhering to their ancestral lineage, have become specialized schools. They stir the heavens and shake the earth, debating right and wrong, opening boundless realms of contention. When Buddhists attack Daoism, they employ marvelous strategies of overturning emptiness. When Daoists attack Buddhism, they employ divine insights of pointing directly to reality.”

When Confucians attack Buddhism and Daoism, they produce inexhaustible essays and endless sophistry. Yet how could they know that all these are but branches of the Dao? Since the Dao divides into three, how could there be no divergence in its offshoots, no distinction between the heterodox and the orthodox? Why, then, do they not contemplate the root but instead debate the branches?

To regard nature as encompassing life is one current; to regard life as encompassing nature is another; to merge nature and life into one is yet another. These three currents are all currents of the Dao. In their return to the non-being, they are one.

A gāthā says:
Blowing out the bright lamp to play games,
Who knows they strike their own kin?
If I speak of those who attack one another,
It is but scattered blossoms dusting your body.

*Volume Two: Secret Teachings of the Three Vehicles*
By Changyi Mountain Man Li Hanxu
Collated by Chen Yingning of Wanjian

**Reader’s Guide to *Secret Teachings of the Three Vehicles***

1. The author of this book, Li Hanxu, is commonly known as the Western School. Though its main principles do not exceed the scope of the *Triple Unification* and *Awakening to Reality*, having formed its own school, it must possess distinctive features not found in other alchemical classics. Scholars should discern these differences.

2. As practitioners’ efforts progress in stages and their levels vary in depth, the text introduces the theory of the three "river chariots." The first river chariot refers to the first stage of practice, the second to the second stage, and the third to the third stage. However, the path of circulating the river chariot remains singular, without divergence. Do not mistakenly assume there are three separate paths.

3. In the sections on the three river chariots, there may be instances of excessive description or inappropriate analogies. Readers should not cling to the literal meaning or force interpretations.

4. The *Mind-Collecting Method* in the appendix is particularly essential. If one studies it carefully, one will surely benefit.

5. The *Daoist Songs, Poems, and Miscellaneous Writings* also contain subtle meanings. If readers can draw connections and extend understanding, it would be most excellent.

6. This book is an old manuscript, transmitted for over eighty years without ever being printed. Aside from the three chapters on the river chariots, other sections—such as the *Mind-Collecting Method* and *Daoist Songs*—were not originally titled separately nor properly attributed under the *Secret Teachings of the Three Vehicles*. Their arrangement was also disordered. Here, two appendices are newly designated, and the text has been reorganized for the reader’s convenience.

7. Previous alchemical works were composed either to seek external protection or to instruct disciples, not for universal salvation or posterity. Thus, they need not be comprehensible to all, and in fact, precautions were taken to prevent universal understanding. Hence, cryptic terms and obscure names abound, leaving readers bewildered, unsure of their meaning. Often, the intended meaning is concealed, and the truth is obscured. This is why no Daoist practitioner fails to lament the difficulty of reading alchemical classics.

8. If the meaning of this book remains unclear, one should seek answers in *Discourses on the Dao’s Mysteries*. If *Discourses on the Dao’s Mysteries* still eludes comprehension, one should consult the *Complete Works of Zhang Sanfeng*, as they can mutually illuminate one another.

Written in May of the 26th year of the Republic of China (1937) by Chen Yingning of Wanjian in Shanghai.

**A Brief Biography of the Perfected Li Hanxu**

The Perfected One was a native of Changyishan in Lijiahe, Leshan County, Jiading Prefecture, Sichuan. Born on the fourth day of the eighth lunar month at the hour of *yin* in the *bingyin* year of the Jiaqing reign (1786), his mother dreamed, at the moment of his birth, of a Daoist priest entering her home cradling a golden book in his arms; upon waking, she found the Perfected One already born. He was the second of three brothers, with his elder and younger siblings both becoming Daoist priests. From childhood, he was exceptionally bright, and in his youth, he entered the county academy as a scholar. Skilled in the zither and fond of poetry and wine, at the age of twenty-four, he encountered the Patriarch Lü but did not recognize him.

Later, afflicted with a blood-wasting illness, he followed his mother’s command to convalesce in Emei County, where he met Master Zheng Pushan. Master Zheng, a man of the Kangxi era, was a senior disciple of the Perfected Sun, whose personal name was Jiaoluan. Sharing lodgings with him for treatment, the master said, “Minerals, plants, and herbs can only address symptoms. To cure the root, one must employ the wondrous medicine of one’s own body—only then can true solidity be achieved.” Upon hearing this, the Perfected One felt as though awakening from a dream and immediately bowed in reverence, taking refuge under the master. Zheng then transmitted oral instructions, urging, “A great kalpa is approaching; you must swiftly cultivate to save the world. Moreover, you will have a primordial master among the Perfected.”

Later, on Mount Emei, he encountered the Patriarchs Lü and Zhang in a monastic hall. His original name was Yuanzhi, styled Pingquan. The Patriarch Lü changed it to Xiyue, styled Hanxu, with an alternate name Tuanyang, secretly imparting the original sound. After several years of secluded cultivation, he achieved the Golden Elixir. The Three Masters reappeared, exhorting him to hasten in writing books to save the world.

In obedience to the command of the Three Perfected Ones, he composed *Annotations on the Thirteen Classics of the Most High*, *Elucidations on the Scripture of the Great Cavern’s Old Immortal*, and two commentaries on *The Rootless Tree*, collectively titled *Fifteen Daoist Discourses*, also known as *Essentials of Preserving the Body*. He edited and revised the Patriarch Lü’s chronicles, sacred traces, alchemical classics, and salvific texts, naming the collection *Marvelous Encounters at Sea and Mountain*. He compiled the complete works of the Patriarch Zhang, titling it *Complete Works of Zhang Sanfeng*. His own writings, including *Ninefold Refinement of the Heart*, *Scripture of the End of Literature*, and *A Treatise on the Postcelestial*, were all published. Additionally, he authored *Inner Chapters of Rounded Peaks*, *Secret Essentials of the Three Vehicles*, and *Discourses on the Dao’s Mysteries*, none of which were published.

In the first month of the *bingshen* year of the Xianfeng reign (1856), I visited his abode at Changyishan and beheld his compassionate countenance, which appeared no older than thirty. After taking leave, the master ascended on the eighth day of the fifth lunar month at the hour of *yin*, with an extraordinary fragrance filling the sky for seven days. On that same day at the hour of *mao*, his immortal form manifested at Ziliujing. Following his ascension, his miraculous traces were too numerous to recount in full.

The master had two sons: the elder pursued Confucian scholarship, while the younger tilled the land. His eldest brother had three sons, the eldest of whom, at eleven years old, was intelligent, kind, and filial—qualities the master often praised with admiration. Though his disciples were many, only Zhou Daochang of Jiangxi achieved the Great Elixir, while several others attained the Jade Fluid Returned Elixir. My virtue being meager and my karmic affinity shallow, I served the master but briefly. Here, I have merely outlined what I personally witnessed.

Respectfully recorded by his disciple Li Daoshan of Qiaoyang, Jianning County, Fujian.

The Three Chariots refer to three types of *heche* (River Chariots). The first is the circulation of *qi*, which is the *xiaozhoutian* (Small Celestial Cycle) of firing at *zi* and *wu*; the second is the circulation of essence, which is the *yuye heche* (Jade Fluid River Chariot) for warming and nourishing with water; the third is the simultaneous circulation of essence and *qi*, which is the *dazhoutian* (Great Celestial Cycle) of refining precelestial gold and mercury, the Sevenfold Returned Elixir, and the Ninefold Returned Great Elixir. These Three Chariots all rely on the true spirit and true intent to govern their course. If one can comprehend the secret principles of the Three Chariots, then essence, *qi*, and spirit—these three treasures—will be perfectly complete, and the Three Immortals of Heaven, Earth, and Humanity will be attained.

The first River Chariot, the practice of circulating *qi*, is for opening the passes and laying the foundation, obtaining the medicine and congealing the elixir. Its sequence begins with nurturing the true breath within emptiness. Collect the mind, regulate the breath, close the eyes, and preserve the spirit. Stillness upon stillness, purity upon purity. Let go of all things, forgetting the entire body. In utter chaos and profound obscurity, when the work reaches this point, it is like winter in the heavens, when the myriad things flourish and each returns to its root; like night in the sun’s course, when the water clock sinks deep and each quiets its heart. This is the time of no-knowledge, no-perception. Yet who knows that in this moment of no-knowledge and no-perception, the first yang revives—just as winter gives birth to spring, or night turns toward dawn? Suddenly, one is startled: though no smoke is present, it seems as if there is smoke; though no *qi* is present, it seems as if there is *qi*. From the lower *dantian*, it steams up to the heart’s palace, awakening one as if from a dream. This moment of initial awakening is called the *huo zishi* (Living Zi Hour). Swiftly activate the first River Chariot to gather and circulate it; delay, and this formless *qi* will transform into something tangible. This *qi* is called *renqian* (Watery Lead), the postnatal, or the yang fire—hence it is said, “At the *zi* hour, advance the yang fire.” What does advancing the yang fire mean? The practitioner must abruptly turn the newly awakened heart, shifting it past the lower Magpie Bridge to the position just before the Big Dipper. This is the true secret never to be transmitted lightly. Move it to the *weilu* (Coccyx Pass), guarding it without disorder. In an instant, the true *qi* warms gently, passing through the two apertures at the tip of the coccyx, penetrating the spine, ascending to the *yuzhen* (Jade Pillow), and drilling into the *niwan* (Mud Pill Palace). The ancient immortals said: *“Through the double passes of the spine to the crown gate—this is the most revered path in cultivation.”* This is what they referred to. Fools, not understanding the circulation of *qi*, immediately press the tongue to the upper palate to receive the sweet dew. Alas! How laughable and pitiable—all due to not having received proper instruction. Know that in the practice of circulating *qi*, one can only guide the *qi* into the throat. As the *Huangting Jing* states: *“Ingest the mysterious *qi* to attain longevity.”* Thus, this yang fire *qi* is purple-black in color and is called the *xuanqi* (Mysterious Qi). The method of ingestion requires oral instructions to send it into the *qi* pipe. Otherwise, if it enters the food throat, where can the foundation of the elixir be established? One must guide this yang *qi* down the *qi* throat to the *xuanying* (Mysterious Throat), where it transforms into the water of sweet dew. As the *Huangting Jing* says: *“The Mysterious Throat’s *qi* pipe receives the essence talisman.”* This is what it means. The *xuanying* is also called *xuanyong* (Mysterious Obstruction), for when a person’s *qi* reaches this point, it becomes obstructed. Common folk, ignorant of this mystery, expect the *qi* to transform into divine water upon reaching the *niwan*, swallowing it like tea. I fear that even a single drop entering the *qi* pipe would cause unceasing coughing. How could water, a tangible substance, enter the *qi* pipe? Thus, the *Huangting Jing* states: *“Exhaling the clear and inhaling the mysterious, the two *qi* shine—if you encounter this, you will ascend to the Milky Way.”* This means the clear *qi* emerges from the *dantian*, while the mysterious *qi* enters the *xuanying*, the two *qi* transforming into one another. When the *qi* transforms into water, it showers the heart’s palace and returns to the Void Cavity. Treasure and nurture it—this is the foundation-laying. After laying the foundation for a long time, the accumulation deepens, and there comes a moment when, while sitting in stillness as usual, something suddenly emerges from the *dantian* with a sound like thunder and a radiance like lightning—this is the precelestial medicine within the postnatal. Immediately apply the first Chariot to circulate it to the *niwan*, where it first transforms into liquid. Ingesting it, one obtains the *yuye dantou* (Jade Fluid Elixir Head). This is the beginning of obtaining the medicine and congealing the elixir. Thereafter, the work must be continuous and unbroken, deepening the root and securing the stem. This is the subtlety of fully nurturing the elixir; for further details, see the explanation below.

The second River Chariot, the practice of circulating essence, is for drawing out the lead from Kan (Water) and restraining the mercury of Li (Fire), refining one’s nature. Previously, after circulating *qi* for a long time, one obtained the minor medicine and congealed the elixir head. Thereafter, the internal breath flows naturally and effortlessly, firmly guarding the *dantian*. Each morning, while sitting or lying in clarity, the elixir rises to the heart’s palace like a soft ball of cotton. It must be gathered back into the Void, vanishing without a trace, to prevent its loss. The secret says: *“When the spirit returns to the body, the *qi* naturally follows.”* This is precisely that moment. As the embrace deepens over time, suddenly the *dantian* feels like spring water newly born, rippling and flowing. Guard the natural internal breath, refining and tempering it. The water suddenly transforms into hot *qi*, flowing from the inner sides of both hips to the *yongquan* (Bubbling Spring). The spirit must focus on the heels, the true breath following—this is what is meant by *“the true person’s breath reaches the heels.”* After a short while, when the *yongquan* settles into stillness, return the heart to the *weilu*, silently guarding and awaiting. Suddenly, one senses something at the *weilu*—soft like cotton, like a steamed bun, like a mass of *qi*, heavy and sluggish, difficult to move. Immediately adjust the internal breath, concentrate the mind single-pointedly, and intensely refine it. Then, a stream of hot liquid surges from the *weilu*, slowly passing through the spine and surging up to the *niwan*. Only then is it called the Yellow River flowing backward, the canal reversed in its course.

This type of river chariot is what the *Dadong Jing* calls *"harnessing essence to reach the Mud Pill Palace,"* and what Ancestor Lü describes as *"transporting essence into the upper palace"*—it is not the same as circulating *qi*. Within the Mud Pill Palace, the sound of rushing water resonates, and after a long while, the water’s sound ceases, and the spirit rests within it. After maintaining this for a short time, press the tongue upward against the palate, hold the breath in the nose, clench the jaw tightly, brace both hands against the seat, and tilt the head back to face the empty beam. Wait until the golden liquid fills the tongue, then hold the nasal breath without exhaling. With a single gulp, it flows into the windpipe, descending the twelve-tiered tower of the *chonglou*, and the divine water irrigates the Flowery Pond.

This Flowery Pond is unknown to most. Some say it lies beneath the tongue’s root, others claim it is the lower *dantian*—both are incorrect. This Flowery Pond resides between the two breasts, called the upper *qi* sea, separated from the *xuanying* by a single layer. As Bai Yuchan states: *"The Flowery Pond lies precisely within the qi sea."* When the water fills the Flowery Pond, it flows without being retained. Reaching the Crimson Palace, the heart-ground becomes cool and clear. Settling into the Yellow Court, the heart-fire attains supreme stillness. This is called *"drawing lead to restrain mercury, guiding the tiger to subdue the dragon."* Once the two trigrams are united, they circulate endlessly—this is the work of refining the self with jade liquid.

Yet this jade liquid cannot be produced daily; one must first apply the earlier *qi*-circulating practice. After several cycles, it may arise once. When one reaches the stage where jade liquid flows continuously, the Yellow Center becomes pervaded with principle, the skin grows lustrous, the heart-monarch rests in leisure, and the nature-body shines with radiance. Facing circumstances, one forgets emotion; in desire, one transcends desire. Living in the world yet apart from it, the true intent becomes unshakable, the sword’s edge keen. Round and full, luminous and bright, red and pure, gleaming and sharp—this is the time when self-refinement is perfected.

Here, one may proceed to the three chariots’ work, as the following section will explain.

**The Third River Chariot**
This involves circulating the primordial essence-*qi*, which the alchemists call *"mercury welcoming lead,"* the return of passion to nature, the work of seven reversals and nine returns. Once self-refinement is perfected, the mercury-nature becomes spiritually responsive, advancing and retreating freely, the feminine and masculine adapting to change. Only at this stage can one undertake the great work of return and restoration.

The seven reversals and return of the elixir begin by taking the already-formed mercury-nature and calling it the inner elixir. Then, entering the chamber and sitting in a circle, one conceals the inner elixir within the hollow void. Above resembles *Qian*, below resembles *Kun*; the nature-side pertains to being, the life-side to non-being. First, one must enter non-being from being, then generate being from non-being.

Its image resembles *Qian*’s essence sown into *Kun* the Mother, where *Kun* fills its belly and becomes *Kan*. *Kun*’s essence stirs from *Qian* the Father, where *Qian* empties its heart and becomes *Li*. Once *Qian* and *Kun* are arrayed, they are called the crucible (the wondrous aperture of being and non-being). The dual functions of *Li* and *Kan* manifest through this.

At the moment of entering non-being from being, all is silent and still—heart dead, spirit preserved. Soon, one’s own consciousness transforms into things that startle or entice, testing the inner spirit. Then, the demonic generals of the heavens take form as good or evil figures, further testing the inner spirit. If undisturbed by any of these, the primordial spirit remains serene.

After a time, a single yang qi arises—like the return of one yang beneath *Kun*’s yin. I then exhale a yin from *Qian*’s palace to welcome it (kidney qi ascending, heart fluid descending, all by nature’s course), called *"mercury welcoming lead,"* or *"the great exchange of Kan and Li,"* or *"the ebb and flow of inner and outer yin-yang."* Once this flow is established, the Celestial Maiden Taiyi, accompanied by Qiu Lan, presents the feminine sword, plucks it, and sets it as the elixir’s foundation—this is the seven-reversal returning elixir.

With the elixir’s foundation laid, spirit and qi harmonize. The single yang gradually grows into *Dui*, and the *Kan* male transforms into the *Dui* female (this is the time of metaphors like *"the moon at Geng,"* *"the moon on the Western River,"* or *"the crescent moon"*). Because of these two characters, *Dui* and *female*, alchemists call it the *"first essence"* or *"heavenly water"* (a classification by analogy—fools who practice blindly do so because they have not met a true teacher).

When the alchemist gathers this first essence, it is called *"drawing passion back to nature."* After five thousand forty-eight days, when it returns to the Yellow Way, it resembles the full moon of the fifteenth night, with metal and water in perfect roundness. Within the human body, this is the time when the primordial essence and qi surge in abundance.

At this stage, the practitioner must swiftly activate the great river chariot, transporting it to the Mud Pill Palace. Soon, a sweet fluid descends into the throat, as large as a sparrow’s egg or grape, neither musk nor honey, yet exquisitely fragrant—this is the nine-return golden liquid great elixir. When the Daoist ingests this golden liquid, it is then called *"lead entering mercury,"* *"metal merging with wood,"* *"the posterior heaven returning to the prior heaven,"* and *"the infant meeting the maiden."*

When the infant and maiden meet, they are nurtured day and night. Over time, the organs become visible, inner and outer radiance shines, and within, a true one appears, resembling oneself—this is the infant and maiden giving birth to another infant. One who attains this infant must quietly cultivate and cherish it, moving it from the Valley of Spirit to the Heavenly Valley. Then, one may transcend form, ascend to the assembly of immortals, and merge with the Dao.

*Method for Gathering the Heart*
Written by Li Hanxu of Changyi Mountain

Chen Yingning of Wanjian’s Collated *Method for Gathering the Heart* – Preface in Verse

The *Method for Gathering the Heart* unfolds plainly and directly,
Heaven rejoices, free from blame or reproach.
Year after year, tirelessly driving the river chariot,
The heart seems laborious yet is truly at ease.

Last night, the spirit soared to pay homage to the Upper Perfected,
Who sealed me as the Great Perfected of the Teachings of Goodness.
The Dao recounted my affairs of four hundred years,
Three playful descents into the red dust.

Clasping the golden elixir, I alone savor its delight,
Yet I share with the assembly of immortals in silent riddles.
Awakening, I first stir the heart of compassion,
And with my own hand inscribe the *Record of the Jade Ring*.

Distinguishing neither good nor evil, wise nor foolish,
All must gather the heart and sit in emptiness.
Only by entering profound obscurity does one behold the Dao—
The very first step is the finest effort.

*Fundamental Practice in the Method for Gathering the Heart*

The Dao of nurturing life takes true breathing as its root.
As Cao Wenyi said: *“I shall tell you gentlemen the truth:
The stem of life has always resided in true breathing.”*
These are indeed essential words.

The initial practice begins with quieting the heart,
Then sealing the mouth,
Then regulating the breath (when the heart is still, the qi is level—
The unregulated regulation is supreme).

Once the nasal breath is calm and harmonious,
Close the eyes and inwardly observe,
Directing the spirit to the root of the kidneys, below the Yin Bridge meridian
(Before the Valley Gate, behind the scrotum).
After a short while, lift the heart-breath upward into the Cavity of Emptiness
(Behind the navel, before the waist, between the heart and kidneys—
Do not cling rigidly to this location).
Rest the spirit and settle the breath,
Taking naturalness as the guiding principle.

If the heart is too severe, it burns—
One must follow its natural course: this is the civil fire.
If the heart is too scattered, it chills—
One must guard its natural state: this is the martial fire.
The civil and martial fires refine and temper,
Their wondrous application enduring from start to finish.

When the inner breath is even and balanced,
Neither forget nor force it.
At this moment, the heart is like empty space:
With breath as its companion, it is not empty;
With breath as its follower, it is not void.
Between non-emptiness and non-void,
Stillness upon stillness,
Purity upon purity.

The breath flows ceaselessly,
The heart-spirit silently.
At this point, release all things,
Forget both self and others—
This is called *boring into profound obscurity*.

Within profound obscurity, there is qi,
A single spirit alone awakens—
This is true breathing.
When true breathing manifests,
It warms the heart with a melting, tingling sensation.
One must press it back into the Cavity of Emptiness within the body’s chamber,
Accumulating and layering it,
So that the stem of life grows and yang qi naturally lengthens—
Only then can one open the passes and circulate the qi.

Concentrating the spirit and regulating the breath
Are the fundamental practices.
Concentrating the spirit means gathering the already-cleansed heart and entering within.
When the heart is not yet clear, do not close the eyes inwardly.
First, encourage and urge oneself,
Drawing it back,
Cool and serene,
Then gather it into the qi cavern—
This is called *concentrating the spirit*.

Sitting in emptiness,
Neither leaning nor tilting—
This is concentrating the spirit in the void.
Regulating the breath is not difficult:
When the heart-spirit is still,
The breath follows naturally.
I merely guard and follow it,
Illuminating it with the spirit’s light—
This is regulation.

Directing the breath of the Yin Bridge
To meet the heart’s breath within the qi cavern—
The spirit resides within the qi,
Silently focusing on the primordial sea.
Without union, they unite of themselves;
Without contact, they connect of themselves—
This is called *spiritual union across the body’s divide*.

Guard one’s nature, preventing dispersion.
Preserve the spirit, avoiding dullness,
Thus entering profound obscurity and subtle indistinction.

When the spirit halts below the navel, it is called *concentrating the spirit*.
When the qi returns below the navel, it is called *regulating the breath*.
Spirit and breath interdependent,
Guarding their natural purity—this is *not forgetting*.
Following their natural purity—this is *not forcing*.
Neither forgetting nor forcing,
In silence and softness,
The breath becomes lively and the heart at ease.
Employ the *boring* technique,
Taking emptiness as the heart’s dwelling,
And profound obscurity as the spirit-breath’s homeland.

After two or three attempts,
Clarifying again and again,
Suddenly, heart and breath forget each other,
Spirit and qi merge—
Unawares, yang stirs in a flash of enlightenment.

*Miscellaneous Discussions on the Method for Gathering the Heart*

A disciple asked: *“The secret of the Three Vehicles has been fully revealed—
Does this not risk heavenly reproach?”*
Hanxu replied: *“I dare not defy heavenly reproach,
But hope that people may reform and renew themselves.
In all practice, one must first discard the human heart
And seek the heart of the Dao.
Set aside ordinary breathing
And seek true breathing.
Then stabilize spirit and qi,
And bore into profound obscurity.
None of these stages can be omitted.
How could one who enters my Dao remain a petty person?”*

*“In all practice, boring into profound obscurity is the most difficult task.
Yet the primordial qi arises from emptiness—
Only with true profound obscurity
Does true emptiness exist.
Alas! First difficulty, then attainment—
One must be willing to relinquish the whole self.
In the past, I spent seven or eight years in the grotto-heaven
Learning to bore into profound obscurity,
And only then gained a slight grasp.
Today’s learners advance swiftly but retreat just as fast—
How can they enter the Dao?”*

A disciple asked: *“Master, you transmit the Dao so freely—
If an inferior person obtains it and fails in practice,
Will they not turn and revile you?”*
Hanxu replied: *“Without the ridicule of inferior persons,
How could the greatness of my Dao be seen?
The Great Dao first requires purifying body and mind,
Regulating spirit and qi.
At its deepest, one must be able to release all things
And bore into profound obscurity.
Only with such true effort
Will true efficacy appear.
Those who lack effort yet vainly seek results
Will forever remain without them.
Why be troubled by their revilement?”*

A disciple asked: *“As you describe it, can even evil people learn the Dao?”*
Hanxu replied: *“Yes.
I shall recite the admonition:
*‘All that came before is like yesterday’s death.
All that follows is like today’s birth.’*
Refrain from all evil,
Practice all good—
Then one can transform hell into heaven,
And change black qi into crimson light.”*

I possess a three-word formula for those who cultivate the Dao: diligence, sincerity, and constancy—none may be lacking. Yet though one be diligent and sincere, the ultimate outcome must rest in constancy. Confucius said: *“A man without constancy cannot even be a shaman or physician—how much less can he grasp the Dao?”* Confucian scholars study literature and arts, setting years—even a decade—as their term. How could the refinement of mind and tempering of qi require less than the reading of books or writing of essays?

Sincerity is the supreme yin image: in the *Book of Changes*, it is the Supreme Ultimate; in Buddhism, it is *tathatā*. Mencius said: *“There has never been one who was perfectly sincere yet unmoved. Without sincerity, none can move others.”* When “movement” is spoken of in contrast to “stillness,” we know sincerity is a yin image. The Dao of Confucius’ school exalts perfect sincerity as spirit-like, and perfect spirit as ceaseless—both are the great essence and great function of stillness. Thus, entering stillness with sincerity stills the heart without disorder; entering concentration with sincerity fixes the heart without wavering; guarding the center with sincerity keeps the center without bias; entering profound obscurity with sincerity unobstructedly penetrates the subtle. Diligence is the foundation of learning, and in the Dao, there is an even deeper “diligence within non-diligence.” Nurturing the breath of spontaneity, settling the mind of spontaneity, acting without acting, and acting yet not acting—what is called *“subtly continuous as if existing, its use never exhausted”* is truly the utmost of diligence.

To subdue the demon of sleep, one must employ diligence. Racing incense and seated meditation that exhaust the spirit are misapplications of diligence. Those skilled in refining sleep sleep yet do not sleep, do not sleep yet remain watchful—effort naturally continues unbroken, and spirit and qi naturally increase. Staying up through the night instead invites the sleep demon. The *Cantong Qi* says: *“Sleep and wakefulness ever embrace; vigilance discerns presence and absence.”* Mastering this formula, one remains naturally clear and unwavering.

A disciple asked: *“Master Lu Qianxu said: ‘The union of yin and yang is the Most High’s most secret teaching.’ May we hear its formula?”*
I replied: The union of yin and yang is the root of supreme yin and the foundation of profound obscurity. Only by boring into profound obscurity can one achieve this union. I advise first refining within emptiness—stillness upon stillness, concentration upon concentration—until there is no self, no other, not even “nothingness.” Naturally, one enters profound obscurity, and without seeking union, union occurs spontaneously, giving birth to supreme yang from within supreme yin.

The method of union differs between pre-heaven and post-heaven. Pre-heaven union establishes life through nature; post-heaven union unites spirit with qi. Thus, the *Mirror for Ingredients* says: *“It is nature and life, not spirit and qi. The lead of the watery realm is but one flavor.”* Pre-heaven has but a single name; post-heaven distinguishes essence, spirit, intent, qi, hun, po, nature, and emotion. In pre-heaven, refining one suffices—all are present. The key is to extract true yang through union.

The five viscera in the human body have their fixed positions—none may be shifted. Daoists speak of *“inverting Qian and Kun, Kan and Li,”* but how could the heart be moved downward or the kidneys upward? Not so. What is inverted is the spirit and qi within the heart and kidneys: the heart’s spirit descends to meet, the kidneys’ qi ascends to rise. When spirit and qi invert, even the physical heart and kidneys seem inverted, and the formless Qian and Kun likewise invert. Through inverted exchange, a single yang is born within Kun to form Kan, and a single yin is born within Qian to form Li. The Li maiden and Kan youth exchange, like the Dui maiden of the west joining the Zhen youth of the east. South and north shift to east and west, water and fire transform into metal and wood. Metal’s emotion and wood’s nature become the White Tiger and Azure Dragon. When the dragon unites with the tiger, it is like the maiden embracing the infant; when the tiger unites with the dragon, it is like the infant embracing the maiden. In essence, these are the two of nature and life. Within life is nature, within nature is life—two yet one. Thus, Master Ziyang said: *“Zhen and Dui are not east and west; Kan and Li are not south and north.”* One may now comprehend this.

Confucians and Daoists cultivate qi differently. Nurturing the qi of spontaneity can preserve life; nurturing the qi of vastness can embrace both life and death. From ancient times, the resolute and humane—those who risked danger to fulfill their mission or died to perfect their humanity—did so. When cultivating this qi, it is purely the heart of principle, filling the cosmos. Thus, Mencius said: *“This qi is supremely great and firm. Nurtured with uprightness and unharmed, it fills the space between heaven and earth—it is born of accumulated righteousness.”*

Daoists cultivate qi by preserving their true nature, neither waiting for danger to retreat nor death to hide. They are the *“noble ones who act upon perceiving the moment, not waiting for the day’s end”* spoken of in the *Book of Changes*.
