In the Daoist initial practice, one must nurture the qi of spontaneity. What is this qi of spontaneity? The *Book of Changes* says: *“This qi is supremely small and soft, bending yet nurtured without harm. It gathers within emptiness—born of accumulated essence.”*

The Daoist elixir of return is also the qi of vastness. Once attained, it too can face danger to fulfill a mission or die to perfect humanity. Since ancient times, this has been called *“knife liberation,”* possessing unfathomable marvels and inexplicable transformations, distinct from Confucianism. Some, after death, reappear elsewhere with youthful features, traveling as immortals—this is the accomplishment of the returned elixir, a body beyond the body. The perfected who attain the Dao live as immortals and die as immortals, whether leaving their forms behind or ascending through liberation.

The humane can attain stillness—living long, dying long—like Master Zeng preserving his body or Yan Yuan’s brief life. There are five failures on the Dao: some taste and depart; some superficially admire its name; some begin diligently but end in slackness; some, with stubborn natures yet uninitiated, rashly slander its profundity; some, of dull disposition, remain deaf to calls and blind to awakening. There are three attainments on the Dao: those who know it may become spirit-people; those who love it may become perfected people; those who delight in it may become supreme people.

**Postnatal Sequential Exposition Concludes (Volume Three)**

I have written commentaries on the *Dao De Jing*, *Huangting Jing*, *Dadong Jing*, and *Wugen Jing*, all expounding the function of the prenatal, not the gateway for beginners. To journey far, one starts nearby; to ascend high, one begins low. Without clarity on the postnatal sequence, it is like aspiring to become a *jinshi* or *hanlin* without first passing the *maocai* or *xiaolian* examinations. Thus, I composed *Postnatal Sequential Exposition* as the path of entry, comprising nine stages:

1. Gathering the mind
2. Seeking qi
3. Congealing spirit
4. Expanding the apertures
5. Opening the passes
6. Laying the foundation
7. Obtaining the medicine
8. Coalescing the elixir
9. Refining the self

The Most High said: “Nobility takes humility as its root; height takes lowliness as its foundation.” Postnatal cultivation is the Way of the humble and lowly. Humility is what the master calls *“speaking of the unsightly.”* Lowliness is what the *Scripture* calls *“taking from below.”* To nurture the elixir’s foundation, essence and qi are treasured as jewels. In practice, one must first gather the mind inward, taking the center as the pole, harmony as the principle, spirit as the substance (stillness), and intention as the function (wisdom).

Seeking qi begins with the yinqiao vessel, where it is lively and fluid, neither seen nor heard. Harmony is the work of concentrating qi to softness, embracing spirit in stillness. From stillness wisdom arises; seated illumination returns to its origin. Mating primordial essence generates primordial qi, and expanding the apertures to open the passes becomes effortless.

Primordial essence is the yinqiao meridian’s daily-generated human qi. The practitioner must seek the qi’s active locus and unite it with stillness—this is called the union of spirit and qi. When spirit and qi unite, male and female essences mate, and the true seed is transformed. The true seed is the postnatal crucible’s true qi. The postnatal crucible is where primordial spirit and primordial qi conjoin, hence named the *spirit-father* and *spirit-mother*. When this qi is refined from the crucible, one must congeal the spirit, soften the intention, and use softness to restrain hardness, naturally entering one’s inner crucible. Harmonize, regulate, forge, and refine it, concealing it within the cinnabar field, breathing within the void—this is called the *life-stem* or *embryonic breathing*.

Suddenly, within the inner crucible, something surges forth, leaping and bounding, hissing and puffing, ascending through the thrusting vessel to the heart’s palace—this is the moment of expanding the apertures. When its force is potent, transform spirit into intention, guide it out from the coccyx, striking the three passes to soar to the mud-pill palace—this is opening the passes. Once the passes and apertures are open, proceed with the work of self-nurturance and discuss laying the foundation.

Laying the foundation means drawing their qi and blood to replenish one’s spirit. Though spirit may be robust, fearing its instability, one uses *ren lead* to restrain it. *Ren lead* is born of the union of the two qi. The qi of the *kan* palace is earthly qi; the qi of the *li* palace is heavenly qi. When heaven and earth conjoin, chaos swirls, misty and dense, coalescing into the *Cavern of the Void*. Within the Cavern of the Void, a qi is born—this qi is *ren lead*, the moment of obtaining lead.

Lead’s nature is qi without substance, thus it is clear and ascends. Upon reaching Kunlun, direct the gaze upward so spirit and qi rest in the crown. After a time of congealment, yang reaches its peak and yin arises. Then, press the tongue against the upper palate, regulate the breath, and after prolonged contact, sweet nectar descends like cold springs, drop by drop. Though not abundant, swallow it down the *heavy tower*, sending it with intention to the yellow court. Strangely, a sound like crashing waves arises—one realizes celestial sweet dew is not easily obtained. Descending into the yellow court, it coalesces into the inner elixir.

Thereafter, one must sever desire while in desire, transcend dust while in dust, forget essence amid circumstances, refine lead to subdue mercury, expel the three corpses and five thieves, and dissolve the six desires and seven emotions. Bones and qi become golden essence; skin and flesh turn to jade. Then, inner celestial mechanisms align with outer human affairs—no deliberation, no insistence, no rigidity, no self. Accumulate merit, refine qi, and nurture spirit; as things arise and events occur, the mind’s realm naturally radiates light. Meeting scenes with the heart, the wisdom-sword cleaves through dusty obstructions. Cultivating merit in the human realm, one’s name resounds in heaven. Feasting on celestial emoluments, one enjoys boundless longevity.

At this stage, embrace simplicity and guard integrity, vigilant against peril and danger. Heaven and humanity unite in manifestation; the myriad transformations settle their foundation. Spirit is eternally firm; qi returns to the void—this completes the work of self-refinement. Beginners must first gather the mind, seek qi, congeal spirit, expand the apertures, open the passes, lay the foundation, obtain the medicine, coalesce the elixir, and refine the self—these nine stages are the gateway to the Dao. Once entered, yin and yang return to unity, and there is no going without return. Exhausting principle and fulfilling nature to reach destiny, one moves from effort to effortlessness, from striving to spontaneity—there has never been a case where the golden elixir failed to form.

**Volume Four: Record of Following the Path**
*(Conclusion of the Scripture on Ninefold Heart Refinement in the Great Dao of Human Primordiality)*
*Handwritten by Hanxuzi, Master of the Purple Radiance Grotto*

**(Discourse on Ninefold Heart Refinement)**

**First Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the unpurified heart. The unpurified heart teems with delusive thoughts and wandering musings. Delusive thoughts arise from craving and desire; wandering musings spring from unawareness. When practitioners sit in meditation, though they may wish to banish worldly concerns, no sooner are delusive thoughts dispelled than wandering musings arise anew. The method lies in *zhi* (cessation) and *guan* (contemplation), through which they may gradually dissolve and refine. *Zhi* means to cease at the space behind the navel’s hall and before the Gate of Life, slightly below its center, where there is a circle of emptiness and void. The heart ceases there and inwardly contemplates, its illumination shining within the void, guarding the qi. It is bound within the compass and square, moving between the circle and the square, each breath returning to the root, uniting with the natural workings of creation. Immovable as a mountain, it establishes the primordial foundation of purity and stillness. From this, a single thread of heart-light connects with a wisp of true qi, merging in profound vastness, tranquil and at ease—this is the initial work of refining the heart and nurturing qi.

**Second Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the heart in samādhi. Previously, the single thread of heart-light connected with the wisp of true qi; if it could penetrate directly into profound obscurity, it would naturally emerge through the Mysterious Aperture. Yet the heart in samādhi is not steadfast, often swayed by the *shishen* (discriminating spirit), causing heart and qi to separate, and the original face remains unseen. The method lies in the moment when heart and breath interdepend: extinguish all knowing and awareness. The heart abides within qi yet knows it not; qi envelops the heart yet is not perceived. In a state of misty fusion, they become one—this is the work of refining the heart to unite with qi.

**Third Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the returning heart. Previously, in misty fusion, they became one; beneath the heavy yin, a single yang returns—this is called the heart of Heaven and Earth, the Mysterious Pass. At this moment, essence, qi, and spirit all reside in the primordial, the initial separation of the primordial chaos, undivided into true essence, true qi, and true spirit—yet this *is* true essence, true qi, and true spirit. If the heart can remain unmoving, one may immediately gather and circulate them. Yet, encountering sights unseen and sounds unheard, with wondrous scenes before one, one is at a loss; the heart stirs and falls into the posterior heaven, and essence, qi, and spirit separate. The method lies in the moment the Mysterious Pass first appears: immediately tread the fire-clouds, proceed to the *weilü* (coccyx), steady the heart, soften the breath, strike the iron drum to pass the Three Barriers, and rest upon Kunlun—this is the work of refining the heart to advance qi.

**Fourth Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the withdrawing and storing heart. Previously, treading the fire-clouds and passing the Three Barriers, heart and qi followed and entered the *nīwán* (Mud Pill Palace). Yet within the *nīwán* Palace, if the *shishen* stirs, the qi chills and congeals, failing to transform into true water, sprinkle, and irrigate the Three Palaces—all prior efforts are lost. The method lies atop Kunlun’s summit: still the heart and abide in quiescence, merging with qi. The qi then transforms into sweet nectar, descending from the upper palate. Curl the tongue to receive the dew, swallow and guide it, pouring the heart into the *jiàngōng* (Crimson Palace), the *huángtíng* (Yellow Court), and the *yuánhǎi* (Primordial Sea), the sound of its passage resonating all the way down. Wait again for the appearance of the Mysterious Pass—this is the work of refining the heart to obtain qi.

**Fifth Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the heart for foundation-laying. Previously, entering the *nīwán* and returning to the qi cavern, the path of the River Chariot is established. From this, proceed with single-minded devotion, day and night without rest—how can the foundation fail to form within a hundred days? Yet if there is laxity or desire, with starts and stops, the elixir foundation will not solidify. Foundation-laying gathers essence and unites spirit; if effort is not diligent, essence and spirit remain scattered—how can one prolong life and serve the Dao? The method lies in following the *zǐ-wǔ* (midnight-noon) cycle, daily drawing and adding, extracting from *kǎn* (Water) to fill *lí* (Fire), accumulating gold to solidify the abdomen—this is the work of refining the heart to accumulate qi.

**Sixth Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the heart to complete nature. Previously, with the River Chariot turning and essence gathered to unite spirit, the numinous root is replete. From this, heart-fluid descends and kidney-qi ascends—this is the union of *kǎn* and *lí*. In profound obscurity, there is a sign, vast as the tide: half is water-qi, misty as fog; half is cloud-qi—this is the initial stirring of metal and water, the stage of refining the Jade Fluid Returning Elixir. If the heart is not single-minded, the task of completing nature remains unfinished. The method lies in the moment when metal and water first arise: from the cinnabar field, they divide and descend to the *yǒngquán* (Bubbling Spring), instantly converging at the *weilü*. Harmonize the true breath, stir and dance it, so it surges upward to the Heavenly Valley; trickling down, it falls into the Yellow Court. Thus, irrigating daily, the heart-ground becomes cool and clear. Blood transforms into cream, intent congeals into earth, and within earth, mercury is born. Mercury’s nature is round and luminous, unchanging in encounter with things—like a spirit-sword in hand. Mencius said, “He who exhausts his heart knows his nature.” Daoists call this the Yin Elixir or Inner Elixir—this is the work of refining the heart to illuminate nature.

**Seventh Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the already-illumined nature. Previously, the River Chariot of metal and water was called “inner refinement” by the immortals. At this stage, there is still the work of outer refinement. By uniting outer with inner, the true heart gathers and does not scatter. Though the inner body is illuminated, mercury’s nature is prone to flight; though inner refinement is achieved, the Yin Elixir is easily corrupted. If preservation is not pure, the heart-nature is extinguished once more. The method lies in blending the emptily luminous heart and the wondrously existent nature with cinnabar and earth, planting them in that domain.

That domain is empty, yet I fill it with substance; that domain is nonexistent, yet I bring it into being. Casting the existent into the nonexistent, infusing the substantial into the void. The dead heart remains unmoved, and in an instant, the primordial qi emerges from the formless. The first period manifests as one Yang, akin to *Zhen*. The second period manifests as two Yangs, akin to *Dui*. At the moment of the second period, it is precisely the time to conjoin the Elixir. From that side issues a thread of true qi—its metaphor is the tiger born from water. From this side descends a speck of mysterious radiance—its metaphor is the dragon emerging from fire. The two, dragon and tiger, meet in union; nature and destiny intermingle in mutual resonance. A great battle ensues, as though heaven and earth were plunged into darkness, and both body and heart become utterly still.

Suddenly, the three Yangs stir into motion, akin to *Qian*. Like tides, like fire, like mist, like smoke, like thunder, like lightning, like snow, like blossoms—within the body, the Yang lead shines brilliantly. I then wield the sword, grasp the seal, tread the *gang*, pace the *dou*, rousing the primordial harmony. With fierce firing and extreme refinement, it pierces the Three Passes and ascends to the *Niwan*, every pore of the body opening—unlike the earlier Jade Liquid River Chariot. Swallowing and ingesting it, the primordial governs the acquired; nature and destiny merge into one—this is the Great Return. Nature pertains to fire, its number seven. Destiny pertains to metal, its number nine. Returning to the origin and reverting to the source, thus is revealed the seven-return, nine-reversion, and the Golden Liquid Great Elixir. Henceforth, lead comes to restrain mercury, the heart remains ever luminous, and mercury ceases to waver. This is the work of refining the heart to preserve spirit.

**Eighth Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the already-subdued heart to enable communion with spirit. Previously, the seven-return, nine-reversion, with lead restraining mercury, has stilled the heart. Yet it must be warmed and nurtured, so that the qi within the body wholly transforms into spirit, and the spirit within the body can roam beyond. Thus, taking the twelve months of the year, excluding the *mao* and *you* months for bathing, the remaining ten months are for advancing and retreating—hence the term "ten-month nurturing," not implying ten months of effort. Otherwise, though the heart is stilled, it remains inert. Refining and forging it, the numinous heart becomes daily more apparent. Numinous, it moves; moving, it transforms; transforming, it transfigures—thus arises the phenomenon of spirit departing, no longer deluded by worldly sentiments. This is the work of refining the heart into spirit.

**Ninth Stage of Heart Refinement** refines the already-numinous heart to return it to emptiness. Previously, the work of nurturing ran deep, and the spirit, having emerged, is no longer confused. Following the heart’s desire, all is fitting; treading upon clouds and mist, traversing the isles of the sea—such is the joy attained. Yet if the numinous heart is not empty, it cannot embrace the ten thousand existences. Thus arises the necessity of refining emptiness. Refining emptiness, the heart’s vastness expands, and the myriad existences all become nonexistent. A single qi of pure emptiness spirals between heaven and earth. It is I, yet not I; it is emptiness, yet not emptiness. Though the world may perish, only emptiness remains indestructible. Though the cosmos may obstruct, only emptiness knows no hindrance—thus the spirit fills the void, and the Dharma pervades the realms of the sands. This is the beginning and end of heart refinement; nothing surpasses it.

The immortals take Laozi as their patriarch. In his dealings with the world, Laozi never contended with others. Outwardly, he relinquished glory and splendor; inwardly, he cultivated longevity. Serene, harmonious, settled, and still—non-action in accord with nature. Yet in non-action, all the world’s heterodox arts, restless clamor, bizarre and strange conduct, cunning schemes, and tumultuous deceits are not of Laozi’s teaching. To say "natural" is to act and move in accord with feeling and reason, flowing with the transformations of creation. Abiding in the center without bounds, contemplating emptiness without form. Attaining emptiness and preserving stillness, deeply concealed yet seemingly vacant. Neither flaunting the extraordinary nor establishing the peculiar, thus the histories call him an ancient reclusive gentleman. The *Daodejing*’s five thousand words draw upon ancient sayings to establish its discourse. It may be applied to governing the world and cultivating the self. Though we, in our daily reading of the *Daodejing*, may not grasp its profound meaning, in our hearts and conduct, we secretly aspire to emulate it. In the depths of mountains and secluded valleys, two or three like-minded companions keep company, wandering amidst mist and clouds, staff in hand, singing of the sagely transformation—unaware, unknowing, following the patterns of the Emperor on High, using this as a means to conceal our inadequacies.

One day, while visiting a Daoist abbey, I came upon several volumes of the *Yuanqiao Waishi*, which expounds the learning of extending life without straying from Laozi’s Dao. Among them was the *Thirteen Classics with Annotations*, all works of Laozi. The commentary on the *Daodejing* was the most refined and detailed. Appended at the end was the *Nine Layers of the Path*, even more accessible and plain. I inquired of a Daoist priest, "Who is the Master of the Tree’s Shade?" He replied, "A recluse among farmers." Thus, I sought the master at Juanshan, and he deemed me a kindred spirit to [Xu] You and [Ni] Qian. He imparted to me the true mechanism. Upon returning, I had the *Thirteen Classics* printed and placed in bookshops to manifest the master’s compassionate intent. Additionally, copies of the *Daodejing* and *Nine Layers* were printed and distributed to Daoist and Buddhist temples.

This book is concise in length yet complete in true instructions. For our small circle, it is convenient to practice and study, fulfilling ourselves and others—does not its potential lie herein? Reflecting on the master’s words about accepting disciples, each of us, following the Daoist lineage, established names for ourselves beyond the worldly realm, like streams and rivulets flowing into the great river. Zixia received teachings from Huiweng, and we, as his successors, share a deep connection. Alas! Among the affairs of heaven and earth, those like us few fools neither need nor are able to contend.

At seventy, Daoyu’s years have grown long; Daosheng, too, nears the threshold of old age; and Daohe is no longer young. What joy can there be within these mountain forests? Only that we bear this single commentary on the *Daodejing* and this single chapter of the *Nine Layers*, singing long songs among cliffs and valleys, our voices echoing through the clouds over the streams—each forgetting our surnames and ages.

In the month of *Nánlǚ* during the *Dūnyīn* year of the Daoguang reign, inscribed by the Three Recluses of Mount Shu.

**Preface by Master Pure Yang**

The *Daodejing* of Lord Lao teaches "non-action as action." The *Zhengyi* commentary elucidates "non-doing as doing." The Dao Ancestor transmitted it; his descendants expounded it, making the Great Dao ever clearer. Then there is one called Hanxuzi, a transcendent talent: golden script entered his dreams, and he was born into a family beneath the trees. His lineage, dwelling in Juanshan, has long transmitted purity and sincerity. That he was endowed with a jade scepter was only fitting.

From childhood, he possessed extraordinary insight; in maturity, he cultivated the mysterious. Once he read the *Fanghu Waishi*, he secretly aspired to ascend to truth and enter transformation, standing beside Master Lu at my side. Hearing of him, I sought him out, assuming the guise of a mountain Daoist. I brought Qianyu with me to observe his resolve. He dwelt in seclusion on the eastern slopes of Mount E, serene and taciturn, indifferent to glory and profit, often contenting himself with a single qin to express his will.

After we met, we kept company for years. I often spoke to him of the profound mystery, and he grasped it instantly. Thereupon, he exerted his talent and composed the *Yuanqiao Waishi*, a counterpart to Master Lu’s work. Thus, where Lu has the *Xuanfu Lun*, he has the *Daoqiao Tan*; where Lu has the *Jiuzheng Pian*, he has the *Xuntu Shuo*—all works of pure truth. Yet his most wondrous achievement is his commentary on the *Daodejing*, which serves as an eternal lamp for all ages. He titled it *Donglai Zhengyi*, standing equal to Master Lu’s *Nanhua Fumo*. These two masters may truly be called those who excel in discoursing on Laozi and Zhuangzi.

In annotating this scripture, there are five faults and three virtues. The five faults are: leaning toward governance of the world, the first; leaning toward cultivation of the self, the second; or knowing the Dao encompasses both self and world yet failing to align one’s words, ultimately straying into side paths, the third; not recognizing that the supreme Dao’s practice requires first pacifying the people and enriching the state to achieve non-action—if one begins by discussing stillness, it will inevitably drift into emptiness, the fourth; not recognizing that the supreme power of virtue demands returning to the root and restoring life to be deemed "having action"—if one begins by discussing sagehood and divinity, it will inevitably resemble mere hymns of praise, the fifth.

What are the three virtues? Harmonizing inner and outer, the commentary distinguishes primary and secondary, further revealing the scripture’s vast meaning—this is the first virtue. Connecting the lineage’s sprouts and veins, filling gaps and ensuring coherence, so the scripture’s meaning flows unimpeded—this is the second virtue. With true realization in the heart and no Zen obstructions in speech, enabling the scripture’s meaning to shine forth—this is the third virtue.

I have long lamented that the *Daodejing*’s essence remains obscure in the world, wishing to take up my brush and annotate it. Yet to this day, I remain unsatisfied. Now, seeing Hanxu’s work, which possesses the three virtues and avoids the five faults, had it appeared a thousand years earlier, other commentators would have had no need to annotate it, and Daoists would not have had to speak further.

Thus, I joyfully undertake its evaluation and annotation, storing it in the famous mountains and transmitting it to scholars of aspiration. The purpose of this evaluation is also to borrow the true meaning of this commentary to supplement what I have not annotated, revealing it through these annotations.

**Poem on the *Donglai Zhengyi***

Master Huiweng’s preface sets the critique,
His own preface further opens the gate to virtue.
Again, we see purple qi arriving from Guanzhong,
Directly beholding the flourishing descendants of Li’s lineage.
Wishing to guide later generations to share the same path,
Able to make the primordial Dao ever more revered.
How many commentators lack such a work?
The treasure case is long guarded, enshrined on Kunlun.

—Sealed by the Jade Pivot Right Minister, Censor of Cultivation and Transgressions in the Great Clarity Grotto Mystery, and Selector of Immortal Affairs, the Mysterious Mystic Daoist of Liaodong, Your Friend Zhang Quanyi, respectfully inscribed at Qingfu.

**Another Poem**

One commentary can unlock the Dao’s profundities,
Once more, purple qi arrives from the east.
A true scholar, plucking the qin and playing the flute,
A great debater, expounding the scriptures and discoursing on the Dao.
I dwell in Fanghu, gazing upon the azure sea,
You ascend Yuanqiao, pressing down Penglai.
Today, seated together by the river pavilion,
Reciting the *Nanhua*, we laugh at our own folly.

—Sealed by the Grand Censor of the Three Clarities’ True Scriptures, Keeper of the Grotto Heavens’ Secret Treasures, Master of Civilized Universal Salvation, Ancestral Master of the Eastern Lineage, Fellow of the Immortal Historians’ Hall, Your Foolish Younger Brother Lu Xixing, respectfully inscribed.

**Another Inscription**

The Dao resides in the body though the self dwells in clouded mountains,
Profound and potent, the spirit’s qi is most majestic.
Writing books beyond the isles of Qingdao and Yanchi,
Quelling the flames of the cinnabar field in tranquil stillness.
People call him the Great Abbot,
I playfully dub him the Old Man of the Great Wall.
Relying on this commentary to fence the *Daodejing*,
All scriptures’ words resonate in harmonious realization.

—Respectfully inscribed by the Heavenly Immortal Bai Qiongguan.

**Commentary on the *Daodejing* (*Dongyan Zhengyi*)**
Purified and bathed in reverence, the Master of Purple Radiance Grotto of Yuanqiao Mountain, Hanxu Sheng, annotates.
Proofread and published by the Dao-following disciples Zhu Daosheng and Li Daoyu at Yueyang Tower.
**Chapter One**

The Dao that can be spoken is not the eternal Dao;
The name that can be named is not the eternal name.
That which is called the Dao—
inwardly, it governs the self; outwardly, it governs the world,
the Dao of daily use and constant practice.

The Dao’s hidden subtlety cannot be spoken,
yet its manifest signs can be spoken.
Encompassing both manifest and hidden,
it is at once most vast and most minute—
thus the Dao is no ordinary Dao.

The name in the realm of the Limitless cannot be named,
yet the name within the Supreme Ultimate can be named.
Born of the Limitless, the Supreme Ultimate,
it is both still and moving—
thus the name is no ordinary name.

**Supplementary Note:**
What all people follow is called the Dao.
That which can be spoken is what can be described.
The Dao that is not ordinary—this is the Great Dao.

To depict its form is called naming.
That which can be named is what can be likened.
The name that is not ordinary—this has no fixed name.

“Nameless, it is the beginning of Heaven and Earth;
Named, it is the mother of the myriad things.”
Nameless—this is the Limitless.
Named—this is the Supreme Ultimate.
From non-being to being,
truly, there is no fixed name.

In the primal undifferentiated state of the Limitless,
there were no omens, no forms,
no sounds or scents to which it could be likened—
thus the Dao is the beginning of Heaven and Earth.
After the Supreme Ultimate divides,
there is generation and nurturance,
evidence of creation to be observed—
thus the Dao is the mother of the myriad things.
The “myriad things” refers to all under Heaven and Earth.

Before Heaven and Earth existed, this Dao already was—
giving birth to Heaven, to Earth, to humanity, to all things,
as if a great father and mother.
By speaking of the mother, the father is implied.

“Always without desire, observe its mystery;
Always with desire, observe its manifestations.”
(*Manifestations* here meaning *apertures*. The *Yongle Encyclopedia* omits the character *gu* [故]; one edition omits *chang* [常].)

Mystery is the nameless thing—thus it congeals,
observed in constant stillness.
Manifestations are the named thing—thus they move,
observed in constant response.
Without desire, with desire—
constant stillness, constant response.
Observe the nameless through desirelessness,
observe the named through desire.
The Daoist alchemists take the Mysterious Pass as the aperture of being and non-being—
this is their source.

These two emerge from the same source yet bear different names,
together called the Mysterious.
Mysterious within the Mysterious—
the gate of all wonders.

The “two” are the mystery and the aperture.
Being arises from non-being—thus they emerge from the same source.
Non-being transforms into being—thus they bear different names,
yet though different, they remain the same.
The aperture of being and non-being—both are one Mystery.
Observing its mystery through desirelessness, one attains one Mystery.
Observing its aperture through desire, one attains another Mystery.
The two Mysteries return to the one Mystery.
The Mysterious encompasses all wonders—
the gate of all wonders lies herein.

**Chapter Two**
(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the “Nurturing the Self” chapter; Peng’s edition calls it the “Excellence” chapter.)

When all under Heaven know beauty as beauty, ugliness arises;
When all know goodness as goodness, un-goodness arises.
Thus being and non-being give rise to each other,
Difficulty and ease complete each other,
Long and short shape each other,
High and low incline toward each other (high as above, inclining as reversal),
Sound and echo harmonize,
Front and back follow one another (the character *xing* [形] in the phrase is rendered as *jiao* [较] in Wang Bi’s commentary).

*Yi* [已] means cessation, or departure (*Guangyun*).
Beauty and ugliness are most opposed.
Knowing beauty as beauty, ugliness must cease.
Goodness and un-goodness are most distant.
Knowing goodness as goodness, un-goodness must depart.

Our innate celestial truth is all beauty and goodness.
Only after being stained by the desires of the posterior world
do ugliness and un-goodness arise.
Yet one cannot but rid oneself of human desire
and seek the celestial truth.

Only by taking emptiness and numinosity as essence,
transformation and movement as function,
can being arise from non-being, non-being from being;
first difficulty, then ease;
long shapes short, short shapes long;
high and low reverse;
like seeks like, as sound answers sound;
the child races behind,
then the noon sun descends before.

This is the Dao of governing the self.

Thus the sage engages in “non-action,”
enacts the teaching of “non-words.”
The myriad things arise yet he does not refuse them,
gives life yet does not possess,
acts yet does not rely,
accomplishes yet does not dwell.
Only by not dwelling does he not depart.

This continues the above discussion of governing the self.
The sage’s governance of the self is the work of non-action.
The sage’s teaching of the self is the teaching of non-words.
Engaging in it to seek his will,
acting to attain his heart’s intent.
The myriad things arise in multitude, looking to him,
awaiting the sage’s ordering—
yet the sage does not refuse.

How could he merely not refuse?
He has the merit of giving life to the people,
yet the sage does not consider it his possession.
He has the merit of governing,
yet the sage does not rely on it.
When great works are accomplished,
he retires to rest—
like the Yellow Emperor visiting Dao on Mount Kongtong,
like Emperor Shun wearying of toil and ascending his throne.
Later generations’ heroes and worthies,
accomplishing great deeds and boldly retiring—
all do not dwell.

Not dwelling means not clinging.
Only by not clinging to merit
does one again seek the Dao of governing the self.
Guarding the self without departure,
one becomes a perfected person.
Governing the self can govern the world;
perfecting oneself can perfect others—
thus it is.

Who says Laozi’s Dao entirely upholds quiescence and extinction?

**Chapter Three**
(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the “Pacifying the People” chapter; Peng’s edition calls it the “Non-Action” chapter.)

Not exalting worthies prevents the people from contending;
not prizing rare goods prevents the people from stealing;
not displaying what may be desired keeps the heart from disorder.
Thus the sage’s governance:
empties their minds,
fills their bellies,
weakens their ambitions,
strengthens their bones.
Ever keeping the people without knowledge, without desire—
so that those with knowledge dare not act.
Acting through non-action,
then nothing remains ungoverned.

The closing passage of the previous chapter spoke of the sage governing the world, accomplishing merit yet not dwelling in it, returning to seek the Dao of governing the self. Yet even in the sage’s governance of the world, this Way of governing operates not by exalting worthiness and ability to compete with the people—thus the mind for fame is already dimmed, and the people do not contend. Not prizing goods and wealth to vie with the people—thus the mind for profit is already severed, and the people do not steal. Not displaying what may be desired and desiring it—thus the mind for desire is already eliminated. The people’s hearts, being thus, are not disordered; the sage’s heart, being thus, is also not disordered. The excellence of governing the world all stems from the excellence of governing the self. Thus the sage’s governance of the self, though non-action, leaves nothing ungoverned.

Fame, profit, and desire all absent,
one guards the center to empty the mind.
Fame, profit, and desire all purified,
one nurtures qi to fill the belly.
Moreover, the will and qi harmonious and gentle,
weakness is employed;
the bones’ texture firm and unyielding,
strength is embodied.
Thus one’s self remains tranquil and indifferent,
at peace with the world in non-affair.
Therefore the people, too, are without knowledge, without desire,
embracing their pristine simplicity—
so that those with wisdom in the world cannot act,
nor dare to act.
Such is the sage’s governance through non-action.

**Chapter Four**
(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the “No Source” chapter.)

The Dao is empty, yet its use is never exhausted.
Profound indeed, it seems the ancestor of the myriad things.
It blunts their sharpness, unties their tangles,
softens their glare, merges with their dust.
Deep and still, it seems ever present.
I know not whose child it is—
it appears before the image of the Lord.

The sage’s Way of non-action lies entirely in emptiness and its application.
*Chong* means emptiness.
The Dao employs emptiness as its function;
its capacity embraces heaven and earth, the state and the family,
yet never appears full—
abysmally it resembles the primal ancestor of the myriad things.
It blunts the world’s sharpness without harm,
untangles the world’s knots without labor,
for its emptiness dissolves sharpness and entanglement.
It harmonizes the world’s brilliance without obscuring,
merges with the world’s dust without defilement,
for its emptiness forgets brilliance and dust.
Only its empty essence is perceived,
constantly abiding in its tranquil nature—
seeming to exist, yet truly without existence.
Ask whose child it is, and none can say;
its name appears before the image of Heaven’s Lord.
This is what is called the beginning without beginning,
the first of the first, the heaven before Heaven.

**Chapter Five**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the “Emptiness in Use” chapter; Peng’s version titles it the “Guarding the Center” chapter.)*

Heaven and earth are not benevolent—
they treat the myriad things as straw dogs.
Heaven and earth have no intention of benevolence;
they merely meet the needs of the myriad things.
And the myriad things each fulfill their own needs,
each serve their own use.
This non-benevolence is in truth the utmost benevolence.
If they waited to be benevolent before nurturing life,
then every single thing would require heaven and earth to bestow favor in order to live—
this would inevitably lead to insufficiency.
Only by not providing beasts with their straw
do beasts eat straw on their own.
Only by not feeding dogs for men
do men feed dogs on their own.
Thus the measure of benevolence becomes even more vast.
Straw dogs are mentioned,
yet fruits, vegetables, fowl, and swine can be inferred.

The sage is not benevolent—
he treats the hundred clans as straw dogs.
The sage, in harmony with heaven and earth,
also allows the hundred clans to each fulfill their own nurturance,
each nurture themselves—
this is what is called “accomplishing through non-action.”

Between heaven and earth,
is it not like a bellows?
Empty yet inexhaustible,
the more it moves, the more it yields.
Much speech leads to exhaustion—
better to guard the center.

Heaven and earth, the sage,
do not create sustenance for the myriad things or the hundred clans,
yet the myriad things and the hundred clans naturally obtain their sustenance.
This is due to the body of emptiness at the center,
its capacity for universal nurturance.
Heaven and earth are divided into two realms,
and between these two realms lies a vast emptiness—
like a bellows without a bottom, a flute with open passages.
Qi flows back and forth, acting without contrivance,
thus emptiness can contain all things without exhaustion.
Its center holds all, moving silently,
ever expanding its full capacity—
inexhaustible within.
Heaven and earth do not speak,
nor does the sage.
If they spoke excessively,
words would have limits,
and limits could be exhausted.
Thus it is better to guard the center alone.

**Chapter Six**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the “Forming Images” chapter; Peng’s version titles it the “Valley Spirit” chapter.)*

The valley spirit does not die—
this is called the mysterious female.
The gate of the mysterious female
is called the root of heaven and earth.
Subtly continuous, it seems to exist;
its use is never exhausted.

The sage guards the center to govern the self,
for the center has the power to nourish the valley spirit.
The valley spirit is the original nature.
*Gu* (valley) suggests emptiness;
*shen* (spirit) suggests numinosity.
When the nature’s body is empty and numinous, it is not obscured.
Not obscured means it does not die.
Yet why is the valley spirit also called the mysterious female?
*Xuan* (mysterious) refers to heaven;
*pin* (female) refers to earth.
When heaven and earth unite, the mysterious female is formed—
within it lies a vast emptiness.
Confucians call it the subtle and hidden,
a realm of neither seeing nor hearing.
Buddhists name it *that*—
a truth beyond good and evil.
The sage governs the self
by borrowing the empty mysterious female
to nurture the empty and numinous valley spirit.
Thus the valley spirit’s name is given to the mysterious female—
this is naming according to function.
One *xuan*, one *pin*—
one *qian*, one *kun*.
Confucius said: “Are *qian* and *kun* not the gates of the *Yi*?”
The *Cantong Qi* states: “*Qian* and *kun* are the gates of the *Yi*.”
The so-called “two apertures method,”
where metal and qi mutually assist,
is precisely this gate of the mysterious female.
Yin and yang come and go within it;
*kan* and *li* unite within it.
It is the chamber where male and female unite,
the place where the sun and moon exchange light.
The sage inverts it to make it the gate of life;
ordinary people use it in sequence, making it the door of death.
The union of earth and heaven is no other than this.
Thus it is also called the root of heaven and earth—
the root where heaven and earth mutually reside.
The root of heaven and earth
is the place of returning to the origin and reverting to the primordial,
the realm of refining qi and transforming spirit.
“Subtly continuous, it seems to exist”—
this is the cultivation of the valley spirit,
natural fetal breathing.
“Its use is never exhausted”—
this is the external furnace’s regulation,
natural alignment with the fire.
“Not exhausted” means not labored.

**Chapter Seven**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the “Concealing Brilliance” chapter; Peng’s version titles it the “Selflessness” chapter.)*

Heaven is eternal, earth enduring.
The reason heaven and earth can be eternal and enduring
is that they do not live for themselves—
thus they can live long.
Therefore the sage places himself last and finds himself first,
sets himself aside and finds himself preserved.
Is this not because he is without self-interest?
Thus he can achieve his self-interest.

Heaven is eternal, earth enduring—
this is the Way of long life.
Yet the reason heaven and earth can be eternal and enduring
is that they are still when closing, active when opening,
still when gathering, active when dispersing,
generating life abundantly,
covering and bearing all without self-interest—
only then do they attain this long life.
If heaven and earth were self-interested in their life,
the lives of things would not flourish,
and heaven and earth would harm their harmony.
The natures of things would not be upright,
and heaven and earth would damage their centers.
The harm to the myriad things
is the harm to heaven and earth.
Only by not being self-interested in their life,
but taking the lives of all beings as their own life.

The ceaseless generation of all beings *is* the long life of heaven and earth—thus they attain long life. The sage emulates heaven and earth. Therefore, in nurturing life, the sage is yielding and weak, seemingly placing the self last to seek life. Yet by gradual filling and fulfillment, in truth he places the self first to obtain life. By guarding the center to control the exterior, he seems to cast life aside as if without life. Yet by first forgetting and later preserving, he preserves his life to possess life. All this arises from not privately possessing the Way. Thus the sage’s tranquility and simplicity restore the people’s nature to purity and goodness. The sage’s longevity brings the people’s lives to fullness and longevity. The great Way is without self-interest—yet in this, the sage appears to possess life for himself. The absence of self-interest becomes his self-interest—this is the ultimate.

**Chapter Eight**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the "Transforming Nature" chapter; Peng’s version titles it the "Supreme Virtue" chapter)*

The supreme virtue is like water.
Water benefits the myriad things without contention,
dwelling in places others disdain—
thus it nearly approaches the Way.
In dwelling, it is good at lowliness;
in heart, it is good at depth;
in association, it is good at kindness;
in speech, it is good at trust;
in governance, it is good at order;
in affairs, it is good at ability;
in action, it is good at timing.
Only by not contending does it avoid blame.

The Way values humility—
by descending, it ascends—
hence it is called "supreme virtue."
This supreme virtue is compared in power to water.
Water’s goodness lies in benefiting the life of all things,
making them all sufficient without contention.
Yet water’s nature flows downward,
often dwelling where others disdain.
Though people may despise it,
ultimately water suffers no harm.
It benefits others without claiming merit,
taking weakness as its aspiration—
thus water nearly approaches the Way.

The goodness of human nature should resemble water’s goodness.
Holding to a humble nature,
all actions become good.
Without a contentious heart,
one chooses dwelling in good places,
conceals the heart in good depth,
associates with good kindness,
delights in good trust,
governs with good order,
handles affairs with good ability,
and acts in good timing.
In every place and circumstance,
one is yielding, good, and non-contending.
Only by not contending does one avoid resentment and blame.
Compared to water, which others may still despise,
humans can descend yet ascend—
thus they surpass the Way in subtlety,
transcending even water in spirit.

**Chapter Nine**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the "Moving the Unyielding" chapter; Peng’s version titles it the "Holding Fullness" chapter)*

To hold and fill it—
know when to stop.
To shape and sharpen it—
it cannot be long preserved.
When gold and jade fill the hall,
none can guard them.
When wealth and nobility breed arrogance,
one invites one’s own misfortune.

*To hold*: to obtain.
*To shape*: to probe.
Once one has obtained qi,
to harbor the desire for fullness
is the beginning of loss—
thus it is better to stop.
When one probes one’s treasures
and suddenly harbors a keen, sharp intent,
this is the omen of certain decline—
thus it cannot be long preserved.
If one should stop yet does not,
is it not like gold and jade filling the hall,
which none can guard?
If one should preserve yet does not,
is it not like wealth and nobility breeding arrogance,
inviting one’s own misfortune?

This is the way of humans.
To withdraw when achievements are complete and fame is attained—
this is the Way of heaven.

Moreover, consider the Way of heaven:
Heaven does not speak of achievement or fame,
yet takes the flourishing of generation and growth as its achievement and fame.
When things mature and their time comes,
heaven and earth withdraw and shift.
Concealing themselves in winter’s season,
they nurture the yang life to come.
What does humanity observe in the Way of heaven?

**Chapter Ten**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this the "Ability" chapter; Peng’s version titles it the "Mysterious Virtue" chapter)*

Can you carry your *po* and embrace the One without separation?
Can you concentrate your *qi* to attain softness, like an infant?
Can you cleanse and purify your mysterious mirror without flaw?
Can you love the people and govern the state without action?
Can you open and close the gates of heaven without the feminine?
Can you comprehend the four directions without knowledge?

To generate and nurture,
to generate without possessing,
to act without relying,
to lead without dominating—
this is called mysterious virtue.

*To carry*: as a cart carries a load.
*Nourishing*: as in the *ying* and *wei* [nutritive and defensive qi].
*Wei* pertains to yang, while *ying* pertains to yin.
*Ying-po*: the yin *po* [corporeal soul].
Some say *ying* is *po*—
to take *ying* as *po* is a mistaken interpretation.
One must understand that when speaking of the yin *po*,
the yang *hun* [ethereal soul] is already within it.
On the fifteenth day of the eighth month,
the *hun* fully infuses the moon’s *po*,
and the moon becomes full, purely yang.
The sage, at this moment,
employs the river chariot to carry it,
and the yang metal becomes his own—
as the scripture says, “Obtain the One, and all affairs are complete.”
Having obtained the One,
one will not lose the One.
The sage carries the *po* and returns,
embracing the One to dwell—
thus the earth’s *po* captures the cinnabar mercury.
Therefore, can one not separate from it?

In the tenth month, warm cultivation—
the inner fire is natural.
With warmth constantly present,
the infant embryo grows.
The sage concentrates *qi* to attain softness—
this is the inner fire.
Thus can one be like a nurtured infant.

*Mysterious vision* is inner observation.
To cleanse and purify the mysterious mirror
is to observe inwardly in clarity and stillness.
Inward observation in clarity and stillness
leaves the heart without flaw or burden—
this is what is meant by:
“Observe emptiness as empty,
and emptiness itself is empty.
When the object of emptiness is gone,
even ‘no emptiness’ is gone.
When ‘no emptiness’ is gone,
profound stillness remains.”

To love the people is to pacify them;
to govern the state is to enrich it.
Only when the people are at peace and the state is prosperous
can one practice governance without action.
In cultivating the self,
stabilizing essence is pacifying the people,
and sufficiency of lead is enriching the state.
Refining the self stabilizes essence;
returning the elixir suffices the lead.
Only after refining the self and returning the elixir
can one practice the Way of embracing the One without action—
it is the same.

In cultivating the self,
guarding the feminine is the work.
In regulating spirit and nurturing the embryo,
one cannot but guard the feminine.
Yet when the gate of heaven is broken through
and the yang spirit moves in and out freely,
opening and closing at will—
can one then be without the toil of guarding the feminine?

In cultivating the self,
knowledge is disturbance.
When the sage’s body is perfected,
the spirit of knowledge transforms
into right enlightenment.

So clear and luminous that the four directions are unimpeded—can one then be without knowledge?

Moreover, there is the further work of begetting the child and producing the heir, the exchange of the crucible and the division of the embryo. There is the capacity to accumulate blessings and virtue, to establish merit and aid the world. Yet though one begets, one does not possess that which is begotten—emptiness shatters into dust. Though one acts, one does not rely on that action—compassion is vast and boundless. The heart that protects the state and blesses the people persists for a thousand kalpas, ten thousand kalpas, ever thus, yet does not boast of heavenly dominion. This is truly called the mysterious virtue.

---

**Chapter Eleven**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this “The Chapter of Uselessness”; Peng’s edition titles it “The Chapter of Utility.”)*

Thirty spokes share one hub;
where it is empty, there lies the use of the cart.
Clay is kneaded to make a vessel;
where it is empty, there lies the use of the vessel.
Doors and windows are cut to make a room;
where it is empty, there lies the use of the room.
Thus, what is present serves as advantage,
while what is absent serves as use.

The thirty-six spokes of the wheel mirror the movements of the sun and moon. Yet the hub lies at the very center of the cart, through which all axles pass. Hollow within, with spokes converging without—thus the hub is fundamentally empty, yet therein lies the use of the cart.

*Shan*: water mixed with earth.
*Zhi*: sticky clay, the material of bricks and tiles.
When clay is first shaped, there is no vessel. But once kneaded and fired, it becomes the use of a vessel. A room gains light through doors and windows; without them, it is as if there were no room. Once carved and adorned, it becomes the use of a room. Thus, what is present serves as advantage, while what is absent serves as use. Being arises from non-being—this is generally the case.

---

**Chapter Twelve**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this “The Chapter of Restraining Desire”; Peng’s edition titles it “The Chapter of the Belly.”)*

The five colors blind the eyes;
the five tones deafen the ears;
the five flavors dull the palate;
riding and hunting madden the heart;
rare and precious goods corrupt conduct.

Therefore, the sage attends to the belly, not the eyes—thus he discards the one and embraces the other.

*Shuang*: loss.
*Kuang*: recklessness.
Rarities and treasures are things all men contend for. Thus, carrying valuables invites harm even while traveling. The damage wrought by colors, sounds, flavors, hunting, and goods is such that the sage holds them in contempt. He guards his inner treasure and scorns external things—thus he discards the one and embraces the other.

---

**Chapter Thirteen**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this “The Chapter of Disdaining Shame”; Peng’s edition titles it “The Chapter of Honor and Nobility.”)*

Favor and disgrace are like alarm;
regard great calamity as you would your own body.

Favor is like alarm—thus one must deeply conceal the jade.
Disgrace is like alarm—thus one must firmly establish the root.
This is the image of a mind roused and a will striving.

Regard the body as noble—thus one delights in the Dao and rests in glory.
Regard calamity as the body—thus one harmonizes light and silences slander.
This is the method of embracing the origin and guarding the true.

What is meant by “favor and disgrace are like alarm”?
Favor is base—gaining it is like alarm, losing it is like alarm. This is called “favor and disgrace are like alarm.”

“Favor is base” means that glory is ever shifting, always subject to decline—thus gain and loss cannot be relied upon. Gaining it is like alarm, losing it is like alarm: whether gaining or losing, one remains vigilant and strives without cease. This is what is meant by “favor and disgrace are like alarm.”

What is meant by “regard great calamity as you would your own body”?
The reason I have great calamity is that I have a body. Once I have no body, what calamity could I have?

Thus, one who values the body as the world can be entrusted with the world;
one who cherishes the body as the world can be entrusted with the world.
This passage expounds on valuing calamity, first clarifying that having calamity must lead to being without calamity.

Yet the cause of great calamity lies in the present form of the body—thus calamity may befall it. But once the shell is shed and the spirit preserved, calamity cannot touch it. Or if one remains in the world while concealing true qi within, veiling one’s light and obscuring one’s traces—what great calamity could disturb such a one?

Thus, one who esteems the body and regards it as the world’s entrusted charge, who does not dare to slight this body of a thousand gold pieces, can be entrusted with the world. This is the lesson Huang Shigong imparted to Zhang Liang. One who preserves the body and regards it as the world’s reliance, who does not reveal his rare and transcendent talents, can be entrusted with the world. This is the admonition Zhang Jiuling gave to the Earl of Ye.

He who excels at preserving the body excels at governing the self.
He who excels at governing the self excels at governing the world.
Confucius said: “The hibernation of the dragon and snake is for the sake of preserving the body. Making use of things to secure the body is for the sake of exalting virtue.” The noble man conceals his talents within, awaiting the proper time to act—what disadvantage could there be?

---

**Chapter Fourteen**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this “The Chapter of Praising the Mysterious.”)*

The invisible is called "even"; the inaudible is called "rare"; the intangible is called "subtle." These three cannot be exhaustively questioned, so they merge into one. Above, it is not bright; below, it is not dark. Vast and boundless, it cannot be named—returning to nothingness, this is called the form without form, the image without image, the state of vagueness. Meeting it, one does not see its beginning; following it, one does not see its end. Holding to the ancient Dao, one governs the present being. To know the ancient origin is called the discipline of the Dao.

The realm of the unseen and unheard is the Gate of Rarity and Evenness. The Gate of Rarity and Evenness is where nature and emotions reside. Evenness conceals nature; rarity conceals emotion. Thus, it is unseen and unheard. Yet the true intent moves between them, intangible when grasped—hence named "subtle." These three cannot be divided or exhaustively questioned, so they should be blended into one, allowing the three to meet. Above and below correspond to "closing above and closing below." Neither bright nor dark, it is as if existing yet not existing. "Vast and boundless" means caution and reverence, like closing above and below, existing yet not existing. Cautious of what one does not perceive, its beginning and end cannot be named. Vague and indistinct, within it there is something—returning to nothingness. This is what is called the form without form, the image without image. Without form or image, this is the state of vagueness. The truth of vagueness has no beginning or end—is this not the primordial qi? The ancient Dao is the original substance. The present being is its current function. Though ancient and present differ, one may hold to the ancient to govern the present, from non-being to being. To know what precedes the primordial origin and extend it to what follows is called the discipline of the Dao. Recording years, months, days, hours, even a single tally or moment—all are disciplines of the Dao.

---

**Chapter Fifteen**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this “The Chapter of Manifesting Virtue”; Peng’s version titles it “The Chapter of Preserving Fullness.”)*

The ancients who excelled as scholars were subtle, wondrous, and profoundly penetrating—too deep to be known. Precisely because they could not be known, one can only forcefully describe them: cautious as if crossing a river in winter, vigilant as if fearing neighbors on all sides, dignified as if a guest, yielding as if ice about to melt, simple as if uncarved wood, open as if a valley, merged as if turbid.

Who can, within turbidity, wait for clarity through stillness? Who can, in tranquility, await life through prolonged movement? Those who preserve this Dao do not seek fullness. Only by not being full can one remain worn yet not newly made.

From ancient cultivators, knowing this subtle and profoundly penetrating Way of governing the self—so deep it cannot be fathomed—they dared not recklessly seek to understand it. Even when describing it, they could only forcefully do so. The seven "as if" phrases in this chapter are all descriptive words. What they describe are scenes and images. Who can, within heavy turbidity, patiently await its light clarity? Who can, with tranquil and simple spirit, long await its gradual arising? Waiting and awaiting, one dares not seek excess. Those who preserve this Dao do not desire fullness—this is not seeking excess. Only by not seeking excess can one uphold constancy. Not creating anew avoids contradicting the true Dao.

Wei Boyang said: *"At the furnace, fix the weights precisely; five parts water are in excess. Taking these two as true, gold outweighs its original state. The third does not enter, and fire accompanies both."* This is the Way of not seeking excess and upholding constancy. Could Cloud Teeth Master’s *Triple Concordance* also embody the *Daodejing* of the Supreme One without creating anew?

---

**Chapter Sixteen**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this “The Chapter of Returning to the Root”; Peng’s version titles it “The Chapter of Emptiness and Stillness.”)*

Attain utmost emptiness; guard deep stillness. The myriad things arise together, and I observe their return. All things flourish abundantly, each returning to its root. Returning to the root is called stillness; stillness is called returning to destiny. Returning to destiny is called constancy; knowing constancy is called illumination. Not knowing constancy leads to reckless action and misfortune. Knowing constancy brings acceptance; acceptance brings impartiality; impartiality brings kingship; kingship brings heaven; heaven brings the Dao; the Dao brings enduring. To the end of one’s days, one will not be imperiled.

"Attain" means to place. "Emptiness" is an empty vessel. "Utmost" means fulfilling its Way. "Guard" means to reside. "Stillness" is non-action. "Deep" means careful and unwavering. "The myriad things arise together" means all things have a beginning. "I" is the master of all things. "Observe" means to await. "Return" means to revert.

The cultivator places the primordial spirit within the empty vessel, thus obtaining its Way. Having obtained the Way, one should reside in a quiet, inactive place, carefully and unwaveringly preserving it. Awaiting the empty vessel’s generation of things, one then awaits their return to the root. Thus, going forth and returning, life transforms and spirit becomes luminous. Knowing this, the Way of seven returns is complete.

The myriad things flourish abundantly and arise together, moving from non-action to action, from action back to non-action, still able to follow the mechanism of transformation in their comings and goings. Thus, spring’s birth, summer’s flourishing, autumn’s gathering, winter’s storing—withering and falling, they return to their root. Things return to their root, moving and then still—hence called stillness. Stillness means returning to nothingness and reverting to creation—hence called returning to destiny. Returning to destiny, one knows the true constancy of the Way. Constancy in stillness can constantly respond. Serene, it becomes profoundly clear—hence knowing constancy is called illumination.

People of the world, not knowing the true constancy of the Way lies in returning to the root and destiny, engage in reckless endeavors, sinking into the forms and names of action, depleting their spirit. Thus, reckless action brings misfortune.

Should one not, then, know constancy? Knowing constancy, one attains capacity. Capacity: to contain the public. Public: greatness. Kingly: nobility. Heaven: reverence. The Dao: the ultimate of emptiness and nothingness. It is to say, containing one’s origin within stillness, one can thereby magnify creation and enter the realm of the most sagely and spiritual, returning to the truth of utmost emptiness and nothingness. Embodied in emptiness and nothingness, one endures unharmed. "No body": nothingness—with spirit but no form, water and fire cannot harm, metal and stone cannot destroy, tigers and rhinoceroses cannot devour, blades and weapons cannot sever. What peril could there be?

**Chapter Seventeen** *(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this "The Pure Wind Chapter"; Peng’s version calls it "The Supreme Chapter")*

The supreme,
The people below merely know of its existence.
The supreme: the sage. The sage dwells above the multitude, hence called "supreme." Some say "supreme" refers to the highest principle—this too is valid.
The sage engages in non-action;
Practices the teaching without words;
Does not boast of ability, so the people do not contend;
Does not covet profit, so the people do not crave;
Does not indulge desire, so the people are not disordered.
Without contention, craving, or disorder, the supreme possesses these, and the people below possess them as well.
"Know of its existence" refers to the people below.

Next,
They draw near and praise him;
Next,
They fear him;
Next,
They revile him.
When trust is insufficient, there will be distrust.
Thus, he values his words.
When his work is accomplished and affairs are settled, the hundred clans all say, "We are naturally so."

The next level is the successor to the Dao.
The supreme Dao accomplishes through non-action and governs without speech.
The next cannot practice non-action or refrain from speech, so they draw near with affection and praise to inspire transformation.
Yet the next, unable to inspire through affection and praise, must rely on laws and decrees to awe and subdue.
And the next, unable to awe and subdue through laws, must employ cunning and artifice to control.
When the Dao descends to cunning and artifice, the wind has already declined—what remains is not worth observing.

Thus, he returns to the root of trust in the heart.
When the ruler values trust, the people dare not but respond with sincerity.
When the ruler’s trust is sufficient, the people speak with true feeling.
When trust is insufficient, the people will be untrustworthy.
Yet the sage must govern through trust.
Valuing words means speaking with caution.
When the ruler personally practices substantive governance, moving with ease and inspiring trust, the people submit without proclamations—thus, he must be circumspect in his commands.
When his work is accomplished and affairs are settled, the hundred clans, unaware and unknowing, follow the celestial pattern, all saying, "We are naturally so."

*My humble observation:* The meaning of this chapter can be applied to governing the world and to cultivating the self.
The highest virtue is cultivated through purity and stillness; the six senses are all settled, practicing non-action without contrivance.
The next is cultivated through love and reverence, moved to penetrate and connect, practicing non-action yet with contrivance.
Yet the next relies on legal discipline to control.
And the next employs cunning and scrutiny—what is called "artifice," the way of action with contrivance.
None surpasses trust in subtlety. Trust pertains to earth; in the elixir of golden cinnabar, from beginning to end, the wondrous function lies purely in the intent of earth—all is naturally so.
Profound indeed are these words, applicable to governing the world and to cultivating the self.

**Chapter Eighteen** *(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this "The Shallow Customs Chapter"; Peng’s version calls it "The Great Dao Chapter")*

When the great Dao is abandoned, there is benevolence and righteousness.
When wisdom and intelligence arise, there is great deceit.
When the six relations are disharmonious, there is filial piety and compassion.
When the state is benighted and chaotic, there are loyal ministers.

Here the chapter speaks of the Way of governance—its flourishing and decay—yet it also reveals the principle of self-cultivation. Let us consider both aspects.

First, the affairs of non-action give rise to policies of kindness and grace, just as the loss of the primal undifferentiated state gives rise to the work of return and restoration. When clarity and artifice are employed to scrutinize and seek, the feelings of the people become ever more deeply concealed—just as when cunning and mechanism are used to probe and extract, the medicinal substances grow ever more adept at mutual concealment.

Within the court, filial piety and compassion serve to harmonize the disharmony of the six relations, just as entering the chamber to cultivate serenity serves to still the restlessness of the six senses. When the state has loyal ministers, they serve to rescue it from benightedness and chaos—just as when the Mysterious Gate holds true gold, it serves to rescue from decline and exhaustion.

Then it laments the pristine and unadorned governance of the ancient age, and the cultivation of the highest virtue through non-action—how distant its wind has become, how long it has been lost.

**Chapter Nineteen**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this "The Return to Simplicity Chapter"; Peng’s version calls it "The Three Abandonments Chapter")*

Abandon sagacity, discard wisdom, and the people benefit a hundredfold.
Abandon benevolence, discard righteousness, and the people return to filial piety and compassion.
Abandon skill, discard profit, and there are no thieves or robbers.
These three, as mere ornament, are insufficient—thus they are made to belong to something greater.

Manifest plainness, embrace simplicity,
Reduce selfishness, diminish desire.

*Abandon* means "great" or "ultimate."
When ultimate sagacity does not employ wisdom, the wind is wholly honest and unadorned, and the people gain manifold benefit.
When ultimate benevolence does not employ righteousness, customs become wholly harmonious, and the people return to filial piety and compassion.
When ultimate skill does not scheme for profit—those who scheme for profit are all followers of cunning and mechanism—if the ruler is without cunning, the people are without thieves.

Sagacity is insufficient in wisdom, benevolence is insufficient in righteousness, skill is insufficient in profit.
These three—sagacity, benevolence, and skill—are like having substance without ornament.
In their primal, undifferentiated state, the Way is one and the wind is uniform; thus the people are each made to have their proper place, following nothing but their essential reality.

Manifest plainness, embrace simplicity,
Reduce selfishness, diminish desire—
the ornament of the people is also insufficient, yet it is beautiful.

**Chapter Twenty**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this "The Different Customs Chapter"; Peng’s version calls it "The Abandonment of Learning Chapter")*

Abandon learning, and there is no sorrow.
Between "yes" and "ah," how great is the distance?
Between good and evil, what is the difference?
What others fear must not be feared.

To abandon learning is to be complete in the Dao and replete in virtue.
When the Dao and virtue are complete, what sorrow can there be?
When the sage regards the multitude, it is as if "yes" is too direct and "ah" is too flattering.
Good is lovable and evil is detestable, yet one does not know how far apart they truly are.
The sage has no sorrow, and thus no fear.
What others fear is the difficulty of abandoning learning—
how can one fear what others fear and not seek to abandon learning?
Thus it is said: "What must not be feared is fear itself."

Vast indeed, it has no end!
The multitude bustle and rejoice,
as if feasting on the grandest sacrifice,
as if ascending the springtime terrace.
I alone am tranquil, without sign or omen,
like an infant who has not yet learned to smile;
adrift, as if with no place to return.
The multitude all have more than enough,
while I alone seem to have lost everything.
I have the heart of a fool—how muddled!
The common folk are bright and clear,
while I alone am dim and dull.
The common folk are sharp and discerning,
while I alone am dull and obscure.
Vast, as if dark;
still, as if without place to abide.
The multitude all have their uses,
while I alone am stubborn and coarse.
I alone differ from others,
and value feeding on the Mother.

The great Dao of prior Heaven is like the unopened state of primordial chaos—it accomplishes without action, governs without speech.
Thus the multitude all attain the transformation of simplicity and thickness, bustling in mutual peace and joy.
They share in the nourishment of its virtue, as if feasting on the grandest sacrifice.
They wander within its realm, as if ascending the springtime terrace.
Even the customs of Huaxu cannot surpass this.
The sage says: I alone am bland and tranquil, utterly without sign or omen, like an infant who has not yet learned to smile.
*Adrift*—riding the Dao, thus it is called "adrift."
Ascending and descending, circulating within—ceaselessly moving, as if with no place to return.
Thus the multitude all have ground to seek, while I alone, in this state, seem to have left the world and stand apart.
Then the multitude are all wise, while I alone seem foolish—yet this is the great skill that appears clumsy.
Vast, like the darkness of heaven and earth; fleeting, like the boundless coming and going.
From this perspective, the multitude all have their uses, while I alone am dim and dull, drifting like one foolish, stubborn, and coarse.
Others do not share my way, and I too differ from them.
A single grain of yang elixir, called the Mother Qi—I alone seek and feed upon it to attain longevity.
That the multitude do not equal the sage is like the distance between "yes" and "ah," between good and evil.
Is this not why the sage alone proclaims the abandonment of learning?

**Chapter Twenty-One**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this "The Empty Heart Chapter"; Peng’s version calls it "The Vast Virtue Chapter")*

The bearing of vast virtue follows only the Dao.
As for the Dao as a thing—only vague, only indistinct.
Indistinct, vague—within it there are images.
Vague, indistinct—within it there are things.
Dark, profound—within it there is essence.
Its essence is utterly real, within it there is sincerity.
From ancient times to the present, its name does not depart,
through which it surveys the origins of the multitude.
How do I know the origins of the multitude are thus?
By this.

*Vast* means "empty" or "great."
The vessel of the most empty and greatest virtue can contain wondrous things, thus the great Dao enters therein.
As for the Dao as a thing—vague and indistinct, without fixed form.
Speaking of its departure from nature, it is originally without image.
Now indistinct, now vague—what is without image seems to have image.
Now vague, now indistinct—what is without thing has already become thing.
Some say *indistinct, vague* rhymes with *image*.

"Vague and indistinct" rhymes with "thing."
Yet the sage in establishing words inverts the order of characters—this itself embodies the inversion of the Dao’s method.
"Indistinct, vague" is the original image of nature.
"Vague, indistinct" is the thing nature plants: with male below and female above, they unite to form essence—a single thing, yet truly two things conjoined.
Thus, in the upper phrase there is image; in the middle phrase, thing; in the lower phrase, essence.
The structure of the phrases again embodies the Dao’s method.
Essence arises when the fire of nature descends to illuminate and evoke response, thereby revealing the hidden emotion of *Kan*. Yet it is subtle and profoundly unfathomable, hence called "dark, profound."
The essence within this darkness is true essence.
To obtain true essence, one must know true sincerity.
Thus, within it there is first sincerity—vast as the surging tide, fluid as melting ice.
The cultivator, awaiting the first arrival of this sincerity, precisely when it is essence, must then subdue and seize it.
With not a moment’s delay, the golden immortal is within reach.
It is called true gold, the primary meridian, true water, spirit-water, true lead, lead-qi, white tiger, tiger-qi—yet it does not depart from true essence.
Therefore, from ancient times to the present, the name of this true essence cannot be discarded by any scripture.
Thus, by observing the truth of this one thing, one surveys the principles of the myriad things—none but revere this primordial qi.
"Surveying the origins of the multitude" means examining the beginnings of all things.
Hence it is also said: "How do I know the origins of the multitude are thus? By this."

**Supplementary Note:** The fourfold repetition of "within it" in this chapter reveals the single aperture of the mysterious pass—the gateway of the great Dao. To refine lead and obtain the elixir, one need not look beyond this.

---

**Chapter Twenty-Two**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "The Chapter of Benefiting Through Humility"; Peng’s version titles it "The Chapter of Complete Return")*

To yield is to be whole,
to bend is to be straight,
to be hollow is to be filled,
to be worn is to be renewed,
to have little is to gain,
to have much is to be perplexed.
Therefore the sage embraces the One and becomes the model for the world.
Not displaying oneself, one is illuminated.
Not asserting oneself, one is distinguished.
Not boasting, one achieves merit.
Not priding oneself, one endures.
Only by not contending does the world have nothing with which to contend against one.
The saying of the ancients, "To yield is to be whole"—was this empty talk?
Truly, wholeness returns to it.
"To yield is to be whole" means reduction becomes increase.
"To bend is to be straight" means softness overcomes hardness.
"To be hollow is to be filled" means humility receives benefit.
"To be worn is to be renewed" means decay heralds return.
"To have little is to gain" means knowing sufficiency avoids disgrace.
"To have much is to be perplexed" means greed and desire lead to delusion.
These are the words of the ancients before the Most High, speaking of the essential Dao of cultivating the self.
Thus, when the sage governs the world, he must embrace the One as the model for all under heaven.
To embrace the One is to not display oneself, not assert oneself, not boast, not pride oneself.
Wang Bi’s commentary aligns these four phrases with "To yield is to be whole," but this interpretation is misguided, for *illuminated*, *distinguished*, *enduring*, and *achieving merit* are intrinsic explanations of their respective phrases.
Someone asked: "Why does the ancient saying again cite 'To yield is to be whole'?"
I reply: The Most High invokes the ancients’ words on cultivating the self to introduce the principles of the world, hence saying, "Was this empty talk?" If one can reverently guard sincerity in the One, then the world will surely return wholly to this model.

---

**Chapter Twenty-Three**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "The Chapter of Emptiness and Nothingness"; Peng’s version titles it "The Chapter of Spontaneity")*

Rare words arise spontaneously.
Thus, whirlwinds do not last the morning,
nor do torrential rains last the day.
Who brings these about? Heaven and Earth.
If even Heaven and Earth cannot endure long,
how much less can man?
Therefore, those who engage in the Dao:
the Dao-aligned merge with the Dao,
the virtue-aligned merge with virtue,
the lost merge with loss.
Those who merge with the Dao—the Dao delights in obtaining them.
Those who merge with virtue—virtue delights in obtaining them.
Those who merge with loss—loss delights in obtaining them.
When sincerity is insufficient, there will be those who do not trust.
"Rare words" means no sound, and also no action.
For those who enter the Dao, non-action and spontaneity are the foundation.
With non-action, there is supreme tranquility; with spontaneity, there is constant progress.
Otherwise, like whirlwinds and torrential rains—though brought about by Heaven and Earth—they cannot endure long.
How much less can man?
Thus, all who follow the path of the Dao, cultivating virtue and practicing the Way, must be spontaneous to unite with the Dao and virtue.
"Loss" means non-action; acting through non-action naturally attains the affairs of non-action.
The Dao, virtue, and loss all delight in this spontaneity and non-action.
When trust and conduct are insufficient, there must first be those who do not trust spontaneity.

---

**Chapter Twenty-Four**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "The Chapter of Suffering and Grace"; Peng’s version titles it "The Chapter of Tiptoeing and Striding")*

One who tiptoes cannot stand,
one who strides cannot walk.
One who displays himself is not illuminated,
one who asserts himself is not distinguished,
one who boasts achieves no merit,
one who prides himself does not endure.
In the Dao, these are called: "leftover food, redundant conduct."
Some beings detest them, thus those with the Dao do not dwell in them.
"Tiptoeing" means gazing upward.
"Striding" means rushing forward.
Tiptoeing raises the head and cannot stand long.
Striding strains the feet and cannot walk long.
Displaying oneself, asserting oneself, priding oneself, boasting—all belong to those who do not trust spontaneity and ultimately achieve nothing.
In terms of the method of practicing the Dao, this is like eating too much or walking too far—one cannot cultivate.
Compared to a dog, overfeeding causes illness.
