Compared to an ox, overwork causes exhaustion.

Thus it is said: some beings detest them—how much more so would humans? Therefore, those who embody the Dao do not dwell in such states.

**Chapter Twenty-Five** *(Heshang Gong’s version titles this "The Chapter of Primordial Image"; Peng’s version, "The Chapter of Primal Chaos")*

There is a thing, chaotically formed, born before heaven and earth.
Silent, oh! Empty, oh! Standing alone without alteration, circulating without exhaustion—it may be called the Mother of all under heaven.
I do not know its name; I style it "Dao," and by force name it "Great."
Great means passing, passing means far-reaching, far-reaching means returning.
Thus the Dao is great, heaven is great, earth is great, and the king is also great.
Within the realm, there are four greats, and the king occupies one of them.
Humans emulate earth, earth emulates heaven, heaven emulates the Dao, and the Dao emulates spontaneity.

Chaotically formed—unbroken, nameless.
The primordial qi of vast chaos, indistinct and without name.
Nameless, it is that which is born before heaven and earth.
Silent: pure. Empty: void.
Standing alone in the realm of purity and emptiness, unchanging in its true constancy—nothing but this chaotic formation.
One thing circulates, pervading the ten thousand things without peril—thus it may be called the Mother of all under heaven.

Since the dawn of creation, the Ancestor of the Dao has known that before the primordial chaos, there existed this Mother-qi.
Heaven, earth, humans, and the ten thousand things—all are gestated within the womb of this Mother-qi.
When asked its name, not knowing, we first style it with the character *Dao*.
*Dao* combines *shou* (head) and *chuo* (movement)—that which moves and generates before heaven and earth.
Forced to name it, we call it *Great*.
*Great* combines *yi* (one) and *ren* (person)—that which emerges before the multitude of things.
From this, through myriad ages, it has been called the Great Dao.

Great, it goes everywhere—above to Qian, below to Kun, passing and leaving you behind.
Passing, it reaches everywhere—north to Kan, west to Dui, far and yet attaining.
This Dao, when exhausted, must return.
Perhaps emerging from Zhen, aligning with Xun, manifesting in Li, completing in Gen.
Great indeed! The Dao, with heaven, earth, and the king, together form the four greats within the realm.

Without the Dao, heaven would not be known—heaven is great.
Without heaven, earth would not be covered—earth is great.
Without earth, the king would not be borne—the king is also great.
The king occupies one of them, standing foremost among the people.
As lord of humanity, the king does not depart from the human.
Humans stand upon earth, thus emulate earth.
Earth lies beneath heaven, thus emulates heaven.
Heaven lies within the Dao, thus emulates the Dao.
The Dao attains its subtlety in spontaneity, thus emulates spontaneity.

**Chapter Twenty-Six** *(Heshang Gong’s version titles this "The Chapter of Weighty Virtue"; Peng’s version, "The Chapter of Weighty Stillness")*

Weight is the root of lightness; stillness is the ruler of restlessness.
Therefore, the noble person travels all day without departing from the balance of weight and lightness.
Though there be splendid sights, they dwell in serenity, transcending.
How could one who rules ten thousand chariots treat his person lightly before the world?
Lightness loses ministers; restlessness loses the ruler.

Weight is water; lightness is fire.
Fire is born within water—thus weight is the root of lightness.
Stillness is stability; restlessness is wisdom.
Wisdom arises from stability—thus stillness is the ruler of restlessness.

Observe the noble person whose talent and virtue are equally weighty: though traveling all day, they do not depart from the balance of weight and lightness, seeking to apply it with ease.
Though there be splendid sights, they dwell in serenity, transcending, not allowing splendor to disturb their stillness.
Alas! The master of the Crimson Palace, honored as one who commands ten thousand chariots—how could they suddenly treat their person lightly before the world, forgetting all?
They wholly fail to uphold weight to nurture lightness, wholly fail to guard stillness to govern restlessness.
I fear that a single impulse of lightness loses the true water within the worthy—fire then has no root.
Fire is born of water; water is fire’s function—thus it is called "minister."
A single impulse of restlessness loses the true stability within the heart—wisdom then has no ruler.
Wisdom arises from stability; stability is wisdom’s master—thus it is called "ruler."

**Chapter Twenty-Seven** *(Heshang Gong’s version titles this "The Chapter of Skillful Application"; Peng’s version, "The Chapter of Essential Subtlety")*

The skillful traveler leaves no wheel tracks,
the skillful speaker utters no flaw or blame,
the skillful reckoner needs no counting rods,
the skillful closer uses no bolt or bar yet cannot be opened,
the skillful binder ties no cord or knot yet cannot be undone.

Thus the sage is ever skillful in saving people—
therefore no one is abandoned.
Ever skillful in saving things—
therefore nothing is abandoned.
This is called inheriting clarity.

Therefore the good person is the teacher of the ungood,
the ungood person is the resource of the good.
If one does not value one’s teacher,
if one does not cherish one’s resource,
though wise, one is greatly deluded.
This is called essential subtlety.

"Leaves no wheel tracks"—the natural river chariot;
if there were tracks, it would be forced visualization and transference.
"Utters no flaw or blame"—the natural ancestral transmission;
if there were flaws, it would violate the宗旨.
"Needs no counting rods"—the natural fire timing;
using rods would be rigid adherence to hexagrams and strategies.
"Cannot be opened"—the natural inner restraint;
if it could be opened, it would be mere pretense of closure to the senses.
"Cannot be undone"—the natural coagulation;
if it could be undone, it would be forced aggregation.

Thus the sage abides in the constant goodness of the natural,
establishing oneself and establishing others—
all people may be esteemed.
Perfecting oneself and perfecting things—
all things may be contemplated.

Inheriting illumination means using prior awakening to awaken later awakening, with the heart transmitting in succession to arouse enlightenment—this is what is called inheriting illumination. Thus the good person fully illuminates the luminous virtue; even the unvirtuous person, by drawing near, also partakes of luminous virtue. The unvirtuous person, unaware of self-reflection, is seen by the good person, who then reflects inwardly. They alternately take each other as teachers, alternately rely on each other. If one does not regard mutual reliance and mutual teaching as precious and beloved, then self-proclaimed cleverness, though possessing wisdom, is still like profound delusion. The essential subtlety of self-cultivation lies nowhere beyond this.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "Returning to Simplicity"; Peng’s version titles it "Constant Virtue")*

"Know the masculine, guard the feminine,
and become the world’s stream."
Becoming the world’s stream,
constant virtue does not depart,
returning to the state of infancy.

This passage holds two meanings, both of which cultivators of the body should know and uphold.

First: the masculine emits, the feminine transforms.
As the *Cantong Qi* states:
"The masculine yang spreads its dark emanation,
the feminine yin governs the yellow transformation."
Knowing this, one can emit and act;
guarding this, one can transform and nurture.
When feminine and masculine interact,
metal is concealed within water,
and in turn water generates its metal.
When the metal qi is sufficient, the tidal signal arrives—
its force like a canal stream.
Flowing backward and ascending,
it becomes the world’s canal stream.
Yet though it is a canal stream,
with yang fire advancing and yin tally arriving,
the constant virtue of returning to the root and reviving life cannot be abandoned.
Thus it returns to the earthen cauldron to nurture the fetal infant.

Second: the masculine returns, the feminine conceals.
As the *Wuzhen Pian* says:
"Within the masculine, the feminine nurtures the sacred embryo."
If discussing the principle of generating things,
when yin reaches its extreme, yang arises—
this is the feminine harboring the masculine.
If discussing the practice of nurturing things,
when yang reaches its extreme, yin arises—
this is the masculine harboring the feminine.
When the masculine harbors the feminine,
having obtained the masculine’s return to conjoin the elixir,
one must further rely on the feminine’s concealment to warm the elixir.
Its force is like a ravine—
flowing from above downward,
descending into the stream.
Thus the way of guarding the feminine
is like the world’s ravines,
possessing flow and return.
This is the true, constant primordial virtue,
which cannot depart from its place.
Returning to the stream
is like returning to the Yellow Court.
Returning to the state of infancy
means entering stillness to nurture the sacred embryo.

"Know the white, guard the black,
and become the world’s model."
Becoming the world’s model,
constant virtue knows no error,
returning to the boundless.

Here the sage exhausts the principles of feminine and masculine
and reveals their subtleties,
again borrowing color imagery to discuss them.
The white is metal essence;
the black is water’s foundation.
Metal essence is the masculine yang,
spread into the feminine to generate it.
Before this essence existed,
the Kun mother’s body was originally empty.
Through exchanging radiance with the Qian father,
Kun became replete and formed Kan.
Once the Kan form was complete,
the moon emitted the Dui trigram—
this is called metal within water.
Metal within water
truly relies on the Kun mother’s nurturance to form,
hence it is called the mother qi.
As the *Wuzhen Pian* says:
"Extracting the white from the black makes the elixir mother."
The mother qi possesses white light,
called yang light.
When yang light appears,
one moves one’s own mercury to welcome it—
this is the "second phase of seeking the medicine."
When both correspond,
the two eights of the same kind,
captured in one moment,
refined into yang elixir—
this is the elixir mother.
Yet its transformation occurs externally,
so the elixir mother counts only as external medicine.
The practitioner uses external medicine to cultivate internal medicine,
using mother qi to subdue child qi.
Within the elixir mother,
yang lead is produced—
one then harnesses the river chariot to transport it,
reversing its course back to the original palace,
concealing it in the earthen cauldron.
With the four phases harmonized
and the three families in joyous union,
this moment of bliss attains immortality.
Though the method’s workings are thus,
knowing the white must first involve guarding the black;
guarding the black enables knowing the white;
knowing the white still requires guarding the black.
Within this lie three layers of marvelous function,
sufficient to serve as the world’s model.
If one can practice accordingly,
then natural constant virtue will know no error.
Having no error,
one can return to verify the boundless
and refine spirit to return to emptiness.

Knowing the white must involve guarding the black:
yang enters within yin.
Guarding the black enables knowing the white:
yin produces yang within.
Knowing the white still requires guarding the black:
spirit returns and qi conceals.
The principles of heaven, earth, and the myriad things
are all thus.
Hence it serves as the world’s model.

"Know glory, guard disgrace,
and become the world’s valley."
Becoming the world’s valley,
constant virtue is thus fulfilled,
returning to simplicity.

When simplicity scatters, it becomes vessels;
the sage employs them,
then becomes an official or leader—
thus great governance does not sever.

Following the world to become human is a glorious matter.
Reversing the course to become immortal is a disgraceful matter.
Yet one should know the glory of becoming human
and guard the disgrace of becoming immortal.
The study of constant disgrace is the study of cessation.
Emptying the heart and nurturing qi,
one becomes like the world’s empty valley.
Able to contend for the world’s empty valley,
then the constant virtue of attaining emptiness and guarding stillness
is thus fulfilled.
With constant virtue fulfilled,
one returns to verify primordial simplicity
and reverts to the root to return to the origin.
The true essence of primordial simplicity,
scattered and seen, generates the myriad things.
The flourishing abundance
can all have their materials taken and fashioned into vessels.
If the sage wishes to employ these vessels,
they become officials of yin and yang,
leaders of the multitudes,
preserving and uniting them to return to the One.
Thus great governance of the world
does not esteem division.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "Non-Action"; Peng’s version titles it "The Sacred Vessel")*

"Those who would take the world and act upon it,
I see that they will not succeed.
The world is a sacred vessel—
it cannot be acted upon.
Those who act upon it ruin it;
those who grasp it lose it."

All things either lead or follow,
either warm with harmony or chill with force,
either grow strong or weaken,
either rise or fall.
Therefore the sage discards excess,
discards extravagance,
discards excessiveness.

The world is like the body within.
The sacred vessel speaks of its utmost weight.
The Great Dao of prior Heaven
accomplishes through natural non-action.
Vulgar people often doubt its emptiness and stillness,
so the Old Master declares this to instruct them:
"Do people take non-action for emptiness and stillness?
If I were to take the world and govern it with action,
I would see that those who act only multiply disturbances,
only multiply contrivances,
achieving nothing without ceaseless toil."

The world’s sacred vessel is of utmost weight—
with action and many affairs,
it is not like non-action with few affairs.
Thus it cannot be acted upon.
Acting to seek completion only brings ruin—
to act is to ruin it.
Grasping to seek attainment only brings loss—
to grasp is to lose it.

The world is thus;
all things are likewise.
What dwells within the body:
some yang advances alone,
some yin arrives to follow,
some converges in warmth,
some drifts in cool breath,
some qi is full and robust,
some qi is tender and frail,
some ascends in bearing,
some descends in collapse—
all is naturally so.

Therefore the sage who practices the Dao
discards excess,
discards extravagance,
discards excessiveness.
These three are all afflictions of fondness for action,
so they are discarded.

**Chapter Thirty**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "Frugality in Warfare"; Peng’s version titles it "Military Strength")*

One who assists a ruler with the Dao
does not rely on military strength to dominate the world,
for such affairs readily rebound.
Where armies encamp,
thorns and brambles arise.
In the wake of great armies,
years of famine inevitably follow.

Thus the adept achieves results and nothing more—
daring not seize strength through force.
Having achieved results, do not boast;
having achieved results, do not brag;
having achieved results, do not grow arrogant;
having achieved results, act only out of necessity;
having achieved results, do not force it.
For when things reach their prime, they age—
this is called "not the Dao,"
and what is not the Dao ends early.

A ruler who strengthens virtue does not strengthen arms.
One who assists a ruler with the Dao—
how could they flaunt military might before the world?
Yet some deploy troops to vaunt their martial prowess,
while others withdraw their forces to cultivate civil virtue.
Worldly affairs often rebound in this way.
Wherever armies march, thorns and brambles spring up,
a sorrowful reminder of wounds and piercing harm.
After the passage of troops, famine arises,
a bitter legacy of war’s lingering poison.
How can warfare be spoken of lightly?

The ancients who excelled in arms
rooted out treachery, curbed violence,
eliminated bandits, and pacified the people.
Their banners lined the roads,
seen as welcome as sweet rain.
They achieved results solely to rescue the distressed—
not to assert strength.
Yet even in achieving results,
one must eradicate boasting, bragging, and arrogance.
Boasting breeds the impulse for war;
bragging fosters the ambition for endless conquest;
arrogance nurtures the desire to flaunt force.
Though results are achieved, no good intent remains.
If good intent guides the results,
and the people’s suffering persists,
then great armies will persist as well.
The aim lies in strenuously rescuing them from hardship—
not in displaying strength.

Should the enemy’s vigor not wane,
and fortifications stand firm in stalemate,
a strong army will inevitably age—
this too is to ravage one’s own forces.
To ravage one’s own forces
while seeking to punish those who act against the Dao
is to act against the Dao oneself.
Punishing those who act against the Dao
only to become what one seeks to destroy—
better to garrison fields, defend the borders,
and rest one’s troops.
Have those who crave strength ever observed things?
When things reach their prime, they age—
one may well imagine that strength must decline.
To use things while exhausting them
is to act contrary to the Dao.
Acting contrary to the Dao,
better to end early.

This chapter exhaustively expounds the gains and losses of deploying troops,
yet the marvel of the Dao turns on a single word: *thing*.
All action and endeavor arise from the decline of essence and the dissipation of qi,
necessitating restorative and guiding efforts—
this too is achieving results.
Whether building the foundation for a hundred days
or refining the self for three years,
it is still achieving results.
Or if the elixir foundation remains unlaid
and one’s inherent nature remains obscured,
one may rebuild and refine again—
this too is achieving results.
Yet do not boast of results as strength,
for relentless fullness invites exhaustion.
When the great medicine is near,
delay renders it useless.
Thus it is said: when things reach their prime, they age—
better to end early.

**Chapter Thirty-One**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "Laying Down Arms";
Peng’s version titles it "Fine Weapons")*

**Chapter Thirty-One**

Fine weapons are instruments of ill omen—some things abhor them, and thus those who embody the Dao do not dwell in their presence. Therefore, the noble person in repose values the left, but in warfare values the right. Weapons are instruments of ill omen, not tools for the noble person. When their use is unavoidable, tranquility is paramount. To triumph yet not glorify it—those who glorify it delight in slaughter. Those who delight in slaughter cannot realize their ambitions under heaven.

Thus, auspicious affairs honor the left, while inauspicious affairs honor the right. For this reason, the deputy general stands on the left, the supreme general on the right—positioned as in mourning rites. When the slain are many, mourn them with sorrowful tears; when victory is won, observe it with mourning rites.

The term *fine* (佳) implies auspiciousness, yet fine weapons are called inauspicious—how then can weapons be esteemed? The harm of warfare is abhorred by all things, for its schemes are endless and its disturbances manifold. How could one who embodies the Dao claim the name of a lover of war? Thus, the noble person in the world, when at peace, values the left—for the left is auspicious. In warfare, they value the right—for the right is inauspicious. This further reveals the ill omen of weapons.

Weapons are not the tools of the noble person, yet when their use is unavoidable, rescuing the distressed is paramount. Do not act with impatience or fervor; remain serene and detached. Even if victory is achieved and triumphant return secured, never regard warfare as a glorious affair. Those who do so must delight in slaughter. How could such a person be allowed to realize their ambitions in court or wage war across the land?

Observing human affairs, one cannot but sigh: auspicious affairs honor the left, inauspicious affairs honor the right. The left pertains to yang and life, the right to yin and death. Thus, in the military, there are supreme generals and deputy generals. The deputy general, without the supreme general’s command, dares not attack or kill—hence their position on the left, which holds the intent of life. The supreme general, bearing the intent of death, stands on the right. The right is the place of mourning rites, and nothing under heaven is more inauspicious. Thus, the supreme general is positioned as in mourning, cautioned against reckless killing.

Alas! A single general’s success is built upon countless bones—such a thing is cause for bitter weeping. Therefore, when the slain are many, mourn them with sorrowful tears; when victory is won, observe it with mourning rites. The heart of the highest sage is the heart of heaven and earth, which cherishes life.

*(This chapter’s metaphor suggests that the "female crucible" is inauspicious and should not be employed. Yet its critique of warfare’s harms is profoundly incisive.)*

---

**Chapter Thirty-Two**

The Dao is eternally nameless and uncarved. Though small, none under heaven dare subjugate it. If lords and kings can guard it, the ten thousand things will naturally submit. When heaven and earth unite, sweet dew descends—no command is given, yet it falls evenly of itself.

Once names are established, they too must know their limit; knowing the limit is how one avoids peril. The Dao in the world is like valleys to rivers and seas.

The great Dao is without name or form, a single mass of undifferentiated simplicity. Like the Limitless, though the uncarved block is small, it resides above the Supreme Ultimate—how could it be controlled or diminished? If lords and kings guard this simplicity, the great system remains unsevered, and the ten thousand things come as guests.

When earth rises to heaven, heaven and earth harmonize, and sweet dew descends. No effort is required to achieve balance—spirit and qi naturally attain equilibrium. When qi transforms into liquid, it is first called the "golden liquid returning elixir." Once the name "golden liquid" is established, it must be halted at the earthen crucible and nurtured. Knowing the limit avoids peril; embracing oneness and emptying the heart, peace naturally prevails.

This Dao, extended to the world, is like valleys to rivers and seas—it has its place of return.

---

**Chapter Thirty-Three**

To know others is wisdom;
to know oneself is clarity.
To overcome others is to have strength;
to overcome oneself is to be mighty.
To know contentment is wealth.
To act with perseverance is to have resolve.
To not lose one’s place is to endure.
To die yet not perish is to attain longevity.

One who understands others possesses wondrous wisdom;
one who understands their original nature attains perfect clarity—
for they have comprehended their inherent nature.
One who overcomes the desires of others has steadfast power;
one who overcomes the corpse-thief within possesses true might—
for they have comprehended their destiny.
To know contentment is to guard wealth and halt the fire to nurture the elixir.
To act with perseverance is to have resolve, like facing the wall for nine years.
To attain once is to attain forever, enduring as long as the earth.
To die in heart yet preserve the spirit is to live as long as heaven.

---

**Chapter Thirty-Four**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "The Task of Completion";
Peng’s version titles it "The Flow of Achievement")*

The great Dao flows boundlessly, extending left and right.
The ten thousand things rely on it to live, yet it does not refuse;
its work complete, it claims no possession.
It clothes and covers the ten thousand things yet does not act as their master—
ever without desire, it may be called "the small."
The ten thousand things return to it yet it does not act as their master—
it may be called "the great."
Thus the sage never deems himself great,
and so accomplishes his greatness.

Vast and without limit, it meets all needs wherever they arise,
allowing each to draw from it as they will.
The ten thousand things depend on the Dao for life, yet the Dao does not refuse,
merely following its timely course—
its work complete, it claims no name.
Clothing and covering without acting as master,
its virtue of nurturing and completion is all-pervading.
Rooted in non-action, none can name it as master.
Abiding in true constancy and free from desire,
its smallness cannot be broken—thus it may be called "the small."
Encompassing all returns yet not acting as master,
its greatness cannot be borne—thus it may be called "the great."
Only the sage does not display his greatness,
and this is why he is truly great.

**Chapter Thirty-Five**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "The Chapter of Benevolent Virtue")*

Hold to the great image, and all under heaven will come.
They come without harm, dwelling in peace and tranquility.
Music and fine food may make the passing traveler stop,
but the Dao, when spoken, is bland and without flavor—
seen, it cannot be perceived; heard, it cannot be listened to;
used, it cannot be exhausted.

The great image is that which has no image yet embodies all images—
thus it is called the great image, still referring to the great Dao.
One who upholds the great Dao will have all under heaven come to him.
Moving within his realm, serene and untroubled,
they suffer no harm, resting only in peace and tranquility.
Fine music and delicacies may make a passing traveler halt his carriage,
seeking a moment’s delight—
yet when the wine is finished and the songs end, they do not stay long.
The Dao is not so:
spoken, it moistens and nourishes, though it seems bland and flavorless.
Who knows that it transcends sight and sound?
Yet its use is inexhaustible.

**Chapter 36** *(He Shang Gong’s commentary titles this "Subtle Illumination")*

To gather, one must first expand;
to weaken, one must first strengthen;
to abolish, one must first exalt;
to take away, one must first give.
This is called subtle illumination.

The soft overcomes the hard,
the weak overcomes the strong.
Fish must not leave the depths;
the state’s sharp instruments must not be shown to others.

*"Fixed"* means *"first."*
To gather, first expand—scattered will return to unity.
To weaken, first strengthen—advance will turn to retreat.
To abolish, first exalt—glory will fade to decline.
To take, first give—departure will return again.
All things move in mutual dependence;
principles, though faint, become manifest—
hence it is called *"subtle illumination."*

The soft and weak overcome the hard and strong,
subduing without struggle.
Knowing fish must not leave the depths,
one knows the Dao does not depart from the self.
Knowing sharp instruments must not be shown,
one knows the Dao must be realized within.

---

**Chapter 37**

The Dao is eternally non-action, yet nothing is left undone.
If lords and kings can hold to it,
the myriad things will transform themselves.
When transformation stirs desire,
I will restrain it with nameless simplicity.
Nameless simplicity itself will then be free of desire.
Without desire, through stillness,
the world will right itself.

Non-action that leaves nothing undone—
this non-action is true action,
what is called *"abiding in the center to govern the outer,"
congealing spirit to form the body.*
If lords and kings can hold to it,
the myriad things will transform themselves.
Governing through reverent non-action,
one may rule all under heaven.
Is this not the legacy of the great antiquity?
Yet when peace endures and activity arises,
when feasting and splendor flourish—
this is no blessing for the state.

I will restrain them with primordial simplicity,
so they may guard simplicity and return to truth,
that those stirred by desire may cease desiring.
When desire ceases, the myriad things grow still.
Without seeking to right the world,
the world will right itself.

---

**Chapter 38**

The highest virtue does not act as virtue—
thus it possesses virtue.
The lesser virtue does not lose virtue—
thus it has no virtue.

The highest virtue takes no action and has no purpose;
the lesser virtue acts and has purpose.
The highest benevolence acts but has no purpose;
the highest righteousness acts and has purpose.
The highest propriety acts, but if none respond,
it rolls up its sleeves and forces compliance.

Thus, when the Dao is lost, virtue arises;
when virtue is lost, benevolence arises;
when benevolence is lost, righteousness arises;
when righteousness is lost, propriety arises.
Propriety is the thinning of loyalty and trust,
the beginning of chaos.
Foresight is the flower of the Dao,
the beginning of folly.

Therefore, the great person dwells in the substantial,
not the superficial;
abides in the fruit, not the flower.
Thus, they discard the latter and take the former.

The person of highest virtue shows no sign of virtue,
yet possesses its full measure.
The person of lesser virtue clings to the name of virtue,
yet becomes one without virtue.

The highest virtue does not act as virtue—
it abides in non-action,
letting the myriad things transform themselves without purpose.
The lesser virtue does not lose virtue—
it delights in action,
stirring the hearts of the people toward purpose.

What benevolence does is love.
The world delights in it—
thus it has no purpose.
What righteousness does is awe.
The world is moved by it—
thus it has purpose.

Yet benevolence is but one benevolence,
righteousness but one righteousness.
As for propriety—
court rituals change, rural customs differ—
enforcing it is most difficult.
If one forces compliance, the people do not respond,
and instead roll up their sleeves in contention,
clinging to their own ways.
Will not the affairs of the world grow ever harder?

The great Dao does not contend,
yet it makes the people not contend—
how superior this principle!
But once lost, it becomes benevolence;
lost again, righteousness;
lost once more, propriety.

The lament that follows speaks of the turning of the times,
not that benevolence and righteousness are partial.
Propriety, rooted in loyalty and trust,
when customs thin, invites disdain and chaos—
only propriety can restore simplicity.

Foresight is the radiance of the Dao’s nature,
yet when its brilliance flames, it breeds folly—
such is the shift in foresight.
Therefore, the great person stands in the thick, not the thin;
dwells in the real, not the flowery.
Discarding and choosing rightly,
one may say simplicity still lingers among people.

---

**Chapter 39**

Of old, those who attained the One:
Heaven attained the One and became clear;
Earth attained the One and became stable;
spirits attained the One and became numinous;
valleys attained the One and became full;
the myriad things attained the One and came to life;
lords and kings attained the One and became the standard for all under heaven.

This attainment was one and the same.
Without clarity, heaven would fear splitting;
without stability, earth would fear erupting;
without numinosity, spirits would fear vanishing;
without fullness, valleys would fear drying;
without life, the myriad things would fear perishing;
without the standard of honor and loftiness,
lords and kings would fear collapse.

Thus, the noble takes the humble as its root,
the high takes the low as its foundation.
Lords and kings call themselves *"the orphaned,"* *"the widowed,"* *"the unworthy."*
Is this not taking the humble as their root?

Therefore, the greatest chariot has no chariot—
one does not desire to gleam like jade
or clatter like stone.

*"Of old"* refers to before the Great Beginning.
*"One"* is the beginning of all numbers.
The first thing born is called *"One."*
Before the Great Beginning, all was formless;
after the Great Beginning, there was surplus.

To attain this One, one must return to the Great Beginning.
Between before and after,
there is qi but no form—
no form, yet trustworthy.

The Five Phases take water as the One.
This water is the utmost purity,
the utmost stillness—
its merit beyond conception.
To call it *"water"* seems forced;
not to call it *"water"* is empty.

Now let us trace back from those who attain the One:

The Dao’s beginning in the past—
Heaven opened from the past through the One.
Yet without this water, how could it divide?
How would its clarity be seen?

Earth unfolded from the past through the One.
Yet without this water, how could it bear?
How would its tranquility be established?

Spirits served from the past through the One.
Yet without this water, how could they be sustained?
How would their numinosity be revealed?

Valleys flowed from the past through the One.
Yet without this water, how could they contain?
How would their fullness be aided?

The myriad things arose from the past through the One.
Yet without this water, how could they be nurtured?
How would their life be sustained?

Lords and kings arose from the past to rule,
taking the One as their lord.
Yet without this water, how could they put it to use?
How would they bring steadfast peace to the world?

Heaven and Earth, spirits and valleys, the myriad things, lords and kings—
though their forms differ,
what enables their clarity, tranquility,
numinosity, fullness, life, and steadfastness
is all through the One.

Clarity without fissure—
the qi of water purifies it.
Tranquility without eruption—
the qi of water bears it.
Numinosity without cessation—
the qi of water moves it.
Fullness without exhaustion—
the qi of water fills it.
Life without extinction—
the qi of water nourishes it.
Steadfastness without constriction—
for the noble and exalted,
the virtue of water assists them,
by its nature lowly and flowing downward.

Since ancient times, rulers have humbled themselves to gain glory,
thus valuing the lowly as their root,
and the high taking the low as their foundation.
*"The orphaned,"* *"the widowed,"* *"the unworthy"*—
the self-designations of lords and kings—
are most lowly and most base.
Yet they do not realize that those who possess the Four Seas,
who are honored as the Son of Heaven,
who feast on the tribute of all directions,
attain this precisely through *"the orphaned,"* *"the widowed,"* *"the unworthy."*
Is this not taking the lowly as their root?

For lords and kings to guard their lowliness
is precisely to guard their One.
Thus, extending this principle to things:
it is like one who constructs a chariot of many parts,
turning things into mere vehicles,
recklessly driving them by their multitude—
unaware of their complete ruin.

Or like jade that gleams too brightly,
or stones that clatter too loudly—
those with the appearance of abundance—
the highest do not desire them.

Reversion is the movement of the Dao,
weakness its function.
The myriad things of the world are born from Being,
Being is born from Non-being.

Reversion: return.
Heaven and earth merge in darkness,
the first yang returns—
this is the Dao’s initial movement.
Weakness: softness.
Body and mind tranquil,
concentrating qi to attain softness—
this is the Dao’s wondrous function.
The work of returning the elixir
lies in taking the Being of Qian and entering the Non-being of Kun,
Qian planting the seed and Kun giving birth,
Non-being generating Being.
Thus the Dao is born from Being,
Being from Non-being.
All things under heaven follow this pattern.

The highest scholar hears the Dao and diligently practices it.
The middling scholar hears the Dao and treats it as if it exists, as if it does not.
The lowest scholar hears the Dao and laughs loudly at it.
Without laughter, it would not suffice as the Dao.
Thus the established sayings declare:
The luminous Dao seems dim;
advancing in the Dao seems like retreat;
the level Dao seems uneven;
supreme virtue resembles a valley;
great purity seems tarnished;
abundant virtue seems insufficient;
established virtue seems indolent;
substance and truth seem altered;
great squareness has no corners;
great vessels are late in completion;
great sound is scarce of voice;
great image has no form.
Only the Dao excels in bestowing and accomplishing.

The Dao of the highest scholar is practiced with diligence as its root.
Within it lies no self-display, no self-sharpening,
no self-differentiation, no self-substantiation,
no self-manifestation, no self-satisfaction.
Though practicing as if slack, believing as if deceived,
only then can one conceal brilliance and refine slowly,
dwell in the dust yet guard emptiness,
obscure one’s traces to seek completion,
cherish time and revere the Dao.
Otherwise, one remains with the middling scholar’s existence or nonexistence,
or the lowest scholar’s loud laughter—
how can this be called diligence?

The middling scholar hears the Dao and practices it intermittently,
hence it is said to exist and not exist.
The lowest scholar hears it and laughs loudly—
laughter is the mark of slander.
Yet even loud laughter does no harm to the Dao.
Without laughter, it would not be recognized as the supreme Dao.
The ancients in their teachings held this meaning:
Those who cultivate the Dao appear dim though luminous—
this is no self-display.
They advance as if retreating—
this is no self-sharpening.
Such is the concealment of brilliance and gradual refinement.
They appear uneven though level—
this is no self-differentiation.
Supreme virtue resembles a valley—
this is no self-substantiation.
Such is dwelling in the dust yet guarding emptiness.
Great purity seems tarnished—
this is no self-manifestation.
Abundant virtue seems insufficient—
this is no self-satisfaction.
Such is obscuring one’s traces to seek completion.
Established virtue seems indolent—
though practicing, one appears slack.
Substance and truth seem altered—
though believing, one appears deceived.
Such is cherishing time and revering the Dao.
All these are the diligent practices of the highest scholar,
beyond the reach of the middling and lowest.

Thus, while ordinary squares have corners,
great squareness has none—
it simply guards the center.
Ordinary vessels are expected to form early,
but great vessels take time—
they seek perfection.
Great sound, great image,
voiceless and formless—
are these not what the *Doctrine of the Mean* calls
"fearful of not hearing, cautious of not seeing"?
Those who embrace the Dao in the world,
endlessly bestowing and bringing great transformation to completion—
these are the true highest scholars.

The Dao gives birth to the One,
the One gives birth to the Two,
the Two gives birth to the Three,
the Three gives birth to the myriad things.
The Dao stands above the Three Powers and before the Five Phases.
The Supreme One discusses creation,
thus necessarily beginning with the Dao.
The Great Dao is formless,
vast and boundless.
When stillness gives rise to movement,
the One appears.
The One is water, in the hexagrams it is Kan.
Kan resides in the north, a laborious hexagram.
All things begin and end through it.
In ending, the myriad beings return to their root;
in beginning, the first yang returns.
Yang is fire, thus when speaking of water, fire is within—
the One gives birth to the Two.
When water and fire harmonize,
yin and yang unite in prosperity,
the essence of wood stirs,
and the myriad beings flourish—
thus the Two gives birth to the Three,
the Three gives birth to the myriad things.
This speaks generally of creation,
yet the Dao of the elixir is also within it.

In my humble opinion:
The postnatal sequence is disordered and complex,
its numbers chaotic and numerous—
only when the Five are enumerated are the phases complete.
Metal generates water: four generates one.
Water generates wood: one generates three.
Wood generates fire: three generates two.
Fire generates earth: two generates five.
Earth generates metal: five generates four.
The prenatal Five Phases are ordered and simple—
when the Three are enumerated, the Five are encompassed.
Fire arises within water: the One generates the Two.
Wood flourishes through fire: the Two generates the Three.
When wood is born, it is the moment the myriad things first arise—
the Three can encompass the myriad.

Moreover, in the method of elixir refinement,
the Five Phases are all contained within the One and Two.
Water and fire are cultivated together,
the Three, Four, and Five all assist—
thus the Three need not be mentioned.
The Supreme One speaks of the Three
only to exhaustively encompass the myriad things,
hence the number Three is brought forth.
Yet even the Two need not be mentioned:
when qi becomes the elixir,
the Two, Three, Four, and Five all assist—
the Five Phases are all contained within the One.
Fire arises within water: the One contains the Two.
Wood mercury is hidden within water: the One contains the Three.
Metal lead appears within water: the One contains the Four.
True earth is held within water: the One contains the Five.
Yet even the One need not be mentioned:
the subtle and obscure,
the embryo of emptiness and nothingness—
these can be encompassed by the Dao.
Is not the art of returning the elixir
the most simple and the easiest?
Though the words are many, they return to the essential—
one finds them only more venerable, not excessive.

The myriad things bear yin and embrace yang,
harmonizing through the qi of the center.
As stated above, the Three gives birth to the myriad things—
the Three is the number of wood.
Things flourish through the vigor of wood,
wood flourishes through the vigor of fire,
fire is born within water—
thus the bodies of the myriad things
externally bear yin and internally embrace yang.

Externally yin and internally yang, outwardly empty and inwardly replete—this emptiness enfolds the yang qi, and this is called the *chong qi*. When the nature of things reaches this state, it unites with the Great Harmony.

Now, speaking in terms of the elixir method: when Kun (Earth) joins with Qian (Heaven/Metal), it transforms into Kan (Water). The body of Kan is outwardly empty yet inwardly replete; thus, one should embrace its qi with *chong xu* (penetrating emptiness), like the misty harmony of the Great Harmony.

What people despise—being orphaned, widowed, or unprosperous—kings and lords take as their titles. Thus, things may be diminished yet gain, or augmented yet lose. What others teach, I also teach: "The strong and overbearing do not meet a natural end"—I take this as the father of my teaching.

Nothing is more despised than base names, yet "orphaned," "widowed," and "unprosperous" are such names, and kings and lords use them to refer to themselves. Though their titles are diminished, in truth, they gain thereby. Thus, observing things, one sees that gain may turn to loss, and loss may yield gain—this is known through contemplating the Way of the *Yi*.

In the *Pi* hexagram, Heaven is above and Earth below; to transform this into benefit, the Way of Heaven does not dare exalt itself. Thus, it diminishes above to augment below, and the stagnation of Heaven and Earth not joining may become the benefit of the above descending to the below. Therefore, the noble person uses this to reform faults and amend errors.

In the *Tai* hexagram, Earth is above and Heaven below; to know the good of diminution, the Way of Earth does not presume to be full. Thus, it diminishes below to augment above, and the harmony of Heaven and Earth joining also follows the principle of the above acting through diminution. Therefore, the noble person uses this to curb anger and restrain desires.

Heaven and Earth gain through loss—thus, employing gain is not as effective as employing loss. Though the two hexagrams stand side by side, one should emulate the side of *Sun* (Diminution). Moreover, when *Shan Ze Sun* (Mountain and Marsh Diminishing) is inverted, it becomes *Feng Lei Yi* (Wind and Thunder Benefiting). The resonance of mountain, marsh, wind, and thunder arises precisely from diminution.

This is the teaching—the teaching of the *Yi*, the teaching of the ancients. The Most High said: "What others teach, I also teach—to teach people to know self-diminution."

The unskilled serve as the resource for the skilled—thus, the strong and overbearing are called the "teaching fathers." The strong and overbearing are those who seize and plunder, diminishing others to augment themselves, yet in the end gain nothing. *Fu* (father) here shares the same pronunciation as *fu* (beginning), read in the rising tone.

**Chapter Forty-Three**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "Partial Application"; Peng’s edition titles it "Utmost Softness")*

The softest under Heaven gallops through the hardest under Heaven. The non-existent enters where there is no gap—thus do I know the benefit of non-action. The teaching without words, the benefit of non-action—few under Heaven can attain this.

The "utmost soft" refers to *qi* and water. *Qi* reaches every unobstructed aperture; water finds every unblocked path—thus they gallop through the hardest without gap. Mountains disgorge stones; springs pierce mountains—this is visible. The "non-existent" refers to that without form or substance, only *qi* and water, which can likewise reach our bodies and enter where there is no gap.

The first two lines speak generally of *qi* and water under Heaven; this line refers specifically to the body, setting forth the analogy. It further states: "Thus do I know the benefit of non-action." Non-action means no forced movement, only natural surging. To unite the teaching without words with the benefit of non-action—truly, none under Heaven can match this.

**Chapter Forty-Four**
*(He Shanggong’s commentary titles this "Establishing Admonitions"; Peng’s edition titles it "Valuing the Name")*

Which is closer—name or self?
Which is greater—self or wealth?
Which is more harmful—gain or loss?

Therefore, excessive attachment incurs great expense; hoarding leads to heavy loss. Knowing contentment avoids disgrace; knowing when to stop avoids danger—this is how one endures.

The self is closer than the name; the self is greater than wealth. Gaining the self brings no harm; losing the self brings harm. This principle is self-evident.

Thus, the Most High uses "which is closer," "which is greater," and "which is more harmful" to awaken people, stirring their innate conscience so they may realize for themselves—not like later generations’ didactic texts, which merely reproach and instead provoke resistance. This is the Most High’s humility and harmony.

Yet in the world, some grow close to their name while estranging their self, amass wealth while diminishing their self, value gain over the self, and feel no pain at its loss. Such deep delusion stems from prizing fame and profit while neglecting self-preservation.

How pitiful when the last breath is drawn, the final hour arrives, the name vanishes into nothingness, wealth becomes an empty fiction, and the lost one falls into ghostly realms, never to regain human form!

For one living in the world, what builds my name depletes my spirit; what enters through perversity exits likewise—this is what is meant by "excessive attachment incurs great expense; hoarding leads to heavy loss." Attachment that incurs great expense is disgrace; hoarding that leads to heavy loss is danger—neither is a path to endurance.

With a heart of compassion, the Most High speaks to awaken and teach: "Knowing contentment avoids disgrace; knowing when to stop avoids danger—this is how one endures." Such is the virtue of the sage.

The Great Achievement appears deficient, yet its function never wears out.
The Utmost Fullness appears empty, yet its function is inexhaustible.
The Utmost Straightness appears bent,
the Utmost Skill appears clumsy,
the Utmost Eloquence appears inarticulate.

Restlessness overcomes cold; stillness overcomes heat.
Clarity and stillness are the correct governance of the world.

The Great Achievement appears deficient—like the light of the sun and moon, round yet temporarily eclipsed, advancing to regain its brightness, thus its function never wears out.
The Utmost Fullness appears empty—like the vastness of the sea, embracing all without overflow, boundless and without limit, thus its function is inexhaustible.
Those who are excessively straight are reckless;
those who rely on cunning are deceitful;
those who force eloquence are false—
none can compare to the great.

The sage and worthy use qi to attain straightness,
not flaunting hardness yet inherently hard, appearing bent;
their divine subtlety becomes skill,
not displaying wisdom yet inherently wise, appearing clumsy;
their virtue and Dao become eloquence,
not speaking much yet speaking well, appearing inarticulate.

As for refining lead and fire into harmony,
three winters can be endured;
sitting steadfast on a rush cushion,
the heat of nine summers can be forgotten—
this is precisely what is meant by "restlessness overcomes cold; stillness overcomes heat."
These two principles embody the workings of yin and yang.
If one can observe the qi of dryness and dampness, cold and warmth
with a heart of clarity, emptiness, and stillness,
then the correct Way of the world is attained.

When the world follows the Dao, warhorses are retired to fertilize the fields. When the world loses the Dao, even mares in the wild give birth on the battlefield.

No crime is greater than indulging desire; no disaster is greater than discontent; no fault is greater than covetousness. Thus, contentment in sufficiency is perpetual sufficiency.

"Following the Dao" refers to the time when the *yin* phase has reached its extreme and *yang* begins to rise after a long period of *pi* (adversity). "Losing the Dao" refers to the time when the *tai* (prosperity) phase has lasted too long and *yin* begins to emerge from the extreme of *yang*. When *pi* (adversity) is resolved and *tai* (order) is established, warhorses are retired to fertilize the fields—nothing in the world remains unaccomplished. When *tai* (prosperity) flourishes and *pi* (disorder) lies dormant, warhorses are bred even in the outskirts—affairs of the world demand action. Yet from disorder, renewal begins, and *tai* (order) brings blessing.

To see what is desirable and desire it—no crime is greater. To be content yet remain discontent—no disaster is greater. To covet what one ought not—no fault is greater. Therefore, one who finds sufficiency in contentment attains perpetual sufficiency.

**Supplementary Note:**
*Que* (却) means "to remove." *Fen* (粪) means "to cultivate." When the world is at peace, though horses are unused, they still exist. To "remove" them means to send them to the mountains and marshes, akin to the idea of "returning horses to pasture." Moreover, horses are not meant for tilling fields; the phrase "to fertilize" is used as a metaphor for the withdrawal of arms and the rise of agriculture.

He who does not step beyond his door knows the world.
He who does not peer through his window sees the Dao of Heaven.
The farther he ventures, the less he knows.
Thus the sage knows without traveling, names without seeing, accomplishes without acting.

*Hu* (户): a small door. By not going out yet knowing worldly affairs, seclusion clarifies worldly matters.
*You* (牖): a small window. Without peering yet seeing Heaven’s Dao, the hidden and subtle reveal the manifest.
Body, mind, nature, and life—these are where the Dao resides.
To abandon the near and pursue the distant is the way of fools.
What cannot be seen, heard, or grasped—where does one begin the search?
He who understands this truth finds the Dao between door and window.
He who does not grows ever more lost with each inquiry, more confused with each question.
Every path seems right, yet none can be fully traversed—
this is what it means to venture farther and know less.

Only the sage knows without traveling, for when the spirit is settled, wisdom arises.
He names without seeing, for holding to non-being gives rise to being.
He accomplishes without acting, for embracing the One returns to emptiness.

**Chapter Forty-Eight**
In the pursuit of learning, one gains daily.
In the pursuit of the Dao, one loses daily.
Lose and lose again, until one reaches non-action.
Through non-action, nothing is left undone.
Thus he who would govern the world must always act without interference.
If he interferes, he is unfit to govern the world.

The pursuit of learning differs from the pursuit of the Dao.
Learning values accumulation, yet even in learning, one may progress from breadth to simplicity—what was gained is not without loss.
The Dao values reduction, yet even in the Dao, one may establish a foundation through worldly affairs—what was lost is not without gain.
Reduction means to strip away.
Lose and lose again, until the leaden *qi* dries and the mercury’s light appears,
until one nurtures the spirit and returns to emptiness,
acting through my non-action—
thus nothing is left undone.

To govern the world, one must first settle the world with a steadfast heart.
Act through non-action, and nothing is left undone.
Respond to movement with stillness, make virtue the constant,
cultivate governance with benevolence—
then the people will join hands and come together,
and all nations will turn their hearts toward unity.
Thus one may govern the world.
This is why one must always act without interference.
If one interferes, there will first be upheaval across the land.
Acting through action, one multiplies action,
meeting force with force, destruction with destruction, violence with violence.
To make war one’s skill and plunder one’s task
is to disturb the world and put oneself before it—
how can one govern the world thus?

Some say that those who govern the world must be men of non-action shouldering the burdens of action,
as were the recluses of Xinye, Panxi, and Nanyang in ancient times.
Yet when the affairs of the world weigh on their hearts,
their minds become disordered—
as with Fan Zeng’s grudge against Liu Bang or Xu Yuanzhi’s devotion to his mother.
This interpretation also holds.

**Chapter Forty-Nine**
The sage has no fixed heart; he takes the hearts of the people as his own.
To the good, I am good; to the not-good, I am also good—thus virtue is goodness.
To the trustworthy, I am trustworthy; to the untrustworthy, I am also trustworthy—thus virtue is trustworthiness.

When the sage is in the world, he humbly unifies his heart with the world.
The people all turn their ears and eyes toward him, and the sage treats them all as children.

Having no fixed heart means having no partiality.
With no partiality, he can shield and embrace the people, harmonizing with their hearts.
What the people hope for is that the sage will treat them with goodness and trustworthiness.
Even the not-good and the untrustworthy receive goodness and trustworthiness—
how great is the sincerity of the sage’s virtue!

Thus when the sage is in the world, humbly unifying his heart with the world,
he shields and embraces the people.
The people all turn their ears and eyes toward him, each with their own hopes,
and the sage treats them all as children—thus he unifies them.
*Diedie* (惵惵): the appearance of sincerity.

**Chapter Fifty**
From birth one enters death.
Those who follow life are thirteen in number.
Those who follow death are thirteen in number.
Those who live yet move toward the realm of death are also thirteen in number.
Why is this so?
Because they overindulge in the pursuit of life.

Here is the translation:

---

It is said that those who skillfully nurture life walk on land without encountering rhinoceroses or tigers, and enter battle without being harmed by weapons. The rhinoceros has nowhere to thrust its horn, the tiger nowhere to place its claws, and weapons nowhere to lodge their blades. Why is this so? Because they dwell not in the realm of death.

All things emerge into life and enter death, all falling within the thirteen. As for these thirteen, past commentaries have not grasped their true meaning, which I now clarify. The number ten represents the generative numbers of Heaven and Earth. One Heaven, one Earth; one *qian*, one *kun*. The *qian* hexagram has three yang lines, the *kun* hexagram three yin lines. All things meet the three yang and are born, meet the three yin and die. Only humans, in receiving life, share the three yang with other beings, yet their entry into the three yin is self-accelerated. The seven emotions and six desires greatly harm the original spirit, hence it is said they "move toward the realm of death." Yet even this self-acceleration returns to the three yin alone.

Why do beings and humans have three births and three deaths? When the pursuit of life is excessive, the dead are extinguished, and the living are replenished. The dead are at rest, the living in motion—this is the cyclical, interdependent pattern. Otherwise, if there were life without death, the teeming multitudes would fill the world; how could there be a place of repose?

Though the death of all things lies in winter and their birth in spring, their life and death are but the alternation of movement and stillness. For humans, however, death does not return to life. This is not because creation singles out humanity for harsh treatment, but because they do not know how to nurture life, thus continually entering the realm of death.

The Supreme Lord possesses the virtue of cherishing life, and the sage possesses the method of nurturing life. If one seeks wisely, one should transform the path of "yang birth and yin death" into the work of "yang departing and yin arriving," thereby attaining long life and enduring vision, no longer sharing the fate of those who, though newly born, move toward the realm of death.

Things are born through three and die through three. The Way of nurturing life thus takes the three yang of the *qian* hexagram and plants one yang within the three yin, so that *kun* becomes substantial and forms *kan*. The three yin of the *kun* hexagram sprout one yin within the three yang, so that *qian* becomes hollow and forms *li*. *Kan* and *li* are the medicinal substances. Entering seclusion and cultivating stillness, one observes the return of one’s solitary yang, then gathers and returns it, gathers and subjugates it. Thus nurturing life is the Way of the Returned Elixir, the work of returning to the root, the art of extending life, the secret of becoming an immortal.

To reverse and return is nurturing; to move from below to above is nurturing; to turn from outer to inner is nurturing; only with the Yellow Dame within can one nurture. Nurturing is not easily spoken of. Mother and child cherish each other, and nurturing runs deep; the infant and maiden unite, and joy is great; lead and mercury conjoin, and the sacred embryo forms; through non-doing, one verifies and the yang spirit emerges. Though tigers, rhinoceroses, and weapons may assail, they have no power to bring calamity. How then could one share the three yang for birth and three yin for death with ordinary people and things?

**Chapter Fifty-One**
The Dao gives them life, virtue nourishes them, things shape them, and conditions complete them. Therefore, all things without exception revere the Dao and honor virtue. The reverence for the Dao and the honor for virtue arise not by command but always naturally.

Thus the Dao gives them life, virtue nourishes, rears, raises, completes, ripens, tends, and shelters them. It gives life yet does not possess, acts yet does not rely, leads yet does not dominate—this is called profound virtue.

The Dao sows things, virtue protects them; thus things are born by the Dao and nourished by virtue. Things manifest form, and through conditions they are completed. All things revere the Dao and honor virtue because the Dao is nameless while virtue has a name. The reverence for the Dao and the honor for virtue arise without command, for the principles of things naturally converge.

Only the Dao presides over their birth, only virtue presides over their nourishment. It rears and raises, completes and ripens, tends and shelters. Preserving and protecting the spirit are all the arts of virtue. Not possessing, not relying, nor dominating, accomplishing through non-doing—this is true understanding. Profound virtue is the highest virtue.

**Chapter Fifty-Two**
The world has a beginning, which serves as the mother of the world. Having attained the mother, one knows her children. Having known the children, one again guards the mother, and to the end of life one does not perish.

Block the openings, shut the gates, and to the end of life one will not toil. Open the openings, aid one’s affairs, and to the end of life one will not be saved. Seeing the small is called clarity, guarding the soft is called strength. Use its light, return to its clarity, and one will leave no calamity for the self. This is called inheriting the constant.

The Way of the world has an end and thus a beginning, yet its beginning is not singular. The Golden Elixir has a place from which it first emerges, a practice with which it first proceeds, and a substance to which it first returns.

What is the place of first emergence? The *kun* furnace and the yin furnace are one. Yin conceals yang, hence it is called the yin-yang furnace. The moon appears in the *dui* direction, hence it is called the inverted moon furnace. *Dui* is the youngest maiden, hence it is called the primordial wondrous tripod. The *Awakening to Reality* calls it the "source of medicinal production"—all are places of beginning.

What is the practice of first proceeding? To reach the utmost emptiness, to guard perfect stillness, I observe their return. Turbidity settles and slowly clears; tranquility endures, and slowly life emerges—all are the first practices.

What is the substance of first return? The undifferentiated and complete, the profound and obscure—within it there is sincerity, within it there is essence. The uncarved block without name, the formless gold—all are the first substances. The Returned Elixir takes this gold as its beginning, hence it is said to have a beginning. Yet this gold is not a native substance of the *kun* household; it is the fiery essence of the *qian* household.

The elixir method takes cinnabar as its primary agent. Entering the *kun* furnace, it forms *kan*, harmonizing with the mercury of the Jade Pond to become *wu* earth—*wu* earth is the yang elixir.

The yang elixir is the external elixir, and the external elixir is the root of the elixir. The Golden Flower is it, the True Seed is it, the Yellow Sprout is it, the White Snow is it. Taking the external elixir as the mother of the internal elixir, thus it is said to have a beginning as the Mother of All Under Heaven. The mother has a sacred name, called Yang Lead. Where there is Yang Lead as mother, there is Yin Mercury as child. Yin Mercury is the postnatal qi of the child, while Yang Lead is the prenatal qi of the mother. When the external Yang Lead subdues the internal Yin Mercury, mother and child meet—hence it is said, "Know the child."

Yet this coming of Yang Lead requires the wondrous application of fire. For Lead is born in the *kan* palace, sunken and unmoving. To capture and restrain the true Mercury of the *li* palace, one must employ fierce fire for intense refinement, whereupon it soars upward. After meeting with true Mercury, one should then guard the city and bathe, refraining from applying fierce fire. At first, the mother comes longing for the child; later, the child lingers longing for the mother—thus it is said, "Having known the child, return to guard the mother." When mother and child long for one another without end, the Great Elixir is accomplished.

The Great Elixir is called the internal medicine; the Sacred Embryo is this, the Infant is this, the True Person is this. In nurturing the internal elixir, one must possess the natural true fire, continuously simmering within the earthen crucible, and also employ the external furnace’s yin-yang talismanic fire, diligently adjusting its intensity. Through the operations of withdrawal and addition, form is transformed into qi, and qi into spirit. When form and spirit are both wondrous, they unite with the Dao in truth. Thus one must block the *dui*, close the gate, and devote oneself to this practice for life.

There are two kinds of nurturing: the internal civil fire and the external talismanic fire. Preserving it for ten months, one abandons effortful action to realize effortless action—hence one dare not busy oneself with external affairs, disturbing the inner stillness of the chamber. As the *Cantong Qi* states: "Securely seal their juncture, striving to make it complete and firm. Observe and attend with utmost care, carefully regulating warmth and coolness. Circulate through the twelve nodes, and when each node is exhausted, renew your closeness." This is the work of nurturing.

The inner realm does not leak outward, the outer realm does not intrude inward—blocking the *dui* and closing the gate is the essential technique. If one does not block the *dui*, one will daily engage with external affairs. Daoists often discuss worldly matters, wishing to aid external endeavors, yet first harming their inner cultivation. When the mouth opens, spirit and qi scatter; when the mind is disordered, the fire’s work grows cold. The great path of longevity, I fear, will not be achieved—hence it is said, "If you assist in such affairs, you will never recover for life."

The "small" refers to the golden light of the elixir, its form like a grain of millet—thus it is called "small." To see this small is called "bright." The "yielding" refers to the body’s royal water, whose qi is fundamentally harmonious—thus it is called "yielding." To guard this yielding is called "strong." The "light" is spirit, the very golden light. The "bright" is qi, the very golden essence. When golden light envelops golden essence, light and brightness are concealed within, spirit and qi interdependent. The work of embryo nurturing is accomplished, and the body escapes calamity—thus it is said, "Use its light, return to its brightness, leaving no misfortune for the body." This is the Way of inheriting the constant, the cultivation of long life and enduring vision.

To "inherit the constant" is to guard one’s true constancy. My mountain appraisal: This chapter is clarified sentence by sentence, each word extracted with precision—the Way of the Returned Elixir is fully revealed here. I have often said that the scriptures of the Most High are concise yet comprehensive, even true immortals cannot fully comprehend them—how exceptionally clear is this commentary! How joyous! How delightful!

Chapter Fifty-Three
*(He Shang Gong’s commentary titles this the "Chapter of Increasing Evidence"; Peng’s version calls it the "Chapter of Unwavering Resolve")*

Were I to possess unwavering knowledge,
I would walk the great Dao,
fearing only deviation.
The great Dao is utterly smooth,
yet the people prefer byways.

Courts are swept clean,
fields lie fallow,
granaries stand empty.
They don fine brocades,
gird themselves with sharp swords,
surfeit on food and drink,
hoard wealth in excess—
this is called the "thieving pole."
How far from the Dao!

*I*: refers to my lineage, not the Old Sage’s self-reference.
*Unwavering (介然)*: steadfast resolve.
My lineage’s nature is perfectly luminous;
were I to stabilize myself with unwavering resolve,
I would possess both steadfastness and complete awakening.
To personally walk the great Dao,
fearing only deviation—
for deviation follows outward dispersion,
greatly depleting my true essence,
and thus is to be feared.

Accumulating essence and amassing qi
can refine one into truth—
this is the great Dao’s level path,
the shared road all should follow.
Yet the people’s hearts prefer byways,
competing to enter crooked paths.
Delusion invites misfortune;
essence withers, spirit scatters.

Like a court’s former halls,
once purged and stripped,
the primordial yang is utterly lost.
Like fields’ boundaries,
all fallen to waste,
the gates and orifices are wholly blocked.
Like granaries’ stores,
all reduced to emptiness,
the essence and qi are entirely gone.

They merely don fine brocades,
gird themselves with sharp swords,
chase after feasts and revelry,
vainly imagining their wealth sufficient.
Such ones fix their hearts on pilfering the Dao’s name,
like thieves who use poles to hook objects over walls—
this is called the "thieving pole" and nothing more.
How far from the Dao!
How far from the Dao!

**Chapter Fifty-Four**
*(He Shang Gong’s commentary titles this "The Chapter on Cultivating Observation"; Peng’s edition titles it "The Chapter on Skillful Establishment")*

What is skillfully established cannot be uprooted;
what is skillfully embraced cannot slip away.
Thus descendants’ sacrifices never cease.

Cultivate it in the self—its virtue becomes true.
Cultivate it in the family—its virtue becomes abundant.
Cultivate it in the village—its virtue becomes enduring.
Cultivate it in the state—its virtue becomes bountiful.
Cultivate it in the world—its virtue becomes universal.

Therefore:
Observe the self through the self;
observe the family through the family;
observe the village through the village;
observe the state through the state;
observe the world through the world.
How do I know the world is thus?
By this.

Establishing the center and standing firm—thus it cannot be uprooted.
Embracing the One without separation—thus it cannot slip away.
Children beget grandchildren, manifesting through division—thus sacrifices never cease.

Self, family, village, state, and world—these are the realms where virtue is cultivated.
*With* (由): through.
*Observe* (观): to demonstrate the method.
Through the self, demonstrate the Dharma-body;
extend this to demonstrate the Dharma-body in family, state, and world—
all are this true body.
When virtue is complete in the self, the demonstration is inexhaustible,
thus by this I universally know the world.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Five**
*(He Shang Gong’s commentary titles this "The Chapter on the Mysterious Token"; Peng’s edition titles it "The Chapter on Embracing Virtue")*

One who embraces profound virtue
is like an infant:
poisonous creatures do not sting,
fierce beasts do not seize,
grasping birds of prey do not strike.
Bones weak, sinews soft, yet the grip is firm.
Unknowing of the union of female and male, yet the organ stirs—
such is the perfection of essence.
All day crying, yet the throat does not grow hoarse—
such is the perfection of harmony.

To know harmony is called constancy;
to know constancy is called clarity.
To augment life is called auspicious.
The heart directing qi is called force.

When things reach their prime, they age—
this is called "not the Dao."
What is not the Dao soon perishes.

*Embrace* (含): to hold within.
Those who hold virtue profoundly are true persons.
The heart of the true person never loses the heart of an infant,
thus they are like an infant, utterly forgetful of externals.
Therefore, noxious creatures, beasts, or birds harm them.
Bones weak, sinews soft—this is the body of a child,
thus the grip is firm.
People know the joy of male-female union,
yet such knowledge leads to the stirring of the organ and the loss of life.
The infant, without knowledge, embodies the perfection of pure essence—
this is the absence of desire.
*Organ* (朘): the infant’s yin; also, contraction.
Vulgarly, *retraction* is called *朘缩*—contraction without arousal.
People, through anger, disrupt their harmony, and their voices grow hoarse.
The infant, however, may cry and wail yet never grow hoarse.
*Cry* (号) is calling; *wail* (嗌) is sobbing.
All day calling and sobbing without hoarseness—
such is the perfection of harmony.
One who knows harmony abides in constancy without peril.
I who know constancy attain true clarity.
What benefits human life is the infant’s auspicious harmony.
Yet if one possesses knowledge and awareness,
using the heart to direct qi,
they turn from softness to force.
Those who love force in the world—
have they ever observed this in things?
The explanation of the final three lines appears in Chapter Thirty.

(This chapter is titled *The Chapter of Mysterious Virtue* in the Heshang Gong commentary and *The Chapter of Mysterious Sameness* in Peng’s version.)

Those who know do not speak; those who speak do not know.
Block the apertures, shut the gates,
blunt the sharpness, untangle the knots,
soften the light, merge with the dust—
this is called *mysterious sameness*.

It cannot be approached with intimacy,
nor distanced with estrangement;
it cannot be gained through profit,
nor harmed by injury;
it cannot be exalted as noble,
nor debased as lowly.
Thus it is most precious under heaven.

Those who *know* are those who have attained through practice. *Not speaking* is because speech is difficult. For their understanding runs deep, and though the heart wishes to speak, the mouth cannot keep pace. If one merely exalts speech, claiming some immortal’s scripture or true transmission has exhausted all that needs to be said—no teacher’s guidance required, the Dao can be practiced—how can they know that while the truth is indeed true, its subtle details, its layered metaphors, its profound mechanisms may yet elude them? Moreover, ascending to truth and entering the Dao ultimately depend on the *returned elixir*. Only when the principle of the returned elixir is clear can one attain the Dao.

Now, let me ask you:
*Qian* and *Kun*, *Kan* and *Dui*—what trigrams are these?
Dragon and tiger, mercury and lead—what alchemical substances are these?
Guest and host, female and male—how are they distinguished in function?
The indistinct and the obscure—how are they embodied?
The floating and the sinking, the inverted and the upright—how are they arranged?
Without clarity on the *mysterious female*, one cannot engage in transformation.
Without summoning the *Yellow Dame*, how can union be achieved?
Beyond this, words are hard to convey,
authority hard to establish,
the primary scripture hard to find,
the firing times hard to grasp.

Furthermore, as the ancients said: *The great matter of the golden elixir must be pursued with the help of one who has power.* Where in which book is the wondrous use of such a powerful one recorded? Moreover, the practice and discipline of the work include elements that cannot be conveyed by books alone: cutting off sensual desire,轻财利, relinquishing affection, guarding virtue—these are all matters of personal cultivation, not things that can be resolved through texts.

Only when a sincere heart seeks the Dao,
moving heaven above,
dissolving worldly thoughts,
and receiving the silent aid of the numinous,
can one come to know the Dao.
To seek a steed by following a picture—how can this suffice to grasp its subtleties?
Thus, *those who know do not speak; those who speak do not know*—because there are things that are difficult to speak of.

Some may ask: *"If knowing is indeed difficult to speak of, how can those who are yet unenlightened come to hear of it? Must there not be those who can speak to guide their knowing?"* The alchemical classics say: *Only with a teacher’s oral instructions can one attain the Dao.* This seems to imply that those who know must speak, and those who speak must know. Let me explain further.

*Oral instructions* are intimate words, subtle words—layer upon layer of breakthroughs, not mere isolated phrases or a few sentences. When the Yellow Emperor sought out Guangchengzi, they spoke in detail for three days. When my teacher met Ancestor Zhong, they sat in discussion for ten days. Even for those of heavenly sincerity and the highest spiritual capacity, a few words cannot suffice—how much less for those below?

Today, people mistake *oral instructions* for shortcuts; this is wrong. When oral instructions are incomplete, the teacher departs and returns. During discussion, one listens closely and commits to memory, so as to connect with ancient texts and illuminate the stages. This is the wonder of oral instructions. When one has read many alchemical classics, a single hearing of oral instructions can bring sudden awakening. The classics verify the instructions, and the instructions verify the classics—true and unfailing, in accord with the great Dao. This is why a true teacher must be sought, and why mere cleverness cannot be relied upon.

Moreover, the root of a true teacher lies in one who has cultivated virtue and refined it into attainment. How could they not know the profound and secret words that heaven bestows upon the good and withholds from the unworthy? If one knows yet speaks lightly of them, why did Master Ziyang receive three divine reprimands? Their silence is not unwillingness to speak, but a reluctance to speak lightly.

Even when great vessels meet and the transmission is urgent, the teacher must repeatedly test and refine the disciple. Only after the disciple’s diligence and the teacher’s toil do they swear an oath, light incense, and pass on the bowl of transmission. From this day forward, the lineage extends into the future—take care not to hear and fail to practice, nor to receive and hoard in secret. Given all this, is speaking not difficult?

In my commentary on the *Daodejing*, though more detailed than those of earlier scholars, each chapter’s annotations follow the text and leave its words intact, unable to fully articulate the wondrous mechanisms of the alchemical Dao in complete passages. Heroes and aspirants, if you can find a wise teacher to point them out, attaining the method will be all the easier.

*Blocking the apertures and shutting the gates* nurtures spirit and qi.
*Blunting sharpness* without being blunted by sharpness—this is the ability to guard weakness.
*Untangling knots* without being entangled by them—this is the ability to remain serene.
*Softening the light* to conceal its brilliance,
*merging with the dust* to transcend it—
Mencius said: *The sage’s relation to the people is also like this.*
This is the essence of *mysterious sameness*.

Thus, it cannot be approached with intimacy, nor distanced with estrangement. If it could be approached with intimacy, it could also be distanced with estrangement. The same applies to profit and harm, nobility and lowliness. Therefore, it is the most precious under heaven—nothing surpasses the sage’s virtue.

**Chapter Fifty-Seven**
(In the Heshang Gong commentary, titled *The Chapter of Pure Wind*; in Peng’s version, *The Chapter of Governing with Rectitude*)

Govern the state with rectitude,
deploy troops with奇,
win the world through non-interference.
How do I know this is so?
By this.

The world abounds with taboos, and the people grow ever poorer. The people possess many sharp tools, and the state sinks deeper into chaos. The people cultivate many clever skills, and strange artifacts proliferate. Laws and decrees multiply, and thieves and bandits abound. Thus the sage declares: *I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves; I cherish stillness, and the people rectify themselves; I engage in no affairs, and the people enrich themselves; I harbor no desires, and the people return to simplicity.*

Rectitude is not mere idle governance—it truly embodies the teaching of reverence, moving people to respond with sincerity. The extraordinary is not mere deceit—it truly embodies the teaching of quiet mastery, making one’s intentions inscrutable.
