Thus one must govern the state with rectitude and deploy troops with the extraordinary. To win the world through non-interference is to achieve success through non-action. By this, I mean: the Way of cultivating the self reveals the Way of governing the world.

When the court teems with taboos, corrupt and base ministers advance, impoverishing the people. When the people possess many sharp tools, scheming and deceitful men arise, plunging the state into deeper chaos. When clever skills please the ruler, strange and extravagant objects multiply. When laws and decrees disdain the people, thieves and bandits flourish. These are all perversions of good governance.

Thus the sage’s words are recalled to observe the constancy of the Way of governance—it lies solely in the distinction between having desires and having none.

**Chapter Fifty-Eight**
*(In the Heshang Gong commentary, titled *The Chapter of Harmonious Transformation*; in Peng’s version, *The Chapter of Governance*)*

When governance is muted and unobtrusive, the people are simple and sincere.
When governance is sharp and exacting, the people are restless and lacking.
Misfortune—fortune leans upon it;
Fortune—misfortune lurks within it.
Who knows their ultimate end? Is there no constancy?
Rectitude reverts to the extraordinary,
Goodness reverts to the ominous.
The people’s delusion has endured for long indeed.

Therefore the sage is square but does not cut,
sharp but does not wound,
straight but does not stretch,
luminous but does not dazzle.

*Muted and unobtrusive (閟閟)*: a manner of honest simplicity.
*Simple and sincere (醇醇)*: a state of tranquil composure.
When governance fosters simplicity, the people rest in stillness.
*Sharp and exacting (察察)*: harsh and demanding.
*Restless and lacking (缺缺)*: negligent and careless.
When governance is harsh and demanding, the people grow negligent.

The beginnings of fortune and misfortune’s mutual dependence—people should know their ultimate end and uphold constancy. The phrase *"who knows"* implies a lament that people do not understand. If one does not know the ultimate end, how can there be any who uphold constancy?

Rectitude, the straight path, reverts to the extraordinary and perverse—all because there is no constancy. Goodness, originally auspicious, reverts to the ominous and baleful—also because there is no constancy. If this is so, how could the people’s descent into delusion be the fault of a single morning or evening?

*Cut*: to sever.
*Wound*: to injure.
*Stretch*: to act without restraint.
*Dazzle*: to display ostentation.
These four are the delusions of the people.

Only the sage is vast and uncornered, requiring no severing;
pure and self-contained, avoiding injury;
straight yet restrained, never overreaching;
radiant yet not dazzling—thus walking the true path of the Dao.

**Chapter Fifty-Nine**
*(In the Heshang Gong commentary, titled *The Chapter of Guarding the Dao*; in Peng’s version, *The Chapter of Roots and Foundations*)*

Governing people and serving Heaven—nothing compares to frugality; this is called "early compliance." "Early compliance" means accumulating virtue in abundance. Accumulating virtue in abundance leads to invincibility; being invincible means none can fathom its limits. When none can fathom its limits, one may possess the state. Possessing the state’s foundational power, one may endure long. This is the Dao of deep roots and firm stems, the Way of long life and enduring vision.

*Frugality*: thrift.
*Early compliance*: referring to enriching the state and pacifying the people.
*Accumulating virtue*: referring to the people’s peace and the state’s prosperity.
*Invincibility*: triumph.
To say it plainly: governing with reverence for thrift enables the enrichment of the state and pacification of the people, ensuring their peace and the state’s prosperity, and rendering all battles victorious. Being ever-victorious, none can discern its bounds. Gradually, with profound benevolence and deep kindness, one may surely attain the state. The *state* here refers to the nation’s foundation. Once the foundation is established, one casts true cinnabar into the jade pool, first refining the lead-mother. When the lead-mother captures the mercury-child, the state may be enjoyed long. This is the cultivation of deep roots and firm stems, the Way of long life and enduring vision.

*Roots and stems*: returning to the root to subdue one’s qi, nurturing the stem to preserve one’s spirit.

**Chapter Sixty**
*(In the Heshang Gong commentary, titled *The Chapter of Occupying the Throne*; in Peng’s version, *The Chapter of Refining the Delicate*)*

Governing a great state is like cooking a small fish.

With the Dao presiding over the world, its ghosts lose their numinosity.
Not that its ghosts lose their numinosity—
their numinosity does no harm to people.
Not that their numinosity does no harm to people—
the sage also does no harm to people.
When the two do not harm one another,
their virtues converge and return.

Cooking differs from cutting: cutting values separation, while cooking values harmony.
*Small fish*: small things.
Viewing a great state as a small thing lies solely in harmonizing the people’s sentiments.

*Ghosts*: demons.
*Numinosity*: spirits.
*Does no harm*: does not disturb.
This means that with the Dao as the foundation of governance, though demons possess spirit, they do not disturb.
It is not that they do not disturb people—
the sage governs with stillness and does not disturb people,
so how dare demons disturb them?
Thus, demons and people forget one another,
and the sage’s virtue subdues all.

*Converge and return*: all submit.

**Chapter Sixty-One**
*(In the Heshang Gong commentary, titled *The Chapter of Humble Virtue*; in Peng’s version, *The Chapter of Lower Currents*)*

A great state is the lower current,
the confluence of the world,
the female of the world.
The female always overcomes the male with stillness,
using stillness as her lower position.

Thus, when a great state humbles itself before a small state,
it wins over the small state.
When a small state humbles itself before a great state,
it wins over the great state.
Therefore, some win by humbling themselves,
while others win by being humble.

A great state, being too great, desires to shelter others.
A small state, being too small, desires to serve others.
When both attain what they desire,
the greater should remain humble.

This chapter instructs the lords of the world to cultivate humility in order to embrace smaller states.
*Lower current*: where all waters converge, likened to a great state uniting lesser ones.
Thus, a great state is the lower current—not that it dwells in a lowly position.
It unites tributary states, hence called the confluence of the world.
It should employ gentle virtue, thus compared to the female of the world.

*Female*: the Way of softness.
*Male*: the Way of hardness.
Though small states may be hard, a great state overcomes them with soft stillness,
so stillness is the virtue of humility.

When a great state humbles itself before a small state,
the small state delights in serving as its vassal,
and each year may offer tribute.
When a small state humbles itself before a great state,
the great state often rewards it,
and each year may bestow gifts.
Thus, some win by humbling themselves,
while others win by being humble.
Great and small naturally rely on one another.

A great state, in its excess, resembles the *Great Exceeding* hexagram:
an old man obtaining a young woman—
excessive yet harmonious—
thus possessing the virtue of sheltering others,
meaning it seeks to preserve small states.
A small state, in its modest success, resembles the *Small Exceeding* hexagram:
moving with the times—
excessive yet properly aligned—
thus possessing the Way of serving others,
meaning it seeks to depend on great states.
When great and small each attain their desires,
it is because the greater humbles itself before the lesser.
Otherwise, though the ruler of a small state may wish to serve a great state,
if the great state oppresses it,
the small state will not willingly submit.

The grand purport of this teaching is to instruct the lords of great states in the principle of gentle reciprocity with distant lands,
yet its subtle implications for self-cultivation are equally profound.

Let us now expound it:

The *great state* is Kunlun.
The *lower current* is the primordial sea.
The fluids of Kunlun descend into the primordial sea,
meaning the primordial sea connects with Kunlun—
thus, a great state is the lower current,
not that Kunlun dwells in a lowly position.

The *lower current* is the confluence of the world.
*Confluence*: attachment.
All submission and dependence reside here—
this is the Yellow Court.

The virtue of the lower current is the female of the world.
*Female*: softness.
Concentrating qi to achieve softness lies here,
hence it is called the female gate.

Within the female gate’s cavity resides primordial essence.
By quietly gathering kidney qi within it,
the kidneys are first regarded as the female gate,
and the heart as the male.
Yet now, the self is considered female,
and the other male—
this reverses hardness and softness,
not female and male.

The heart’s fire is by nature hard and restless;
water’s stillness can restrain fire’s movement—
this is the coming of lead,
which can restrain mercury’s flight.

*Using stillness as her lower position*:
employing silence and softness,
cultivating humility to stabilize the heart-mind.

Thus, Kunlun’s sweet fluids flow into the primordial sea.
The fluids transform into qi,
entering the cinnabar field.
A great state humbling itself before a small state
is like descending from Kunlun to the cinnabar field.
*Winning over the small state*:
gathering the cinnabar field’s metal-water,
reversing its flow to the celestial valley.
A small state humbling itself before a great state
is like ascending from the cinnabar field to Kunlun.
*Winning over the great state*:
merging Kunlun’s golden fluids,
both descending into the Yellow Court.

Thus, when Kunlun’s fluids flow down to the cinnabar field to generate qi,
gathering the cinnabar field’s qi is *winning by humbling oneself*.
Or when the cinnabar field’s qi ascends to Kunlun to generate fluids,
swallowing Kunlun’s fluids is *winning by being humble*.

The *great state’s excess* is modeled on the Dao of the *Yi*.
The *Marsh Wind* hexagram is favorable for advancement,
its image likening marsh water submerging wood—
when marsh waters rise high,
all trees are submerged,
this is the image of *Great Exceeding*.
When Kunlun’s sweet dew descends,
it originally advances with success,
aligning perfectly with *Great Exceeding*—
spirit transforms into qi,
and qi transforms into essence,
which fills the cinnabar field.
Thus, it possesses the virtue of sheltering others.

The *small state’s modest success* is also modeled on the Dao of the *Yi*.
The *Thunder Mountain* hexagram is favorable for maintaining integrity,
its image likening a bird’s lingering call—
just as a flying bird should descend,
so its call may be heard,
this is the image of *Small Exceeding*.
When the lower cinnabar field’s true fluids are contained within,
originally maintaining integrity is auspicious,
aligning perfectly with *Small Exceeding*—
essence generates qi,
and qi generates spirit,
which ascends to depend on the Yellow Court.
Thus, it possesses the Way of serving others.

One ascends and one descends, mutually supporting and preserving,
inverting Heaven and Earth,
reversing the flow of the Yellow River.
The wondrous secret of cultivating the body lies in nothing beyond this.

**Supplementary Annotation:**
Thus, some say: "When the Yellow Court resides below to gather the fluids of Kunlun, it is *taking by descending*."
Others say: "When the Yellow Court illuminates below to absorb the qi of the cinnabar field, it is *taking by descending*."
This meaning may also be considered.

**Wu Shan’s Commentary:**
The Yellow Court is the palace for nurturing the heart,
while the *mysterious gate* is the source for nurturing the kidneys.
Now, what nurtures the heart is called the *mysterious gate*—
for in preserving spirit and guarding essence, the Dao values stillness.
In congealing spirit and gathering qi, the Dao values softness.
In the empty spirit receiving qi, the Dao values humility.
In guarding spirit and awaiting qi, the Dao values weakness.
In spirit uniting with qi, the Dao values lowliness.
In harmonizing spirit and qi, the Dao values harmony.
The *mysterious gate* possesses the six virtues of stillness, softness, humility, weakness, lowliness, and harmony.
Thus, it borrows the name of the *mysterious gate* to provisionally replace the name of the Yellow Court—
this is not to move the gate’s location into the Yellow Court.
Those who practice the elixir often invert the trigrams and lines of the hexagrams, frequently borrowing names from one another.
When Hanxu speaks plainly, and Master Huidao further elucidates,
each phrase is a true secret,
sweeping away a thousand analogies.
Those who attain it should treasure it; do not show it to the unworthy.

---

**Chapter Sixty-Two**
*(He Shanggong’s edition titles this "The Dao Chapter"; Peng’s edition titles it "The Mystery of the Dao")*

The Dao is the mystery of the ten thousand things.
It is the treasure of the good,
and the refuge of the unworthy.
Fair words can be traded in the marketplace,
and noble conduct can elevate a person.
If people are unworthy, how can they be abandoned?
Thus, when the Son of Heaven is established and the Three Dukes are appointed,
though one may offer a jade disk preceded by a team of four horses,
it is not as valuable as sitting and advancing in this Dao.

Why was this Dao prized in ancient times?
Was it not said: "Seek and you shall obtain; with it, even the guilty may be absolved"?
Thus, it is the most precious under heaven.

"The mystery of the ten thousand things" means the source of creation.
The good treasure it to refine the elixir and become a sage.
Even the unworthy preserve it to replenish qi and prolong life.
This is the utmost impartiality of the Dao.

Fine words about this Dao can make followers gather like a marketplace.
Exalted practice of this Dao can elevate a person.
The Dao does not fail anyone.
Those who abandon the Dao and run astray are unworthy themselves—
the Dao has never abandoned anyone.
Thus, when comparing the noble ones like the Son of Heaven and the Three Dukes,
or the precious objects like jade disks and teams of four horses,
with the Dao,
none is as valuable as sitting and advancing in this Dao.

*Sitting* means *guarding*. As in the *Zuozhuan*: "The people of Chu *guarded* their northern gate."
It also means *kneeling*. As in the *Classic of Rites*: "When *kneeling*, one shifts position."

Why was this Dao prized in ancient times?
Because by seeking it, one obtains it,
and by obtaining it, one is absolved of guilt.
Thus, nothing under heaven is as precious as this.
*"Was it not said"* is an archaic phrase meaning *"Did the ancients not say?"*

---

**Chapter Sixty-Three**
*(He Shanggong’s edition titles this "Beginning with Grace"; Peng’s edition titles it "The Great Chapter")*

Act without acting,
attend to affairs without affairs,
taste without tasting.
Regard the great as small, the many as few,
and repay resentment with virtue.
Address difficulties while they are easy,
achieve greatness while it is small.
The difficult affairs of the world must arise from the easy.
The great affairs of the world must arise from the small.
Thus, the sage never acts as great,
and therefore accomplishes greatness.

One who makes promises lightly will surely lack trust;
one who treats things lightly will surely encounter many difficulties.
Thus, the sage treats even the difficult as difficult,
and therefore ultimately encounters no difficulty.

*Acting without acting*, *attending to affairs without affairs*, *tasting without tasting*—
all refer to tranquility and simplicity.
*Regarding the great as small, the many as few*—
this is impartiality in dealing with things.
*Repaying resentment with virtue*—
responding with uprightness is itself repaying with virtue,
not adding extra generosity.

*Addressing difficulties while they are easy*—
when others treat things lightly, I alone treat them as difficult;
this is not putting the difficult before the easy.
*Achieving greatness while it is small*—
disdaining small conduct will ultimately harm great virtue.
This aligns with the *Book of Documents*.
Thus, it is also said: "The difficult affairs of the world must arise from the easy.
The great affairs of the world must arise from the small."
This is clearly understood.

*Arising from the easy* begins with caution against treating things lightly.
*Arising from the small* begins with valuing small conduct.
The sage never acts as great,
and therefore accomplishes greatness—
further clarifying that the above must *arise from the small*.

To emphasize that it must *arise from the easy*,
another argument is presented:
For example, one who makes promises lightly will surely become untrustworthy.
Thus, one who speaks lightly will surely end as one whose actions are difficult to accomplish.
Therefore, the sage treats even the undifficult as difficult,
and thus ultimately encounters no difficulty.

---

**Chapter Sixty-Four**

What is secure is easy to maintain;
what has no sign is easy to plan for.
What is fragile is easy to break;
what is minute is easy to scatter.
Act before things arise,
govern before disorder begins.
A tree of arm-spanning girth grows from a tiny sprout.
A nine-tiered terrace rises from a basket of earth.
A journey of a thousand *li* begins with a single step.
One who acts will fail;
one who grasps will lose.
The sage acts not, and thus does not fail.
He does not grasp, and thus does not lose.

In their undertakings, people often fail when near completion.
If one is as cautious at the end as at the beginning,
there will be no failure.

Thus, the sage desires what others do not desire,
and does not value rare goods.
He learns what others do not learn,
restoring what the multitude has passed over,
to assist the self-so of the ten thousand things without daring to act.

For those cultivating the great Dao:
What is secure is easy to maintain—otherwise, when trouble arises, it is difficult to hold.
What has no sign is easy to plan for—otherwise, when things have gone astray, it is difficult to plan.
The fragile is easy to break; the minute is easy to scatter—
this speaks of the difficulty of preserving the tender and the challenge of seizing the small.

At the stage of non-being, action ensures what is secure may be maintained, and what has no sign may be planned for. Governing before disorder arises ensures the fragile does not break and the minute does not scatter. How can one not be cautious in their method?

Moreover, progress in skill must be gradual, lest there be cause for retreat. It is like a living tree, growing from small to great; like building a terrace, rising from low to high; like traveling a road, advancing from near to far. These are clear proofs.

Those who act desire success but bring about failure. Those who grasp desire gain but incur loss. The sage, however, practices non-action. Yet even in non-action and non-grasping, one must remain unwavering from beginning to end to achieve completion.

I have often observed foolish people undertaking tasks, many failing when near success. How can one not be as cautious at the end as at the beginning?

Only the sage desires what others do not desire—desiring, he cherishes the Dao; not desiring, he scorns goods and values virtue. And he learns what others do not learn—learning, he possesses method; not learning, he remains serene in non-action. This is how he reverses the excesses of the multitude, assisting the self-so principle of the ten thousand things without daring to act.

As stated in the fifty-seventh chapter: *"I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves; I cherish stillness, and the people rectify themselves; I do nothing, and the people enrich themselves; I have no desires, and the people simplify themselves."* This is precisely the same principle.

The version of Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this the "Chapter on Pristine Virtue," while Peng’s version calls it the "Chapter on the People’s Simplicity."

Those adept in the Dao in ancient times did not seek to enlighten the people but instead to render them simple. The people are difficult to govern because of their excessive cleverness. Governing the state through cleverness is the state’s ruin; governing the state without cleverness is the state’s blessing. To understand these two paths is to grasp the model. One who comprehends this model is said to possess mysterious virtue. Mysterious virtue runs deep and far, reversing the course of things, and thus attaining great harmony.

The Dao does not value knowledge or discernment, for these diminish its undifferentiated wholeness; it values instead the state of not-knowing and not-discerning, thereby preserving its namelessness. Thus, those adept in the Dao do not seek to enlighten the people but to render them simple.

“The people are difficult to govern because of their excessive cleverness” echoes the earlier saying: “When cunning and wisdom arise, great falsity follows.” “Governing the state through cleverness is the state’s ruin” corresponds to: “The more meticulous the governance, the more deficient the people.” “Governing the state without cleverness is the state’s blessing” aligns with: “Abandon sagacity, discard wisdom, and the people will benefit a hundredfold.”

These two paths—cleverness and non-cleverness—truly determine the state’s fortune or misfortune and serve as the model for governance. One who understands this may be called possessed of mysterious virtue. Mysterious virtue sees profoundly, its radiance reaches far, appearing simple yet not simple, reversing the dullness of things. *Great harmony* means great transformation. Only through such mysterious virtue can one attain this great transformation.

Chapter Sixty-Six. (Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this the "Chapter on Putting Oneself Last," while Peng’s version calls it the "Chapter on Excellence in Humility.")

The reason rivers and seas can be kings of the hundred valleys is that they excel in staying low—thus they become kings of the hundred valleys. Therefore, when the sage wishes to rise above the people, they must speak as though beneath them. When they wish to lead the people, they must place themselves behind. Thus, though the sage stands above, the people do not feel burdened; though they stand ahead, the people do not feel harmed. Therefore, the world delights in exalting them without weariness. Because they do not contend, none in the world can contend with them.

Rivers and seas excel in staying low, so all streams flow toward them—hence they are called kings of the hundred valleys. This illustrates the sage. To rise above the people while speaking humbly is what was previously called “the high takes the low as its foundation.” To lead the people while placing oneself behind is what was previously called “putting oneself last, yet finding oneself first.” Thus, the sage’s virtue is empty and harmonious: standing above, they possess no awe-inspiring majesty, yet the people do not feign reverence; standing ahead, they carry no overbearing force, yet the people feel no need to guard against harm. Therefore, the world exalts the sage with joy and never tires of it. None can contend with them because the sage, like rivers and seas that excel in staying low, leaves the hundred valleys with no way to divide their power.

Chapter Sixty-Seven
(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this the "Chapter on the Three Treasures," and Peng’s version concurs.)

The world all calls me great, yet I seem unworthy. Precisely because I am great, I seem unworthy. Were I worthy, I would long since have been small!

I possess three treasures, which I hold and cherish:
The first is compassion,
The second is frugality,
The third is not daring to be first in the world.
Through compassion, one can be courageous;
Through frugality, one can be expansive;
Through not daring to be first in the world, one can become the vessel of leadership.

Now, to abandon compassion yet seek courage,
To abandon frugality yet seek expansion,
To abandon the last yet strive to be first—
This is the path to death.

With compassion, one triumphs in battle and stands firm in defense.
Heaven will save them, shielding them with compassion.

The Dao is fundamentally without form or body, yet it is called great. If it had a form or body to imitate, it would no longer resemble its greatness. Only the great cannot be named as great, and so it does not resemble what people call great. If it resembled what people call great, then even the great would be small. Hence it is said, “Were it worthy, it would long since have been small!”

The highest speaks of the three treasures, which are but scattered manifestations of greatness:
The first is compassion—compassion must be resolute in benevolence, thus it can be courageous.
The second is frugality—frugality must excel in accumulation, thus it can be expansive.
The third is not daring to be first in the world—not daring to be first means great vessels mature late, thus one can become the vessel of leadership.

Yet to abandon compassion for courage is to harbor a heart of cruelty.
To abandon frugality for expansion is to harbor a heart of greed.
To abandon the last for the first is to harbor a heart of contention.
All these are paths to death.

Though the three treasures are presented in order, the first is most noble, so compassion is emphasized to complete the discourse.
With compassion, one triumphs in battle—seeing righteousness, one must act.
With it, one stands firm in defense—preserving benevolence, one must be steadfast.
“Saving them” refers to humanity: Heaven will save people, and it too must shield them with compassion.

How great is the Dao of compassion!

Chapter Sixty-Eight
(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this the "Chapter on Matching Heaven," and Peng’s version concurs.)

A masterful warrior is not martial.
A masterful fighter is not enraged.
A masterful conqueror does not contend.
A masterful leader humbles himself before others.
This is called the virtue of non-contention,
This is called the power of employing others,
This is called matching the ancient ultimate of Heaven.

Reliance on force is called martiality,
Reliance on qi is called rage,
Engagement in slaughter is called contention—
None of these are the way of the masterful warrior, the masterful fighter, or the masterful conqueror.

Of old, those who masterfully employed others,
Even when facing a tiger-like general among their foes,
Would always humble themselves to summon him,
Causing him to lay down his armor and come in surrender.
This is called the virtue of non-contention,
That is, the power of employing others’ strength.
Moreover, such virtue suffices to match Heaven,
For Heaven does not vie for merit yet accomplishes all,
The ancient ultimate indeed.

The esoteric meaning of this chapter,
For those who cultivate the elixir,
Speaks of taming the White Tiger by first subduing the True Dragon.
For the White Tiger is a creature difficult to restrain,
And if the Dragon does not humble itself, the Tiger will surely flee.
Only when the self is refined without impurity
Can one wield the Tiger’s power.

Chapter Sixty-Nine
(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this the "Chapter on the Mysterious Application," and Peng’s version titles it the "Chapter on Underestimating the Enemy.")

The strategist has a saying:
“I dare not act as host but as guest,
Dare not advance an inch but retreat a foot.”
This is called marching without marching,
Repelling without arms,
Engaging without an enemy,
Wielding without weapons.
No misfortune is greater than underestimating the enemy;
Underestimating the enemy nearly forfeits my treasure.
Thus, when opposing forces clash,
The grieving side prevails.

In warfare, let the other be host while I remain guest,
Yielding to their challenge,
Thus able to rest while they toil,
Advancing an inch and retreating a foot.
If I allow them to pursue,
I can then strike unexpectedly and capture them—
This is the subtle strategy of warfare.

“Marching without marching” means having no feet:
The left step is *chì*, the right *chù*,
Without both feet, one cannot march.
“Repelling without arms”—how can one repel without arms?
“Engaging without an enemy”—*rēng* means mutual reliance;
If enemies rely on one another, without mutual engagement, there is no enmity.
Weapons are instruments of advantage;
The warrior wields them to charge.
Without weapons, one cannot fight.

No misfortune is greater than underestimating the enemy;
Underestimating the enemy nearly forfeits my treasure.
The greatest treasure is benevolence and righteousness.
Thus, when opposing forces clash,
Victory must belong to the grieving side—
“Grieving” echoes the previous chapter’s “compassion in battle.”

This chapter also contains the esoteric secret of the Returned Elixir,
Comparing warfare to tending the furnace.
“I” refers to cinnabar:
My household’s cinnabar dare not act as host
But must enter the other’s jade pool,
There to refine true essence—
Thus, the other becomes host and I the guest,
A reversal of host and guest.

“Advancing and retreating” refer to firing times:
Advancing an inch and retreating a foot
Does not mean failing to apply fire,
But rather exercising caution in firing,
Fearing missteps in the process.

“Marching without marching”—
Words fail to communicate, kindness and authority do not reach.
“Repelling without arms”—
The firing schedule is unclear, the matchmaker not yet found.
“Engaging without an enemy”—
Dragon and Tiger are unbalanced, weights do not match.
“Wielding without weapons”—
The talisman has not been transmitted, the medicinal ingredients not obtained.
How dare one tend the furnace and apply effort,
Risking the loss of my treasure?
My treasure: cinnabar.
Master Zǐyáng said:
*“When deploying troops, distinguish left and right armies*
*(The Upper Yang commentary notes: left and right refer to the other and myself),*
*Let them be host while I remain guest.*
*I urge you: when facing battle, do not underestimate the enemy,*
*Lest you forfeit my household’s priceless treasure.”*
This is the source of his meaning.

When opposing forces clash—
That is, the other and myself face each other—
If I can use my inherent compassion and benevolence
To bind the other’s metal nature with compliant righteousness,
Then metal will come to submit to wood,
And I will surely prevail.
Thus it is said: the grieving side prevails.
Master Wúshān observes:
*“The true secret is entirely found here—*
*Where else would one seek immortal scriptures?”*

**Chapter Seventy**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this the “Chapter on Knowing Difficulty,” and Peng’s version titles it the “Chapter on Knowing Me.”)*

My words are very easy to know,
Very easy to practice.
Yet under Heaven none can know them,
None can practice them.
Words have their ancestry,
Affairs have their lord.
It is only through ignorance
That they do not know me.
Those who know me are rare,
Thus I am esteemed.
Therefore the sage wears coarse cloth while holding jade.

The Supreme’s words are all of the Dao,
Yet the Dao is not beyond the human body—
Thus it is easy to know, easy to practice.
Yet under Heaven, none can ultimately know or practice it,
As the *Doctrine of the Mean* says:
*“The Way does not prevail—I know why:*
*The wise overshoot it, the foolish do not reach it.”*
*“The Way is not evident—I know why:*
*The worthy overshoot it, the unworthy do not reach it.”*
Thus none can know or practice it.

Yet these words can serve as the origin of cultivation,
And these affairs can set forth the guiding principles of life—
Thus they have their ancestry and their lord.
This is the profound meaning of the Supreme’s scripture.
It is only because people do not know this Dao
That they do not know the Supreme.
The Supreme says:
*“Those who know me are rare,*
*Thus I am esteemed.”*
Like the sage who wears coarse cloth while holding jade:
Coarse cloth is the garb of the lowly,
Worn outwardly while fine jade is held within—
Even if few know, the esteem is undiminished.
This is what is meant by *“withdrawing from the world without being seen,*
*Knowing yet not regretting”—*
Only the sage can do this.

**Chapter Seventy-One**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this the “Chapter on Knowing Illness,” and Peng’s version titles it the “Chapter on the Illness of Illness.”)*

To know yet seem not to know is supreme.
Not to know yet claim to know is illness.
Only by regarding this illness as illness
Does one avoid illness.
The sage is not ill
Because he regards illness as illness—
Thus he is not ill.

To know what others do not know
Is to have a clear Dao and manifest virtue—
Thus it is supreme.
To feign knowledge where there is none
Brings great harm to the self—
Thus it is called illness.
Only by regarding the illness of feigned knowledge as a grievous affliction
Can one be free of the illness of feigned knowledge—
Thus one avoids illness.
The sage’s freedom from illness
Follows the same principle.

**Chapter Seventy-Two**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this the “Chapter on Loving the Self,” and Peng’s version titles it the “Chapter on Fearing Authority.”)*

When the people do not fear authority,
Then authority arrives.
Do not constrict their dwelling,
Do not stifle their livelihood.
It is only by not stifling
That one is not stifled.
Thus the sage knows himself but does not display himself,
Loves himself but does not exalt himself.
Therefore he discards that and takes this.

Authority possesses the qi of stern severity;
If the people do not fear authority,
Heaven will magnify its severity.
Yet Heaven fundamentally favors life and dislikes killing—
It is people who bring killing upon themselves.
Thus one must not constrict their dwelling
Nor stifle their livelihood.

“Constrict” means to narrow;
“Dwelling” is the abode of spirit.
“Stifle” means to cut off;
“Livelihood” is the mechanism of qi.
One should broaden the abode to settle the spirit,
Extend the mechanism to guide the qi.
Only by not cutting off the qi
Can one nurture it without cessation.

Only the sage knows himself but does not display himself,
Loves himself but does not exalt himself.
Knowing himself, he retreats into solitude and self-vigilance,
Daring not flaunt himself before others.

Self-love places preservation and nurturance above all,
Daring not to boast or exalt oneself before others.
Thus one discards the heart of self-display and self-exaltation,
And embraces the heart of self-knowledge and self-love—
All centered on the earnest care of one’s own person.

Chapter Seventy-Three
Courage in daring leads to death;
Courage in not daring leads to life.
These two—one brings benefit, the other harm.
Who knows the reason for Heaven’s aversion?
Even the sage finds this difficult.

The Dao of Heaven:
Though it does not contend, it excels in victory;
Though it does not speak, it excels in response;
Though not summoned, it comes of itself,
Serene yet excelling in strategy.
Heaven’s net is vast and wide,
Sparse yet nothing slips through.

*Courage* means resoluteness.
*Death* means the reckless heart is extinguished.
*Life* means the primordial spirit is revived.
To extinguish the reckless heart requires decisiveness—hence one must dare.
To revive the primordial spirit requires gradual cultivation—hence one must not dare.
Between death and life, harm contains benefit—dying to the heart revives the spirit.
Benefit contains harm—reviving the spirit first requires dying to the heart.
Thus it is said: “One brings benefit, the other harm,”
For benefit and harm complement each other.
Within this lies the Dao of Heaven.

Heaven has its likes and dislikes,
Silently meting out punishment and virtue,
Yet mortals struggle to fathom its reasons.
To speak only of its aversion:
The azure vault’s fundamental nature favors life,
Yet its killing mechanism remains inscrutable.
Thus even the sage, when speaking of Heaven’s Dao,
Does not dare to treat it lightly.

What is the Dao of Heaven like?
It does not dispute with mortals over right and wrong,
Yet in matters of length and brevity, it prevails.
It does not preach goodness to mortals,
Yet when fortune and misfortune arrive, all respond.
Unsummoned, it comes of itself,
Serene yet excelling in strategy.
This describes how swiftly or slowly, well or ill,
Rewards and punishments are meted out—
Applied to each person without the slightest error.

Heaven’s net is vast and wide,
Sparse yet nothing slips through—
How great and dense is its encompassing!
Those who cultivate themselves
Must steadfastly uphold their virtue to align with Heaven.

Chapter Seventy-Four
If the people do not fear death,
How can one threaten them with death?
If the people were always to fear death,
Then for those who act strangely,
I could seize and execute them—who would dare?
Yet there is always one who executes.
To execute in place of the executor
Is like hewing wood in place of the master carpenter.
Few who hew in place of the master carpenter
Do not injure their own hands.

If the people do not fear death, they will not dread execution—
Why then threaten them further with death?
If the people’s hearts always harbored a fear of death,
Then among the multitude, only those who act strangely
Could be seized and executed—who would dare grow complacent in their ways and defy death?
Confucius once said to Ji Kangzi:
“In governing, why resort to execution? If you desire goodness, the people will be good.”
When he later served in Lu, he executed only one man, Shaozheng Mao,
To strike awe into all who acted strangely—this was his intent.
Only thus could he be called a *heavenly officer*.
A *heavenly officer* is one who executes—
Heaven has its executor to eliminate the strange and perverse,
So that no one usurps the power of execution.

To forcibly take the place of the executor in carrying out executions
Is like replacing the master carpenter in hewing wood.
Few who replace the master carpenter in hewing
Do not injure their own hands.
Thus we know that few who replace the executor in executing
Do not injure their own benevolent heart.
Why resort to execution?
It is better to lead the people through goodness.

Note: *Resorting to execution* implies a deliberate intent to kill.
*Not resorting to execution* implies the absence of such intent.
If one aligns one’s spirit with uprightness,
All deviant forces will not dare to encroach—
This is the secret of effortless, virtuous execution.

Chapter Seventy-Five

The people’s hunger arises from their superiors consuming excessive taxes—this is why they hunger. The people’s unruliness arises from their superiors’ meddling—this is why they are unruly. The people’s disregard for death arises from their excessive pursuit of life’s comforts—this is why they disregard death. Only those who do not treat life as something to be clung to are wiser than those who prize life.

When superiors consume excessive taxes, the people’s famine arises. When superiors meddle excessively, the people’s unruliness becomes evident. The people’s disregard for death stems from their greed for wealth and profit, their desire to enrich their lives—thus they treat their bodies lightly, heedless of danger. As the saying goes, “Men die for wealth”—this is the case. Only those who do not value wealth and profit highly maintain a clear mind and few desires, and they are wiser than those who prize life.

*My humble note: Excessive taxes starve the people, just as overtaxing the body with labor leads to withering. Excessive meddling makes governance difficult, just as overusing the spirit leads to chaos.*

---

**Chapter Seventy-Six**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this “Against Strength”; Peng’s version titles it “On Abiding Above”)*

When people are alive, they are supple and soft; when dead, they are rigid and hard. When the myriad things—plants and trees—are alive, they are tender and fragile; when dead, they are withered and dry. Thus, the rigid and hard are companions of death; the supple and soft are companions of life. Therefore, when an army is strong, it will not prevail; when a tree is hard, it will be felled. The strong and great dwell below; the soft and weak dwell above.

The supple and weak pertain to *qi*; the rigid and hard pertain to bone. When *qi* gathers and the body harmonizes, there is life; when *qi* scatters and bones stand alone, there is death. The life and death of the myriad things follow this pattern. When tender and fragile, water-*qi* is abundant—thus life. When withered and dry, water-*qi* is exhausted—thus death. Rigidity and hardness are tied to death; suppleness and softness are tied to life. Should one rely solely on the easily visible forms and appearances to understand this?

Consider further the analogy: When an army is strong but does not prevail, its *qi* has already declined while the enemy’s *qi* is still flourishing. When a tree is hard, its joints knot—its hard *qi* cannot flow, and its growth halts at the size of a hand’s span. Thus, the distinction between above and below is also determined by rigidity and softness. In cultivating the self, can one not regard humility and harmony as paramount?

---

**Chapter Seventy-Seven**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this “The Dao of Heaven”; Peng’s version concurs)*

Is not the Dao of Heaven like the drawing of a bow? What is high is pressed down; what is low is lifted up. What has excess is reduced; what is deficient is supplemented. The Dao of Heaven reduces excess to supplement deficiency. The Dao of man is not so—it reduces the deficient to serve the excessive. Who can take their excess to serve the world? Only those who possess the Dao.

Thus, the sage acts without relying on his actions, accomplishes without dwelling on his achievements—does he not wish to display his worth? The highest teaching speaks in plain terms: The Dao of Heaven is like the drawing of a bow, its essence lying in balance and uprightness. Press down the high, lift up the low—then both arms are level. Reduce excess, supplement deficiency—then the body is aligned. The Dao of Heaven’s marvel in harmonizing the seven celestial bodies and ordering the five phases lies precisely in its balance and uprightness. Hence it is said: “The Dao of Heaven reduces excess and supplements deficiency.”

The Dao of man is otherwise: it reduces the deficient to serve the excessive, causing the deficient to become even more deficient and the excessive even more excessive. Who can take their excess to serve the world’s deficiencies? Only those who embody Heaven’s Dao and desire that all things attain their proper place—such is the sage. He acts without reliance, accomplishes without dwelling, reduces his excess to serve the world without claiming virtue—does he not avoid displaying his worth?

*Vulgar annotation in verse:*
Heaven’s Dao diminishes the full and humility receives benefit;
Human nature consumes the meager, and loss brings greater misfortune.
If one can grasp the meaning of this classic,
The elixir of gold and cinnabar aligns with the sagely lineage.

---

**Chapter Seventy-Eight**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this “Trust and Responsibility”; Peng’s version titles it “Softness and Weakness”)*

Nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water, yet in attacking the hard and strong, nothing can surpass it—nothing can replace it. That the weak overcomes the strong, the soft overcomes the rigid, is known to all under Heaven, yet none can practice it. Therefore, the sage says: “One who bears the state’s disgrace is called the lord of the altars of soil and grain. One who bears the state’s misfortune is called the king of all under Heaven.” Straightforward words seem paradoxical.

These three lines echo the earlier saying: “The softest under Heaven rides roughshod over the hardest under Heaven.” Nothing can replace it—no other thing can substitute for this power to attack the hard. That softness and weakness overcome rigidity and strength is a principle all know, yet none ultimately practice. Thus, the Supreme again cites the sage’s words to illustrate the great virtue of softness and weakness.

Bearing the state’s disgrace, bearing the state’s misfortune—these are the sage’s self-assumed burdens, as in: “I alone am at fault; what guilt have the people? My virtue is meager and thin; Heaven inflicts this calamity.” That “straightforward words seem paradoxical” means that the sage’s true words all turn inward, never blaming others. This, too, is the Dao of softness and weakness.

---

**Chapter Seventy-Nine**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this “Fulfilling Agreements”; Peng’s version concurs)*

Reconciling great resentment inevitably leaves residual bitterness—how can this be called true goodness? Thus, the sage holds the left tally and makes no demands upon others. Those with virtue oversee the contract; those without virtue enforce scrutiny. The Way of Heaven shows no partiality, yet it ever aligns with the good.

When the myriad regions brim with resentment, the ruler’s virtue must be meager. To encounter the people’s grievances and still refuse self-reproach, instead seeking to reconcile great resentment—even if reconciliation succeeds, unresolved bitterness remains within, and residual grievance cannot be dispelled. How can this be deemed perfect goodness? Confucius said: “Be severe with oneself and lenient toward others, and resentment will stay far away.” Thus, the sage governs the world by cultivating the virtue of self-rigor, earning the people’s trust. Without demanding of others, the people naturally place their faith in him. It is like a contract split into left and right halves, each party holding one as perpetual proof—then you harbor no deceit toward me, and I harbor no suspicion toward you. The sage holds virtue as one holds the left tally; how could the people not rejoice? Therefore, those with virtue oversee the contract, while those without virtue enforce scrutiny. *Scrutiny* means examining faults. To be meticulous in fault-finding while never reflecting on oneself is to act in a way the people will not follow.

The Way of Heaven shows no partiality, yet it ever aligns with the good. As the *Book of Documents* says: “August Heaven has no favorites; it befriends those who revere it. The people have no constant allegiance; they cleave to those who are humane.”

---

**Chapter Eighty**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this “Standing Alone”; Peng’s version titles it “Small States”)*

Let the state be small, its people few. Though they possess tools for ten or a hundred men, let them not use them. Let the people regard death as weighty and not wander far. Though there be boats and carriages, let there be no occasion to ride them. Though there be armor and weapons, let there be no occasion to deploy them. Let the people return to knotting cords for use.

Let them find sweetness in their food, beauty in their clothing, contentment in their dwellings, and joy in their customs. Neighboring states may gaze upon one another, the sounds of their chickens and dogs heard across the way, yet the people grow old and die without ever visiting one another.

If there be those who wish to enlarge the state and multiply its people, let them possess tools for a hundred million yet not use them—surely their sufficiency will be all the greater. This is to emulate the return to antiquity and the restoration of primal simplicity.

The Supreme One does not act thus. He takes the small to exemplify the great, so that from the small, the great may be known. When a state is small and its people few, though they possess tools for ten or a hundred men and do not use them, their sufficiency will only increase. What difficulty is there in returning to unadorned simplicity? Thus, he lets the people not use their tools, treasuring only their own lives. Let them dwell in their native land and not migrate elsewhere. Let them hold death in awe and not die, nurture life and live long—what more could they seek beyond this?

In such a time, the people of the state walk with light and vigorous steps, so though they have boats and carriages, they do not ride them. Within the state’s borders, there are no thieves or bandits, so though they have armor and weapons, they do not deploy them. The governance of the state is simple and unadorned, so the practice of knotting cords may continue. Sweet food, fine clothing, secure dwellings, and joyful customs—neighboring states gaze upon one another, the sounds of their chickens and dogs heard across the way, yet the people grow old and die without ever visiting one another. Thus, social obligations are few, and extravagance does not arise; material goods are abundant, and each family dwells in peace.

The lofty ones of the state say: “A single hill, a single ravine—we deem ourselves beyond the mortal world. A spring, an autumn—we seem to forget time within a gourd. Grandparents and parents live long in health; husbands and wives remain together for life. Brothers and sisters play the *xun* and *chi* in harmony; children and grandchildren train phoenixes and cranes. How is this different from the grotto heavens and blessed lands, the Pure Land of Ultimate Bliss?”

---

**Chapter Eighty-One**
*(Heshang Gong’s commentary titles this “Revealing Simplicity”; Peng’s version titles it “Non-Accumulation”)*

Truthful words are not beautiful; beautiful words are not truthful. The good do not dispute; the disputatious are not good. The wise are not broadly learned; the broadly learned are not wise.

The sage does not accumulate. The more he does for others, the more he possesses; the more he gives to others, the more he has. The Way of Heaven benefits and does not harm. The Way of the sage acts and does not contend.

Truth, goodness, and wisdom all pertain to establishing words. This passage concludes the entire scripture’s meaning. Sincere discourse is simply genuine, not embellished to please the world. Those who seek to please the world employ embellishment, not sincerity—hence: “Truthful words are not beautiful; beautiful words are not truthful.” The excellence of inherited teachings is simply correctness, not contrived to confuse others. Those who seek to confuse others employ contrivance, not proper transmission—hence: “The good do not dispute; the disputatious are not good.” Penetrating writings are simply coherent, not overflowing to display talent. Those who seek to display talent overflow without restraint, not truly penetrating—hence: “The wise are not broadly learned; the broadly learned are not wise.”

This is followed by establishing virtue and merit to fulfill the great function of this scripture. *Accumulation* means accumulating goodness. The sage does not speak of accumulating goodness but establishes his own great merit and virtue. Thus, his merit is boundless, his virtue all-encompassing. By acting for others through merit and virtue, he gains ever more; by bestowing merit and virtue upon others, he possesses ever more.

The Dao operates silently to generate and complete, thus it benefits without harming. The Way of the sage works with effortless spontaneity, not contending with others in accumulating good deeds—thus its greatness equals that of Heaven. As the ancient saying goes: “The highest establish virtue, the next establish merit, the next establish words—all three are imperishable.” This may serve as a colophon for this text.

After completing his commentary on this scripture, Master Hanxu reflected and pondered, aligning words with deeds, and declared that though he did not rely on written words, he had expressed the true mechanism. The final chapter’s commentary concludes with merit and virtue, which he deemed fine and good. Yet upon further reflection, he wondered how the four characters “acts and does not contend” could serve as the root of the eighty-one chapters. Suddenly, he realized: “Yes, yes—this single phrase is the very keystone of the entire scripture!”

The dread of cultivators lies in hearing the Dao yet still vying for petty merit, petty virtue, petty literary fame, or chasing after all ephemeral riches and honors—thus squandering their vital essence in vain. The sagely Dao aligns with the celestial Dao; the celestial Dao governs generation and completion, inherently beneficial and never harmful. Does not the sagely Dao likewise oversee creation, equally beneficial and never harmful? My wish is that those who achieve merit and fame, fulfill filial piety and loyalty, may each dwell in truth and each attain the Dao. First, practice the work of jade refinement, concealing oneself amidst the marketplace. Next, undertake the labor of gold refinement, dwelling deep in mountain valleys. Within, fulfill what must be done; without, extinguish all thoughts of contention. Only through non-contention can none in the world contend with one—such is the perfected person. The great conclusion of the sacred scripture reaches its utmost here.

*The Authentic Transmission of Laozi*
Laozi was a native of Chu. His father, Qianganyuan Gao, served as Senior Censor of Shang (alternatively, Zhou). He married Lady Hong, whose given name was Yingfu. One day, as she napped, she saw a five-colored radiance cradling the Supreme Old Lord descending from the heavens, suddenly transforming into a shooting star that flew into her mouth. There it congealed into a jade-like embryo, and after twelve years, she gave birth beneath a plum tree in Quren Village, Lai District, Ku County. Thus, he took Li as his surname. Because he was born after dreaming of the Old Lord, he was called Laozi; because his ears were long, he was named Chong’er and styled Baiyangfu. This occurred on the fifteenth day of the third month in the first year of King Wuding of Shang.

*(The above is recorded in the Immortal Scriptures and the* Lu Shi. *The* Nei Zhuan *states: “His surname was Li, his given name Er. His mother saw the essence of the sun descend like a shooting star, entering her mouth, and thus conceived. She carried him for seventy-two years before giving birth beneath a plum tree by the Guo River in Chen. Pointing to the plum tree, she said, ‘This shall be my surname.’ Born with white hair, he was called Laozi. As his ears had three openings, he was also called Lao Dan.” The* Wu Zong Wang Ji *records: “His surname was Li, his given name Zai, born on the fourteenth day of the ninth month in the year Dingsi of King Ding.” The* Yue Ling Guang Yi *states: “The Jade Maiden was Laozi’s mother. She dreamed of a five-colored radiance forming like a pellet, flowing into her mouth, which she swallowed and thus conceived. After eighty-one years of pregnancy, she rested beneath a date tree and gave birth by splitting open her left armpit. This occurred on the fifteenth day of the third month in the first year of Wuding.”)*

The *Suo Sui Qianliang Lu* states: “Qian Yuan Gao, at seventy-two years of age, remained unwed. He formed a union with his neighbor’s wife, the Lady of Extended Longevity, and after eighty years of pregnancy, she gave birth.”

The *Youyang Zazu* records: “Li’s mother was the Primordial Lady. The essence of the sun entered her mouth, which she swallowed and thus conceived. After seventy-two years, she gave birth.”

Liu Xie’s *Xin Lun* says: “Laozi was conceived by the influence of a fire star.”

The *Lu Shi* recounts: “Conceived by a shooting star’s shock, he was born after twelve years. At birth, he could speak, his face golden and hair white—hence called Laozi. His village was in Lai Township of Ku, where ‘lai’ means ‘goosefoot,’ so he was also called Lao Laizi.”

The *Gao Shi Zhuan* notes: “Laozi was born during the Yin dynasty.”

The *Shen Xian Zhuan* relates: “Laozi’s name was Chong’er. His mother conceived after sensing a great shooting star. Some say she carried him for seventy-two years before giving birth. Others claim his mother was unwed, and Laozi was her maternal surname. Still others say his mother happened upon a plum tree and gave birth beneath it. At birth, he could speak and, pointing to the plum tree, said, ‘Let this be my surname.’”

The *Lie Xian Zhuan* states: “Laozi entered his mother’s womb riding a white deer.”

The *Tang Ji* records: “Laozi’s mother conceived after eating a plum. His parents abandoned him, and after eighty years, he was born beneath a plum tree. Emperor Gaozu of Tang traced his lineage to Laozi, hence the phrase ‘the immortal plum roots entwine.’”

The *Za Zu* says: “The Mysterious Wondrous Jade Maiden descended with a cloud of dark and yellow qi, which she swallowed and thus conceived. After three thousand seven hundred years, she gave birth in the kingdom of Xina. This pertains to the Heavenly Worthy of Dao and Virtue, not Laozi.”

Utterly filial by nature, he transformed the customs of Lai.

From birth, he was serene and free of desire, outwardly forsaking glory and splendor, inwardly cultivating essence and qi. Through the transition from Shang to Zhou, he endured for centuries without decline, earning the name of an ancient recluse.

The *Records of the Grand Historian* states: “He lived to be over one hundred sixty years old, or perhaps over two hundred.” Ge Hong said: “Laozi remained in the Zhou court for over three hundred years.” The *Lu Shi* records: “His mother lived to be four hundred forty, or perhaps two hundred twenty.” The *Extensive Records* says: “He lived two hundred seventy years.” The *Shantang Sikao* claims: “Born in the reign of King Wuding of Yin, he lived until the ninth year of the First Emperor of Qin, spanning nine hundred ninety-six years.” Yet reckoning from the first year of Wuding to the ninth year of the First Emperor yields one thousand eighty-seven years in total.

During his time in Zhou, Laozi often changed his name. In the reign of King Wen, he was called Xieyi Zi (or Zhi Yi Xiansheng) and served as Archivist of the Royal Treasury. Under King Wu, he was called Yucheng Zi and served as the Chronicler Beneath the Pillar. In the reign of King Kang, he was called Guoshu Zi, and under King Qing, he was called Lao Laizi. His wife was profoundly wise, and together with Lao Laizi, they withdrew to live among the mulberry fields and plows.

He composed a work in fifteen chapters expounding the application of the Daoist arts. When King Zhuang of Chu heard of him, he sought to compare himself to Laozi. At the time, Lao Laizi was weaving, and the king approached to ask, “I wish to govern the state—may you assist me in this?” Laozi replied, “Very well.” The king then departed.

When his wife returned from gathering firewood, she asked, “Did you give him your consent?” He replied, “I did.” His wife said, “I have heard that ‘those who may be fed with wine and meat may be followed with whips and cudgels; those who may be granted office and emolument may be followed with axes and halberds.’ I cannot bear to be controlled by others.” She cast aside her basket and left. Overjoyed, Lao Laizi immediately fled with his wife.

Sima Qian records: “Laozi’s son was named Zong, who served as a general in Wei and was enfeoffed at Duan. Zong’s son was Wang; Wang’s son was Yan; Yan’s great-great-grandson was Xia, who served in the Han. Xia’s son, Jie, became Grand Tutor to the Prince of Jiaoxi and settled his family in Qi.” At that time, their ancestors and descendants, fathers and sons, must have wandered without fixed abode, branching into separate lineages.

Some say: in Yue he was Fan Li; in Qi, the leather sack (Chiyi); in Wu, Tao Zhugong; in the early Han, the Yellow Stone Master; and in the reign of Emperor Wen, the Master by the River. All these remain unfathomable.

Ge Zhichuan states: Laozi appeared in every age, repeatedly changing his name. The reason, according to the *Nine Palaces*, *Three and Five Classics*, and *Yuanchen Jing*, is that each person encounters calamitous conjunctions in life. When such times arrive, one must change one’s name to align with the transformations of primordial qi, thereby transcending misfortune and prolonging life. Many Daoist practitioners today follow this practice. Laozi remained in Zhou for several hundred years, during which he must have faced more than one such conjunction, hence his many names. To properly establish the truth of Laozi’s life, one should rely primarily on historical records and verified accounts, cross-referencing them with Laozi’s esoteric scriptures. Other popular tales are largely fictitious.

Furthermore, the *Western Ascension’s Central Embryo*, *Returning Mandate’s Essence*, *Pearl Strategem and Jade Mechanism*, and *Golden Internal Classic* all describe Laozi as having a pale yellow complexion, fine brows and a broad forehead, long ears and large eyes, spaced teeth and a square mouth, thick lips, a forehead marked with three or five lines of insight, sun-like temples and moon-like brows, a nose with pure bone and twin pillars, ears with three passages, feet bearing the pattern of two and five, hands grasping the ten celestial stems, a height of nine feet, and sixty-eight teeth—such was the countenance of a sage. Those who receive the Mandate of Heaven possess divine foresight and differ from ordinary people, destined to become masters of the Dao. Thus, they are aided by celestial spirits and followed by immortals. For this reason, the methods he transmitted for transcending the world numbered nine hundred and thirty scrolls, and his talismanic writings seventy scrolls—all recorded in the central chapters of *Laozi’s Original Account*. The catalogues still exist and can be verified. Works not included in these numbers were later additions by Daoist practitioners, not authentic texts (as noted by Zhichuan).

Laozi embodied non-action and self-transformation, purity and self-rectification (*Records of the Grand Historian*). He devoted himself entirely to the pursuit of longevity, and though he remained in Zhou for a long time, his rank and position never advanced. This was because he sought to harmonize with the world while inwardly preserving his natural essence. Only after attaining the Dao did he depart—truly an immortal (*Biographies of Divine Transcendents*).

When Confucius traveled to Zhou to inquire about ritual from Laozi, Laozi said, “The man of whom you speak—his flesh and bones have long since decayed; only his words remain. Moreover, when the noble person finds the right moment, he mounts his chariot; when he does not, he drifts like tumbleweed. I have heard that ‘the good merchant conceals his wealth as if empty, and the noble person of great virtue appears foolish.’ Rid yourself of your arrogance and excessive desires, your affected demeanor and reckless ambitions—none of these benefit you.”

One day, Laozi asked Confucius, “Have you attained the Dao?”

Confucius said, “I have sought it for twenty-seven years and still have not attained it.”
Laozi said, “If the Dao could be offered to others, then none would fail to offer it to their lord. If the Dao could be presented to others, then none would fail to present it to their kin. If the Dao could be told to others, then none would fail to tell it to their brothers. If the Dao could be transmitted to others, then none would fail to transmit it to their children. Yet it cannot be done—there is no other reason: without an inner master, the Dao cannot reside within.”

When Confucius visited Laozi and spoke of benevolence and righteousness, Laozi said, “Chaff in the eyes makes the four directions seem displaced. Mosquitoes and gadflies biting the skin keep one awake all night. Now, benevolence and righteousness assail my heart and mind with such vehemence—no greater disorder exists! You, sir, should let the world not lose its uncarved simplicity, move with the wind, stand by embracing virtue, and return together to the natural—what need is there to act so strenuously, as if carrying a great drum in search of a lost child? The swan does not bathe daily to remain white, nor does the crow blacken itself daily to stay black. The natural black and white are not sufficient to distinguish beauty from ugliness. Unadorned observation does not expand one’s fundamental nature. When the spring dries up, fish find themselves on dry land, where they moisten one another with breath and foam—yet how much better to forget one another in rivers and lakes!”

Confucius said to Laozi, “I have studied the *Book of Songs*, the *Book of Documents*, the rites, music, the *Changes* and its images, and the *Spring and Autumn Annals*—using them to seek audience with seventy-two lords.”

Debating the Way of the former kings and elucidating the traces of Zhou and Shao—yet not a single ruler could put them to use. How difficult it is to persuade others, and how hard to clarify the Dao! ”

Laozi said, “The *Six Classics* are but the worn traces of the former kings—how could they be the essence of those traces? What you speak of now are still only traces. Footprints are what shoes produce, yet are footprints the shoes themselves? ”

Confucius returned home and for three days did not speak. Zilu, finding this strange, asked him about it. Confucius said, “Of birds, I know they can fly. Of fish, I know they can swim. Of beasts, I know they can run. Those that run may be caught in nets, those that swim with lines, those that fly with arrows. But as for the dragon, I know not how it rides the wind and clouds to ascend the nine heavens. Today, having seen Laozi, is he not like the dragon? ”

Laozi dwelt in Zhou for many years. Seeing the virtue of Zhou in decline, he then rode a black ox cart westward through the Qin Pass to ascend Kunlun. The Pass Prefect Yin Xi, having observed the purple qi, knew in advance of his coming and swept the road for forty *li* to welcome him. Soon after, Laozi indeed arrived. Yin Xi said, “Master, you are about to withdraw from the world—pray compose a book for me.” Thereupon, Laozi halted his carriage at the pass and wrote the *Dao and Virtue* in over five thousand characters, imparting it fully to Yin Xi before departing.

During the Eastern Han, in Qingzhou there was a man of the Wang clan named Cheng, styled Xuanfu, with the Daoist name Donghua. He encountered the Queen Mother of the West, Wang Qizhu. Pleased by their shared surname, she bestowed upon him the title *Supreme Perfected of White Clouds* and transmitted to him Laozi’s *Azure Talismans and Jade Scriptures*, along with the *Golden Regulations and Numinous Writings*. Xuanfu practiced diligently for three years without slackening. Moved by his devotion, Laozi descended and condensed the *Inner Radiance of the Yellow Court* into a single scroll, naming it the *Outer Radiance*, which he imparted to Xuanfu for cultivation, along with the nine revolutions and eight jade elixir’s firing times. Xuanfu then concealed his brilliance, built a hermitage, and refined himself in seclusion. Upon achieving perfection, he was titled the *Lord Emperor Donghua*, governing the lesser yang qi of the East. He was also called the *Lesser Yang of the Purple Palace* and the *Great Emperor of Fusang*, serving as a minister and assistant. After three transmissions, it reached the Pure Yang Master, who said: “Laozi’s *Outer Radiance* and the Jade Emperor’s *Inner Radiance* are both preserved in the Fusang Palace. The Donghua Patriarch extolled and transmitted them, thereby revealing Laozi’s intent to deliver the world.”

Now, Laozi’s former incarnation was as the Most High Lord Lao, and thus his concealments and transformations were as one with the Lord Lao. In high antiquity, the Lord Lao first manifested as the Three Purities. During the era of the Heavenly Sovereign, he changed his name to the *Celestial Master of Myriad Laws*; during the Earthly Sovereign’s time, to the *Mysterious Center Master*; during the Human Sovereign’s time, to *Master Unyielding*. After the Three Sovereigns, he became the *Golden Gate Lord Emperor*. In the time of Fuxi, he was *Yu Hua* (later changed to *Yu Mi*); during Shennong’s reign, *Old Master of Nine Spirits*; under Emperor Ku, *Lu Tu*; in the time of Yao, *Master of Accomplishment*; under Shun, *Master Yin Shou*; during the Xia of Yu, *Master True Conduct*; and in the Shang of Tang, *Master Bestowed Principle*. By the reign of King Wuding of Shang, he first descended in full spirit as Laozi—this was a time of opening a new phase and re-creating heaven and earth.

Later generations, not understanding, conflated the Lord Lao with Laozi, thus merging the deeds of his former incarnation with his later one. Some claim Laozi existed before heaven and earth, others that he was the essence and spirit of heaven. Those who seek the truth sigh at such vagueness, for these accounts do not appear in the *Authentic Scripture of the Old Immortal*. *The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* states: “Where there is heavenly virtue, there is the arts of the Dao. The adepts of the Dao—when have they ever been absent? From the times of Fuxi and the Yellow Emperor down to the Three Dynasties, those who manifested the arts of the Dao have appeared in every age. Why must it always be the same Laozi?” This is all due to later students who, fond of the marvelous and valuing the extraordinary, sought to exalt Laozi and thus fabricated such tales. In truth, Laozi was merely one who attained the Dao with exceptional precision—nothing divine or strange.

Why? If Laozi is said to have attained the Dao, then others may strive to emulate him. But if he is deemed divine and strange, then immortality becomes unattainable. In my humble view, the Lord Lao in earlier times was divine and strange, while Laozi in later times was like other men. What is divine and strange cannot be learned, but what is like other men can be. My intent, Zhichuan, is only to follow the factual evidence.

The Confucians say that the birth of sages arises from human qi and endows heavenly essence—this is most fitting. Laozi’s bodily existence borrowed the four great elements to form his shape. Through refined cultivation and grand endeavor, he became the model for divine immortals, the patriarch of virtue and the Dao’s tradition. When his merit was complete and his conduct fulfilled, he returned to the assembly of all heavens. Dispersed, he becomes myriad; united, he is one. His divided spirits descend into the world, all taking cultivation of the Dao as their foundation. What cannot be learned lies here, and what can be learned also lies here.

As for Zhuangzi, Dongfang Shuo, and the like, who are said to be Laozi under different names—these claims are unreliable, for they were merely those to whom Laozi granted gifts. The *Treasure Eulogy* says: “Those who produced Zhuangzi of the Mulberry Grove and Dongfang Shuo discerned subtle traces like cinnabar.” This is akin to the mother of Danyang, who dreamed of receiving a divine cinnabar script and gave birth to a great immortal. The *Immortals’ Biographies* state: “Those who follow Laozi’s teachings are all pure, empty, tranquil, and uncontentious, thus able to conceal jade within coarse robes and remain unshaken in perilous times.” Its source is deep and its flow vast—could this not be the teacher and model for all ages, as ordained by heaven and earth?

Having dwelt in Qingdao for several years, I once instructed the latecomers Zhu, Jiecheng Li, and Huifeng Liu in Laozi’s method of purity and stillness. I was ever cautious and reverent, never daring to forget the ancestral teachings.

Before was the Lord Lao, after was Laozi—are they one or two? Their harmony and distinction are perfectly clear.

The literary style of this text is like flowing water today and the former incarnation of the bright moon. (Wushan) interweaves narrative and discourse, blending history with profundity. The collected biographies of various masters all distill the finest essence. This is the first precious transmission of Laozi’s tradition.

*(Baohe)*
*Categorized Explications of the* Yin Fu Jing
*Composed by Shuxia Hanxu*

**Preface to the** *Explication of the* Yin Fu Jing
The *Yin Fu Jing* establishes the methods of cultivation and refinement upon the principles of the Three Powers and the foundation of the myriad transformations. It is as if it were the primordial text since the opening of heaven and earth, for all it speaks of is the natural. Some say it is a work from the late Zhou or Warring States period, confined merely to the mechanisms of theft, life, and death. They suspect it of being bizarre and extraordinary, unaware that its principles are in truth level and upright. Some claim it was authored by Li Quan of the Daguanzi, and if Quan could speak thus, I would rank him alongside Guangcheng. Others say Guangcheng was Laozi’s former incarnation in the era of qi-transformation and should not have spoken in such terms. Little do they realize that the ancient was also heaven and earth, and the present is also heaven and earth; the ancient was also sun and moon, and the present is also sun and moon. Yet this text was obtained from the Tiger’s Mouth Crag on Mount Song, and only emerged in the Tang dynasty. Since the time of the Yellow Emperor, thousands of years had passed, so later generations could not but harbor doubts. Quan encountered the Old Mother of Mount Li, who transmitted to him the profound meaning of the *Yin Fu Jing*. She admonished him: “The *Yin Fu Jing* contains over three hundred characters: a hundred expound the Dao, a hundred expound the arts, and the three are interwoven as one. Above lies the Way of divine immortals embracing the One; in the middle, the methods for enriching the state and pacifying the people; below, the techniques for strengthening the army and securing victory. These are no empty words.”

The terms "upper," "middle," and "lower" do not refer to the names of three chapters but rather to three levels of meaning. These three are interwoven and blended within the text, recurring and unfolding subtly throughout its passages—precisely what is meant by "interweaving the three and merging them as one."

Daoist adepts regard "strengthening the army and securing victory" as matters pertaining to the refinement of the elixir and the upward ascent of the Dao. Why, then, is it labeled as the "lower" level? Laozi said: *"Fine weapons are an inauspicious qi; they are used only when there is no other choice."* In my earlier commentary on the *Daode Jing*, I mistakenly received the approval of a sagely teacher. Recently, upon continuing with the *Yin Fu Jing*, I admired its brevity of text, its profound intent, and how each character cuts to the essence of secret alchemical language. Thus, I once more settled my mind to observe things clearly and composed this commentary to elucidate its meaning.

*Preface by Juanshi Shanren Huoxi Yue on the Great River*

People know the divine as divine but do not understand why the non-divine is divine. I observe Hanxu—his appearance is that of a fool, and others take him for one. Yet I do not. When Hanxu annotated the *Daode Jing*, the *Qingjing Jing*, the *Dingguan Jing*, and the *Wuchu Jing*, people marveled at him, but I did not. Recently, upon contemplating the heavens and discerning the "Five Thieves," examining the earth and discussing the pacification of the people—principles for a peaceful populace and a prosperous state, techniques for strengthening the army and securing victory—all were expounded in his commentary on the *Yin Fu Jing*. From this, even I cannot fathom the depths of Hanxu’s wisdom.

The *Preface* asserts that *"since the beginning of creation, there must have been writing"* and vigorously defends its antiquity, demonstrating both talent and insight. The *Yin Fu Jing* emerged in the time of the Yellow Emperor, after the slaughter of Chiyou. The Yellow Emperor sought out Guangchengzi, who transmitted this text to him. Silently observing the ministers at court—such as Feng Hou, Li Mu, Qi Bo, Tongjun, Dou Bao, and Da Nao—all of whom understood the mechanisms of killing, the cycles of life and death, and the workings of the *jiazi* calendar, Guangchengzi surveyed them and spoke in cryptic, perilous language, concealing within it a path of perfect clarity. His intent was for all in the Yellow Emperor’s retinue to hear this wondrous teaching. To enlighten the Yellow Emperor was to enlighten his ministers—how vast was Guangchengzi’s true capacity!

Confucians of the Three Dynasties and later have doubted whether this is a text from before the Three Dynasties, but their skepticism merely skims the surface of its words. Suppose there were an elixir of immortality brought to market for sale. If one falsely claimed it was rat poison, or even said it could cure illness, people would surely dismiss and ridicule it, perhaps even revile it, for how could a poison also heal? Yet those with discernment would purchase and consume it, ascending to the heavens in broad daylight. This is precisely the meaning of the *Yin Fu Jing*—precisely the meaning of the *Yin Fu Jing*.

*Composed with reverence by Zhang Quanyi, Daoist friend of the Purple Radiance Grotto on Penglai Mountain*

*Collected Evaluations from Various Masters*

**Daguanzhi** said: *"Internally, it reveals the mechanisms of heaven; externally, it aligns with human affairs. In its subtlety, even the Eight Scenes of the* Huangting *are insufficient to match its profundity. In its essential teachings, the hundred schools of philosophy and history cannot compare. In its strategic ingenuity, even the works of Sunzi, Wuzi, and Han Fei pale in wonder. Thus, the nature of all things, the calculations of success and failure, the principles of life and death—all are but mechanisms."*

**Zhang Guo** said: *"To observe the Dao of nature is to observe nothing. Not observing with the eyes but with the mind. Observing the profoundly subtle yet seeing nothing—thus one illuminates the nature of the natural. This is what is meant by* yin. *To grasp the conduct of nature is to grasp nothing. Not grasping with the hands but with the mechanism. The mechanism is adaptable and unbound—thus one harmonizes with the manifestations of the natural. This is what is meant by* fu *(another interpretation)."*

Lü Chunyang said: *"The Song Confucian Shaozi was skilled in reading the* Yin Fu Jing. *‘The cosmos lies within the palm, the myriad transformations arise from the body.’ These are words from the* Yin Fu Jing, *and the* Jirang Pian *borrowed its lines—how subtle the resonance of those who grasp it without words."*

Lü Qiu Cimeng said: *"What the* Yin Fu Jing *calls ‘The Dao of nature is quiescent, thus heaven and earth and the ten thousand things are born. The Dao of heaven and earth is gradual, thus yin and yang prevail. Yin and yang mutually impel one another, and transformations proceed in accord.’ These few lines—even the words of the Six Classics cannot surpass them."*

Master Zhu said: *"The four lines beginning with ‘The Dao of nature is quiescent’ are exquisitely put. That stillness can generate movement means it gradually recedes in this way, then gradually grows in that. The Dao of heaven and earth constantly reveals itself to humanity in just this manner."* He further remarked: *"The line ‘The Dao of heaven and earth is gradual’ is superb.
