The Dao of yin and yang is such that they prevail over one another without a single day’s pause, steadily pressing forward bit by bit—this one retreats a fraction, that one advances a fraction."* And again: *"Were it not for utter stillness, heaven, earth, and the ten thousand things would not arise. The word ‘gradual’ is masterfully chosen—‘gradual’ means incremental. The Dao of heaven and earth incrementally waxes and wanes, thus hardness and softness prevail. This is the principle underlying good fortune, misfortune, constancy, and victory. The* Yin Fu Jing *is particularly fine in such passages."*

Master Zhu also said: *"The* Yin Fu Jing’s *teaching of ‘three returns in day and night’ resembles the cultivation practices of the midnight-noon circulation—today thus, tomorrow thus, and the more familiar one becomes, the more efficacious the results."*

The *Yin Fu Kao Yi* states: *"The commentary attributed to the Old Mother of Mount Li is often a later forgery, its language and meaning remarkably shallow."*

The inclusion of Zhang Guo’s sayings reveals that this text postdates him.

Ouyang Xunqi said: *"When the Dao and its arts fragmented, those who could write each composed their own books. Straightforward words often appear flawed or impure, yet the* Yin Fu Jing *alone employs paradox to align with truth."*

Wei Heshān said: *"Li Jiayou was broadly versed in the classics and the hundred schools, and deeply steeped in the* Book of Changes*. In his later years, he grasped the teaching of ‘specializing in qi to attain suppleness,’ and through exhaustive study and reflection on the* Yin Fu Jing *and* Cantong Qi*, he held fast to them without wavering. Thus, those who comprehend the Dao necessarily unite these two texts with the* Book of Changes*, employing them as one."*

Lu Qianxu said: *"The* Yin Fu Jing *and* Daode Jing *both speak of the mechanisms of inversion and counter-application. As for governing the state, waging war, seizing the realm, or the phrases ‘there are wondrous devices,’ ‘the ten thousand images arise,’ ‘the eight trigrams and the sexagenary cycle,’ and ‘divine mechanisms and hidden spirits’—all contain profound principles. Yet the world, not understanding, labels the* Yin Fu Jing *as a treatise on military strategy and uses the* Laozi *for governance, missing the mark entirely."*

The *Yin Fu Jing* is a manual of cultivation. *Yin fu* is spoken of in contrast to *yang fire*; it speaks of yin without mentioning yang, for yang is contained within it. The *Yi Yi* states: *"The Dao is called the alternation of yin and yang."* That yin is placed before yang reflects the principle of heaven, earth, and the ten thousand things: without stillness, there is no movement, just as *Bo* precedes *Fu*. This single scroll of the *Yin Fu Jing* is the pivot of yin-yang interpenetration. The numinous perishes here, and the non-numinous numinous arises here. This is where concealment and manifestation, reversal and return, ingress and egress, the waxing and waning of sun and moon, the ebb and flow of great and small, the root of life and death’s restraint, the ground where kindness and harm contend, and the realm of water and fire’s advance and retreat all unfold. When yin and yang restrain each other, yin and yang prevail. The *Yin Fu* commands the numinous transformations of change. Yet those who wield the *Yin Fu* must know that the body possesses wondrous devices. Concealing these devices within, awaiting the moment to act, the trigrams, the sexagenary cycle, heaven, earth, ghosts, and spirits will all accord with the Dao—what difficulty then remains in enriching the state, pacifying the people, strengthening the army, securing victory, and embracing unity to preserve the true?"

Zheng’s *Bibliographic Survey* records thirty-eight transmitted commentaries on the *Yin Fu Jing*, totaling fifty-one scrolls. Over the centuries since, the number of annotations has grown beyond measure. I have now synthesized explanations from the *Laozi*, the *Book of Changes*, and the *Cantong Qi*, entitling it *A Categorized Exegesis*. *"Observe the Dao of heaven, grasp the movement of heaven—this is all."*

*"Observe the Dao of heaven"*—the text opens by speaking of the *Yin Fu*. Laozi said: *"Achieving merit, attaining fame, then withdrawing—this is the Dao of heaven."* *"Grasp the movement of heaven"*—the next phrase supplements *yang fire*. The *Xici* states: *"Heaven’s movement is ceaseless; the noble person strengthens themselves without pause."* To observe the Dao of heaven is to witness its flourishing and fulfillment, its retreat and storage at winter’s advent—the meaning of *Kun*’s six lines reaching exhaustion lies herein. To grasp the movement of heaven is to seize its latent emergence, its vigilant leaping, until the ninth-five, when the flying dragon ascends to heaven—the meaning of *Kun* following heaven’s movement rides in its wake. The opening two sentences subtly encompass the two hexagrams *Qian* and *Kun*, for the *Yin Fu* is the Dao of the *Book of Changes*. *"This is all"*—an exclamation of ultimate insight.

*"Heaven has five thieves; those who perceive them prosper. The five thieves reside in the heart, enacted upon heaven. The cosmos lies within the hand, the ten thousand transformations arise within the body."*

*Thieves* here align with the meanings of *restraint* and *plunder* below. The *five thieves* are the five phases. Heaven possesses the five phases, where mutual restraint harbors the subtlety of mutual generation, and mutual plunder conceals the mechanism of mutual appropriateness. One who penetrates this principle will see the flourishing of the Dao and qi. The five phases reside within the human heart; only by inverting and counter-applying them can mutual restraint and generation, mutual plunder and appropriateness be achieved. Observing this profound principle, one sees that it is already so in heaven. Those who embody heaven’s enactment will hold the cosmos in their hand and give birth to the ten thousand transformations within their body. *"Thus it is."*

Having spoken of *"giving birth to the body,"* the five thieves are no longer thieves. Heaven’s nature is humanity; the human heart is the trigger. *"Establish the Dao of heaven to stabilize human life."* *Nature* is principle. The *five thieves* are heaven’s principle, which is also humanity’s principle. Within the person, they are activated by the heart, enabling restraint to harbor generation and plunder to conceal appropriateness—all through the single word *trigger*. The *Yi Yi* states: *"Establish the Dao of heaven as yin and yang."* Speaking of heaven includes earth; speaking of earth includes humanity. Thus, *"establish the Dao of heaven to stabilize human life."* Yin and yang encompass the five phases—how could humanity exist outside yin and yang?

*"When heaven unleashes its killing mechanism, the stars and planets conceal (or fall) themselves. When earth unleashes its killing mechanism, dragons and serpents rise from the land. When humanity unleashes its killing mechanism, heaven and earth overturn. When heaven and humanity jointly unleash it, the ten thousand transformations establish their foundation."*

*Unleashing* is the mechanism of life; *killing* is the mechanism of death. Life and death are the triggers of movement and stillness. Concealing the mechanism of life within the mechanism of death is stillness preceding movement. *"Conceal themselves"* speaks of covert transformation—stillness is spoken of, yet movement is implied. *"Rise from the land"* speaks of surging forth from the earth—movement is spoken of, yet stillness is implied. The phrases of the *Yin Fu* often each express half, prompting the reader to infer the rest. *"When humanity unleashes its killing mechanism, heaven and earth overturn"*—humanity and heaven-earth form a triad, their killing mechanisms naturally analogous to the *Book of Changes*’ *"reversing its path, returning in seven days."* *Reversal* is the handle of life and death’s alternation, the power of waxing and waning’s control. *"When heaven and humanity jointly unleash it, the ten thousand transformations establish their foundation."*

"This mechanism is the same, this principle is the same. Long ago, Patriarch Lü transformed himself into a servant in the Chen household. Chen and a Daoist were discussing how *'when humanity unleashes its killing mechanism, heaven and earth overturn,'* yet they did not grasp the essence of the killing mechanism. Patriarch Lü interjected from the side, saying: *'The living do not truly live; the dead do not truly die. If one kills life after it has arisen and learns death before it arrives, then one attains longevity.'*

Now, considering Patriarch Lü’s words, we understand that the killing mechanism is the pivotal point of life and death. Alas, Chen was misled by the Daoist—startled at first, then swiftly bewildered.

Nature possesses both decay and clumsiness, which can be concealed. The nine orifices’ deviations lie in the three essentials, where movement and stillness operate. Fire arises from wood, yet disaster inevitably consumes it. Treachery arises in the state, and when the moment moves, it must collapse.

To know this and cultivate refinement is to be called a sage.

There is the nature of *qi*-constitution and the original nature. The nature of *qi*-constitution appears skillful but is truly clumsy. The original nature appears clumsy but is truly skillful. These two can be concealed. When concealed, they remain profoundly still; when stimulated, they penetrate all. The skillful conceals clumsiness, and clumsiness conceals skill.

The reason people cannot conceal themselves lies in the deviations of the nine orifices. Among these, the ears, eyes, and mouth are the three essentials. These three can move and can be still. In stillness, one contains the light of the eyes, condenses the resonance of the ears, and seals the *qi* of the tongue—thus the three essentials reverse into three treasures. In movement, form blinds, sound deafens, and flavor dulls—thus the three essentials become three calamities.

Do you not see fire? It arises from wood yet consumes wood, just as sight arises from the eyes yet harms them, hearing arises from the ears yet injures them, and flavor arises from the mouth yet dulls it. Likewise, treachery arises in the state: in stillness, deviance lies hidden; in movement, it collapses.

If one knows the mechanism of movement and stillness, masters stillness, and cultivates refinement, then one may be called a sage.

Heaven gives life, heaven takes life—such is the principle of the Dao. Heaven and earth are the thieves of the ten thousand things. The ten thousand things are the thieves of humanity. Humanity is the thief of the ten thousand things. When the three thieves are fitting, the three powers are at peace. Thus it is said: *'Eat in accord with the seasons, and the hundred parts of the body are ordered; move in accord with the mechanism, and the ten thousand transformations are at peace.'*"

Life and death are the wondrous principles of Heaven, which are the wondrous principles of the supreme Dao. That which kills first steals and conceals the qi of life. Heaven and earth are the thieves of the ten thousand things. Thus, at winter solstice when spring returns, the qi of life first conceals itself in the place of returning to the root. The ten thousand things are the thieves of humanity, so when sprouts emerge and husks split, the qi of life first conceals itself within the union of essence. Humanity is the thief of the ten thousand things, so when taking much and using broadly, the qi of life first conceals itself within preservation and nurturance. Within killing there is life—this is to cultivate primordial qi while awaiting the proper time, which is called "theft."

In every movement and stillness, the constant of the Two Modes is that they nurture and advance together, and the three thieves nourish one another. Once they nourish one another, Heaven, earth, and humanity mutually conceal their functions. The Three Powers are Heaven, earth, and humanity; though "things" are not mentioned, they are included. If one is not at peace, there must be something that does not accord. When the three thieves are fitting, the Three Powers are also at peace. When the Three Powers are at peace, they give birth to and nurture the ten thousand things, moving in accord with the seasons.

The phrase *"Thus it is said"* continues the above and cites an ancient saying: the time when the mechanism of life first stirs is the moment when the qi mechanism first moves. If one swallows it into one’s own domain at that very moment, then the hundred parts of the body are ordered. The mechanism is the mechanism of killing; after great stillness, the first stirring of life arises, and if one ingests it in accord with the time, then the ten thousand images are at great peace. Thus it is said: *"Move in accord with the mechanism, and the ten thousand transformations are at peace."*

People know the numinousness of the numinous, but do not know why the non-numinous is numinous. The sun and moon have their cycles, their waxing and waning have their measures, and sagely achievement arises from this, while the workings of spirit emerge from this. In the practice of refinement, nothing is more wondrous than spirit. People know that what is spiritually active is numinous, but do not know why what does not employ spiritual activity is numinous. This spirit arises from the extreme of stillness; when the time comes, the spirit knows, and skillfully discerns the mechanism of movement.

In its movement, the mechanism is like the sun and moon having their cycles. The *Cantong qi* says:
*"On the third day, brightness emerges, as Zhen and Geng receive from the west.
On the eighth day, Dui receives Ding, and the waxing half is level as a string.
On the fifteenth, the Qian body is complete, and the fullness of Jia fills the east.
On the sixteenth, it turns to receive governance, as Xun and Xin appear at dawn.
Gen meets Bing in the south, and the waning half is on the twenty-third.
On the thirtieth, Kun and Yi are in the northeast, where its light is lost."*
These are the cycles of the waxing and waning of the sun and moon.

The six gates and great medicine are measured by the sage using the waxing and waning of the sun and moon. And the six stages of obtaining the elixir should also be aligned with the measures of yin and yang. The *Cantong qi* says: *"Yin and yang intertwine, the small departs and the great arrives."* When the small departs, the preceding movement is brief—this is the second stage of seeking the medicine. When the great arrives, the subsequent movement is long—this is the fourth stage of compounding the elixir. The twelvefold firing times lie within the cycles of the sun and moon and the measures of their waxing and waning. The sage’s achievement arises from this, and the workings of spirit emerge from this.

As for the mechanism of theft, none in the world can see it, none can know it. The noble person who obtains it strengthens the self; the petty person who obtains it treats life lightly. The mechanism of theft is the mechanism of seizing and plundering. Not only are the mutual conquests of the Five Phases and the mutual encroachments of the Three Powers mechanisms of theft, but even embracing spirit and guarding qi, extracting from Kan to fill Li, are also mechanisms of theft. The perfected person silently moves the numinous mechanism, enters the watery palace, creates in the golden land, follows in the footsteps of the profoundly subtle, and cuts off seeing and hearing—within this, there is that which none can see, none can know. The numinousness of the numinous dies here, and the numinousness of the non-numinous arises here. Thus, the noble person who obtains this mechanism of theft can create destiny and strengthen the self, while the petty person who obtains it will surely harm the self and treat life lightly. This is what is meant by: *"The upright person uses it for uprightness, the deviant person uses it for deviance."*

The blind are skilled in listening, the deaf are skilled in seeing. Cutting off profit from one source yields tenfold advantage from the teacher. Three returns in day and night yield ten thousandfold advantage from the teacher. The blind use their ears as eyes, listening to discern people’s smiles and expressions—thus it is said: *"The blind are skilled in listening."* The deaf use their eyes as ears, using them to gauge people’s voices and emotions—thus it is said: *"The deaf are skilled in seeing."* Yet within our own bodies, there are those without eyes who can listen with subtlety, and those without ears who can see with acuity. Skill in seeing and listening lies in distinguishing whether the source of water is clear or turbid.

Laozi said: *"Water is good at benefiting the ten thousand things."* From this, we know that cutting off excessive beauty reveals the true source of the One. *"Yields tenfold advantage from the teacher"* refers to the *Yijing*’s hexagram of Earth over Water as "the teacher." Earth conquers water yet does not move it, so the water becomes even clearer. Using the teacher means employing earth to conquer water, advancing through danger yet proceeding smoothly. With this, one poisons the world, yet the people follow—what blame could there be? Ten is spoken of as the number of completion.

*"Three returns"* refers to the number three belonging to wood. The fire essence and water mercury of the Li palace are born within wood, and the fire concealed within wood sinks together into the Kun palace. Kun truly completes Kan and gives birth to water, and the true qi within water becomes metal. After wood carries metal back, one must use *Zhun* and *Meng* to draw and add—thus it is said: *"Three returns in day and night yield ten thousandfold advantage from the teacher."* Ten thousand is spoken of as the number of wholeness.

The fire tally of ten months can refine the treasure into the elixir. Seven returns and nine refinements are the three returns. The mind is born from things and dies from things. The mechanism lies in the eyes. Heaven is without kindness, yet great kindness arises from it. Swift thunder and fierce winds—none do not stir foolishly. The mind moves because of things—this is being born from things. Pursuing things to the point of loss—this is dying from things.

The deviance of the nine orifices lies in the three essentials. Of the deviance of the three essentials, nothing is more essential than the eyes. To halt the mechanism, one must employ "no kindness." Heaven is without kindness, yet great kindness arises from it—within death there is life, within movement there is stillness. Swift thunder and fierce winds all arise from the midst of foolish stirring, yet naturally possess numinous activity.

Should Heaven perpetually employ its wind and thunder, the numinous qi of wind and thunder would eventually be exhausted. How then, beneath the heavy yin, could it await the resurgence of earthly thunder and summon the renewing winds of *xun*?

Supreme joy is abundant in nature; supreme stillness is restrained in nature. Heaven’s utmost partiality becomes the utmost impartiality in its use. Extending the principle of "no kindness" from above, "no kindness" is akin to "non-action"—the utmost of joy, the utmost of stillness. Supreme joy is leisurely and abundant in nature; supreme stillness is pure and incorrupt in nature. Thus, at times Heaven thunders without wind, appearing utterly partial. Yet from this seeming chaos, it issues commands—this is Heaven’s utmost impartiality. Utmost partiality resembles "no kindness," while utmost impartiality gives rise to great kindness.

The regulation of creatures lies in qi. Life is the root of death; death is the root of life. Kindness arises from harm, and harm arises from kindness. Sinking water into fire invites self-destruction. (These two lines, in Wang Fengzhou’s edition, follow "harm arises from kindness," forming a coherent meaning; here, the text follows his version.)

The *Baihu Tong* states: "禽 (*qin*) means to be captured and controlled by man." The regulation of creatures lies in qi—qi is spoken of in terms of fire, and creatures in terms of the Vermilion Bird. To regulate creatures with qi is to regulate fire with fire. The *Cantong Qi* says: "The Vermilion Bird is the essence of fire, holding balance and adjusting victory and defeat." This refers to the moment when earth suppresses water and prevents its rise; only the Vermilion Bird’s fire can maintain equilibrium, adjust the balance of power, and through intense refinement, cause water to boil and steam to rise, carrying metal upward with it. Thus, the regulation of the Vermilion Bird lies in sinking fire into water to lift water and restrain fire.

Within this lies the mechanism of life and death: when water boils and fire ascends into the *Li* palace, the *Li* fire is extinguished by the *Kan* water—this is "life as the root of death." Subdued and constrained, unable to fly or flee, lead and mercury both perish, returning together to the fertile earth. Only through this great death does true life emerge—this is "death as the root of life."

"Kindness arises from harm": sinking fire into water. "Harm arises from kindness": drawing water into fire. The marvel of sinking water into fire lies in its self-generated life and death within the process, hence it is said: "self-invited destruction." Fools revere the patterns of Heaven and Earth as sacred, while I regard the patterns of the seasons and things as wise.

Fools deem those who feign ignorance as sages; I deem those who do not feign ignorance as sages. Fools regard those who await the appointed time as sages; I regard those who do not await the appointed time as sages. (These six lines are said by tradition to be the words of Wu Bo’s praise. The Fengzhou manuscript omits them, but I retain them here, placing them below “self-invited destruction.”)

Fools take mastery of celestial patterns and scrutiny of earthly principles as sagacity—yet if this is all, what benefit does it bring to body and mind? I examine the marvel of seasonal movement within celestial patterns and contemplate the mechanism of life’s emergence within earthly principles, stealing from Heaven and Earth to seize the workings of Creation—only then do I not shame the name of wisdom. This is what is meant by “observing the Dao of Heaven and grasping the movements of Heaven”—it is perfection itself.

“Fools deem those who feign ignorance as sages; I deem those who do not feign ignorance as sages.” This is what is meant by “those who refine through knowledge are called sages.” “Fools regard those who await the appointed time as sages; I regard those who do not await the appointed time as sages.” This is what is meant by “people know the divine as divine, yet do not know the divinity of the non-divine.”

Moreover, the sun and moon follow fixed paths, the great and small adhere to their measures, the Three Powers steal from one another—all are the sagely workings of nature. What is strange about this?

These six lines all seem to elaborate and praise the preceding passage.

The Dao of nature is quiescent, thus Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are born. The Dao of Heaven and Earth is gradual, thus yin and yang prevail. Yin and yang propel one another, and transformations proceed in harmony. The Dao of nature is simply quiescence alone. From quiescence arises movement, and through movement, Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are born. The Dao of Heaven and Earth is simply gradualness alone. Gradualness is the image of nature. Yin gradually descends, yang gradually ascends. The marvel of yin and yang’s ascent and descent lies in their mutual overcoming. Without mutual overcoming, there is no mutual propulsion. Yin and yang propel one another, and transformations proceed in accordance with nature.

Transformations are the image of advance and retreat. Master Zhu said: “These four lines on nature are exquisitely put.” He also said: “The character ‘gradual’ is wonderfully chosen.”

Thus, the sage knows the Dao of nature cannot be defied and thereby harnesses it. The Dao of utmost quiescence cannot be matched by calendrical calculations. Hence, there is the marvelous device from which arise the ten thousand images, the Eight Trigrams, and the celestial stems and earthly branches. Within it lie the divine mechanisms, the hidden workings of ghosts, and the arts of yin and yang’s mutual overcoming. How luminous—it transcends mere images!

To “thereby harness it” means to take the Dao of nature and fashion it into the methods of refinement. The Dao of nature truly emerges from utmost quiescence. Within this quiescence, there exists another realm of years and months, Heaven and Earth—unfathomable by human calendars and measurements.

Where does quiescence reside? There is a marvelous device: the Mysterious Pass, the single aperture. Within it, the ten thousand images arise, the Eight Trigrams transform, and the celestial stems and earthly branches revolve. The divine mechanisms are unfathomable, the hidden workings of ghosts are inscrutable, and the numerology of yin and yang’s mutual overcoming all emerge from within. How luminous—this is a principle visible to all, not some obscure or esoteric matter. It transcends mere images, for images are but the images of the *Yi*.

The *Taishang Huangting Waijing Jing*
Secret Manuscript of the Two Perfected Ones, Danxia and Qingxia
Commentary by Hanxuzi of Zixia Cave, Mount Yuanqiao

**Chapter One**
Laozi, in leisure, composed seven-character verses,
Explaining the body’s form and the various spirits.
Above lies the Yellow Court, below the Pass of Origin,
Before the Dark Gate, behind the Gate of Life.
Breathing the void into the Cinnabar Field,
Jade Pool’s pure water irrigates the numinous root.
The Youth Who Governs the True consumes fetal essence—
Those who can refine this may endure forever.

This chapter is the opening hymn of the Lesser Yang Patriarch. The Patriarch said: Lord Lao often recited the Jade Texts, and from the *Neijing Jing*, he distilled the essentials into seven-character verses, still calling it the *Huangting*, but distinguishing it as the *Waijing*. “Inner” and “outer” refer to before and after, not the internal and external of the body. What it expounds are the five forms of the body and the ten thousand spirits within—truly essential teachings.

The Most High takes emptiness and non-being as the root. Between above and below, before and behind, there exists a circle of emptiness and non-being. Those who can guard this will find their breath deeply entering the Cinnabar Field, nurturing the primordial, utterly pure water in the Jade Pool of another’s household to irrigate their own numinous root. When the numinous root is firm, the foundation of the elixir is established.

The Youth Who Governs the True is the fetal infant. The fetal infant is the ancestor of the ten thousand spirits. Nurturing it daily, consuming the mysterious essence of fetal breathing—those who refine this may attain immortality. Irrigating the numinous root speaks of nourishing the self with the postnatal; consuming fetal essence speaks of nurturing the elixir with the prenatal. Such is the marvelous meaning of this hymn.

The *Huangting Jing* transmitted in the world omits Laozi’s two lines on leisure and alters the rest into four-character verses: “Above lies the Yellow Court, below the Pass of Origin, before the Dark Gate, behind the Gate of Life. Breathing beyond the hut, entering and exiting the Cinnabar Field—those who can practice this may endure forever.” The line about the Youth Who Governs the True is missing from all versions. Without seeing the true text, one nearly loses the true visage of Mount Lu.

When I first intended to annotate the *Huangting*, I was suddenly visited in my mountain studio by the Perfected One Danxia, who said: “I received a letter from Lady Wei, instructing you to collate and annotate the *Huangting*, and to show you the secret manuscript from the Orchid Terrace for a day.” There was a Daoist, Canxia, who obtained a manuscript from Yang, the Prefectural Governor of Nanyue, and sent it to Chipingzi, who then showed it to me. Comparing it with Danxia’s version, I found that the celestial palace’s secret records were indeed identical. Delighted, I recorded this in the annotations.

**Chapter Two**

The Perfected One of the Yellow Court wears crimson robes,
With bolts and locks sealing the double gates.
The Dark Gatekeeper makes them towering and majestic,
While within the Cinnabar Field, essence and qi grow subtle.

The Jade Pool’s limpid water nurtures fertile soil,
The numinous root stands firm, never aging nor withering.
*Yao* and *yao* are both locks—their mechanism has yin and yang:
When the gate is closed, the yang bolt enters the yin socket,
so the lock fastens and the double gates seal.
This metaphor speaks of spirit and qi uniting,
like yin and yang interlocking.
Those who write *zhuang* (壮) are mistaken.

"Nurturing fertile soil" means drawing the purest, undisturbed water
from another’s Jade Pool to irrigate daily.
Thus, barren earth transforms into rich soil,
and the numinous root takes deep, stable hold,
remaining steadfast and unyielding,
never withering even in old age.
I have seen this in the fourth chapter of the *Inner Radiance*.

---

**Chapter Three**
Within the Central Pool dwells a scholar clad in crimson,
Three inches below the field lies the spirit’s abode.
Inner and outer are kept apart, doubly sealed—
Within the spirit’s chamber, devote yourself to cultivation.
The Mysterious Throat’s qi-channel receives the essence-tally;
Hasten to secure your essence to sustain yourself.

The Central Pool is the Sea of Qi, also called the Center.
Within it resides one who cultivates the body,
clad in crimson robes—this refers to the heart-spirit.
The *field* is the Cinnabar Field.
One inch below it is the Pass of Origin,
two inches, the Central Pole,
three inches, the Meeting of Yin—
all are dwelling places of the spirit,
not just the Central Pool.
Though the Central Pool and the three inches below the field
are separated by inner and outer,
if they are tightly sealed layer upon layer,
the inner spirit will not escape.

The *spirit’s chamber* is the spirit’s dwelling.
Within it, one must cultivate purity and stillness,
allowing no external distractions to intrude.
Preserve it as if it were ever-present,
nurturing the inner fire of spirit.
When water suddenly arrives to balance it,
the Mysterious Throat’s qi-channel bears the essence-tally.
The *essence-tally* is the union of essence and spirit,
using water to temper fire.
Essence within my body is called *child-essence*.
From this, swiftly secure and harmonize it to sustain yourself,
and the Orthodox Unity will contain its bloom.

---

**Chapter Four**
Within the dwelling resides a scholar ever clad in deep red—
If you can perceive him, you will not fall ill.
A horizontal path, a foot in length, binds him above;
If you can guard it, you will know no harm.
Inhaling and exhaling within the chamber to replenish yourself,
Preserve its wholeness and firmness, and the body will receive blessings.
Within the square inch, carefully conceal and store—
Spirit and essence return, and old age regains youth.
Let the Dark Gatekeeper’s flow descend to its end,
Nurture the jade tree of the child so it may serve as a staff.

The opening line echoes the *Inner Radiance*:
"Within the dwelling resides a Perfected One, ever clad in cinnabar."
The *dwelling* is the numinous abode,
the ground where yin elixir is cultivated.
It is also called the *chamber* or the *square inch*.
Measured horizontally, its length does not exceed a foot,
binding the inner spirit above.
Ensure the inner realm does not leak out,
and the outer realm does not intrude.
Guard it here, breathe here,
preserve and nurture here,
conceal and store here,
and spirit and essence return to their root here.

The Dark Gatekeeper makes the flow surge downward to its end—
*end* means cessation.
*Nurturing the jade tree of the child* means irrigating the numinous root,
so its jade branches flourish,
able to serve as a staff.
This metaphor speaks of the inner elixir’s accomplishment,
the meaning of supporting old age.

---

**Chapter Five**
The Supreme Dao is not burdensome nor deviates from its course,
The numinous terrace stands at heaven’s center, overlooking the central wilds.

Within the square inch lies the space beneath the Dark Gate,
Within the Jade Chamber stand the spirit’s gates and doors.
All these were taught to me by the Lord’s Son.

*Wù* means opposition, but also movement.
The Supreme Dao moves in reversal,
yet differs from the heterodox paths’ reversal—
those that ascend left and descend right are not the true course.
It must rise behind and descend before,
for this is the great road of nature.

The Numinous Terrace arises at heaven’s center,
the very place where the true heart manifests.
Below, it overlooks the central wilds,
its attention fixed upon the Central Yellow Court.
The *square inch* is the compass’s center;
*beneath the Dark Gate* is below the navel.
The Jade Chamber is the *Inner Radiance*’s Golden Casket and Jade Chamber—
when speaking of the Jade Chamber, the Golden Casket is also within.

Man’s spirit dwells in the eyes,
hence they are the spirit’s gates and doors,
as the classic says: “The mechanism lies in the eyes.”
This is the essence of the Supreme Dao,
all taught to me by the Lord’s Son, Wood’s Duke.
The Lord’s Son is the Thunder Sovereign,
the eldest son, the Dragon.
The Dragon emerges from fire,
mercury’s nature is numinous and penetrating.
The Dao teaches one to first clarify one’s own nature;
when one’s own nature is clarified,
one awakens of oneself.
Awakening of oneself
is like the Lord’s Son teaching me.

---

**Chapter Six**
The Hall of Light extends in four directions, the source of the Dharma Sea.
Before me stands the True Man’s Elixir Child.
Between the Three Passes, essence and qi run deep—
if you wish not to die, cultivate Kunlun.

The Hall of Light is the Hall of Light Palace.
This palace lies within an inch between the brows,
where sun, moon, and constellations shine with radiance,
hence “extends in four directions.”
When the medicine is circulated here,
it is the gathering place of the elixir method,
the source of living water from which all transforms—
hence “the source of the Dharma Sea.”

The *Elixir Child* is the Golden Embryo.
The Golden Embryo is the True Man’s body.
With metal restraining wood,
the mother comes to see the child,
forming the true body—
hence “the True Man’s Elixir Child.”
From the Hall of Light, one enters the Spirit Chamber;
the Spirit Chamber lies before the Hall of Light,
hence “stands before me.”

The Three Passes are explained in the *Inner Radiance* commentary.
*“If you wish not to die, cultivate Kunlun”*—
this is a wondrous phrase within the mysterious,
not to be overlooked.

Upper Kunlun is the place for refining the yang spirit,
the realm of longevity and distant death—
this is what all know.
Yet there is more:
Middle Kunlun, hidden within the body,
is a realm of utmost purity and emptiness.
Qi and spirit merge,
blending into oneness,
as if entering a boundless void.
Though the body contains the Three Passes—upper, middle, and lower—
here, no such divisions exist.
The One True shines alone,
all phenomena are void,
transcending all extremes,
attaining the towering realm.
As Master Zhang Sanfeng’s *Daoist Song* says:
*“The idle True Man hides within,
the Master sits atop Kunlun.”*
Here, one beholds the precelestial, immortal yang spirit,
free from the postcelestial, karmic yin spirit—
hence *“Between the Three Passes, essence and qi run deep—
if you wish not to die, cultivate Kunlun.”*

Those who recite the *Yellow Court Classic*
must focus on the words *“between.”*

There is also Lower Kunlun,
the source of firing the elixir—
all should know this.

Those who cultivate Kunlun
must concentrate their spirit between the Three Passes.
Here lies the purest essence,
the most refined qi.
When spirit enters this realm,
it is profound, vast, and boundless,
utterly empty—
the heart is perfectly clear yet free from thoughts.
In an instant, one transcends to the highest vehicle.

Ordinary people, upon seeing “Kunlun,”
say the secret lies in the Mud Pill Palace.
Had it not been for the repeated guidance of my masters Wushan and Baohe,
who would have known to direct their attention
between the Three Passes?

---

**Chapter Seven**
The Crimson Palace’s twelve-tiered tower,
within its halls, the five hues gather.
The Red Spirit’s Child stands in the Central Pool,
below lies the Long Wall and the Dark Valley’s town.

The essential wonder of longevity is received in the chamber.
The Crimson Palace lies beneath the tower.
When the medicine enters the Crimson Palace,
it must first descend the lung’s twelve-tiered tower.

*“Within its halls”* refers to the Yellow Court.
Pursuing the two qi along the Yellow Path,
uniting the three natures in the Yellow Court,
the five qi return to their origin,
all beauties assemble—
hence *“the five hues gather.”*

The *Red Spirit’s Child* is true intent.
Born from the heart, it is called the Red Spirit’s Child.
The *Central Pool* is the Primordial Sea.
When the five hues gather,
true earth seizes true lead,
true lead restrains true mercury,
stabilized and unmoving,
spirit and qi congeal—
hence *“stands.”*

Below the Central Pool lies the Long Wall—
the Golden Dike.
There is also the Dark Valley—
the Water Realm.
The town within is where cultivators receive their sustenance,
like a human city with walls enclosing it,
moats surrounding it—
a place of utmost security.

The essential wonder of longevity
is obtained from the chamber.
Daoists call it the *life-extending technique*,
akin to the union of man and woman,
yet not the art of sexual combat—
merely the mutual resonance of spirit and qi,
the merging of metal and wood.

---

**Chapter Eight**
Abandon lust and desire, focus on your essence;
the inch-field and foot-dwelling can sustain life.
Tethered to the long flow, the heart finds peace,
observing the will, the spirit roams—threefold numinosity.
At leisure, without affairs, the heart is at great peace.

The first line urges one to renounce lust
and secure essence by guarding against desire.
The *inch-field* is the Gate of Life.
The *foot-dwelling* is the spirit’s abode.
The *Inner Radiance* says:
*“Square and round, an inch within the Gate of Life.”*
This text says:
*“Measured horizontally, its length does not exceed a foot,
binding the inner spirit above.”*
This is the place to nurture oneself and preserve the heart—
the common folk mistake it for the face, but this is wrong.

If the heart is not at peace,
it is due to outward distraction and restless wandering.
Thus, one must tether it within to calm the heart.
*“Observing the will”* means closing off and inwardly gazing
to uphold one’s resolve.
*“The spirit roams”* means the True Man sinks into the deep abyss,
floating and guarding the compass’s center,
with the bearing of one swimming in self-contentment.

At this moment, essence, qi, and spirit unite as one, undivided—hence called the Three Wondrous Numinous. Leisurely and at ease, without affairs or action, the heart’s realm attains supreme peace.

**Chapter Nine**
Constantly preserve the Jade Chamber so spirit achieves clarity,
Timely contemplate the Great Granary to avoid hunger and thirst.
Command the Six Ding spirit-maidens to attend,
Seal the path of essence—thus may one live long.

"Constantly preserve the Jade Chamber" means moment by moment abiding in spirit; with eyes closed and inwardly guarded, spirit does not wander outward, growing ever more settled and wise. People know the spirit’s numinosity as clarity, yet do not realize that the spirit’s non-numinosity is true clarity. "Timely contemplate the Great Granary" means regulating and nurturing it with light flavors. The Great Granary is the stomach, where intention-as-earth resides; where intention abides, there too is the Great Granary—one must not cling to the stomach as a physical form. Daoist adepts regard the Mysterious Pass as the Great Granary Cavity, the place of embryonic breathing and abiding breath. The *Inner Radiance* states, *“Square and round, an inch within the Gate of Life.”* My mountain’s teaching holds it as the dwelling of intention—a truly penetrating discourse. When intention-as-earth flourishes, it enables the stomach to digest grains and liquids, transforming food and drink, hence “to avoid hunger and thirst.” The Six Ding spirits are called “maidens” as they are yin spirits. When yang spirit resides within me, I can command the Six Ding, summoning them to attend. Such is the Way of long life—all hinges on sealing the path of essence.

**Chapter Ten**
Within the central chamber, spirit’s dwelling place,
Wash the heart and govern oneself—let no defilement dare intrude.
Survey the five viscera, inspect their order,
The six bowels refined and purified, pristine as silk.
Emptiness and spontaneity are the Dao’s origin.

The "central chamber" is the spirit’s central abode, neither biased nor partial. With the heart cleansed and withdrawn, one diligently cultivates and governs, allowing no impurity to defile it. Thus, inwardly observing the five viscera, each appears as if illuminated by candlelight; the body’s rhythms may all be scrutinized. Thus, inwardly observing, the six bowels are each refined and purified, immaculate as undyed silk, utterly free of filth. The Dao of emptiness and spontaneity has been thus since antiquity. *Gu* means origin.

**Chapter Eleven**
Things possess their nature—affairs need not be burdensome,
Hands folded in repose, non-action brings the body ease.
Embodying emptiness, the heart finds leisure,
Silent and vast, the mouth utters no word.
Cultivating harmony, one stands alone in the True Person’s palace,
Serene and desireless, wandering the garden of virtue.
Pure and fragrant, immaculate, the Jade Maiden abides,
Cultivating virtue and illuminating goodness—this is the Dao’s gate.

Things include yin things and yang things,
Dividing inner and outer within Heaven and Earth.
Here, the term *thing* refers to what lies within.
For those who cultivate inner things, there is a principle of spontaneity—
No need for excess; their affairs are inherently unburdensome.
When the body is at ease, essence remains unmoved;
When the heart is at leisure, spirit is undisturbed;
When the mouth speaks not, qi does not scatter.
One should simply sit upright in non-action,
Embodying emptiness, free of things,
Silent and vast.
Cultivating the Great Harmony, guarding the center, standing alone—
This is called the True Person’s palace.
Serene and desireless within,
Wandering the garden of virtue, the jade sprout glows resplendent.
At this time, purity is untainted, fragrance unstained,
And the Jade Maiden endures.
The Jade Maiden is a metaphor for inner mercury.
*Virtue* is what one receives from Heaven—
Empty, numinous, and unobscured,
Possessing all principles and responding to the myriad things.
Cultivation restores its empty, numinous essence.
*Goodness* is what Heaven bestows upon humanity—
The primal spark,
The root of nature and the stem of the heart.
Illuminating it reveals one’s original truth.
The gate of the Great Dao first teaches one to fulfill their nature—
This lies in cultivating virtue and illuminating goodness.

---

**Chapter Twelve**
Practicing the Dao in leisure, the body dwells alone,
Nurturing nature and life, guarding emptiness and nothingness.
Serene and desireless—what thoughts or cares arise?
Wings fully formed, upright and flourishing,
One attains long life and enduring vision, then takes flight.

*Practicing the Dao* refers to cultivation.
The cultivator, whether in the world or beyond it,
Must move with ease and contentment,
Guarding their body and dwelling alone.
Within the body lie nature and life—
One must daily uphold and nurture them.
First refine jade to illuminate nature,
Then refine gold to establish life—
The essential secret lies in inwardly guarding emptiness and nothingness.
Born between Heaven and Earth,
Humans are beset by thoughts and cares.
If one guards emptiness and nothingness,
They become serene and desireless,
Acting without action—what thoughts or cares remain?
The immortals refine qi to forge wings;
When spirit is complete and qi is full,
The wings are formed.
*Upright and flourishing* describes the flourishing of spirit and qi.
From this, nature is preserved within,
The body is fortified without,
One may appear or vanish in the world,
Attaining long life and enduring vision.
Weary of the dusty realm,
They shed their shell and take flight.

---

**Chapter Thirteen**
The Five Phases interweave, sharing a common root and node,
Three and five unite—essence lies in their oneness.
Who shares this with the Dipper and Sun-Moon?
Embracing the pearl, cherishing the jade, harmonizing the Chamber of the Child.
If you can guard the One, all affairs are complete;
It is yours to hold—do not lose it.
If you wish not to die, conceal it in the golden chamber.

*Interweaving* refers to their intricate interplay.
In the Five Phases’ sequence,
Metal, Water, Wood, Fire, and Earth generate and nourish one another.
In their interweaving,
Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth restrain and regulate each other.
Between them, Wood descends and Metal ascends,
Water presides and Fire serves—
Their functions cannot be equalized,
Thus the Five Phases interweave.
Yet when their qi converges,
They share a common root and node.
Three and five unite—
Their sum is eight,
Dividing yin and yang,
Fitting the return after seven days.
Tracing their origin,
They arise from the void, generating the One qi.
This One qi produces yin and yang—
Thus their essence lies in oneness.
This One qi is nurtured in emptiness.
*Who shares this?* The Northern Dipper and the Two Luminaries.
The Dipper’s handle and scoop move with the stars to mark time,
The Sun of Li and Moon of Kan—
Water and Fire dwell in the same palace, refining together.
I merely embrace the bright pearl,
Cherish the fine jade,
And cultivate harmony in the Chamber of the Child.
The Chamber of the Child is emptiness.
If you can guard the One qi of emptiness,
Awaiting its arrival to seize it,
Then nature and life are yours—
All affairs are complete.
The One is a thing you inherently possess—
Simply hold it firmly and do not lose it.
Lose it, and you die;
Retain it, and you do not die.
If you wish not to die,
You must conceal this One within the golden chamber,
Never parting from it—
Thus the Great Elixir is accomplished.

---

**Chapter Fourteen**
Emerging with the sun, entering with the moon—this is my Dao,
Heaven’s seven and Earth’s two guard together.
Ascending and descending, advancing and retreating—one unites with nine,
Jade and stone gleaming—this is my treasure.
You possess it yourself—why not guard it?

The heart understands the root and stem, nurturing radiant splendor,
Serving Heaven and following Earth, concealing the sea of essence.
Within seven days, they return and unite,
The nature of Kunlun remains unconfused.

*Emerging with the sun, entering with the moon* describes the path of yang departing and yin arriving—
Thus it is my Dao.
*Heaven’s seven* is the fire’s completion number.
Fire completes in Heaven, descending from above—
The hexagram *Wei Ji* (Before Completion).
*Earth’s two* is the fire’s generating number,
Fire arises from Earth, ascending from below—
The hexagram *Ji Ji* (After Completion).
These two hexagrams guard one another—
This is the work of Metal and Water.
*Ascending and descending, advancing and retreating* refer to above and below.
*One unites with nine*—Metal and Water must not part.
Advancing, Water’s Metal ascends;
Retreating, Metal’s Water descends—
This is the wonder of augmentation and reduction.
*Jade and stone gleaming* compares the golden liquid,
Droplet by droplet, like eggs or pills,
Descending the throat—
The supreme treasure of my body.
The Golden Liquid Returned Elixir is inherent in everyone—
Having attained it, how could one not guard it?
The work of guarding it is to deepen the root and firm the stem.
Those who understand this Dao know no method more wondrous than the heart.
When the heart comprehends the root and stem,
It nurtures the Returned Elixir with Metal and Water—
Glowing brilliantly, gradually producing radiant splendor.
This method requires only serving Qian Heaven,
Following Kun Earth,
Acting in accord with Heaven’s timing,
And concealing the sea of essence.
Within seven days, they return and unite,
The nature of Kunlun remains unconfused.

The Sea of Essence is the Primordial Sea. Within the seven days of returning and uniting, the revolving celestial cycle rises into the inner courtyard, merging with the elixir already conjoined within me. It is nurtured in the Central Kunlun and harmonized in the Upper Kunlun, where emptiness becomes clarity and the original nature shines with perfect brightness. Thus it is said, "The nature of Kunlun remains unconfused."

**Chapter Fifteen**
How towering stands the Mountain of Nine Springs!
Within it dwells a true person who may be summoned.
Shielded by the Crimson Palace and the Elixir City Tower,
Flanked by the sun and moon like luminous pearls.
For ten thousand years, its radiance shines without end—
Externally, the Three Yang substances arrive of themselves;
Internally, nurturing the Three Spirits grants longevity.
When the hun-soul longs for heaven and the po-soul sinks into the abyss,
To return the hun and restore the po is the Dao’s natural way,
That one may form the pearl and secure the numinous root.

The Nine Springs are the Nine Liquid Sources spoken of in the *Inner Landscape*. Nine is Metal; the source is Water. The preceding text spoke of the function of Metal and Water; this chapter speaks of their root. The Nine Springs lie within the Lower Kunlun, hence "the Mountain of Nine Springs." *Tingting* describes the mountain’s height. Within the mountain dwells a true person, named the Mysterious Dark Youth or Peach Vitality, who may be employed as a servant. "Shielded by the Crimson Palace and the Elixir City Tower, flanked by the sun and moon like luminous pearls" describes the strictness of its protection. When protection is strict, the inner fire remains pure, and the immortal Dao may be attained. "For ten thousand years, its radiance shines"—enduring brightness and prolonged vision cannot be measured by any fixed term. This is the joy of the Great Return.

When one first refines the elixir within and establishes the crucible without, Qian’s Three Yang and Kun’s Three Yin are inverted, host and guest intermingled. When Qian enters Kun, the Three Yang lie externally, and in an instant, the primordial single entity arrives of itself, unbidden—this is called "lead within water." With lead, one restrains mercury; from without, one returns within, gathering essence to nurture spirit, using the Three Yang to replenish the Three Yin, restoring the Qian body and thereby achieving longevity. The Three Spirits are the Three Yin. To nurture the Three Spirits internally is to subdue the outer elixir with the inner elixir. Once the inner elixir is subdued, clarity and stability prevail, and one may attain long life. Those who do not attain long life, at the moment of death, find their hun-soul longing for heaven and their po-soul sinking into the abyss—hun and po estranged, lead and mercury each following their own path. To return the hun and restore the po is to draw the hun downward when it seeks to ascend and raise the po upward when it seeks to descend, so that hun and po constrain one another. As the *Awakening to Reality* says, "The earthly po seizes the cinnabar mercury; the celestial hun restrains the watery metal." This is the natural affair of the Great Dao. When elixir refinement reaches this point, one may nearly have formed the luminous pearl, forever securing the wondrous root and becoming an immortal.

Chapter Sixteen

The revolving pivot suspends the pearl in endless gyre,
Jade key and golden key seal the body whole and firm.
Borne by earth, it revolves through heaven, circling Qian and Kun,
Its image the four seasons, red as cinnabar bright.

Before high, behind low—each a different gate,
One sends forth the returning elixir, the other the mysterious spring.
The tortoise draws qi to the numinous root.

The revolving pivot: when advancing the Yang fire, the pivot revolves upward.
The suspended pearl: when withdrawing the Yin tally, the pearl jade descends.
These two, like a ring without end.

When they enter within, the Yang elixir and Yin elixir congeal in union,
Jade key and golden key seal and secure the original body, whole and firm.
Borne by earth: the Yellow Court’s Kun earth.
Revolving through heaven: the white fluid’s Qian metal.
Within one celestial cycle, the wonders of Qian and Kun’s Yin and Yang are exhausted.
Its bathing at Mao and You, its waxing and waning, its generation and completion,
Again mirror the division of the four seasons.
As the four seasons proceed, with the blossoms and fruits of the four seasons,
In the tenth month, frost flies, leaves yellow, and fruit ripens—
Its redness like cinnabar—this is why returning to the root and reverting to the origin is called "elixir."

The place where the elixir is nurtured: before high, behind low.
Before, the Ren vessel ends at the lower abdomen—its image high;
Behind, the Du vessel begins at the tailbone—its form low.
One high, one low—these are the gates for the Golden Elixir’s exit and entry.
Their comings and goings differ: the outward journey sends forth, the inward journey returns.
When the returning elixir is in hand, it is entrusted to the mysterious spring.
The mysterious spring is the deep abyss within the void.
Vulgar explanations take it as the sublingual spring—this is exoteric speech.
The closing line says, "The tortoise draws qi to the numinous root."
If it were not deep, how could it be called the numinous root?

---

Chapter Seventeen

Within is a true person, head wrapped in golden cloth,
Bearing armor and holding talismans, opening the seven gates.
This is no branch or leaf—it is the very root.
Day and night, ponder it, and you may long endure.

"Within" refers to the midst of the deep abyss, not within the fleshly heart.
The seven gates correspond to the seven orifices.
The fleshly heart has seven orifices; the numinous heart also has seven orifices.
This may be compared with chapter twenty-eight of the *Inner Landscape*.

---

Chapter Eighteen

Immortal and Daoist are not possessed of spirits—
They accumulate essence and amass qi to become true.
All people eat grains and the five flavors,
Yet they alone consume the Great Harmony’s Yin-Yang qi.
Thus they do not die, and their heavenly phase is exhausted.

The qi of Great Harmony is that which Yin and Yang unite to form.
"Exhausted" means "fully realized."
To be exhausted with heaven is truly to be without exhaustion.

---

Chapter Nineteen

Let me speak of the five viscera, each with its direction:
The heart is the lord of the state, king of the five viscera.
When intent moves or rests, qi accompanies it—
The Dao naturally guards my spirit’s luminous light.
It connects my flowery essence to harmonize Yin and Yang,
All day radiant, at night self-guarding.
Thirst is quenched, hunger sated of itself,
Passing through the six bowels, stored at Mao and You.
Turning Yang into Yin, hidden within the nine,
If one can practice this with care, one will never grow old.

The five viscera take the heart as their ruler; the person takes the body as their state—
Thus the heart is called the king.
Intent arises from the true heart: at rest, it depends on qi; in motion, it assists qi.
These two are one, hence it is said they "accompany" it.
To attain true intent, the Dao lies in guarding the spirit’s luminosity.
If one can guard the spirit’s luminosity, one can generate true intent,
Connect one’s flowery essence, and harmonize Yin and Yang.
Flowery essence is true lead.
When lead and mercury unite, Yin and Yang harmonize.
From this, at dawn one practices *zhun*, at dusk *meng*;
By day, radiant and advancing fire; by night, silent and advancing the tally.
Withdrawing metal and adding water, thirst is quenched and hunger sated.
"Passing through the six bowels" means primordial qi fills the entire body,
Storing the elixir at the Mao and You gates.
To store is to turn Yang into Yin, like the hidden dragon concealed in the first nine—
All is the work of intent’s movement and rest.
If one can practice this with care, one will never grow old.

---

Chapter Twenty

The qi of the liver is supple and long,
Arraying the five viscera to generate the three luminaries.
Above, it joins the three burners; below, the jade nectar.
My spirit, hun-soul, and po-soul reside at the center.
Essential fluids flow like springs, transforming foulness into fragrance,
Standing at the suspended hall, embracing the Hall of Light.
Thunder and lightning, claps and bolts, follow one another—
Left Mao, right You—these are my treasures.
Hidden at the mysterious gate, awaiting the heavenly Dao,
Near to my body, yet guarding itself.
In purity and non-action, the spirit lingers and abides,
Essence and spirit ascend and descend, each in its proper place.
The heavenly Dao is unobstructed, the herb of long life grows—
The seven orifices are opened, and one never grows old.
Sitting at the Gate of Heaven, awaiting Yin and Yang,
Descending through the throat to connect with the spirit’s luminosity.
Passing beneath the flowery canopy, cool and clear,
Entering the icy abyss, one sees one’s own form.
When the returning elixir is complete, one may long endure.
Below is the flowery pond, stirring the kidney’s essence.
Standing at the Hall of Light, facing the cinnabar field,
It commands the various spirits to open the Gate of Life.
The heavenly Dao is unobstructed, reaching the numinous root—
Yin and Yang arrayed like streaming stars.

(One version ends with "The liver’s qi circulates without end," which seems to capture the essence of this chapter. Perhaps a later commentator’s note, these seven characters were mistakenly incorporated into the text and are preserved here.)

The liver’s qi is expansive and flowing, abundant and vigorous—
Truly able to shelter the entire body.
Earlier it spoke of the six bowels; here, the five viscera—
Both viscera and bowels are moistened by the wood’s fluid.
Arraying the five viscera, each viscus supports the others,
Joining the three burners and descending jade nectar,
Then uniting with the primordial qi to circulate without cease.
The liver stores the hun-soul; here, it speaks of my heart-spirit and lung-po,
All gathering in the central yellow palace.
Wood generates essential fluids, transforming them into flowing springs,
Which can wash away foulness and impart their pure fragrance.
"Standing at the suspended hall" means the fluids hesitate to descend,
Their qi already embracing the Hall of Light.
In an instant, the flowing spring surges forth,
Irrigating the three palaces; in the stomach, sounds like thunder and lightning—
Then it settles in the Hall of Light, following one another,
Descending to the Crimson Palace and returning to the primordial sea.

Here at the Gate of Mao and You, when turning left the qi emerges, when turning right the fluids enter—this is the supreme treasure of my body. Between Xu and Hai, it lies concealed at the Mysterious Gate, awaiting the revolutions of the Great Dao.

Men speak of the celestial Dao as distant; I say the celestial Dao is near. The cycles of the celestial degrees reside within my body—one must simply guard its principles. Through purity and non-action, the inner spirits abide; essence and spirit separate and order themselves above and below. Coming and going, they dredge the joints, opening the great Dao of the entire body—this is the immortal herb of longevity. Through diligent irrigation, the mind-ground becomes clear and bright; the seven apertures open, and aging is forever unknown.

This great fortune relies entirely on the two qi of yin and yang. The qi ascending above the Celestial Gate is "pure"; the qi descending below the Celestial Gate is "mysterious." As the *Inner Landscape* states: "Emerging pure, entering mysterious—these two qi shine forth." Thus, one must concentrate the spirit and still the breath, returning to sit at the Celestial Gate, awaiting the separation of yin and yang. When the pure ascends and the mysterious descends, then the cold spring drips drop by drop, flowing down the throat to connect with the divine luminosity. Passing through the floral canopy, it is clear and cool; entering the deep abyss, it is crystalline and cold. It washes the heart and purifies thoughts, revealing my true form—serene and desireless. This is called the Returned Elixir. Once the Returned Elixir is achieved, one may attain longevity.

Before the Golden Elixir returns, below lies the Floral Pool, called the Sea of Qi. The essence within the kidneys stirs here; I stand in the Hall of Light to observe it, illuminating the Cinnabar Field. This is the Office of the General, governing plans and deliberations. The General’s intent is to command all spirits to open the Life Gate, dredging the celestial Dao to reach the numinous root. Then yin and yang array themselves swiftly as shooting stars.

**Chapter Twenty-One**
The lungs govern the qi; the Triple Burner arises,
Concealed at the Celestial Gate, awaiting the old path.
The pure fluid, sweet spring, flows through the six viscera,
Following the nose up and down, known to both ears.
Gazing inward at heaven and earth, the Youth is preserved;
Harmonizing essence and brilliance, teeth and hair are moistened.
The complexion glows, radiant without medicine,
As the fluid descends the throat—how freely it falls!
All spirits gather, seeking one another;
Below, in the Crimson Palace, purple blossoms unfold.
Hidden beneath the floral canopy, it connects to the divine hut,
Focusing the heart-spirit, calling to one another.
Observing my divine luminosity, it repels all evil,
The spleen-spirit returns to its great home.
Reaching the stomach’s passage, it connects to emptiness,
Nurturing the numinous root, never to wither again.
Sealing the Life Gate like a jade capital,
Longevity extends ten thousand years and more.

The opening line refers to the internal commentary. To cultivate the lung qi, one must first lie concealed at the Celestial Gate, awaiting the old path of ingress and egress. When qi transforms into water, the pure fluid, sweet spring, irrigates the six viscera. This qi, following the nose up and down, bridges the magpie bridge—above as qi, below as fluid. The sound of flowing springs is heard in the void, known to the two ears. Through the work of inner illumination, one gazes at the ascent and descent of heaven and earth, preserving the White Prime Youth to assist the six qi. Harmonizing the essence of the entire body, it moistens teeth and hair, brightens the complexion, and eliminates the need for medicine. The fluid descends the throat like scattered rain; all spirits seek it, and each viscus partakes of its sweetness. Below lies the Crimson Palace, where purple blossoms unfold along the trickling stream. Hidden beneath the floral canopy, it connects to the divine hut. Heart and spirit are two yet one. By focusing the heart-spirit, one ensures the heart calls to the spirit, the spirit calls to the heart—now in the heart, now in the spleen—ever luminous, never dim. Observing my divine luminosity, it truly repels all evil influences. In the spleen, the spleen-spirit returns as if home, always abiding there. Reaching the passage of the stomach, it connects to the realm of emptiness, nurturing the numinous root so it never withers again. All this is the cultivation of lung qi. Yet in this nurturing, one must seal the Life Gate, pure as a jade capital, untouched by dust, all thoughts stilled. Thus the numinous root is fortified, and longevity extends ten thousand years with abundance.

**Chapter Twenty-Two**
The spirit within the spleen governs the center,
Summoning the five spirits to harmonize the Three Lights.
Above, it joins the Celestial Gate and meets the Hall of Light,
Dredging the six viscera and regulating the Five Phases.
Metal, wood, water, fire, earth reign as kings;
Sun, moon, and constellations array yin and yang.
Dredging the blood vessels, sweat becomes nectar;
Cultivating the seven apertures dispels misfortune.
The two spirits unite to descend jade blossoms,
Receiving primordial qi, the years grow ever longer.

The spleen belongs to earth, so its spirit presides over the center. "Summoning the five spirits" means gathering the Five Phases; "harmonizing the Three Lights" refers to the revolutions of the Dipper, sun, and moon.

**Continuation of Chapter Twenty-Two**
The phrase *"Above, it joins the Celestial Gate and meets the Hall of Light"* distills the wondrous workings of the preceding two chapters. The Mysterious Gate awaits the Dao of Heaven—this is the spirit of the spleen. The Celestial Gate awaits yin and yang—this, too, is the spirit of the spleen. The Celestial Gate awaits the ancient Way—still, it is the spirit of the spleen. *"Meeting the Hall of Light"* refers not to the Hall of Light between the brows nor the Crimson Palace, but to the central void of nonbeing. *"Dredging the six viscera and regulating the Five Phases"*—among metal, wood, water, and fire, earth reigns as king. The sun, moon, and constellations harbor yin and yang within; who divides them? The spleen’s spirit governs their unfolding. *"Dredging the blood vessels, sweat becomes nectar"* and *"cultivating the seven apertures to dispel misfortune"*—all depend on the spleen’s spirit establishing the center, for intention arises from the heart. The *"two spirits"* are the spleen’s spirit and the heart’s spirit. *"Uniting"* means the two become one. *"Descending jade blossoms"* signifies jade liquid irrigating the heart, as earth also generates the yellow sprout. *"Receiving primordial qi"* means using qi to nourish spirit, for spirit relies on qi; when qi is full, spirit is complete, and the years grow ever longer.

**Chapter Twenty-Three**
Among the five viscera, the kidneys hold the highest rank,
Concealed in the Great Yin, they safeguard their truth.
Exiting and entering through the two apertures, they merge with the Yellow Court,
Inhaling and exhaling the void, my form becomes visible.
They strengthen my sinews and bones, enriching blood and vessels,
In the indistinct and imperceptible, I transcend to pure numinosity.
Seated within the hut, I observe the youth,
With inner breath and focused thought, the light of spirit shines.
Emerging from the Celestial Gate, I enter the boundless,
Serene and desireless, I nurture the root of radiance.
Ingesting primordial qi grants longevity,
Returning to the Seven Gates, I drink from the Great Abyss.
Passing through the Mysterious Throat, I traverse pure numinosity,
Seated within the hut, I behold the youth.
He asks me of the immortal Dao and wondrous methods—
I tell him to consume the purple blossoms of the magic mushroom,
To wear white silk upon his head, his feet planted at the Cinnabar Field,
To bathe in the Flowery Pond and irrigate the numinous root.
When the five viscera harmonize, the Gate of Life opens,
The five flavors all arrive, and good qi returns.
Practicing this with unbound hair ensures enduring existence.

*The Outer Radiance* concludes the five viscera with the kidneys, returning all to the One. The One is water, governed by the kidneys, so among the five viscera, the kidneys hold the highest rank. The Great Yin belongs to the lungs; *"concealed in the Great Yin"* means true metal generates true water, and true water conceals true metal. *"Exiting and entering through the two apertures"* refers to the gates of Qian and Kun, the doors of yin and yang—one metal, one water, one going forth, one returning. Their center is called the Yellow Court, the place where metal and water are stored and where exit and entry converge. The center is emptiness; emptiness is nonbeing. Guarding the center in breath, one takes the space between heaven and earth as a bellows, vibrant and fluid, vast and boundless, subtle and continuous, merging into a single whole—round and luminous, with qi and spirit full, so that one’s true form becomes visible. Thereafter, the sinews and bones grow strong, the blood and vessels flourish, and in the indistinct and imperceptible, one’s form vanishes, soaring beyond the realm of pure numinosity to ascend to the Central Elixir. This is the hut of spirit’s light. Seated beneath it, one observes a youth whose appearance mirrors one’s own—yet pay him no mind. Simply guard the inner breath, focus the thought, and the celestial light of wisdom will shine forth, the spirit growing ever brighter. From there, move the spirit to the Upper Elixir, emerging above the Celestial Gate to commune with the single qi of heaven and earth, vast and boundless, clear and empty—this is me yet not me, this is emptiness yet not emptiness. In harmony with the Dao, one enters the boundless, serene and desireless, nurturing the root of radiance. The root of radiance is the immortal seed; immortals abstain from grain, ingesting only the qi of the Mysterious Gate to attain undying longevity. Yet those who consume primordial qi must also guide the spirit inward, returning to the Seven Gates to drink daily from the Great Abyss. In returning, one takes the path of the Mysterious Throat, passing through pure numinosity to sit once more within the hut, where the youth appears again, asking of the immortal Dao and its wondrous methods. I tell him: there is no other wonder. Simply consume the purple blossoms of the magic mushroom, wear white silk upon your head, plant your feet at the Cinnabar Field, bathe in the Flowery Pond, and irrigate the numinous root. When the five viscera harmonize and a single qi surges in harmony, the Gate of Life opens, the elixir light emerges from the crucible, the five flavors all arrive, and good qi returns to the body. Practicing this with unbound hair—obtaining the medicine, laying the foundation, refining the self, returning the elixir, ascending to the origin, returning to the root, illuminating goodness, and reverting to the beginning—all lies within these few words. This is the immortal Dao and its wondrous method, ensuring enduring existence.

**Chapter Twenty-Four**
The great Dao flows boundlessly—let not the heart grow tangled.
My words are complete; do not recklessly expound them.

Here, Laozi concludes his scripture with two closing exhortations: All that has been spoken above is the great Dao—vast and unobstructed, utterly upright, without crooked paths or deviant byways. Those who practice it must not let their hearts become entangled in complexity and stray onto divergent paths. My words are complete; do not recklessly expound them to the unworthy, lest you bring calamity upon yourself. Treasure and remember them.

**Explanation of the *Classic of Purity***
Laozi said: The great Dao is formless, giving birth to heaven and earth.
The Limitless generates the Supreme Ultimate.
The great Dao is passionless, revolving the sun and moon.
It is non-doing, yet nothing is left undone.
The great Dao is nameless, nurturing the myriad things.
Nonbeing gives birth to being.
I know not its name, so I forcefully call it *"Dao."*
The character for *"Dao"* is formed by combining *"head"* and *"walk"*—it moves and generates prior to heaven and earth. Though called a forced name, it is nonetheless natural.
The Dao possesses purity and turbidity, movement and stillness.
Without pure heaven and turbid earth, there would be no heaven’s movement and earth’s stillness.
Man is pure, woman turbid; man is active, woman still.
Descending from the root, flowing to the branches, the myriad things are born.
Purity and movement are the root; turbidity and stillness are the branches.
One descends, one flows, one bestows, one receives.
When purity and turbidity merge, movement and stillness unite in marriage, the myriad things flourish therefrom.

Purity is the source of turbidity. Nonbeing gives rise to being.
Movement is the foundation of stillness. Being returns to nonbeing.
If one can constantly abide in purity and stillness, heaven and earth will all return to it.
Heaven is pure, earth is still—together they revert entirely to nonbeing.

Now, the spirit of man delights in purity, yet the heart disturbs it.
The heart that disturbs the spirit is the deluded heart.
The human heart delights in stillness, yet desires drag it away.
To attain the heart that delights in stillness, one must first relinquish affairs.
To still the heart, one must first dispel desires.
When the heart is settled, the spirit becomes clear of itself.
To settle the heart is to wash it and withdraw it into concealment.
When the heart is like still water, the spirit becomes clear of itself.
Naturally, the six desires do not arise;
without eyes, ears, nose, tongue, body, or mind,
there are no forms, sounds, scents, tastes, touches, or dharmas.
The three poisons are extinguished.
The three poisons are: the yin spirit that harms human nature,
the yin essence that harms human life,
and the yin qi that assaults the five viscera—these are the three corpses.
The three corpses are the yin bandits within the human body.
Those who speak of the "three Pengs" or "three Gu" are deluded.
The reason one cannot succeed is that the heart is not settled and desires are not dispelled.
The spirit cannot become clear because desires have not been dispelled.
One who can dispel them inwardly observes the heart, yet the heart is no-heart.
One knows that the three minds cannot be grasped.
Outwardly, one observes form, yet form is no-form.
From afar, one observes things, yet things are no-things.
One knows that the four marks are all forgotten.
When these three are awakened to, only emptiness is seen.
Reflecting back, one enters emptiness.
Observing emptiness, even emptiness is empty.
When observing emptiness reaches the place of no-where,
emptiness becomes vast.
If there is something, it does not become emptiness.
When what is emptied is already nothing, even nothingness is nothing.
After observing emptiness reaches the state of no-where,
even emptiness is no more.
If there is emptiness, it cannot be without something.
Nothingness upon nothingness—even nothingness does not stand.
When nothingness upon nothingness is no more,
there is profound and constant stillness.
All dharmas are empty.
Stillness without anything to still—how can desires arise?
Stillness upon stillness—what worry, what thought?
When desires do not arise, this is true stillness.
This is why stillness must first dispel desires.

True constancy responds to things; true constancy abides.
When true stillness is constant, it is called true constancy.
My master said:
*"True constancy can respond to things,
responding to things without delusion,
without delusion, one’s nature naturally abides."*
This expresses the same meaning.
Constant response, constant stillness—this is constant purity and stillness.
The *Ding Guan* states:
*"Whether in affairs or not, always as if without mind.
Whether in stillness or clamor, one’s resolve remains single."*
Through such purity and stillness, one gradually enters the true Dao.
Having entered the true Dao, it is called attaining the Dao.
When the deluded heart is absent, the true heart is present.
Though called attaining the Dao, in reality there is nothing attained.
It is used with emptiness, never self-satisfied or pretentious.
For the sake of transforming sentient beings, it is called attaining the Dao.
Abandoning delusion and returning to truth—this may be called a Daoist.
One who can awaken to this may transmit the sagely Dao.
When the work of nature is awakened, the work of life is transmitted.

Someone asked:
*"Above it is said there is nothing attained, yet here it speaks of transmitting the sagely Dao.
Does this mean the *Qing Jing* scripture has not yet spoken of the Dao?"*
Hanxu replied: Not so. You have misunderstood "nothing attained."
This is entering the Dao and reflecting back—it is the wondrous secret of original nature.
Lao Jun said:
*"The heart is long, the words are weighty—thus I expound again."*

The superior person engages in no contention; the inferior person delights in contention.
The highest virtue does not assert virtue; the lesser virtue clings to virtue.
Those who cling are not called possessors of the Dao and virtue.
Without contention, there are no desires.
With contention, desires multiply.
Not asserting virtue—this is true virtue.
Clinging to virtue—this is no virtue at all.
Without perfect virtue, the perfect Dao does not coalesce.
Those who cling to virtue cannot be expansive—how then could they embody the vast, numinous, and boundless virtue to unite with my vast, numinous, and boundless Dao?

Sentient beings fail to attain the true Dao because they harbor deluded minds.
With deluded minds, they disturb their spirit.
Disturbing their spirit, they become attached to the myriad things.
Attached to the myriad things, they give rise to craving.
With craving, there is affliction.
Affliction and delusion torment body and mind,
leading to defilement and disgrace, drifting through the cycles of birth and death.
Ever submerged in the sea of suffering, they forever lose the true Dao.
This passage employs reverse exposition to reinforce the meaning of entering the true Dao through purity and stillness.

Yet what is lamentable is this: with restless, deluded minds, they agitate the utterly pure true spirit.
Attached to things and lost in delusion, the heart cannot be still—
the true spirit is instead transformed into a deluded mind.
How then could they attain the Dao?

The ever-true Dao is attained through awakening.
Those who awaken to the Dao abide in constant purity and stillness.
The awakened never depart from purity and stillness.
The method of purity and stillness never departs from dispelling desire.
Dispelling desire is the crucial point of this chapter.
Moreover, when the heart is still, fire descends; when the spirit is clear, qi becomes wise.
Wisdom arises in samadhi, and the original destiny naturally returns.
There is also that which arises spontaneously, beyond knowing how or why.

*Commentary on the Jade Pivot Scripture*
Annotations by Xuanyi Hanxuzi

Dwelling at the center and revolving—this is called the *pivot*. The first star of the Northern Dipper is named *Celestial Pivot*, for it revolves from the center. In the human body, the spirit serves as the pivot; when not a speck of dust clings to it, it is as luminously pure as jade—hence the name *Jade Pivot*. The great purport of this scripture is to teach people to refine the primordial spirit.

The Heavenly Worthy declared: “You, all gods and humans, wish to hear the supreme Dao. The supreme Dao is profoundly subtle, not to be found elsewhere. Since you wish to hear it, the one who does not hear is the one. Without hearing, without seeing—this is the true Dao. Even hearing and seeing must be dissolved; only you remain, yet you are not a thing, how much less so this?”

Clear as ice and bright as snow, no further explanation is needed. The word *this* refers to hearing, seeing, and speech.
