The Heavenly Worthy said: “The Dao is entered through sincerity, preserved through silence, and employed through softness. Employing sincerity appears as foolishness; employing silence appears as inarticulateness; employing softness appears as clumsiness. Thus one may forget form, may forget even forgetting. All these are the workings within emptiness and nothingness.

Those who enter the Dao know when to stop; those who preserve the Dao know how to be reverent; those who employ the Dao know subtlety. Knowing subtlety, wisdom-light arises; knowing reverence, sagely wisdom is complete; knowing when to stop, great stability and peace are attained. With great stability and peace, sagely wisdom is complete; with sagely wisdom complete, wisdom-light arises; with wisdom-light arising, one becomes one with the Dao—this is called true forgetting.

Only in forgetting yet not forgetting, where nothing remains to be forgotten, does the supreme Dao reside. The Dao pervades heaven and earth, yet heaven and earth do not know it. Sentient and insentient beings are ultimately one and without duality.”

To enter, one must be able to preserve; to preserve, one must be able to employ. Knowing when to stop, one does not stray; knowing reverence, one does not deviate; knowing subtlety, one deeply enters the profound and obscure. From the profound and obscure arises the indistinct and vague, where numinous awareness attaches—this is wisdom-light. Not deviating, the spirit is tranquil; not straying, qi is still. When qi is still, the spirit is tranquil; when the spirit is tranquil, within the Great Void a single spirit moves alone, knowing only the Dao and nothing else—thus it is said: “This is called true forgetting.” True forgetting is forgetting yet not forgetting, where nothing remains to be forgotten. True forgetting is the supreme Dao.

The Heavenly Worthy said: “In this present world, how shall I benefit living beings? For all gods and humans, I expound this wondrous treasure. Those who awaken to it shall ascend to the immortal’s throne.

Disciples of the Dao, know that there is *qi* and *number*. As wind and earth differ, so too do endowments naturally vary—hence it is called *qi*. As wisdom and foolishness differ, so too do purity and turbidity naturally vary—hence it is called *number*. Number is tied to destiny; qi is tied to heaven. Constrained by qi and number, destiny is bound (bound, as in fettered), and the true Dao cannot be attained. Foolishness may become wisdom; turbidity may become purity—only destiny decrees it. Foolishness is dim; turbidity is dark—these too are shaped by the endowments of wind and earth, which shift and transform.

Heaven and earth move their mechanism, causing people not to know—this is called *spontaneity*. Even when one knows that one does not know, it is still called *spontaneity*. The wonder of spontaneity, though wondrous in knowing, arises precisely from not knowing. Yet in relation to the Dao, it has never begun with foolishness or turbidity.”

All gods heard this, and the four assemblies rejoiced.

*Summary of the Scripture on Protecting Life*

At that time, the Most High Lord of the Primordial Beginning was within the Fivefold-Luminous Palace of the Seven-Jewel Grove, together with the boundless assembly of sages, radiating boundless light that illuminated the boundless worlds. He beheld the boundless sentient beings enduring boundless suffering, tossed and turned in the world, revolving through birth and death, adrift in the river of love, blown about in the sea of desire, sunk in the mire of sound and form, deluded by existence and non-existence. There is no emptiness, yet emptiness; no form, yet form; no non-existence, yet non-existence. There is existence, yet no existence. From beginning to end, all is dark and obscure, unable to illumine themselves, utterly lost in delusion.

The beginningless is the ancestral qi. When ancestral qi transforms and engenders, the true spirit is revealed. This spirit resides within the heart of heaven, inside the Yellow Court. When the one spirit is rightly positioned, the ten thousand spirits pay homage. It is not like the boundless sentient beings, who are all but a single stream of consciousness-spirit, enduring endless suffering in the destinies. Those sentient beings are all clinging to delusion. Clinging to emptiness is wrong; clinging to form is wrong; clinging to existence within non-existence is wrong; clinging to non-existence within existence is also wrong. How pitiable indeed!

The Heavenly Worthy declared: “You sentient beings arise from non-existence into existence, from non-nothingness into nothingness, from non-form into form, from non-emptiness into emptiness. Existence is not made existence; nothingness is not made nothingness; form is not made form; emptiness is not made emptiness. Emptiness is just emptiness, yet emptiness has no fixed emptiness. Form is just form, yet form has no fixed form. Form is emptiness; emptiness is form. If you can know that emptiness is not emptiness and that form is not form, this is called illumination, the beginning of attaining the wondrous sound. Recognizing the dharma of no-emptiness, perceiving without obstruction, entering the gate of manifold wonders, you will naturally awaken. Free from the net of doubts, not attached to the view of emptiness—this passage breaks through the delusion of emptiness, form, existence, and non-existence for sentient beings. If any sentient being awakens to this, they will be able to leave all delusions behind. Purifying the six senses, cutting off evil obstructions, I shall then expound for you this wondrous scripture, called *Protecting Life*. It delivers and saves sentient beings, spreads the teaching in the world, and circulates through recitation. Then flying heavenly deities, vajra warriors who shatter evil, dharma-protecting spirit youths, deliverers from suffering, golden-eyed fierce beasts—each in hundreds of millions—will all attend and guard this scripture. Wherever it is revered, they will ward off danger, support the weak, and deliver all sentient beings from defilements and attachments.”

This passage emphasizes purity once more. Only by purifying the six senses can one attain this wondrous treasure of *Protecting Life* and leave behind all suffering.

At that time, the Heavenly Worthy spoke a gatha:
*“Seeing yet not seeing me,
Hearing yet not hearing me.
Departing from all extremes—
This is called the wondrous Dao.”*

Not seeing me, not hearing me—then there is no self. The self is the harm of all extremes; without self, all extremes are departed. Confucius had no prejudices, no inflexibility, no obstinacy, and thus returned to no-self. For with no-self, prejudices, inflexibility, and obstinacy all cease.

*Summary of the Scripture on Daily Practice*
Daily practice refers to what cannot be departed from even for a moment in the twelve hours. Where there is effort, there will be efficacy—all depends on purifying body and mind. The scripture contains two passages: “In the twelve hours, constantly contemplate purity,” and at the end: “In the twelve hours, constantly maintain purity.” This is the crucial point for the followers of the Most High, hence it is reiterated. In the twelve hours, purity must constantly be cultivated as the foundation of daily practice. The scripture’s language is clear and requires no further annotation.

Respectfully noted by Xiyue of the Mysterious Lineage.

As for daily practice: when eating and drinking, be settled. Seal the mouth, sit upright, and do not arouse a single thought. Forget all things, preserve the spirit, and stabilize the intent. The eyes do not see objects; the ears do not hear sounds. The mind is inwardly guarded, regulating the breath continuously, exhaling gently without interruption, as if present yet absent. Naturally, the heart-fire descends, the kidney-water ascends, saliva is generated in the mouth, and the numinous true essence attaches to the body, attaining the path to longevity.

The first passage speaks of the initial effort: “In the twelve hours, constantly contemplate purity.” Not arousing a single thought is called purity; the numinous terrace being empty of things is called clarity. The body is the dwelling of qi; the mind is the abode of spirit. Where intent moves, spirit moves; where spirit moves, qi scatters. When spirit gathers, the five phases’ true qi coalesces into the elixir pellet, and naturally, within the body there is another body. The harmonious qi penetrates, and the finest essence showers upon the crown. In movement, stillness, sitting, and lying down, one constantly feels the body as if the wind is passing, the abdomen as if thunder is rumbling, the ears hearing the music of immortals without strings. Without speech, there is sound; without striking, there is resonance. Spirit and qi intertwine, the infant revolves. One perceives inner visions, can speak of oneself, sees the affairs of the formless, and dwells with the sages. Spirit moves in and out freely, and one’s lifespan equals heaven and earth, transcending birth and death.

The two lines “In the twelve hours” refer to the time of entering the chamber, when body and mind are pure, and efficacy naturally manifests. All that follows lies within these two lines. Do not allow any harm—“In the twelve hours, constantly maintain purity.” Spirit is the child of qi; qi is the mother of spirit. Like a hen brooding over her eggs, can one preserve spirit and nurture qi without separation? Wondrous! Wondrous!

Within the "twelve hours," these two lines are reiterated, now speaking of the time of nurturing the elixir. Observing the four characters "lest there be harm," one understands.

*Brief Explanation of the* **Great Permeation Sutra** *by Hanxuzi*

"Great Permeation" signifies the meaning of vastness and all-encompassing penetration. Within the *Sutra*, formlessness, bodilessness, imagelessness, signlessness, and non-action—all are boundlessly vast, unimpeded in their penetration.

**Chapter on True Emptiness**
It arises before heaven yet is formless in its arising.
It abides after heaven yet is bodiless in its abiding.
Yet in its bodilessness, it has never truly abided.
Thus it is said: "Inconceivable."
The qi of pre-heaven has no substance; the spirit of post-heaven has no direction.
Yet though spirit has no direction, its transformations operate within direction.
Thus it is said: "It has never truly abided." "Inconceivable"—its wondrous function is inexhaustible.

**Chapter on Profound Principle**
Stillness is its nature, and the heart resides within it.
Activity is the heart, and nature resides within it.
When the heart arises, nature vanishes; when the heart ceases, nature appears.
Like space devoid of images, it is serene and perfectly full.
Stillness is nature, and the heart resides within it.
When all is forgotten, nature is settled, and the heart too is settled.
Activity is the heart, and nature also resides within it.
With solitary awareness, the heart perceives, and nature too is perceived.
The reason ordinary people do not perceive nature is the presence of deluded thoughts.
With deluded thoughts, nature is concealed.
Without deluded thoughts, nature is revealed.
For the substance of nature is fundamentally empty.
Like space devoid of images, the substance of nature is serene.

**Chapter on the Profound and Wondrous**
The Great Dao is signless, thus it inwardly gathers what exists.
True nature is non-active, thus it outwardly does not generate the heart.
It is thus of itself, boundlessly vast.
Confronting circumstances yet forgetting circumstances, it does not sink into the demons of the six thieves.
Dwelling in the dust yet transcending the dust, it does not fall into the transformations of the ten thousand conditions.
Attaining stillness without movement, attaining harmony without change.
Wisdom illumines the ten directions, emptily transforming without action.

*Verse:*
With form, one awakens to formlessness;
Without cultivation, one dissolves cultivation.
It embraces the bodies of the ten thousand images,
Yet clings not to a single thread.

Signless, thus without existence—by inwardly gathering what exists, one returns to the Dao of signlessness.
Non-active, thus without heart—by outwardly not generating the heart, one reverts to the nature of non-action.
Gathering existence to return to non-existence is signlessness; collecting the heart to return to nature is non-action.
Signless and non-active, thus boundlessly vast.
From this, when confronting circumstances and dwelling in the dust, one may seem to perceive signs and action.
Yet without sinking or falling, one still perceives signlessness and non-action.
What concern are the six thieves and ten thousand conditions?
Stillness without movement is concentration; harmony without change is tranquility.
Wisdom illumines and emptily transforms—thus the primordial spirit operates wondrously.
In essence, it is signless and non-active.

*Brief Explanation of the* **Ancient Red Writings of the Cavern**

**Chapter on Holding to the Real**
Movement within movement arises from non-movement.
Action within action arises from non-action.
Movement within movement is yang qi.
It arises from quiescent stillness, thus it is said: "arises from non-movement."
Action within action is returning and restoring.
It is practiced within purity and stillness, thus it is said: "arises from non-action."
In non-action, spirit returns; when spirit returns, the ten thousand things are quiescent.
In non-movement, qi dissolves; when qi dissolves, the ten thousand things are born.
In non-action, spirit returns—for spirit delights in purity.
When spirit returns, things are quiescent—for purity is stillness.
In non-movement, qi dissolves—for qi enters *hai*.
When qi dissolves, things are born—for *hai* becomes *zi*.
Spirit guards spirit; things rely on things—their root returns to the origin.
What guards is non-union in non-union.
What relies is return in return.
Thus, the post-heaven returns to the pre-heaven, and the root is firmly established.
In silence, one awakens; I myself recognize it—entering the seamless.
Silent awakening is the arising of the dim and obscure.
"I myself recognize it" is solitary awareness.
Thus one deeply enters the profound and permeating, without obstruction.
Neither dying nor living, one becomes one with heaven and earth.
The Dao of holding to the real is attained.

**Chapter on Entering Sagehood**
Forgetting the eyes, light overflows boundlessly.
Forgetting the ears, the heart’s knowledge abides in the profound.
When both mechanisms are forgotten, the gate to all wonders is cut off.
When the mechanism of the eyes is still, wisdom-light naturally arises.
When the mechanism of the ears is still, the heart-ground naturally clarifies.
To observe wonders without desire—this is the moment.
Attaining this one wonder, all wonders are cut off in emptiness.
Pure and complete, it accords with the great square.
Vast and boundless, it accords with the measureless.
The greatness of heaven and earth is what I sustain.
The multitude of the ten thousand things is what I uphold.
How can there be an end, to speak of its limitations?
There is a thing formed in chaos, born before heaven and earth.
Vast without directional form, lofty without equal measure.
Heaven and earth, the ten thousand things—all are what I sustain.
Though there be those who speak well, none can fathom its limitations, for it is inexhaustible.
What is finite in the world has limitations to speak of.
The Dao is without end—how can there be limitations to speak of?

**Chapter on Abiding in the World**
Nurture its imagelessness, and images thus endure.
Guard its bodilessness, and the body thus preserves the real.
When the real is fully preserved and mutually aided, one can be long-lasting.
Heaven attains its real and thus is eternal; earth attains its real and thus is enduring; humans attain their real and thus are long-lived.
The reason worldly people cannot be long-lasting is that they lose their imagelessness and scatter their bodilessness, unable to make the nine orifices and hundred bones coexist with the real body—thus they die.
Imagelessness is qi.
Bodilessness is spirit.
When spirit unites with qi, the elixir path is naturally accomplished.
Thus it is said: "When the real is fully preserved and mutually aided, one can be long-lasting."
The following nine sentences are clear and easily understood; annotations are omitted here.

*Brief Explanation of the* **Sutra of Concentrated Contemplation*

Purple Radiance Permeating the Void Master respectfully expounds *Concentrated Contemplation*, which embodies the Buddhist principle of *śamatha-vipaśyanā*. Contemplation here encompasses internal observation, external observation, distant observation, and observation of emptiness. If one can practice concentrated contemplation, the spirit remains unshaken, enabling internal observation of the mindless, external observation of the formless, distant observation of the objectless, and observation of emptiness as emptiness itself.

*The Complete Collection of Daoist Texts* includes annotations by Master Harmonious Simplicity, but his explanations are fragmented and fail to clarify the essential meaning. Here, the exposition follows the original scriptural intent.

The Heavenly Worthy addressed Left Mystery Perfected One, saying: "Left" (zuǒ) means to assist, to aid—one who assists in the mysterious work. As the *Book of Documents* states, "The Duke of Zhou assisted the former kings," where "assist" (zuǒ) is read in the departing tone, meaning "to aid." If one wishes to cultivate the Dao, one must first relinquish worldly affairs. Sever all external matters that conflict with the heart—external matters being those unrelated to body and mind. "Conflict" (wǔ) means opposition, which breeds affliction; thus, one must first abandon and entirely sever such matters to prevent the arising of afflictive thoughts. Only then may one sit in tranquility and inwardly observe the arising of the mind. If even a single thought arises, it must be eradicated to ensure stillness. Here, "thought" refers to those with attachment. As Buddhist scriptures state, one must work at the point where the mind stirs and thoughts arise; thus, they must be removed and extinguished. Even if the thoughts are not explicitly attached but are merely wandering and chaotic, they too must be entirely eradicated. One must practice diligently day and night without the slightest lapse.

"Explicit attachment" refers to the aforementioned "thoughts," such as those for fame and profit. "Wandering and chaotic thoughts" are those without attachment, arising aimlessly, drifting and ceaseless, disordered and inconsequential. "Lapse" means abandonment. One must extinguish the moving mind but not the illuminating mind. One should congeal the empty mind but not fixate the abiding mind. Without relying on any single method, the mind remains constantly settled. "Thought" is the moving mind; "illumination" is the mind of reflexive observation; "emptiness" is the desireless mind; "abiding" is the mind of attachment. "Not relying on any single method" means that all things are empty, yet the mind remains perpetually settled.

However, the mundane mind is restless and competitive. To conclude the above: For beginners, calming the mind is exceedingly difficult. Even if one momentarily succeeds, the mind soon strays again. The struggle between departure and retention causes the entire body to circulate restlessly. Only through prolonged and focused reflection can one achieve mastery. Do not abandon the endeavor of a thousand lifetimes because of a momentary failure to collect the mind. "Calming the mind" is what Buddhists call "pacifying the mind." "Momentarily succeeding but soon straying" is what Confucians describe as "maintaining when present, losing when absent." When lost, it departs; when departed, it is drawn by external conditions. When maintained, it remains; when it remains, it returns to the mind. The interplay of mind and conditions resembles a battle. Following the breath's ebb and flow, it is said: "The hundred parts of the body circulate." To achieve the method of retention, one must ponder the principle of harmonizing the mind. There is nothing that does not proceed from the temporary to the constant; through prolonged and focused reflection, one can achieve mastery. Initially, one may temporarily collect the mind, but subsequently, it abides constantly. The endeavor of a thousand lifetimes refers to the constantly abiding mind mentioned above.

Once one has attained a measure of stillness, then in walking, standing, sitting, and lying down—in all places of engagement and amidst clamor—one must intentionally cultivate calm. Whether occupied or unoccupied, one should remain as if mindless. In quietude or in noise, one's resolve should be singular. "Measure" means "slightly"; "attained" means "ceased." Having slightly attained stillness, one must test it in various conditions to observe whether the mind remains unmoved. Walking, standing, sitting, lying down, quietude, and noise—all are conditions. "Intentionally" means to establish and maintain. The four phrases regarding occupation, non-occupation, movement, and noise all describe intentional effort.

If one binds the mind too tightly, the tension may cause illness. The arising of qi may lead to madness and frenzy—these are its symptoms. The mind tends toward movement, and restraining it too forcefully will result in violent and agitated illness. Qi here refers to the qi within the mind; when the mind moves, qi moves. "Madness" is the symptom of violence; "frenzy" is the symptom of agitation. Madness is minor; violence and agitation are severe. If the mind does not move, one must allow it freedom. When tension and relaxation are balanced, one naturally remains harmoniously adjusted. To restrain without attachment, to release without movement, to remain unperturbed in noise, and to remain unagitated in engagement—this is true concentration. "Balanced" means appropriate. "Naturally harmoniously adjusted" means to be at ease, free, and unhurried. "Without attachment or movement" is the balance of tension and relaxation. "Unperturbed and unagitated" is natural harmony. "True concentration" is perpetual stillness.

Do not seek more engagement because one remains unagitated amidst affairs, nor seek noise because one remains unperturbed in clamor. Regard the absence of affairs as one's true dwelling and the presence of affairs as responsive traces. Like water or a mirror serving as a reflection, one manifests forms in accordance with things. Skillful means alone enable entry into concentration. How can one know that seeking more affairs will not generate agitation, or that forcibly seeking noise will not produce perturbation? "True dwelling" refers to the realm of stillness; "responsive traces" refers to the realm of movement. "Skillful means" means responding to things with the mind, which is the responsive trace. Constant response and constant stillness both aim to achieve the work of concentration. Thus, it is said: "Skillful means alone enable entry into concentration."

The arising of wisdom may be swift or slow, but this does not depend on the individual. Do not urgently seek wisdom within concentration, for urgency harms one's nature, and harm precludes wisdom. If, without seeking wisdom in concentration, wisdom arises naturally, such concentration is called true wisdom. If wisdom is not employed, true insight resembles foolishness, further enriching concentration and wisdom, both of which attain boundless excellence. Wisdom arises from concentration and follows its natural course. To use wisdom is not wisdom; not using it is wisdom. Cultivating both concentration and wisdom brings limitless joy.

If thoughts arise during concentration, one may attract numerous demonic influences. Malevolent spirits and myriad specters will manifest in accordance with the mind. The visions of Heavenly Worthies, immortals, and venerable figures are auspicious signs.

Signs: omens. Ancient scholars distinguished between auspicious signs and ominous signs; here, the latter is meant. Only when the mind in concentration is suddenly open and unobstructed above, vast and foundationless below, will past karma daily dissolve and new karma cease to form. Unentangled and free, one escapes the cage of the dusty world. With long practice, the Dao is naturally attained. Above, no obstruction; below, no foundation—nothing but emptiness. This represents an advanced stage in concentration practice, echoing the earlier scripture’s teaching: “Observe emptiness as empty; how can desire arise?”

Those who attain the Dao exhibit seven progressive signs. First, the mind easily enters concentration (governs itself), becoming aware of all sensory leaks. Second, chronic illnesses vanish entirely, and body and mind feel light and refreshed. Third, premature depletion is replenished, restoring vitality and lifespan. Fourth, one lives for thousands of years, known as an immortal. Fifth, one refines form into qi, known as a perfected person. Sixth, one refines qi into spirit, known as a spirit person. Seventh, one refines spirit to merge with the Dao, known as a realized person. Discernment sharpens with each stage. Upon attaining the supreme Dao, wisdom becomes complete—completeness meaning all seven stages have been traversed.

If, after long practice in concentration, the body shows none of these signs—if one’s brief, impure life fades and perishes, yet claims to possess awakened wisdom or to have attained the Dao—then the principles of the Dao remain unrealized.

A verse explains:
Knowing arises from conditions,
as fire arises from causes.
Each is the movement of true nature,
yet following the current, the source of the Dao is lost.
Striving to still knowing with the mind,
knowing arises and the mind grows more troubled.
Realizing that nature is fundamentally empty,
knowing becomes the gateway to all wonders.

This scripture guides people in cultivating the mind to enter the Dao, its compassion thorough and all-embracing. Here, the meaning is elucidated in accordance with the text to reveal its earnest intent.

*Inscription by Hanxu*

**Commentary on the *Five Kitchens Sutra***
*(The sutra employs a five-character classical verse structure, which is highly intricate, hence this detailed exposition.)*

**Original text annotated by Shi Zihanxu**

The Tang dynasty Perfected Master Yin Min of the Su Ming Abbey in the capital stated: “When one preserves the primordial qi in harmonious balance, the five viscera become full and the five spirits settle into correctness. When the five viscera are full, flavors are sufficient; when the five spirits are settled, cravings disappear.” This scripture addresses the nourishment of the five viscera, as if seeking food from a kitchen—hence the title *Five Kitchens*.

When the primordial qi is harmoniously balanced, the One Dao is entirely at peace. Harmony itself is without singular harmony, its profound principle merging with the profound boundary. The “primordial qi” refers to the precelestial One Qi. “Harmonious balance” means uniting the disharmonious qi within the five viscera. Once the qi of the five viscera is harmonized, the One Qi becomes the Dao’s qi, and thus the entire body is pervaded by harmonious qi. When the body is filled with harmonious qi, that qi circulates fully, vast and boundless, leaving no singular harmony to be named.

Principle: nature, wherein life resides. Boundary: intersection, where two profundities cannot be separated. When the profound intersects with the profound, each rectifies its nature and life, preserving the Great Harmony.

Do not employ the mind to pursue thought, nor seek thoughtlessness. When the mind is without thought, uphold the Dharma thus. The latter “mind” refers to true intention—wisdom’s light. “Do not employ the mind to pursue thought” means that at the intersection of the two profundities, the arising of wisdom is not subject to human control; one must not urgently seek wisdom in concentration. Yet neither should one seek thoughtlessness. Instead, one should attend to the center of the circle, forgetting yet not forgetting, not forgetting yet forgetting. In the space between intention and non-intention, the One Qi arises and wisdom’s light appears—this is the proper practice. Thus it is said: “When the mind is without thought, uphold the Dharma thus.”

Do not use the mind to contemplate the mind,
nor cling to the absence of contemplation.
When the mind is present, do not fixate on the mind;
in truth, guard the abyss of truth.

Do not use the inwardly illuminating mind to externally contemplate the mind of human desire, so that inner thoughts do not leak out and outer thoughts do not intrude. Having established a mind free from external contemplation, do not cling to the mind of absolute detachment. This means one must allow for appropriate flexibility, naturally adjusting, regulating without attachment, releasing without disturbance. The mind resides within, yet do not fixate on a mind that is within, lest it become bound by the Dharma. Simply abide in true constancy, silently guarding the deep abyss—what the *Cantong Qi* calls “floating and wandering, guarding the center of the circle.”

Cultivate principle, detach from intention;
accumulated cultivation does not align with detachment.
When intention is not cultivated,
one’s own karma remains unknown.

In cultivating one’s nature, first uphold intention, then detach from it. Upholding intention ensures focus; detaching from it entrusts intention to emptiness. If one forcefully cultivates nature by rigidly upholding intention, this is called an extreme view, inevitably leading to fixation and conflicting with the principle of detachment. To uphold intention without upholding it, to maintain it subtly—this is the cultivation of one’s own karma, free from personal views, naturally at ease, tranquil and effortless, thus achieving purity and non-action.

All who consume qi bind qi,
but not those who have long stabilized it.
Qi returns to its original qi;
take it as it comes, release it as it goes.

The *Yellow Court Scripture* states: “All people consume grains and the five flavors, but only the perfected consume the harmonious qi of yin and yang.” From this, we understand that those who consume flavors to nourish qi merely sustain temporarily bound qi, which waxes and wanes—unlike the harmonious qi of the One, which abides long and stabilizes within. Within the five viscera, each possesses harmony as its foundation. The realized person gathers the five qi into a cauldron. When the One Qi arises within the cauldron, it returns to the source of each qi. I take it as it comes and release it as it goes. Taking it, the five qi return to the origin; releasing it, the hundred bones are all regulated.

The *Book of Changes* speaks of "yellow-centered, penetrating principle, rightly positioned within the body—its beauty lies within, flowing through the four limbs, manifesting in deeds and enterprises: this is the perfection of beauty." *Xie* (泄) here means "to flow forth."

The *Luminous Mirror Casket Scripture* states: The luminous mirror casket reveals the subtle workings of the Yellow and White, like a precious mirror drawn from its case—radiant, clear, and bright. Its essence lies in quicksilver alone; its method begins with the death of quicksilver, and its foundation lies in first refining the true earth. The Most High, in great compassion, has directly inscribed the essential principles of the Great Elixir upon bamboo and silk. If one can reach the heavens with utmost sincerity, a Perfected One of the Great Clarity will surely break open these subtle words, and all methods will become clear. The laws of immortality are most strict; I dare not rashly annotate them. I humbly reveal the essential wonders of the true scripture within these marks and circles.

The world does not recognize the One—yet from the One Qi, the myriad things are born. If one knows the One and pursues it, attaining the One, all affairs are complete.

The One is the foundation of water, continuing as the Wood of the East. *Gui*-lead gives rise to *Xin*-metal, white and lustrous as jade. The Two is the essence of fire, unerringly the father of the *Geng* direction. *Ren*-water generates yellow gold, and gold becomes the Earth of the Central Palace. Yellow gold is the father of cinnabar; white gold is the mother of quicksilver. The essence of *Ren* and *Gui* waters—recognize them as *Wu* and *Ji*. Black lead is not true lead, for within it lies the primordial water. Cinnabar is not true mercury, for within it lies the mysterious primordial fire. When water and fire achieve mutual completion, they do not stray from the Earth of the *Kun* direction. The true mother, if not spiritually attuned, must be supplemented with the correct yang. Extracting *Kan* to restore *Li*, all things return to the primordial ancestor.

The *Kun* mother nurtures the infant boy; the *Qian* father nurtures the pregnant girl. The maiden weds the infant boy, and they unite as husband and wife. Water and fire coalesce into a single mass, and the couple naturally regard each other. Male and female then conjoin their essences, and from their union, sons and daughters are born. Without a father, a mother cannot form; without a mother, a father is not permitted. When father and mother embody the true form, they do not lose the true Dragon and Tiger. Dragon and Tiger are the Yellow Sprout; the Yellow Sprout is the true earth. True earth is the supreme medicine; the supreme medicine does not depart from the ancestor. Departing from the ancestor, the Elixir cannot form—forming the Elixir is the true principle.

A hen cannot hatch chicks if there is a mother but no father. In vain she broods over single eggs; the qi scatters, and no offspring are born. If you wish to kill quicksilver, you must first kill its mother. When the mother dies and unites with the mysterious primordial, she may then pair with the cinnabar father. Only when father and mother conjoin their true essences is the quicksilver child put to death. Dead quicksilver becomes lead, akin to the Earth of the *Kun* direction. *Kun* earth nurtures the infant boy, transforming into the mother of quicksilver. Quicksilver is but a single substance, never departing from the fire of the South.

In the *Qian* palace dwells the Vermilion Bird; in the *Kun* palace dwells the Dark Warrior. The Dark Warrior produces *Kun* silver; the Vermilion Bird transforms into yellow earth. Only when they converge in the Central Palace is quicksilver put to death. The Northern Dipper is the pivot of heaven, containing within it the water of the Central Palace. When the Four Symbols gather in the Central Palace, South and North become *Wu* and *Ji*. True essence gives rise to *Qian* and *Kun*; lead and mercury are father and mother. Father and mother are *Geng* and *Xin*, truly endowed with the number of the Southwest. Recognize the essence of *Geng* and *Xin*, and you may then refine the earth of the Central Palace. True earth is produced within it, and only then does it govern the central water.

If one recognizes true mercury, within yellow gold lies the spirit-fire. If one recognizes true lead, within white gold lies the spirit-water. When the two qi conjoin to form the Elixir, one need not fear the death of quicksilver. If one does not recognize water and fire, how can one understand the principle of *Qian*-mercury? Damp earth cannot dry water; dry earth can restrain water. If one knows the mechanism of generation and destruction, one grasps the principle of the Returned Elixir.

Yellow gold is true fire; white gold is true water. How many elixir-refiners have become lost ghosts! *Ji*-earth is not true sulfur; *Wu*-earth is not celestial *Gui*. If one takes these as earth, one does not recognize the subtleties within. Quicksilver must mutually generate; quicksilver must be born and die. To kill quicksilver, one must first kill its mother.

The essence of prior-heaven *Qian* and *Kun* is the true *Wu* and *Ji*. *Qian* is yellow, *Kun* is white—yellow and white are medicines beyond compare. Use only the essence of yellow and white, not their physical forms. How is the essence of yellow and white obtained? Use only cinnabar as its match. When cinnabar dies and turns red, only then is quicksilver put to death. Quicksilver cannot fly—here lies the formation of the medicine. If immortals obtain this essence, they steal from heaven and earth. If worldly people obtain this essence, they amass gold and become wealthy. From one revolution to nine revolutions, nine times nine is eighty-one. If one understands this principle, one must firmly and secretly secure it. Conceal it without speech, and one ascends to the rank of immortals. If obtained, broadly aid others; if not obtained, do not waste it in vain. If one carelessly reveals this, even the celestial spirits will be displeased. Be cautious, again and again—these are a thousand words, ten thousand phrases.

The *Golden Valley Scripture*:
The *Golden Valley Song* clarifies the method of planting the medicine. The Great Elixir is like a grain of millet, first sown in the Jade Field. Cultivate, harvest, and reap the golden grain to consume it. Its essence lies in working within lead; its use lies in quicksilver alone.

The Elixir-Refining Formula, the Elixir-Refining Formula—listen carefully as I explain to you:
The mother qi first transmits to the child; the first child’s nature is still unrefined. The second child is likewise; in chaos, they are hard to distinguish. The third child begins to shine, transformed and divided into hardness and decisiveness. One child gives birth to another, and the nine children are fierce in nature. Ascending in purity to the Great Void, celestial immortals come to welcome you.

This Elixir conveys the supreme Dao—do not speak of it to the unworthy.
Elixir, Elixir, Elixir—a single grain grants longevity for ten thousand kalpas.
One gives rise to two, two to three, three to the myriad things without end.
Without karmic affinity, one cannot encounter this Elixir Scripture—this is the true oral transmission of the sages.

The crucible has no head; the cauldron has no ears.
*Geng* is the exterior; *Xin* is the interior.
There are no other medicines to take—only water and fire paired together.
Swallows do not hatch phoenixes; solitary hares do not nurse horses.
Without true father and mother, all that is born is false.
To plant grain, one must use millet—without millet, no grain will grow.

Refining the Elixir requires treasures—without treasures, the Elixir will not form.
If one uses common or杂 substances, all will fail to be true.
Use lead yet do not use lead—one must work within the lead.
When it comes to using lead, to use lead is still mistaken.
If one wishes mercury to die, first one must kill the mercury.
If mercury does not die, how can mercury die?
Raw and cooked mutually restrain each other; through restraint, they naturally attain numinosity.
Black and red cinnabar and mercury congeal—know clearly this principle is most profound.
It does not depart from male and female begetting male and female; the male and female begotten give rise to further descendants.
Mercury is one thing, yet nothing else—first it becomes flesh, then it becomes bone.
Bone and flesh intimately transform into the true; from this, the river chariot may freely reverse.
This medicine has no furnace, only a crucible—a single crucible transforms into ten thousand crucibles.
Gold transforms into gold, silver into silver—when was there ever another external spirit?
After three cycles of turning, refining, and parting the embryo, one even scorns the ancestral lineage as clamor and dust.
The medicine is gold, and gold is the medicine—the mother’s power to nurture the child runs deep.
The pregnant woman must be securely concealed within the chamber of spirit; reared in the inner chamber, her resolve naturally hardens.
Jade fields and golden valleys are sown in due season—I now expound this with utmost clarity.
If one obtains a person to guard it, one should keep it secret and hidden; if not secret and hidden, one will incur Heaven’s wrath.

*Commentary on "The Rootless Tree Lyrics"*
*Annotated by Liu Wuyuan of Mount Qiyun*

Li Hanxu of Changyi Mountain’s Commentary

Preface

The *Wuzhen* of Tiantai elucidates the great matter of returning to the origin through both inner and outer medicines. Yet in its time, those of shallow understanding and ignorance mistook it for a text on furnace fires and sexual arts. Ye Wenshu, failing to grasp the principle of returning, further appended superficial doctrines of purity to ride its fame. Weng Baoguang, upon reading it, laughed and said, “This is one who knows nothing of the Golden Elixir.” He then composed annotations to correct it. Lu Ziye and Chen Shangyang expanded upon it, and with the emergence of the three commentaries on *Wuzhen*, Ye’s annotations were soon buried.

Master Zhang Sanfeng of Liaoyang was a celestial immortal. During his time in Wudang, he composed *Twenty-Four Daoist Songs of the Rootless Tree*, a work peerless with Ziyang’s *Wuzhen*, though the world also misconstrued it as concerning sexual arts and furnace fires. Hanxu declared with clarity, “The Dao does not prevail because the Dao is not made clear.” He longed to compose an exegesis to illuminate it but lamented the lack of like-minded alchemical companions.

Liu Wuyuan of Qiyun, with his vast and discerning talent, authored dozens of works transmitted to the world. Among them was *Annotations on the Rootless Tree*. Hanxu obtained and examined it, finding its diction profound and its principles perfectly harmonious. Thereupon, he rejoiced greatly, for it had anticipated what his own heart had long affirmed. Where Liu’s work left some matters unexhausted, Hanxu supplemented them, revealing without omission the inner and outer medicines, the fire phases of returning, and the sequence of furnaces and crucibles. Thus, the two commentaries on *The Rootless Tree* stand as the three annotations to *Wuzhen*.

Having completed the commentary, he asked me for a preface. Though I am unworthy, I record here the depth of his intent.

Respectfully inscribed by Qingxia Cave’s Master, along with his fellow disciple He Xifu, on the day of the Beginning of Autumn in the year *dingwei*.

Two Lyrics on *The Rootless Tree* to the Tune of *Zhegu Tian*
Self-Composed by the Author

The Dao’s methods circulate—some orthodox, some heterodox;
Those who stray from orthodoxy and embrace the perverse spread across the earth.
Rarely seen is the phoenix soaring through the clouds,
While many, sunk in delusion, become frogs fallen into wells.
Hard to distinguish, the clamor grows chaotic—
All mistake red earth for cinnabar.
To know the true path that penetrates the mysterious,
Study closely the blossoms beneath the Rootless Tree.

*To the Tune of "Selling Flowers"*
Beneath the Rootless Tree, the eternal constant is expounded:
The six realms of sentient beings share a single light.
When you grasp the affairs before and after *Vairocana’s* voice,
You see there is fundamentally no coming or going—your form is majestic.

Inscribed by Zhang Sanfeng, remnant elder of the Great Yuan, on the Day of Balanced Harmony in the *jiazi* year of the Hongwu reign (1484), at the thatched hut on Mount Wudang’s Heavenly Pillar Peak.

---

[Liu’s Annotation]
"The Rootless Tree" is the title of these lyrics.
All trees have roots to grow and flourish;
Without roots, they cannot endure.
Human life in the world—birth, aging, sickness, death—
appears and vanishes in an instant.
A hundred years pass like a spark or lightning flash,
just like a tree without roots.
The Immortal’s twenty-four lyrics, named after the "Rootless Tree,"
awaken worldly people to see through the illusory dream of floating life
and cultivate nature and destiny early.

[Li’s Annotation]
"The Rootless Tree" refers to the *qi* of the human body.
The hundred meridians of the body all arise from *qi*,
and *qi* emerges from the realm of emptiness—hence "rootless."
The alchemists cultivate roots within the realm of emptiness;
both precelestial and postcelestial *qi* arise from nothingness.
Thus, the rootless is the origin of the rooted.
Those refining postcelestial *qi* must enter emptiness to seek being,
then merge being into emptiness.
Those refining precelestial *qi* must merge being into emptiness,
then return from emptiness to being—
the cultivation of roots follows this pattern alone.
Yet the *qi* of the human body differs in youth, prime, and old age,
and the *qi* of refinement varies in its early, middle, and late stages.
The twenty-four chapters correspond to the solar terms of a year,
all urging people to observe the blossoms beneath the Rootless Tree in due season.
This is the perfection of the Dao’s sentiment.

---

[Liu’s Note] Lamenting the world.
[Li’s Note] Exhorting people to nurture the hidden blossom.

The Rootless Tree, its blossoms deep in seclusion—
Who would cease their craving for glory and splendor?
Floating life’s affairs are a boat on the sea of suffering,
Tossed to and fro without freedom.
No shore, no boundary—hard to moor,
Ever drifting in the perilous haunts of fish and dragons.
If you would turn your head, there lies the shore;
Do not wait until the storm wrecks your boat.

[Liu’s Annotation]
Blossoms are the radiant spirit of a tree.
The human body is like a tree,
and the true numen is like the tree’s blossoms.
All trees have roots, so they can grow and bloom.
Only the human body is rootless—life and death are uncertain,
entirely dependent on the movement of a single spark of true numinous *qi*.
When the true numen flourishes, the body lives;
when it declines, the body dies.
Human existence and demise hinge on the flourishing or decline of the true numen.
Thus, though the true numen is the blossom of the human tree,
it is in truth the root of the human tree.
Wang Chongyang called this true numen the "Yellow Sprout,"
Laozi called it the "Golden Blossom,"
Lü Dongbin called it the "Numinous Root,"
Zhang Boduan called it "True Gold,"
Shao Yong called it the "Heavenly Root,"
the Immortal called it "Golden Essence,"
and various alchemical classics call it the "Primordial Unity of *Qi*."
Though its names are manifold, they all describe this one thing.
This thing is born of the precelestial, stored in the postcelestial,
governing Heaven and Earth, unifying yin and yang,
directing the Five Phases, nurturing the myriad beings.
Vast beyond measure, minute beyond limit,
when released, it fills the Six Directions;
when gathered, it retreats into secrecy.
In essence, for Confucians it is the Supreme Ultimate,
for Daoists, the Golden Elixir,
for Buddhists, Perfect Enlightenment.
In function, for Confucians it is Illustrious Virtue or the Heart-Mind of Heaven and Earth,
for Daoists, the Numinous Treasure or the Mysterious Pearl of Millet,
for Buddhists, the Treasury of the True Dharma Eye or the Wondrous Heart of Nirvana.
The true numen of humans is originally round and luminous,
pure and bare, untainted and free,
neither born nor extinguished, neither form nor emptiness.
It neither increases in sages nor decreases in commoners.
Yet, entangled with the postcelestial,
the multitude loses it, while the noble preserves it—
thus the division between sage and commoner.
The multitude loses it by chasing false numina,
while the noble preserves it by not pursuing false numina.
Because the multitude chases false numina,
the true numen becomes dim and obscure.
Following their desires, they crave glory and splendor,
compete for fame and profit, never ceasing.
Mistaking the false for the true,
a hundred worries afflict their hearts,
ten thousand affairs weary their forms—
like a boat on the sea of suffering,
tossed to and fro, drifting in the perilous haunts of fish and dragons.
If one could suddenly awaken and turn back,
leaping to the other shore,
one would not wait for the storm to wreck the boat
and lose one’s nature and life.
To lose the human form once is to be trapped for ten thousand kalpas.

[Li’s Explanation]
The mountain recluse has dwelt for years beneath the "Rootless Tree," ever wishing to expound its profound mysteries and summon those of like kind. One day, strolling through the mountain garden, he saw the flowers and trees in their serene seclusion, naturally abundant in grace, and there he grasped that this word "secluded" was the wellspring from which the twenty-fourth chapter’s "Rootless Tree" unfolds. Secluded—profound, the realm of emptiness and nothingness. All such realms of emptiness in the world are the altars of flowers and trees where Daoists practice. Thus did my mountain teacher inscribe upon the Bamboo-Embracing Study: *"Three paths of secluded blossoms, fragrance at ease; / Four gardens of cultivated bamboo, shadows intertwined."* How wondrous the words! They resonate with this same truth. If flowers are not deeply secluded, their fragrance cannot be at ease; amidst the red dust of the world, peace is elusive day and night.

As for *"shadows intertwined"*—this is the hollow heart and tranquil qi depending upon each other, so that one’s qi nourishes the heart, and the heart nourishes the qi. When qi flourishes, principle is full; when the heart is at peace, the spirit is whole. Thus may one preserve one’s true nature and contain one’s numen. Master Wu Yuan was right to take *"true numen"* as the flower and root of the human tree. True numen is true knowing and numinous knowing. Numinous knowing belongs to the nature, which is yin; true knowing belongs to the emotions, which is yang. If nature and emotions remain uncorrupted, the true numen is complete. Yet alas! The seven emotions and six desires consume and deplete it, inwardly damaging the nature and outwardly harming the emotions, so that the true numen vanishes. When the true numen vanishes, there is a tree without flowers, a tree without roots.

Master Wu Yuan’s meaning is thus. I have more to say: flowers grow from the tree, the tree grows from the root, the root grows from nothingness. The essence of the true numen is indeed conceived within emptiness, hence the phrase *"the flower is truly secluded."* That which is conceived within emptiness is the primordial wisdom of the human being, the emptiness and numen within. Without shadow or form, it embodies all principles and responds to all affairs. Sages and worthies employ it with abundance; immortals and buddhas cultivate it to sufficiency. Yet it must not be entangled with emotions and desires. If emotions and desires intrude, one daily extracts being from non-being to meet one’s needs. The more one extracts being, the more one loses non-being; the more one loses non-being, the more one loses the root. Lose the root, and the tree is lost; lose the tree, and the flower is lost. As the saying goes: *"When a man grows old, his mind wanders east; when a tree grows old, its heart is hollow."* Wisdom is exhausted, emptiness and numen scatter—what true numen remains?

All this comes from craving glory and splendor, refusing to rest, daily pursuing the affairs of floating life, never considering how a hundred years pass in an instant, how death severs all concerns. One sits in the boat of the sea of suffering, yet never thinks that in a single moment of impermanence, the nature may sink with the overturned boat—boundless, shore-less, mooring nowhere, drifting and wandering through the perilous haunts of fish and dragons, endlessly toiling, ceaselessly laboring, only to wound one’s wisdom and fetter one’s emptiness and numen. What benefit is there in this?

The immortal master, moved by profound compassion, turns the compassionate vessel to ferry them, saying: The reason worldly people sink deeply into the sea of suffering and struggle to escape is that they refuse to turn back and do not recognize the shore. If they would but turn back, that is the shore. The shore is the path of awakening. If one can tread the path of awakening, wisdom returns, emptiness and numen endure. Past errors may yet be corrected; future rightness may still be pursued. Though spirit and qi decline, there is a way to restore them. Only do not wait until the storm’s surging waves shatter the boat. Then, if the boat remains, the person remains—ready for the needs of the other shore, an aid to emerging from the abyss.

[Liu’s Words]
Strive, diligent seekers.

[Li’s Words]
Exhort others to graft and renew.
The rootless tree, its blossoms faint,
An aged trunk must sprout fresh shoots anew.
Plum on willow, mulberry on pear—
Such patterns passed to those who cultivate the true.
Since ancient times, immortals’ art of grafting stands:
Even the old may yet be cured by healing hands.
Seek a wise teacher, ask the way,
Begin at once—yet still the hour is late to start the Way.

[Liu’s Annotation]
Many doubt that age and feeble strength, with spirit spent,
Are like a tree whose flowers fade, its vital force undone,
Never to return to primal source, nor root reclaim,
And so they cast themselves away, awaiting death alone.
Yet mark how aged trees, grafted with tender shoots,
Burgeon anew in lush array—
Plum on willow, mulberry on pear,
All lifeless things, yet life restored.
How much more man, most numinous of beings,
Endowed with heaven and earth’s true qi,
Could age not also find its cure?
What is this healing art?
It is the Way of grafting age anew.
To know this Way, seek out a wise teacher,
Beg the true method without delay.
Once found, begin at once—yet still the hour is late.

[Li’s Exegesis]
Faint means decline.
When one grows old, the primal qi wanes,
And grafting must be hastened then.
Observe the plum on willow, mulberry on pear—
There lies the model.
*To lodge* is as in the elixir method:
Lodging in the Gate of Dui, or the Western Neighbor’s abode;
*To graft* is as in the elixir method:
Joining life to nature, self to other.
Thus plum on willow, mulberry on pear
Are patterns for today’s true cultivators,
Ancient immortals’ grafting Way.
Grafting is the cure for age.
Though grafting trees has proven arts,
Plum and willow, mulberry and pear are kindred kinds.
The old branch split, the tender shoot inserted,
Bound and wrapped, good soil applied,
Yin and yang conjoined—
Such is the tree-grafting art.
The cure for age must likewise match kind with kind,
Refined soil fused, then youth and primal source restored.
This method wise teachers know;
It rests with seekers to inquire.
“Begin at once—yet still the hour is late”:
Lest time outrun us, impermanence arrive unlooked-for,
Nature unenlightened, life unsecured,
And one stray into the boundless ghostly realm.
Immortal He Xiangu said:
“In the Gardens of Lang,
Peach trees bear willow branches;
On Fusang’s boughs, pears entwine—
These are the Eastern King and Queen Mother’s
Pointers to the immortal’s Way.”
Zhang Sanfeng’s words are not without foundation.

[Liu’s Words] On the work of refining the self.
[Li’s Words] On the subtleties of the flower and willow.

The rootless tree—its blossoms pure and green,
From ancient days to now, flower-wine makes immortals.
The brothel’s haze, the meat-and-wine grove:
No need to shun flesh’s scent, nor stray into lust.
Lust wastes the treasure of long life;
Meat and wine pass through the gut—yet the Dao abides in the heart.
Open the gate, I tell you this:
Without flower, without wine, the Dao cannot be achieved.

[Liu’s Annotation]
The Way of the Golden Elixir takes utter purity—without the slightest dross—as its goal. Yet to attain such purity, one must first pass through the realm of utter turbidity. Only by remaining unstained amid the deepest filth does true purity arise. Thus it is said: “The rootless tree—its blossoms pure and green; from ancient days to now, flower-wine makes immortals.”

How can flower and wine give rise to immortals? The brothel’s haze and the meat-and-wine grove are deft at ensnaring mortals. Yet if one can walk through the brothel seeing color as no-color, untempted by its haze; if one can dwell in the meat-and-wine grove, taking each day as it comes, unburdened by its fare—then one neither strays into lust nor shuns flesh’s scent. Appetite and desire are both transformed, the heart of the Dao abides, the human heart fades, and the true spirit remains unharmed, undiminished. The Way may then be hoped for.

The phrase “no need to shun flesh’s scent” does not mean craving it, but rather that meat and wine pass through the gut while the heart makes no reckoning. Unstained by lust, the heart is free of the brothel’s haze; meat and wine pass through the gut, yet the heart knows no meat or wine. A heart free of the brothel’s haze naturally bears the immortal’s blossom of long life; a heart free of meat and wine naturally holds the immortal’s wine of life’s extension. With immortal blossom and immortal wine, one arrives at the realm of pure truth. Those in the world who shun the brothel’s haze and avoid meat and wine—how could they know that immortals emerge from the midst of mortal blossoms and mortal wine? How could they know that without blossom, without wine, the Dao cannot be achieved?

The *Knocking on the Hexagrams Song* says:
“Wine is a good friend, blossoms a companion;
In the willow lanes and flower streets, seek the true person.
The true person is found only in the flower streets’ play.”
This is the subtle use of “blossoms pure and green.”

[Li’s Explanation]
For most people, the nature of appetite and desire is strongest. Master Immortal Zhang Sanfeng thus used flower and wine as pointers. Many crave flower and wine—yet who knows of the flower-wine immortals? The primordial qi within the body is fresh, verdant, and tender. If one can consume it, harness it, drink it, and wear it, one’s spirit becomes clear and qi invigorated. This is what is meant by “flower-wine immortals,” who have existed from ancient times to the present. Yet these are not the brothel’s haze or the meat-and-wine grove of the world.

The haze of brothels and the fare of meat and wine dull the spirit. The qi of meat and wine is rank, and the haze of brothels stirs lust—both are harmful, though lust is the graver. Those skilled in refining the self eat when food is at hand, without seeking more. Thus they do not shun flesh’s scent, yet forget it; they see color as no-color, without clinging to beauty. Thus they do not stray into lust, and lust is extinguished. Otherwise, essence scatters and fluids leak, causing no small harm. As the *Yellow Court Classic* says: “Leaves fall, the tree withers, losing its verdancy; only by sealing and guarding the light can one attain lasting peace.” From this we know that straying into lust must waste the treasure of long life.

Meat and wine pass through the gut—yet the Dao still abides in the heart. How could flower and wine delude one? It is people who delude themselves with flower and wine! Without seeking mortal blossoms or mortal wine, one must find the immortal blossom and immortal wine. Immortal blossom and immortal wine aid the achievement of the Dao—they are the fresh, tender blossoms on the rootless tree, rich in flavor and fine in hue, most potent for nourishment.

The immortal master opens the primordial gate and tells you this: without such blossoms and wine, the Dao is difficult to achieve.

---

[Liu’s Words] Refuting heterodox paths.
[Li’s Words] Lamenting solitary cultivation.

The rootless tree—its blossoms solitary.
May I ask: have you found the matching yin and yang?
A hen’s egg—hard to hatch a chick,
For it has turned its back on the alchemical furnace of yin and yang.
A woman without a husband is a resentful maiden;
A man without a wife is a widowed man.
I sigh for the deluded,
So muddled—
Sitting in stillness, cultivating alone, their qi withers.

[Liu’s Annotation]
The Way of cultivating truth requires the matching of yin and yang to fully form the treasure of the true spirit. If there is yin without yang, or yang without yin, this is called “solitary blossoms without match,” and the true spirit cannot be formed—just like a hen’s egg. It is hard to hatch a chick because it has turned its back on the alchemical furnace of yin-yang union.

Again, like a woman without a husband or a man without a wife—how can they bear offspring? Those blind fools in the world, not delving into the principles of yin and yang, not tracing the source of creation, muddled in their affairs: some contemplate emptiness, some regulate breath, some fixate on spirits, some guard the apertures, some move qi—all are sitting in stillness, cultivating alone, yin without yang. Not only do they fail to benefit their nature and life, but they harm them, and the more they cultivate, the more their qi withers.

[Li’s Explanation]
“Solitary” refers to inner cultivation. Cultivating nature alone cannot establish life, for it is solitary and without counterpart, unable to give birth to the treasure of life. It is like a hen without a rooster—though it can lay eggs, it cannot hatch chicks. Today, those who cultivate nature to nurture qi do not realize that their qi is merely solitary yin. To avoid withering, true yang must be matched with true yin—only then are they of the same kind.

May I ask those who cultivate the Dao: have you obtained the matching kind? Here, true yang is righteousness, and true yin is the Dao. Matching righteousness with the Dao, one is no longer solitary. Yet there are three layers to this process: first, true yin gives rise to true yang; next, true yang is matched with true yin; finally, from the union of yin and yang, the vast qi of the true spirit is born. How could this be like a hen’s egg, hard to hatch a chick?

Those that cannot hatch chicks do so because they have abandoned the principle of yin and yang and the furnace of creation. Yin and yang are husband and wife. The Dao of the sage begins with husband and wife, and from them all things are transformed and born—so too is it with the men and women of the world. If a woman has no husband, she is solitary yin that cannot give birth and becomes a resentful maiden; if a man has no wife, he is solitary yang that cannot be nurtured and becomes a widowed bachelor. This principle is clear and easy to understand, yet the deluded of the world remain utterly confused, believing that sitting in stillness alone can clarify the mind, reveal the nature, and allow one to ascend directly—all without ever speaking of the matching of yin and yang. I fear that day after day of sitting and cultivating will only lead one to wander the halls of empty nothingness or play in the sea of extinction, until eventually their qi withers and fades.

*[Liu’s verse:]*
Matching yin and yang.
*[Li’s verse:]*
Inverting yin and yang.
The rootless tree, its blossoms askew—
separated from yin and yang, the Dao is incomplete.
Gold blocks wood, mercury blocks lead,
yang widowed, yin solitary, each to one side.
In the world, yin and yang—man pairs with woman—
bear sons and grandsons, generation after generation.
Following the flow makes one mortal; reversing it makes one immortal—
the difference lies solely in this inversion.

*[Liu’s commentary:]*
The *Book of Changes* states: “One yin, one yang—this is called the Dao.” *Awakening to Reality* says: “When yin and yang are of the same kind, they return to mutual resonance; when the two eights are balanced, they naturally unite.” If yin and yang are each askew—if yang stirs but yin does not respond, if yin seeks but yang does not beckon, if yang is excessive while yin is deficient, or if yang is abundant while yin is lacking—then the blossom of true spirit is askew, neither centered nor upright, and the Dao cannot be complete.

A person’s true emotion is like gold, true knowing like lead—both are firm. The numinous nature is like wood, the numinous knowing like mercury—both are soft. True emotion and true knowing, being firm, easily sink; the numinous nature and numinous knowing, being soft, easily float. If nature seeks emotion, emotion returns to nature; if the true restrains the numinous, the numinous returns to the true—then firm and soft respond to each other, yin and yang harmonize, and they transform into one qi, with life’s mechanism enduring unceasingly. But if emotion does not return to nature, if the numinous does not return to the true—this is what is meant by “gold blocks wood, mercury blocks lead, yang widowed, yin solitary, each to one side.” How then can one return to the root and restore the origin, coalescing the elixir of true spirit?

Observe the world: when man and woman unite, they bear sons and grandsons, passing life from generation to generation without end. Know then that the Dao of cultivating the true is the same—when yin and yang unite, they give birth to immortals and sages, also passing on without end. The difference lies only in whether one follows the flow or reverses it, distinguishing immortals from mortals. Following the flow makes one mortal; reversing it makes one immortal—the contention lies solely in this inversion.

This “inversion” is profound in principle and subtle in practice. It is not the center of inside and outside, nor the center of the body’s upper and lower. It is the center of the union of bright yang, formless and imageless, called the root of heaven and earth, the aperture of yin and yang, the abode of life and death, the gate of primordial transformation. Human life resides here, human death resides here; becoming a sage or worthy resides here, becoming human or beast also resides here. If one cultivating the Dao can stand firm in this place, reverse the flow, and invert it, then calamity transforms into fortune, punishment into virtue—what is called “the elixir forming in a single hour.”

Alas! The “inversion” is not easily known, nor is the person who inverts it. The sexual arts take the place of union between man and woman as the “inversion,” with the man absorbing the woman’s blood as the reversal. The circulation arts take the Yellow Court as the “inversion,” with qi and blood rising in the back and descending in the front as the reversal. All these are but crude imitations—how could they understand the meaning of inversion in the path of immortals? Those who seek the truth should discern this carefully.

*[Li’s explanation:]*
“Askew” refers to yin and yang being separated, unable to complete the elixir. When yin and yang unite at the center, the knife-point of the elixir congeals, and the arts of the Dao are fully realized. Gold and wood, lead and mercury—these are yin and yang. The essence of wood and the nature of mercury belong to yin; the qi of gold and the emotion of lead belong to yang. Essence and qi require each other, nature and emotion resonate—gold cherishes the benevolence of wood, wood loves the righteousness of gold; mercury goes to welcome lead, lead comes to join mercury. Only then is there no illness of separation, and the great medicine of the true body is born. If yin is solitary and yang widowed, each to one side, then yin and yang do not pair, askew and incomplete—how can they transform and give birth to the supreme treasure, passing it down for ten thousand generations?

The method of matching is similar for immortals and mortals—only mortals use the flow, while immortals use the reversal. Master Wu Yuan said that the “inversion” is not easily known; I say that even the word “reverse” is not understood. To invert at the center, one must first know the wondrous use of “reverse.” If one knows reversal, then gold, wood, lead, and mercury are all within it; yin and yang, qian and kun, are all inverted—and moreover, all events revolve within the word “reverse.” Obtaining the medicine and returning the elixir can be achieved in an instant.

*[Liu’s verse:]*
When the medicine is born.
*[Li’s verse:]*
The principle of *kun* and *shen*.
The rootless tree, its blossoms fresh—
born in the *kun* direction, *kun* is the person.
Pluck the blossom, wear it;
gather the blossom’s heart,
its pistils layered in spring’s splendor.
People of the world do not grasp the principle within the blossom—
a single secret of heaven’s mechanism is worth ten thousand gold.
Borrow the blossom’s name, become the blossom’s body—
every line of *Knocking on the Trigrams* speaks truly.

*[Liu’s commentary:]*
“Fresh” means that the original thing, long buried, suddenly reappears. When the blossom is fresh, its radiance is reborn—like the moon appearing in the southwest *kun* direction, beneath pure yin, where a speck of subtle yang emerges. This is like the empty room generating brightness, the true spirit manifesting, and the original face reappearing.

This original face is none other than the undying true person within me—with this person, one is human; without it, one is not. It is what I receive from heaven to be human. Yet this true person does not readily appear, nor is it often seen. Only in moments of utter emptiness and profound stillness, when all conditions are stilled, does its image fleetingly emerge. Utter emptiness and profound stillness are the very image of *kun* in its pure yin—hence it is said, “born in the *kun* direction, *kun* is the person.”

This person has long been buried beneath dust and grime, leaving no trace. When its image suddenly appears, it is like a fresh blossom—an opportunity not to be missed. One must act swiftly, plucking and gathering it to make it one’s own. To “pluck the blossom and wear it” is to take up this true person’s flower; to “gather the blossom’s heart” is to gather this true person’s heart. Gradually plucking and gathering, from little to much, one accumulates depth and radiance, and the true spirit remains undimmed. Then the pistils layer upon layer, all principles become luminous, and wherever the heart turns, the Way is present—its splendor as bright as spring, with yang qi spreading everywhere and blossoms blooming in every place.

Yet this flower belongs to everyone; everyone sees it, yet no one grasps it. Most let it slip past them, face to face. If one does grasp it, transcending the mundane and entering sagehood occurs in an instant—hence it is said, “a single secret of heaven’s mechanism is worth ten thousand gold.” The immortal elder, out of compassion, borrows the blossom’s name to become the blossom’s body—now form, now formless—each line of *Knocking on the Trigrams* expounding the intent of the primordial, wishing for all to attain the Dao and each to become an immortal. Alas, how can those of this age not grasp the principle within the blossom, and instead recklessly speculate with “bedchamber arts” and “inner elixirs of the boudoir”? Even the immortal elder can do nothing about it. How lamentable!

*[Li’s commentary:]*
Master Wuyuan speaks of the character “person” as the original face—one who has glimpsed this person cannot help but speak at length and with deep feeling, seeking to awaken both self and others with earnest compassion. Yet the word “is” in “kun *is* the person” has not been fully illuminated.

Now, the blossom is likened to the person, and the person borrows the blossom as a metaphor. The blossom being fresh is like a person returning to the root, attaining emptiness and guarding stillness, observing the return of that single yang—unaware, the spring scenery is renewed. This blossom appears in the *kun* direction, meaning *kun* sees the blossom, the blossom sees the person, and where the blossom is born is where the person is born—hence “kun *is* the person.”

In the alchemical method, lead is sown in the metal village, mercury is scattered in the fire land. Metal and fire reside in the southwest; the southwest gains its companion. Where metal and fire unite is precisely above the *kun* direction. This person is a golden body and fiery form, a realm of pure yang—our true qi, also called true emotion. Vague and indistinct, within it there is an image: hot as fire, radiant as a blossom. Its fragrance intoxicates like wine, and to obtain it is to be as if drunk. At this moment, one must pluck and wear it without delay. One must further gather its heart, swallowing it whole. From blossom to pistil, penetrating layer upon layer, it is truly the splendor of spring’s palace. Yet people know only its exterior and not its interior, and thus cannot grasp the wondrous principle within the blossom.

The wondrous principle within the blossom is pure heaven’s mechanism. When heaven’s mechanism is revealed, a single secret is worth ten thousand gold. The fourth, fifth, sixth, and seventh lines here are all from Lü Zu’s *Knocking on the Trigrams*. Master Feng said: “Lü Zu borrows the blossom’s name for the human body and takes the blossom’s body as the human’s body. My lines, each drawn from *Knocking on the Trigrams*, speak with utmost truth and savor.”

[Liu’s words] Seize the moment to pluck and gather.
[Li’s words] Before the furnace, settle into stillness.

The rootless tree—its blossoms now in full bloom,
Fair and delicate, their beauty rivals powdered jade.
Guard against the ape and horse, stubborn and wild;
Don the iron mask of your natal face.
Raise the true treasure-sword of the Azure Dragon,
Pluck every fresh blossom from the garden wall.
With wind-filled sails, return fully laden—
How could one pass the mountain of treasures in vain?

[Liu’s commentary] When the primordial true spirit shines forth, one root gives rise to ten thousand branches. In every moment, contemplate its images—nothing exists apart from the blossoms in their abundance. At the height of their bloom, when radiance is fully revealed and spirit expends itself outwardly, one is prone to vie for marvels and flaunt refinement. Thus it is said: “Fair and delicate, their beauty rivals powdered jade.” At this very moment, one must guard against peril and danger, firmly tether the ape and horse, don the iron mask of one’s natal face, and wield the treasure-sword of the Azure Dragon. In the presence of phenomena, forget emotion; pluck every blossom that emerges from the wall, allowing not the slightest attachment to form or dissipation of true qi. The “natal face” is the iron mask of unknowing and unawareness. The “treasure-sword of the Azure Dragon” is the true nature untouched by defilement. The natal iron mask and the Azure Dragon’s treasure-sword arise from the same source but bear different names: in terms of essence, it is the natal iron mask; in terms of function, it is the Azure Dragon’s treasure-sword. The iron mask is the settled body; the treasure-sword is the instrument of wisdom. Stillness employs wisdom; wisdom perfects stillness. Stillness and wisdom depend on each other; essence and function are never apart. The primordial true spirit is form yet emptiness, ever responsive yet ever still, without leakage or seepage—this is what is meant by “plucking every fresh blossom from the garden wall.” The blossoms on the wall have just emerged but have not yet left the wall, the moment when true qi has not yet dispersed. Pluck them at this instant, and they are utterly free of dross, purely celestial truth. Gradually grow, gradually gather; gradually pluck, gradually withdraw—one must pluck until nothing remains to be plucked. Alas! The great medicine is hard to encounter; the great method is hard to meet. Having chanced upon them, the moment must not be missed. With wind-filled sails, swiftly pluck the fresh blossoms, return fully laden—how could one pass the mountain of treasures in vain, only to regret it later?

[Li’s Explanation]
"Abundance" refers to the moment of fullness. The beauty of a fair face and delicate features is likened to the primordial unity of qi—just as the Immortal Elder’s *Five Watches Daoist Ballad* says: *"When all yin is spent, the radiant yang season arrives, / A branch of spring’s splendor, golden blossoms in bloom."* "Surpassing the powdered mass" describes the birth of the medicine, the very instant when the flower-demon vies in beauty. As the ancient immortals said: *"When the primordial arises, the demon of medicine readily stirs."* If one’s cultivation is not yet pure and unwavering in the face of beauty, who could seize the golden blossom in but half a moment? Thus it is said: *"Guard against the ape and horse, ever more stubborn"*—just as the *One Branch of Flowers* ballad puts it: *"With delicate, alluring grace, / Ten slender fingers, soft and fair, / Yet they stir not my intent-steed or mind-ape."*

Raise the iron mask of your birth-given face, bring forth your power of stillness—*"Let them try, yet not a trace of wavering remains."* Draw the true treasure-sword of the Azure Dragon, pluck every fresh blossom from the garden wall—this aligns with *"Repel the demon horde, fiercely wield the keen-edged sword of wisdom, / Dare to gather those blossoms newly sprung beyond the wall."* Wu Yuan takes the iron mask as stillness and the treasure-sword as wisdom, truly a kindred understanding. Yet if stillness and wisdom do not arise from the refinement of the self, then stillness is not true stillness, nor wisdom true wisdom—they cannot be employed when the moment arrives.

The *One Branch of Flowers* says: *"Moment by moment, guard the intent-steed, / Instant by instant, lock the mind-ape, / Day and night without rest, / Refine the self with unbroken effort."* One must cultivate until *"I am a sea of purity, unstained by a single speck of dust"*—only then is it true stillness. *"I am a night-shining pearl, suspended in the void"*—only then is it true wisdom.

The "garden wall" signifies the blossom that has emerged beyond the wall yet still clings to it—this is called the "blossom beyond the wall." The fire is at its purest, the timing at its truest; without a transmitted teaching, none can comprehend it, and without having traversed the path, none can know it. If one recognizes the moment the flower’s branch emerges beyond the wall and immediately gathers it, this is the immortal’s art. "Plucking every blossom" means drawing it all in with a single breath, swallowing it into one’s own domain—not the gradual gathering and gradual withdrawal. Gradual gathering and withdrawal belong to the later stages of nurturing, drawing out, and replenishing.

Seize the wind-filled sails, return fully laden—when the four signs converge, the elixir is formed, and the River Chariot is swiftly set in motion to carry it back. How could one pass the mountain of treasures in vain? This mountain of treasures is the birthplace of the primordial.

In the alchemical method, what is refined is called medicine, what is gathered is called medicine, and what is nurtured is called fire. Yet where there is medicine, there is fire—though not the fire of gentle nurturing. This chapter’s annotation correctly interprets the gathering of medicine.

---

[Liu’s Note] Advancing and retreating yin and yang.
[Li’s Note] Nurturing the returned elixir.

The rootless tree, its blossoms in flight,
Once shed, they bloom again at the appointed time.
When the lead-flower appears, as *gui* is spent,
Once more the western garden’s branches burst with bloom.
Just gathered beneath the moon’s waning light,
Again they mend the robe in morning’s glow.
This mystery, rare in the world,
Must be pondered deeply with the immortals.

[Liu’s Annotation]
When a person’s spirit declines and true numen scatters, it is like blossoms flying and withering away. Yet even fallen blossoms have their season of rebirth; so too does the decline of a person hold the way of return. What is this way of return?
